49𓀴
50𓀴I. A SCANDAL IN BOHEMIA
51𓀴
52𓀴
53𓀴I.
54𓀴
55𓀴To Sherlock Holmes she is always _the_ woman. I have seldom heard him
56𓀴mention her under any other name. In his eyes she eclipses and
57𓀴predominates the whole of her sex. It was not that he felt any emotion
58𓀴akin to love for Irene Adler. All emotions, and that one particularly,
59𓀴were abhorrent to his cold, precise but admirably balanced mind. He
60𓀴was, I take it, the most perfect reasoning and observing machine that
61𓀴the world has seen, but as a lover he would have placed himself in a
62𓀴false position. He never spoke of the softer passions, save with a gibe
63𓀴and a sneer. They were admirable things for the observer—excellent for
64𓀴drawing the veil from men’s motives and actions. But for the trained
65𓀴reasoner to admit such intrusions into his own delicate and finely
66𓀴adjusted temperament was to introduce a distracting factor which might
67𓀴throw a doubt upon all his mental results. Grit in a sensitive
68𓀴instrument, or a crack in one of his own high-power lenses, would not
69𓀴be more disturbing than a strong emotion in a nature such as his. And
70𓀴yet there was but one woman to him, and that woman was the late Irene
71𓀴Adler, of dubious and questionable memory.
72𓀴
73𓀴I had seen little of Holmes lately. My marriage had drifted us away
74𓀴from each other. My own complete happiness, and the home-centred
75𓀴interests which rise up around the man who first finds himself master
76𓀴of his own establishment, were sufficient to absorb all my attention,
77𓀴while Holmes, who loathed every form of society with his whole Bohemian
78𓀴soul, remained in our lodgings in Baker Street, buried among his old
79𓀴books, and alternating from week to week between cocaine and ambition,
80𓀴the drowsiness of the drug, and the fierce energy of his own keen
81𓀴nature. He was still, as ever, deeply attracted by the study of crime,
82𓀴and occupied his immense faculties and extraordinary powers of
83𓀴observation in following out those clues, and clearing up those
84𓀴mysteries which had been abandoned as hopeless by the official police.
85𓀴From time to time I heard some vague account of his doings: of his
86𓀴summons to Odessa in the case of the Trepoff murder, of his clearing up
87𓀴of the singular tragedy of the Atkinson brothers at Trincomalee, and
88𓀴finally of the mission which he had accomplished so delicately and
89𓀴successfully for the reigning family of Holland. Beyond these signs of
90𓀴his activity, however, which I merely shared with all the readers of
91𓀴the daily press, I knew little of my former friend and companion.
92𓀴
93𓀴One night—it was on the twentieth of March, 1888—I was returning from a
94𓀴journey to a patient (for I had now returned to civil practice), when
95𓀴my way led me through Baker Street. As I passed the well-remembered
96𓀴door, which must always be associated in my mind with my wooing, and
97𓀴with the dark incidents of the Study in Scarlet, I was seized with a
98𓀴keen desire to see Holmes again, and to know how he was employing his
99𓀴extraordinary powers. His rooms were brilliantly lit, and, even as I
100𓀴looked up, I saw his tall, spare figure pass twice in a dark silhouette
101𓀴against the blind. He was pacing the room swiftly, eagerly, with his
102𓀴head sunk upon his chest and his hands clasped behind him. To me, who
103𓀴knew his every mood and habit, his attitude and manner told their own
104𓀴story. He was at work again. He had risen out of his drug-created
105𓀴dreams and was hot upon the scent of some new problem. I rang the bell
106𓀴and was shown up to the chamber which had formerly been in part my own.
107𓀴
108𓀴His manner was not effusive. It seldom was; but he was glad, I think,
109𓀴to see me. With hardly a word spoken, but with a kindly eye, he waved
110𓀴me to an armchair, threw across his case of cigars, and indicated a
111𓀴spirit case and a gasogene in the corner. Then he stood before the fire
112𓀴and looked me over in his singular introspective fashion.
113𓀴
114𓀴“Wedlock suits you,” he remarked. “I think, Watson, that you have put
115𓀴on seven and a half pounds since I saw you.”
116𓀴
117𓀴“Seven!” I answered.
118𓀴
119𓀴“Indeed, I should have thought a little more. Just a trifle more, I
120𓀴fancy, Watson. And in practice again, I observe. You did not tell me
121𓀴that you intended to go into harness.”
122𓀴
123𓀴“Then, how do you know?”
124𓀴
125𓀴“I see it, I deduce it. How do I know that you have been getting
126𓀴yourself very wet lately, and that you have a most clumsy and careless
127𓀴servant girl?”
128𓀴
129𓀴“My dear Holmes,” said I, “this is too much. You would certainly have
130𓀴been burned, had you lived a few centuries ago. It is true that I had a
131𓀴country walk on Thursday and came home in a dreadful mess, but as I
132𓀴have changed my clothes I can’t imagine how you deduce it. As to Mary
133𓀴Jane, she is incorrigible, and my wife has given her notice, but there,
134𓀴again, I fail to see how you work it out.”
135𓀴
136𓀴He chuckled to himself and rubbed his long, nervous hands together.
137𓀴
138𓀴“It is simplicity itself,” said he; “my eyes tell me that on the inside
139𓀴of your left shoe, just where the firelight strikes it, the leather is
140𓀴scored by six almost parallel cuts. Obviously they have been caused by
141𓀴someone who has very carelessly scraped round the edges of the sole in
142𓀴order to remove crusted mud from it. Hence, you see, my double
143𓀴deduction that you had been out in vile weather, and that you had a
144𓀴particularly malignant boot-slitting specimen of the London slavey. As
145𓀴to your practice, if a gentleman walks into my rooms smelling of
146𓀴iodoform, with a black mark of nitrate of silver upon his right
147𓀴forefinger, and a bulge on the right side of his top-hat to show where
148𓀴he has secreted his stethoscope, I must be dull, indeed, if I do not
149𓀴pronounce him to be an active member of the medical profession.”
150𓀴
151𓀴I could not help laughing at the ease with which he explained his
152𓀴process of deduction. “When I hear you give your reasons,” I remarked,
153𓀴“the thing always appears to me to be so ridiculously simple that I
154𓀴could easily do it myself, though at each successive instance of your
155𓀴reasoning I am baffled until you explain your process. And yet I
156𓀴believe that my eyes are as good as yours.”
157𓀴
158𓀴“Quite so,” he answered, lighting a cigarette, and throwing himself
159𓀴down into an armchair. “You see, but you do not observe. The
160𓀴distinction is clear. For example, you have frequently seen the steps
161𓀴which lead up from the hall to this room.”
162𓀴
163𓀴“Frequently.”
164𓀴
165𓀴“How often?”
166𓀴
167𓀴“Well, some hundreds of times.”
168𓀴
169𓀴“Then how many are there?”
170𓀴
171𓀴“How many? I don’t know.”
172𓀴
173𓀴“Quite so! You have not observed. And yet you have seen. That is just
174𓀴my point. Now, I know that there are seventeen steps, because I have
175𓀴both seen and observed. By the way, since you are interested in these
176𓀴little problems, and since you are good enough to chronicle one or two
177𓀴of my trifling experiences, you may be interested in this.” He threw
178𓀴over a sheet of thick, pink-tinted notepaper which had been lying open
179𓀴upon the table. “It came by the last post,” said he. “Read it aloud.”
180𓀴
181𓀴The note was undated, and without either signature or address.
182𓀴
183𓀴“There will call upon you to-night, at a quarter to eight o’clock,” it
184𓀴said, “a gentleman who desires to consult you upon a matter of the very
185𓀴deepest moment. Your recent services to one of the royal houses of
186𓀴Europe have shown that you are one who may safely be trusted with
187𓀴matters which are of an importance which can hardly be exaggerated.
188𓀴This account of you we have from all quarters received. Be in your
189𓀴chamber then at that hour, and do not take it amiss if your visitor
190𓀴wear a mask.”
191𓀴
192𓀴“This is indeed a mystery,” I remarked. “What do you imagine that it
193𓀴means?”
194𓀴
195𓀴“I have no data yet. It is a capital mistake to theorise before one has
196𓀴data. Insensibly one begins to twist facts to suit theories, instead of
197𓀴theories to suit facts. But the note itself. What do you deduce from
198𓀴it?”
199𓀴
200𓀴I carefully examined the writing, and the paper upon which it was
201𓀴written.
202𓀴
203𓀴“The man who wrote it was presumably well to do,” I remarked,
204𓀴endeavouring to imitate my companion’s processes. “Such paper could not
205𓀴be bought under half a crown a packet. It is peculiarly strong and
206𓀴stiff.”
207𓀴
208𓀴“Peculiar—that is the very word,” said Holmes. “It is not an English
209𓀴paper at all. Hold it up to the light.”
210𓀴
211𓀴I did so, and saw a large “E” with a small “g,” a “P,” and a large “G”
212𓀴with a small “t” woven into the texture of the paper.
213𓀴
214𓀴“What do you make of that?” asked Holmes.
215𓀴
216𓀴“The name of the maker, no doubt; or his monogram, rather.”
217𓀴
218𓀴“Not at all. The ‘G’ with the small ‘t’ stands for ‘Gesellschaft,’
219𓀴which is the German for ‘Company.’ It is a customary contraction like
220𓀴our ‘Co.’ ‘P,’ of course, stands for ‘Papier.’ Now for the ‘Eg.’ Let us
221𓀴glance at our Continental Gazetteer.” He took down a heavy brown volume
222𓀴from his shelves. “Eglow, Eglonitz—here we are, Egria. It is in a
223𓀴German-speaking country—in Bohemia, not far from Carlsbad. ‘Remarkable
224𓀴as being the scene of the death of Wallenstein, and for its numerous
225𓀴glass-factories and paper-mills.’ Ha, ha, my boy, what do you make of
226𓀴that?” His eyes sparkled, and he sent up a great blue triumphant cloud
227𓀴from his cigarette.
228𓀴
229𓀴“The paper was made in Bohemia,” I said.
230𓀴
231𓀴“Precisely. And the man who wrote the note is a German. Do you note the
232𓀴peculiar construction of the sentence—‘This account of you we have from
233𓀴all quarters received.’ A Frenchman or Russian could not have written
234𓀴that. It is the German who is so uncourteous to his verbs. It only
235𓀴remains, therefore, to discover what is wanted by this German who
236𓀴writes upon Bohemian paper and prefers wearing a mask to showing his
237𓀴face. And here he comes, if I am not mistaken, to resolve all our
238𓀴doubts.”
239𓀴
240𓀴As he spoke there was the sharp sound of horses’ hoofs and grating
241𓀴wheels against the curb, followed by a sharp pull at the bell. Holmes
242𓀴whistled.
243𓀴
244𓀴“A pair, by the sound,” said he. “Yes,” he continued, glancing out of
245𓀴the window. “A nice little brougham and a pair of beauties. A hundred
246𓀴and fifty guineas apiece. There’s money in this case, Watson, if there
247𓀴is nothing else.”
248𓀴
249𓀴“I think that I had better go, Holmes.”
250𓀴
251𓀴“Not a bit, Doctor. Stay where you are. I am lost without my Boswell.
252𓀴And this promises to be interesting. It would be a pity to miss it.”
253𓀴
254𓀴“But your client—”
255𓀴
256𓀴“Never mind him. I may want your help, and so may he. Here he comes.
257𓀴Sit down in that armchair, Doctor, and give us your best attention.”
258𓀴
259𓀴A slow and heavy step, which had been heard upon the stairs and in the
260𓀴passage, paused immediately outside the door. Then there was a loud and
261𓀴authoritative tap.
262𓀴
263𓀴“Come in!” said Holmes.
264𓀴
265𓀴A man entered who could hardly have been less than six feet six inches
266𓀴in height, with the chest and limbs of a Hercules. His dress was rich
267𓀴with a richness which would, in England, be looked upon as akin to bad
268𓀴taste. Heavy bands of astrakhan were slashed across the sleeves and
269𓀴fronts of his double-breasted coat, while the deep blue cloak which was
270𓀴thrown over his shoulders was lined with flame-coloured silk and
271𓀴secured at the neck with a brooch which consisted of a single flaming
272𓀴beryl. Boots which extended halfway up his calves, and which were
273𓀴trimmed at the tops with rich brown fur, completed the impression of
274𓀴barbaric opulence which was suggested by his whole appearance. He
275𓀴carried a broad-brimmed hat in his hand, while he wore across the upper
276𓀴part of his face, extending down past the cheekbones, a black vizard
277𓀴mask, which he had apparently adjusted that very moment, for his hand
278𓀴was still raised to it as he entered. From the lower part of the face
279𓀴he appeared to be a man of strong character, with a thick, hanging lip,
280𓀴and a long, straight chin suggestive of resolution pushed to the length
281𓀴of obstinacy.
282𓀴
283𓀴“You had my note?” he asked with a deep harsh voice and a strongly
284𓀴marked German accent. “I told you that I would call.” He looked from
285𓀴one to the other of us, as if uncertain which to address.
286𓀴
287𓀴“Pray take a seat,” said Holmes. “This is my friend and colleague, Dr.
288𓀴Watson, who is occasionally good enough to help me in my cases. Whom
289𓀴have I the honour to address?”
290𓀴
291𓀴“You may address me as the Count Von Kramm, a Bohemian nobleman. I
292𓀴understand that this gentleman, your friend, is a man of honour and
293𓀴discretion, whom I may trust with a matter of the most extreme
294𓀴importance. If not, I should much prefer to communicate with you
295𓀴alone.”
296𓀴
297𓀴I rose to go, but Holmes caught me by the wrist and pushed me back into
298𓀴my chair. “It is both, or none,” said he. “You may say before this
299𓀴gentleman anything which you may say to me.”
300𓀴
301𓀴The Count shrugged his broad shoulders. “Then I must begin,” said he,
302𓀴“by binding you both to absolute secrecy for two years; at the end of
303𓀴that time the matter will be of no importance. At present it is not too
304𓀴much to say that it is of such weight it may have an influence upon
305𓀴European history.”
306𓀴
307𓀴“I promise,” said Holmes.
308𓀴
309𓀴“And I.”
310𓀴
311𓀴“You will excuse this mask,” continued our strange visitor. “The august
312𓀴person who employs me wishes his agent to be unknown to you, and I may
313𓀴confess at once that the title by which I have just called myself is
314𓀴not exactly my own.”
315𓀴
316𓀴“I was aware of it,” said Holmes dryly.
317𓀴
318𓀴“The circumstances are of great delicacy, and every precaution has to
319𓀴be taken to quench what might grow to be an immense scandal and
320𓀴seriously compromise one of the reigning families of Europe. To speak
321𓀴plainly, the matter implicates the great House of Ormstein, hereditary
322𓀴kings of Bohemia.”
323𓀴
324𓀴“I was also aware of that,” murmured Holmes, settling himself down in
325𓀴his armchair and closing his eyes.
326𓀴
327𓀴Our visitor glanced with some apparent surprise at the languid,
328𓀴lounging figure of the man who had been no doubt depicted to him as the
329𓀴most incisive reasoner and most energetic agent in Europe. Holmes
330𓀴slowly reopened his eyes and looked impatiently at his gigantic client.
331𓀴
332𓀴“If your Majesty would condescend to state your case,” he remarked, “I
333𓀴should be better able to advise you.”
334𓀴
335𓀴The man sprang from his chair and paced up and down the room in
336𓀴uncontrollable agitation. Then, with a gesture of desperation, he tore
337𓀴the mask from his face and hurled it upon the ground. “You are right,”
338𓀴he cried; “I am the King. Why should I attempt to conceal it?”
339𓀴
340𓀴“Why, indeed?” murmured Holmes. “Your Majesty had not spoken before I
341𓀴was aware that I was addressing Wilhelm Gottsreich Sigismond von
342𓀴Ormstein, Grand Duke of Cassel-Felstein, and hereditary King of
343𓀴Bohemia.”
344𓀴
345𓀴“But you can understand,” said our strange visitor, sitting down once
346𓀴more and passing his hand over his high white forehead, “you can
347𓀴understand that I am not accustomed to doing such business in my own
348𓀴person. Yet the matter was so delicate that I could not confide it to
349𓀴an agent without putting myself in his power. I have come _incognito_
350𓀴from Prague for the purpose of consulting you.”
351𓀴
352𓀴“Then, pray consult,” said Holmes, shutting his eyes once more.
353𓀴
354𓀴“The facts are briefly these: Some five years ago, during a lengthy
355𓀴visit to Warsaw, I made the acquaintance of the well-known adventuress,
356𓀴Irene Adler. The name is no doubt familiar to you.”
357𓀴
358𓀴“Kindly look her up in my index, Doctor,” murmured Holmes without
359𓀴opening his eyes. For many years he had adopted a system of docketing
360𓀴all paragraphs concerning men and things, so that it was difficult to
361𓀴name a subject or a person on which he could not at once furnish
362𓀴information. In this case I found her biography sandwiched in between
363𓀴that of a Hebrew rabbi and that of a staff-commander who had written a
364𓀴monograph upon the deep-sea fishes.
365𓀴
366𓀴“Let me see!” said Holmes. “Hum! Born in New Jersey in the year 1858.
367𓀴Contralto—hum! La Scala, hum! Prima donna Imperial Opera of Warsaw—yes!
368𓀴Retired from operatic stage—ha! Living in London—quite so! Your
369𓀴Majesty, as I understand, became entangled with this young person,
370𓀴wrote her some compromising letters, and is now desirous of getting
371𓀴those letters back.”
372𓀴
373𓀴“Precisely so. But how—”
374𓀴
375𓀴“Was there a secret marriage?”
376𓀴
377𓀴“None.”
378𓀴
379𓀴“No legal papers or certificates?”
380𓀴
381𓀴“None.”
382𓀴
383𓀴“Then I fail to follow your Majesty. If this young person should
384𓀴produce her letters for blackmailing or other purposes, how is she to
385𓀴prove their authenticity?”
386𓀴
387𓀴“There is the writing.”
388𓀴
389𓀴“Pooh, pooh! Forgery.”
390𓀴
391𓀴“My private note-paper.”
392𓀴
393𓀴“Stolen.”
394𓀴
395𓀴“My own seal.”
396𓀴
397𓀴“Imitated.”
398𓀴
399𓀴“My photograph.”
400𓀴
401𓀴“Bought.”
402𓀴
403𓀴“We were both in the photograph.”
404𓀴
405𓀴“Oh, dear! That is very bad! Your Majesty has indeed committed an
406𓀴indiscretion.”
407𓀴
408𓀴“I was mad—insane.”
409𓀴
410𓀴“You have compromised yourself seriously.”
411𓀴
412𓀴“I was only Crown Prince then. I was young. I am but thirty now.”
413𓀴
414𓀴“It must be recovered.”
415𓀴
416𓀴“We have tried and failed.”
417𓀴
418𓀴“Your Majesty must pay. It must be bought.”
419𓀴
420𓀴“She will not sell.”
421𓀴
422𓀴“Stolen, then.”
423𓀴
424𓀴“Five attempts have been made. Twice burglars in my pay ransacked her
425𓀴house. Once we diverted her luggage when she travelled. Twice she has
426𓀴been waylaid. There has been no result.”
427𓀴
428𓀴“No sign of it?”
429𓀴
430𓀴“Absolutely none.”
431𓀴
432𓀴Holmes laughed. “It is quite a pretty little problem,” said he.
433𓀴
434𓀴“But a very serious one to me,” returned the King reproachfully.
435𓀴
436𓀴“Very, indeed. And what does she propose to do with the photograph?”
437𓀴
438𓀴“To ruin me.”
439𓀴
440𓀴“But how?”
441𓀴
442𓀴“I am about to be married.”
443𓀴
444𓀴“So I have heard.”
445𓀴
446𓀴“To Clotilde Lothman von Saxe-Meningen, second daughter of the King of
447𓀴Scandinavia. You may know the strict principles of her family. She is
448𓀴herself the very soul of delicacy. A shadow of a doubt as to my conduct
449𓀴would bring the matter to an end.”
450𓀴
451𓀴“And Irene Adler?”
452𓀴
453𓀴“Threatens to send them the photograph. And she will do it. I know that
454𓀴she will do it. You do not know her, but she has a soul of steel. She
455𓀴has the face of the most beautiful of women, and the mind of the most
456𓀴resolute of men. Rather than I should marry another woman, there are no
457𓀴lengths to which she would not go—none.”
458𓀴
459𓀴“You are sure that she has not sent it yet?”
460𓀴
461𓀴“I am sure.”
462𓀴
463𓀴“And why?”
464𓀴
465𓀴“Because she has said that she would send it on the day when the
466𓀴betrothal was publicly proclaimed. That will be next Monday.”
467𓀴
468𓀴“Oh, then we have three days yet,” said Holmes with a yawn. “That is
469𓀴very fortunate, as I have one or two matters of importance to look into
470𓀴just at present. Your Majesty will, of course, stay in London for the
471𓀴present?”
472𓀴
473𓀴“Certainly. You will find me at the Langham under the name of the Count
474𓀴Von Kramm.”
475𓀴
476𓀴“Then I shall drop you a line to let you know how we progress.”
477𓀴
478𓀴“Pray do so. I shall be all anxiety.”
479𓀴
480𓀴“Then, as to money?”
481𓀴
482𓀴“You have _carte blanche_.”
483𓀴
484𓀴“Absolutely?”
485𓀴
486𓀴“I tell you that I would give one of the provinces of my kingdom to
487𓀴have that photograph.”
488𓀴
489𓀴“And for present expenses?”
490𓀴
491𓀴The King took a heavy chamois leather bag from under his cloak and laid
492𓀴it on the table.
493𓀴
494𓀴“There are three hundred pounds in gold and seven hundred in notes,” he
495𓀴said.
496𓀴
497𓀴Holmes scribbled a receipt upon a sheet of his note-book and handed it
498𓀴to him.
499𓀴
500𓀴“And Mademoiselle’s address?” he asked.
501𓀴
502𓀴“Is Briony Lodge, Serpentine Avenue, St. John’s Wood.”
503𓀴
504𓀴Holmes took a note of it. “One other question,” said he. “Was the
505𓀴photograph a cabinet?”
506𓀴
507𓀴“It was.”
508𓀴
509𓀴“Then, good-night, your Majesty, and I trust that we shall soon have
510𓀴some good news for you. And good-night, Watson,” he added, as the
511𓀴wheels of the royal brougham rolled down the street. “If you will be
512𓀴good enough to call to-morrow afternoon at three o’clock I should like
513𓀴to chat this little matter over with you.”
514𓀴
515𓀴
516𓀴II.
517𓀴
518𓀴At three o’clock precisely I was at Baker Street, but Holmes had not
519𓀴yet returned. The landlady informed me that he had left the house
520𓀴shortly after eight o’clock in the morning. I sat down beside the fire,
521𓀴however, with the intention of awaiting him, however long he might be.
522𓀴I was already deeply interested in his inquiry, for, though it was
523𓀴surrounded by none of the grim and strange features which were
524𓀴associated with the two crimes which I have already recorded, still,
525𓀴the nature of the case and the exalted station of his client gave it a
526𓀴character of its own. Indeed, apart from the nature of the
527𓀴investigation which my friend had on hand, there was something in his
528𓀴masterly grasp of a situation, and his keen, incisive reasoning, which
529𓀴made it a pleasure to me to study his system of work, and to follow the
530𓀴quick, subtle methods by which he disentangled the most inextricable
531𓀴mysteries. So accustomed was I to his invariable success that the very
532𓀴possibility of his failing had ceased to enter into my head.
533𓀴
534𓀴It was close upon four before the door opened, and a drunken-looking
535𓀴groom, ill-kempt and side-whiskered, with an inflamed face and
536𓀴disreputable clothes, walked into the room. Accustomed as I was to my
537𓀴friend’s amazing powers in the use of disguises, I had to look three
538𓀴times before I was certain that it was indeed he. With a nod he
539𓀴vanished into the bedroom, whence he emerged in five minutes
540𓀴tweed-suited and respectable, as of old. Putting his hands into his
541𓀴pockets, he stretched out his legs in front of the fire and laughed
542𓀴heartily for some minutes.
543𓀴
544𓀴“Well, really!” he cried, and then he choked and laughed again until he
545𓀴was obliged to lie back, limp and helpless, in the chair.
546𓀴
547𓀴“What is it?”
548𓀴
549𓀴“It’s quite too funny. I am sure you could never guess how I employed
550𓀴my morning, or what I ended by doing.”
551𓀴
552𓀴“I can’t imagine. I suppose that you have been watching the habits, and
553𓀴perhaps the house, of Miss Irene Adler.”
554𓀴
555𓀴“Quite so; but the sequel was rather unusual. I will tell you, however.
556𓀴I left the house a little after eight o’clock this morning in the
557𓀴character of a groom out of work. There is a wonderful sympathy and
558𓀴freemasonry among horsey men. Be one of them, and you will know all
559𓀴that there is to know. I soon found Briony Lodge. It is a _bijou_
560𓀴villa, with a garden at the back, but built out in front right up to
561𓀴the road, two stories. Chubb lock to the door. Large sitting-room on
562𓀴the right side, well furnished, with long windows almost to the floor,
563𓀴and those preposterous English window fasteners which a child could
564𓀴open. Behind there was nothing remarkable, save that the passage window
565𓀴could be reached from the top of the coach-house. I walked round it and
566𓀴examined it closely from every point of view, but without noting
567𓀴anything else of interest.
568𓀴
569𓀴“I then lounged down the street and found, as I expected, that there
570𓀴was a mews in a lane which runs down by one wall of the garden. I lent
571𓀴the ostlers a hand in rubbing down their horses, and received in
572𓀴exchange twopence, a glass of half-and-half, two fills of shag tobacco,
573𓀴and as much information as I could desire about Miss Adler, to say
574𓀴nothing of half a dozen other people in the neighbourhood in whom I was
575𓀴not in the least interested, but whose biographies I was compelled to
576𓀴listen to.”
577𓀴
578𓀴“And what of Irene Adler?” I asked.
579𓀴
580𓀴“Oh, she has turned all the men’s heads down in that part. She is the
581𓀴daintiest thing under a bonnet on this planet. So say the
582𓀴Serpentine-mews, to a man. She lives quietly, sings at concerts, drives
583𓀴out at five every day, and returns at seven sharp for dinner. Seldom
584𓀴goes out at other times, except when she sings. Has only one male
585𓀴visitor, but a good deal of him. He is dark, handsome, and dashing,
586𓀴never calls less than once a day, and often twice. He is a Mr. Godfrey
587𓀴Norton, of the Inner Temple. See the advantages of a cabman as a
588𓀴confidant. They had driven him home a dozen times from Serpentine-mews,
589𓀴and knew all about him. When I had listened to all they had to tell, I
590𓀴began to walk up and down near Briony Lodge once more, and to think
591𓀴over my plan of campaign.
592𓀴
593𓀴“This Godfrey Norton was evidently an important factor in the matter.
594𓀴He was a lawyer. That sounded ominous. What was the relation between
595𓀴them, and what the object of his repeated visits? Was she his client,
596𓀴his friend, or his mistress? If the former, she had probably
597𓀴transferred the photograph to his keeping. If the latter, it was less
598𓀴likely. On the issue of this question depended whether I should
599𓀴continue my work at Briony Lodge, or turn my attention to the
600𓀴gentleman’s chambers in the Temple. It was a delicate point, and it
601𓀴widened the field of my inquiry. I fear that I bore you with these
602𓀴details, but I have to let you see my little difficulties, if you are
603𓀴to understand the situation.”
604𓀴
605𓀴“I am following you closely,” I answered.
606𓀴
607𓀴“I was still balancing the matter in my mind when a hansom cab drove up
608𓀴to Briony Lodge, and a gentleman sprang out. He was a remarkably
609𓀴handsome man, dark, aquiline, and moustached—evidently the man of whom
610𓀴I had heard. He appeared to be in a great hurry, shouted to the cabman
611𓀴to wait, and brushed past the maid who opened the door with the air of
612𓀴a man who was thoroughly at home.
613𓀴
614𓀴“He was in the house about half an hour, and I could catch glimpses of
615𓀴him in the windows of the sitting-room, pacing up and down, talking
616𓀴excitedly, and waving his arms. Of her I could see nothing. Presently
617𓀴he emerged, looking even more flurried than before. As he stepped up to
618𓀴the cab, he pulled a gold watch from his pocket and looked at it
619𓀴earnestly, ‘Drive like the devil,’ he shouted, ‘first to Gross &
620𓀴Hankey’s in Regent Street, and then to the Church of St. Monica in the
621𓀴Edgeware Road. Half a guinea if you do it in twenty minutes!’
622𓀴
623𓀴“Away they went, and I was just wondering whether I should not do well
624𓀴to follow them when up the lane came a neat little landau, the coachman
625𓀴with his coat only half-buttoned, and his tie under his ear, while all
626𓀴the tags of his harness were sticking out of the buckles. It hadn’t
627𓀴pulled up before she shot out of the hall door and into it. I only
628𓀴caught a glimpse of her at the moment, but she was a lovely woman, with
629𓀴a face that a man might die for.
630𓀴
631𓀴“‘The Church of St. Monica, John,’ she cried, ‘and half a sovereign if
632𓀴you reach it in twenty minutes.’
633𓀴
634𓀴“This was quite too good to lose, Watson. I was just balancing whether
635𓀴I should run for it, or whether I should perch behind her landau when a
636𓀴cab came through the street. The driver looked twice at such a shabby
637𓀴fare, but I jumped in before he could object. ‘The Church of St.
638𓀴Monica,’ said I, ‘and half a sovereign if you reach it in twenty
639𓀴minutes.’ It was twenty-five minutes to twelve, and of course it was
640𓀴clear enough what was in the wind.
641𓀴
642𓀴“My cabby drove fast. I don’t think I ever drove faster, but the others
643𓀴were there before us. The cab and the landau with their steaming horses
644𓀴were in front of the door when I arrived. I paid the man and hurried
645𓀴into the church. There was not a soul there save the two whom I had
646𓀴followed and a surpliced clergyman, who seemed to be expostulating with
647𓀴them. They were all three standing in a knot in front of the altar. I
648𓀴lounged up the side aisle like any other idler who has dropped into a
649𓀴church. Suddenly, to my surprise, the three at the altar faced round to
650𓀴me, and Godfrey Norton came running as hard as he could towards me.
651𓀴
652𓀴“‘Thank God,’ he cried. ‘You’ll do. Come! Come!’
653𓀴
654𓀴“‘What then?’ I asked.
655𓀴
656𓀴“‘Come, man, come, only three minutes, or it won’t be legal.’
657𓀴
658𓀴“I was half-dragged up to the altar, and before I knew where I was I
659𓀴found myself mumbling responses which were whispered in my ear, and
660𓀴vouching for things of which I knew nothing, and generally assisting in
661𓀴the secure tying up of Irene Adler, spinster, to Godfrey Norton,
662𓀴bachelor. It was all done in an instant, and there was the gentleman
663𓀴thanking me on the one side and the lady on the other, while the
664𓀴clergyman beamed on me in front. It was the most preposterous position
665𓀴in which I ever found myself in my life, and it was the thought of it
666𓀴that started me laughing just now. It seems that there had been some
667𓀴informality about their license, that the clergyman absolutely refused
668𓀴to marry them without a witness of some sort, and that my lucky
669𓀴appearance saved the bridegroom from having to sally out into the
670𓀴streets in search of a best man. The bride gave me a sovereign, and I
671𓀴mean to wear it on my watch chain in memory of the occasion.”
672𓀴
673𓀴“This is a very unexpected turn of affairs,” said I; “and what then?”
674𓀴
675𓀴“Well, I found my plans very seriously menaced. It looked as if the
676𓀴pair might take an immediate departure, and so necessitate very prompt
677𓀴and energetic measures on my part. At the church door, however, they
678𓀴separated, he driving back to the Temple, and she to her own house. ‘I
679𓀴shall drive out in the park at five as usual,’ she said as she left
680𓀴him. I heard no more. They drove away in different directions, and I
681𓀴went off to make my own arrangements.”
682𓀴
683𓀴“Which are?”
684𓀴
685𓀴“Some cold beef and a glass of beer,” he answered, ringing the bell. “I
686𓀴have been too busy to think of food, and I am likely to be busier still
687𓀴this evening. By the way, Doctor, I shall want your co-operation.”
688𓀴
689𓀴“I shall be delighted.”
690𓀴
691𓀴“You don’t mind breaking the law?”
692𓀴
693𓀴“Not in the least.”
694𓀴
695𓀴“Nor running a chance of arrest?”
696𓀴
697𓀴“Not in a good cause.”
698𓀴
699𓀴“Oh, the cause is excellent!”
700𓀴
701𓀴“Then I am your man.”
702𓀴
703𓀴“I was sure that I might rely on you.”
704𓀴
705𓀴“But what is it you wish?”
706𓀴
707𓀴“When Mrs. Turner has brought in the tray I will make it clear to you.
708𓀴Now,” he said as he turned hungrily on the simple fare that our
709𓀴landlady had provided, “I must discuss it while I eat, for I have not
710𓀴much time. It is nearly five now. In two hours we must be on the scene
711𓀴of action. Miss Irene, or Madame, rather, returns from her drive at
712𓀴seven. We must be at Briony Lodge to meet her.”
713𓀴
714𓀴“And what then?”
715𓀴
716𓀴“You must leave that to me. I have already arranged what is to occur.
717𓀴There is only one point on which I must insist. You must not interfere,
718𓀴come what may. You understand?”
719𓀴
720𓀴“I am to be neutral?”
721𓀴
722𓀴“To do nothing whatever. There will probably be some small
723𓀴unpleasantness. Do not join in it. It will end in my being conveyed
724𓀴into the house. Four or five minutes afterwards the sitting-room window
725𓀴will open. You are to station yourself close to that open window.”
726𓀴
727𓀴“Yes.”
728𓀴
729𓀴“You are to watch me, for I will be visible to you.”
730𓀴
731𓀴“Yes.”
732𓀴
733𓀴“And when I raise my hand—so—you will throw into the room what I give
734𓀴you to throw, and will, at the same time, raise the cry of fire. You
735𓀴quite follow me?”
736𓀴
737𓀴“Entirely.”
738𓀴
739𓀴“It is nothing very formidable,” he said, taking a long cigar-shaped
740𓀴roll from his pocket. “It is an ordinary plumber’s smoke-rocket, fitted
741𓀴with a cap at either end to make it self-lighting. Your task is
742𓀴confined to that. When you raise your cry of fire, it will be taken up
743𓀴by quite a number of people. You may then walk to the end of the
744𓀴street, and I will rejoin you in ten minutes. I hope that I have made
745𓀴myself clear?”
746𓀴
747𓀴“I am to remain neutral, to get near the window, to watch you, and at
748𓀴the signal to throw in this object, then to raise the cry of fire, and
749𓀴to wait you at the corner of the street.”
750𓀴
751𓀴“Precisely.”
752𓀴
753𓀴“Then you may entirely rely on me.”
754𓀴
755𓀴“That is excellent. I think, perhaps, it is almost time that I prepare
756𓀴for the new role I have to play.”
757𓀴
758𓀴He disappeared into his bedroom and returned in a few minutes in the
759𓀴character of an amiable and simple-minded Nonconformist clergyman. His
760𓀴broad black hat, his baggy trousers, his white tie, his sympathetic
761𓀴smile, and general look of peering and benevolent curiosity were such
762𓀴as Mr. John Hare alone could have equalled. It was not merely that
763𓀴Holmes changed his costume. His expression, his manner, his very soul
764𓀴seemed to vary with every fresh part that he assumed. The stage lost a
765𓀴fine actor, even as science lost an acute reasoner, when he became a
766𓀴specialist in crime.
767𓀴
768𓀴It was a quarter past six when we left Baker Street, and it still
769𓀴wanted ten minutes to the hour when we found ourselves in Serpentine
770𓀴Avenue. It was already dusk, and the lamps were just being lighted as
771𓀴we paced up and down in front of Briony Lodge, waiting for the coming
772𓀴of its occupant. The house was just such as I had pictured it from
773𓀴Sherlock Holmes’ succinct description, but the locality appeared to be
774𓀴less private than I expected. On the contrary, for a small street in a
775𓀴quiet neighbourhood, it was remarkably animated. There was a group of
776𓀴shabbily dressed men smoking and laughing in a corner, a
777𓀴scissors-grinder with his wheel, two guardsmen who were flirting with a
778𓀴nurse-girl, and several well-dressed young men who were lounging up and
779𓀴down with cigars in their mouths.
780𓀴
781𓀴“You see,” remarked Holmes, as we paced to and fro in front of the
782𓀴house, “this marriage rather simplifies matters. The photograph becomes
783𓀴a double-edged weapon now. The chances are that she would be as averse
784𓀴to its being seen by Mr. Godfrey Norton, as our client is to its coming
785𓀴to the eyes of his princess. Now the question is, Where are we to find
786𓀴the photograph?”
787𓀴
788𓀴“Where, indeed?”
789𓀴
790𓀴“It is most unlikely that she carries it about with her. It is cabinet
791𓀴size. Too large for easy concealment about a woman’s dress. She knows
792𓀴that the King is capable of having her waylaid and searched. Two
793𓀴attempts of the sort have already been made. We may take it, then, that
794𓀴she does not carry it about with her.”
795𓀴
796𓀴“Where, then?”
797𓀴
798𓀴“Her banker or her lawyer. There is that double possibility. But I am
799𓀴inclined to think neither. Women are naturally secretive, and they like
800𓀴to do their own secreting. Why should she hand it over to anyone else?
801𓀴She could trust her own guardianship, but she could not tell what
802𓀴indirect or political influence might be brought to bear upon a
803𓀴business man. Besides, remember that she had resolved to use it within
804𓀴a few days. It must be where she can lay her hands upon it. It must be
805𓀴in her own house.”
806𓀴
807𓀴“But it has twice been burgled.”
808𓀴
809𓀴“Pshaw! They did not know how to look.”
810𓀴
811𓀴“But how will you look?”
812𓀴
813𓀴“I will not look.”
814𓀴
815𓀴“What then?”
816𓀴
817𓀴“I will get her to show me.”
818𓀴
819𓀴“But she will refuse.”
820𓀴
821𓀴“She will not be able to. But I hear the rumble of wheels. It is her
822𓀴carriage. Now carry out my orders to the letter.”
823𓀴
824𓀴As he spoke the gleam of the sidelights of a carriage came round the
825𓀴curve of the avenue. It was a smart little landau which rattled up to
826𓀴the door of Briony Lodge. As it pulled up, one of the loafing men at
827𓀴the corner dashed forward to open the door in the hope of earning a
828𓀴copper, but was elbowed away by another loafer, who had rushed up with
829𓀴the same intention. A fierce quarrel broke out, which was increased by
830𓀴the two guardsmen, who took sides with one of the loungers, and by the
831𓀴scissors-grinder, who was equally hot upon the other side. A blow was
832𓀴struck, and in an instant the lady, who had stepped from her carriage,
833𓀴was the centre of a little knot of flushed and struggling men, who
834𓀴struck savagely at each other with their fists and sticks. Holmes
835𓀴dashed into the crowd to protect the lady; but, just as he reached her,
836𓀴he gave a cry and dropped to the ground, with the blood running freely
837𓀴down his face. At his fall the guardsmen took to their heels in one
838𓀴direction and the loungers in the other, while a number of better
839𓀴dressed people, who had watched the scuffle without taking part in it,
840𓀴crowded in to help the lady and to attend to the injured man. Irene
841𓀴Adler, as I will still call her, had hurried up the steps; but she
842𓀴stood at the top with her superb figure outlined against the lights of
843𓀴the hall, looking back into the street.
844𓀴
845𓀴“Is the poor gentleman much hurt?” she asked.
846𓀴
847𓀴“He is dead,” cried several voices.
848𓀴
849𓀴“No, no, there’s life in him!” shouted another. “But he’ll be gone
850𓀴before you can get him to hospital.”
851𓀴
852𓀴“He’s a brave fellow,” said a woman. “They would have had the lady’s
853𓀴purse and watch if it hadn’t been for him. They were a gang, and a
854𓀴rough one, too. Ah, he’s breathing now.”
855𓀴
856𓀴“He can’t lie in the street. May we bring him in, marm?”
857𓀴
858𓀴“Surely. Bring him into the sitting-room. There is a comfortable sofa.
859𓀴This way, please!”
860𓀴
861𓀴Slowly and solemnly he was borne into Briony Lodge and laid out in the
862𓀴principal room, while I still observed the proceedings from my post by
863𓀴the window. The lamps had been lit, but the blinds had not been drawn,
864𓀴so that I could see Holmes as he lay upon the couch. I do not know
865𓀴whether he was seized with compunction at that moment for the part he
866𓀴was playing, but I know that I never felt more heartily ashamed of
867𓀴myself in my life than when I saw the beautiful creature against whom I
868𓀴was conspiring, or the grace and kindliness with which she waited upon
869𓀴the injured man. And yet it would be the blackest treachery to Holmes
870𓀴to draw back now from the part which he had intrusted to me. I hardened
871𓀴my heart, and took the smoke-rocket from under my ulster. After all, I
872𓀴thought, we are not injuring her. We are but preventing her from
873𓀴injuring another.
874𓀴
875𓀴Holmes had sat up upon the couch, and I saw him motion like a man who
876𓀴is in need of air. A maid rushed across and threw open the window. At
877𓀴the same instant I saw him raise his hand and at the signal I tossed my
878𓀴rocket into the room with a cry of “Fire!” The word was no sooner out
879𓀴of my mouth than the whole crowd of spectators, well dressed and
880𓀴ill—gentlemen, ostlers, and servant maids—joined in a general shriek of
881𓀴“Fire!” Thick clouds of smoke curled through the room and out at the
882𓀴open window. I caught a glimpse of rushing figures, and a moment later
883𓀴the voice of Holmes from within assuring them that it was a false
884𓀴alarm. Slipping through the shouting crowd I made my way to the corner
885𓀴of the street, and in ten minutes was rejoiced to find my friend’s arm
886𓀴in mine, and to get away from the scene of uproar. He walked swiftly
887𓀴and in silence for some few minutes until we had turned down one of the
888𓀴quiet streets which lead towards the Edgeware Road.
889𓀴
890𓀴“You did it very nicely, Doctor,” he remarked. “Nothing could have been
891𓀴better. It is all right.”
892𓀴
893𓀴“You have the photograph?”
894𓀴
895𓀴“I know where it is.”
896𓀴
897𓀴“And how did you find out?”
898𓀴
899𓀴“She showed me, as I told you she would.”
900𓀴
901𓀴“I am still in the dark.”
902𓀴
903𓀴“I do not wish to make a mystery,” said he, laughing. “The matter was
904𓀴perfectly simple. You, of course, saw that everyone in the street was
905𓀴an accomplice. They were all engaged for the evening.”
906𓀴
907𓀴“I guessed as much.”
908𓀴
909𓀴“Then, when the row broke out, I had a little moist red paint in the
910𓀴palm of my hand. I rushed forward, fell down, clapped my hand to my
911𓀴face, and became a piteous spectacle. It is an old trick.”
912𓀴
913𓀴“That also I could fathom.”
914𓀴
915𓀴“Then they carried me in. She was bound to have me in. What else could
916𓀴she do? And into her sitting-room, which was the very room which I
917𓀴suspected. It lay between that and her bedroom, and I was determined to
918𓀴see which. They laid me on a couch, I motioned for air, they were
919𓀴compelled to open the window, and you had your chance.”
920𓀴
921𓀴“How did that help you?”
922𓀴
923𓀴“It was all-important. When a woman thinks that her house is on fire,
924𓀴her instinct is at once to rush to the thing which she values most. It
925𓀴is a perfectly overpowering impulse, and I have more than once taken
926𓀴advantage of it. In the case of the Darlington Substitution Scandal it
927𓀴was of use to me, and also in the Arnsworth Castle business. A married
928𓀴woman grabs at her baby; an unmarried one reaches for her jewel-box.
929𓀴Now it was clear to me that our lady of to-day had nothing in the house
930𓀴more precious to her than what we are in quest of. She would rush to
931𓀴secure it. The alarm of fire was admirably done. The smoke and shouting
932𓀴were enough to shake nerves of steel. She responded beautifully. The
933𓀴photograph is in a recess behind a sliding panel just above the right
934𓀴bell-pull. She was there in an instant, and I caught a glimpse of it as
935𓀴she half drew it out. When I cried out that it was a false alarm, she
936𓀴replaced it, glanced at the rocket, rushed from the room, and I have
937𓀴not seen her since. I rose, and, making my excuses, escaped from the
938𓀴house. I hesitated whether to attempt to secure the photograph at once;
939𓀴but the coachman had come in, and as he was watching me narrowly, it
940𓀴seemed safer to wait. A little over-precipitance may ruin all.”
941𓀴
942𓀴“And now?” I asked.
943𓀴
944𓀴“Our quest is practically finished. I shall call with the King
945𓀴to-morrow, and with you, if you care to come with us. We will be shown
946𓀴into the sitting-room to wait for the lady, but it is probable that
947𓀴when she comes she may find neither us nor the photograph. It might be
948𓀴a satisfaction to his Majesty to regain it with his own hands.”
949𓀴
950𓀴“And when will you call?”
951𓀴
952𓀴“At eight in the morning. She will not be up, so that we shall have a
953𓀴clear field. Besides, we must be prompt, for this marriage may mean a
954𓀴complete change in her life and habits. I must wire to the King without
955𓀴delay.”
956𓀴
957𓀴We had reached Baker Street and had stopped at the door. He was
958𓀴searching his pockets for the key when someone passing said:
959𓀴
960𓀴“Good-night, Mister Sherlock Holmes.”
961𓀴
962𓀴There were several people on the pavement at the time, but the greeting
963𓀴appeared to come from a slim youth in an ulster who had hurried by.
964𓀴
965𓀴“I’ve heard that voice before,” said Holmes, staring down the dimly lit
966𓀴street. “Now, I wonder who the deuce that could have been.”
967𓀴
968𓀴
969𓀴III.
970𓀴
971𓀴I slept at Baker Street that night, and we were engaged upon our toast
972𓀴and coffee in the morning when the King of Bohemia rushed into the
973𓀴room.
974𓀴
975𓀴“You have really got it!” he cried, grasping Sherlock Holmes by either
976𓀴shoulder and looking eagerly into his face.
977𓀴
978𓀴“Not yet.”
979𓀴
980𓀴“But you have hopes?”
981𓀴
982𓀴“I have hopes.”
983𓀴
984𓀴“Then, come. I am all impatience to be gone.”
985𓀴
986𓀴“We must have a cab.”
987𓀴
988𓀴“No, my brougham is waiting.”
989𓀴
990𓀴“Then that will simplify matters.” We descended and started off once
991𓀴more for Briony Lodge.
992𓀴
993𓀴“Irene Adler is married,” remarked Holmes.
994𓀴
995𓀴“Married! When?”
996𓀴
997𓀴“Yesterday.”
998𓀴
999𓀴“But to whom?”
1000𓀴
1001𓀴“To an English lawyer named Norton.”
1002𓀴
1003𓀴“But she could not love him.”
1004𓀴
1005𓀴“I am in hopes that she does.”
1006𓀴
1007𓀴“And why in hopes?”
1008𓀴
1009𓀴“Because it would spare your Majesty all fear of future annoyance. If
1010𓀴the lady loves her husband, she does not love your Majesty. If she does
1011𓀴not love your Majesty, there is no reason why she should interfere with
1012𓀴your Majesty’s plan.”
1013𓀴
1014𓀴“It is true. And yet—! Well! I wish she had been of my own station!
1015𓀴What a queen she would have made!” He relapsed into a moody silence,
1016𓀴which was not broken until we drew up in Serpentine Avenue.
1017𓀴
1018𓀴The door of Briony Lodge was open, and an elderly woman stood upon the
1019𓀴steps. She watched us with a sardonic eye as we stepped from the
1020𓀴brougham.
1021𓀴
1022𓀴“Mr. Sherlock Holmes, I believe?” said she.
1023𓀴
1024𓀴“I am Mr. Holmes,” answered my companion, looking at her with a
1025𓀴questioning and rather startled gaze.
1026𓀴
1027𓀴“Indeed! My mistress told me that you were likely to call. She left
1028𓀴this morning with her husband by the 5:15 train from Charing Cross for
1029𓀴the Continent.”
1030𓀴
1031𓀴“What!” Sherlock Holmes staggered back, white with chagrin and
1032𓀴surprise. “Do you mean that she has left England?”
1033𓀴
1034𓀴“Never to return.”
1035𓀴
1036𓀴“And the papers?” asked the King hoarsely. “All is lost.”
1037𓀴
1038𓀴“We shall see.” He pushed past the servant and rushed into the
1039𓀴drawing-room, followed by the King and myself. The furniture was
1040𓀴scattered about in every direction, with dismantled shelves and open
1041𓀴drawers, as if the lady had hurriedly ransacked them before her flight.
1042𓀴Holmes rushed at the bell-pull, tore back a small sliding shutter, and,
1043𓀴plunging in his hand, pulled out a photograph and a letter. The
1044𓀴photograph was of Irene Adler herself in evening dress, the letter was
1045𓀴superscribed to “Sherlock Holmes, Esq. To be left till called for.” My
1046𓀴friend tore it open, and we all three read it together. It was dated at
1047𓀴midnight of the preceding night and ran in this way:
1048𓀴
1049𓀴    “MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES,—You really did it very well. You took
1050𓀴    me in completely. Until after the alarm of fire, I had not a
1051𓀴    suspicion. But then, when I found how I had betrayed myself, I
1052𓀴    began to think. I had been warned against you months ago. I had
1053𓀴    been told that, if the King employed an agent, it would certainly
1054𓀴    be you. And your address had been given me. Yet, with all this, you
1055𓀴    made me reveal what you wanted to know. Even after I became
1056𓀴    suspicious, I found it hard to think evil of such a dear, kind old
1057𓀴    clergyman. But, you know, I have been trained as an actress myself.
1058𓀴    Male costume is nothing new to me. I often take advantage of the
1059𓀴    freedom which it gives. I sent John, the coachman, to watch you,
1060𓀴    ran upstairs, got into my walking clothes, as I call them, and came
1061𓀴    down just as you departed.
1062𓀴
1063𓀴    “Well, I followed you to your door, and so made sure that I was
1064𓀴    really an object of interest to the celebrated Mr. Sherlock Holmes.
1065𓀴    Then I, rather imprudently, wished you good-night, and started for
1066𓀴    the Temple to see my husband.
1067𓀴
1068𓀴    “We both thought the best resource was flight, when pursued by so
1069𓀴    formidable an antagonist; so you will find the nest empty when you
1070𓀴    call to-morrow. As to the photograph, your client may rest in
1071𓀴    peace. I love and am loved by a better man than he. The King may do
1072𓀴    what he will without hindrance from one whom he has cruelly
1073𓀴    wronged. I keep it only to safeguard myself, and to preserve a
1074𓀴    weapon which will always secure me from any steps which he might
1075𓀴    take in the future. I leave a photograph which he might care to
1076𓀴    possess; and I remain, dear Mr. Sherlock Holmes,
1077𓀴
1078𓀴
1079𓀴    “Very truly yours,
1080𓀴
1081𓀴    “IRENE NORTON, _née_ ADLER.”
1082𓀴
1083𓀴
1084𓀴“What a woman—oh, what a woman!” cried the King of Bohemia, when we had
1085𓀴all three read this epistle. “Did I not tell you how quick and resolute
1086𓀴she was? Would she not have made an admirable queen? Is it not a pity
1087𓀴that she was not on my level?”
1088𓀴
1089𓀴“From what I have seen of the lady, she seems, indeed, to be on a very
1090𓀴different level to your Majesty,” said Holmes coldly. “I am sorry that
1091𓀴I have not been able to bring your Majesty’s business to a more
1092𓀴successful conclusion.”
1093𓀴
1094𓀴“On the contrary, my dear sir,” cried the King; “nothing could be more
1095𓀴successful. I know that her word is inviolate. The photograph is now as
1096𓀴safe as if it were in the fire.”
1097𓀴
1098𓀴“I am glad to hear your Majesty say so.”
1099𓀴
1100𓀴“I am immensely indebted to you. Pray tell me in what way I can reward
1101𓀴you. This ring—” He slipped an emerald snake ring from his finger and
1102𓀴held it out upon the palm of his hand.
1103𓀴
1104𓀴“Your Majesty has something which I should value even more highly,”
1105𓀴said Holmes.
1106𓀴
1107𓀴“You have but to name it.”
1108𓀴
1109𓀴“This photograph!”
1110𓀴
1111𓀴The King stared at him in amazement.
1112𓀴
1113𓀴“Irene’s photograph!” he cried. “Certainly, if you wish it.”
1114𓀴
1115𓀴“I thank your Majesty. Then there is no more to be done in the matter.
1116𓀴I have the honour to wish you a very good morning.” He bowed, and,
1117𓀴turning away without observing the hand which the King had stretched
1118𓀴out to him, he set off in my company for his chambers.
1119𓀴
1120𓀴And that was how a great scandal threatened to affect the kingdom of
1121𓀴Bohemia, and how the best plans of Mr. Sherlock Holmes were beaten by a
1122𓀴woman’s wit. He used to make merry over the cleverness of women, but I
1123𓀴have not heard him do it of late. And when he speaks of Irene Adler, or
1124𓀴when he refers to her photograph, it is always under the honourable
1125𓀴title of _the_ woman.
1126𓀴
1127𓀴
1128𓀴
1129𓀴
1130𓀴II. THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE
1131𓀴
1132𓀴
1133𓀴 I had called upon my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, one day in the
1134𓀴 autumn of last year and found him in deep conversation with a very
1135𓀴 stout, florid-faced, elderly gentleman with fiery red hair. With an
1136𓀴 apology for my intrusion, I was about to withdraw when Holmes pulled
1137𓀴 me abruptly into the room and closed the door behind me.
1138𓀴
1139𓀴“You could not possibly have come at a better time, my dear Watson,” he
1140𓀴said cordially.
1141𓀴
1142𓀴“I was afraid that you were engaged.”
1143𓀴
1144𓀴“So I am. Very much so.”
1145𓀴
1146𓀴“Then I can wait in the next room.”
1147𓀴
1148𓀴“Not at all. This gentleman, Mr. Wilson, has been my partner and helper
1149𓀴in many of my most successful cases, and I have no doubt that he will
1150𓀴be of the utmost use to me in yours also.”
1151𓀴
1152𓀴The stout gentleman half rose from his chair and gave a bob of
1153𓀴greeting, with a quick little questioning glance from his small
1154𓀴fat-encircled eyes.
1155𓀴
1156𓀴“Try the settee,” said Holmes, relapsing into his armchair and putting
1157𓀴his fingertips together, as was his custom when in judicial moods. “I
1158𓀴know, my dear Watson, that you share my love of all that is bizarre and
1159𓀴outside the conventions and humdrum routine of everyday life. You have
1160𓀴shown your relish for it by the enthusiasm which has prompted you to
1161𓀴chronicle, and, if you will excuse my saying so, somewhat to embellish
1162𓀴so many of my own little adventures.”
1163𓀴
1164𓀴“Your cases have indeed been of the greatest interest to me,” I
1165𓀴observed.
1166𓀴
1167𓀴“You will remember that I remarked the other day, just before we went
1168𓀴into the very simple problem presented by Miss Mary Sutherland, that
1169𓀴for strange effects and extraordinary combinations we must go to life
1170𓀴itself, which is always far more daring than any effort of the
1171𓀴imagination.”
1172𓀴
1173𓀴“A proposition which I took the liberty of doubting.”
1174𓀴
1175𓀴“You did, Doctor, but none the less you must come round to my view, for
1176𓀴otherwise I shall keep on piling fact upon fact on you until your
1177𓀴reason breaks down under them and acknowledges me to be right. Now, Mr.
1178𓀴Jabez Wilson here has been good enough to call upon me this morning,
1179𓀴and to begin a narrative which promises to be one of the most singular
1180𓀴which I have listened to for some time. You have heard me remark that
1181𓀴the strangest and most unique things are very often connected not with
1182𓀴the larger but with the smaller crimes, and occasionally, indeed, where
1183𓀴there is room for doubt whether any positive crime has been committed.
1184𓀴As far as I have heard, it is impossible for me to say whether the
1185𓀴present case is an instance of crime or not, but the course of events
1186𓀴is certainly among the most singular that I have ever listened to.
1187𓀴Perhaps, Mr. Wilson, you would have the great kindness to recommence
1188𓀴your narrative. I ask you not merely because my friend Dr. Watson has
1189𓀴not heard the opening part but also because the peculiar nature of the
1190𓀴story makes me anxious to have every possible detail from your lips. As
1191𓀴a rule, when I have heard some slight indication of the course of
1192𓀴events, I am able to guide myself by the thousands of other similar
1193𓀴cases which occur to my memory. In the present instance I am forced to
1194𓀴admit that the facts are, to the best of my belief, unique.”
1195𓀴
1196𓀴The portly client puffed out his chest with an appearance of some
1197𓀴little pride and pulled a dirty and wrinkled newspaper from the inside
1198𓀴pocket of his greatcoat. As he glanced down the advertisement column,
1199𓀴with his head thrust forward and the paper flattened out upon his knee,
1200𓀴I took a good look at the man and endeavoured, after the fashion of my
1201𓀴companion, to read the indications which might be presented by his
1202𓀴dress or appearance.
1203𓀴
1204𓀴I did not gain very much, however, by my inspection. Our visitor bore
1205𓀴every mark of being an average commonplace British tradesman, obese,
1206𓀴pompous, and slow. He wore rather baggy grey shepherd’s check trousers,
1207𓀴a not over-clean black frock-coat, unbuttoned in the front, and a drab
1208𓀴waistcoat with a heavy brassy Albert chain, and a square pierced bit of
1209𓀴metal dangling down as an ornament. A frayed top-hat and a faded brown
1210𓀴overcoat with a wrinkled velvet collar lay upon a chair beside him.
1211𓀴Altogether, look as I would, there was nothing remarkable about the man
1212𓀴save his blazing red head, and the expression of extreme chagrin and
1213𓀴discontent upon his features.
1214𓀴
1215𓀴Sherlock Holmes’ quick eye took in my occupation, and he shook his head
1216𓀴with a smile as he noticed my questioning glances. “Beyond the obvious
1217𓀴facts that he has at some time done manual labour, that he takes snuff,
1218𓀴that he is a Freemason, that he has been in China, and that he has done
1219𓀴a considerable amount of writing lately, I can deduce nothing else.”
1220𓀴
1221𓀴Mr. Jabez Wilson started up in his chair, with his forefinger upon the
1222𓀴paper, but his eyes upon my companion.
1223𓀴
1224𓀴“How, in the name of good-fortune, did you know all that, Mr. Holmes?”
1225𓀴he asked. “How did you know, for example, that I did manual labour.
1226𓀴It’s as true as gospel, for I began as a ship’s carpenter.”
1227𓀴
1228𓀴“Your hands, my dear sir. Your right hand is quite a size larger than
1229𓀴your left. You have worked with it, and the muscles are more
1230𓀴developed.”
1231𓀴
1232𓀴“Well, the snuff, then, and the Freemasonry?”
1233𓀴
1234𓀴“I won’t insult your intelligence by telling you how I read that,
1235𓀴especially as, rather against the strict rules of your order, you use
1236𓀴an arc-and-compass breastpin.”
1237𓀴
1238𓀴“Ah, of course, I forgot that. But the writing?”
1239𓀴
1240𓀴“What else can be indicated by that right cuff so very shiny for five
1241𓀴inches, and the left one with the smooth patch near the elbow where you
1242𓀴rest it upon the desk?”
1243𓀴
1244𓀴“Well, but China?”
1245𓀴
1246𓀴“The fish that you have tattooed immediately above your right wrist
1247𓀴could only have been done in China. I have made a small study of tattoo
1248𓀴marks and have even contributed to the literature of the subject. That
1249𓀴trick of staining the fishes’ scales of a delicate pink is quite
1250𓀴peculiar to China. When, in addition, I see a Chinese coin hanging from
1251𓀴your watch-chain, the matter becomes even more simple.”
1252𓀴
1253𓀴Mr. Jabez Wilson laughed heavily. “Well, I never!” said he. “I thought
1254𓀴at first that you had done something clever, but I see that there was
1255𓀴nothing in it after all.”
1256𓀴
1257𓀴“I begin to think, Watson,” said Holmes, “that I make a mistake in
1258𓀴explaining. ‘_Omne ignotum pro magnifico_,’ you know, and my poor
1259𓀴little reputation, such as it is, will suffer shipwreck if I am so
1260𓀴candid. Can you not find the advertisement, Mr. Wilson?”
1261𓀴
1262𓀴“Yes, I have got it now,” he answered with his thick red finger planted
1263𓀴halfway down the column. “Here it is. This is what began it all. You
1264𓀴just read it for yourself, sir.”
1265𓀴
1266𓀴I took the paper from him and read as follows:
1267𓀴
1268𓀴“TO THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE: On account of the bequest of the late
1269𓀴Ezekiah Hopkins, of Lebanon, Pennsylvania, U.S.A., there is now another
1270𓀴vacancy open which entitles a member of the League to a salary of £ 4 a
1271𓀴week for purely nominal services. All red-headed men who are sound in
1272𓀴body and mind and above the age of twenty-one years, are eligible.
1273𓀴Apply in person on Monday, at eleven o’clock, to Duncan Ross, at the
1274𓀴offices of the League, 7 Pope’s Court, Fleet Street.”
1275𓀴
1276𓀴
1277𓀴“What on earth does this mean?” I ejaculated after I had twice read
1278𓀴over the extraordinary announcement.
1279𓀴
1280𓀴Holmes chuckled and wriggled in his chair, as was his habit when in
1281𓀴high spirits. “It is a little off the beaten track, isn’t it?” said he.
1282𓀴“And now, Mr. Wilson, off you go at scratch and tell us all about
1283𓀴yourself, your household, and the effect which this advertisement had
1284𓀴upon your fortunes. You will first make a note, Doctor, of the paper
1285𓀴and the date.”
1286𓀴
1287𓀴“It is _The Morning Chronicle_ of April 27, 1890. Just two months ago.”
1288𓀴
1289𓀴“Very good. Now, Mr. Wilson?”
1290𓀴
1291𓀴“Well, it is just as I have been telling you, Mr. Sherlock Holmes,”
1292𓀴said Jabez Wilson, mopping his forehead; “I have a small pawnbroker’s
1293𓀴business at Coburg Square, near the City. It’s not a very large affair,
1294𓀴and of late years it has not done more than just give me a living. I
1295𓀴used to be able to keep two assistants, but now I only keep one; and I
1296𓀴would have a job to pay him but that he is willing to come for half
1297𓀴wages so as to learn the business.”
1298𓀴
1299𓀴“What is the name of this obliging youth?” asked Sherlock Holmes.
1300𓀴
1301𓀴“His name is Vincent Spaulding, and he’s not such a youth, either. It’s
1302𓀴hard to say his age. I should not wish a smarter assistant, Mr. Holmes;
1303𓀴and I know very well that he could better himself and earn twice what I
1304𓀴am able to give him. But, after all, if he is satisfied, why should I
1305𓀴put ideas in his head?”
1306𓀴
1307𓀴“Why, indeed? You seem most fortunate in having an _employé_ who comes
1308𓀴under the full market price. It is not a common experience among
1309𓀴employers in this age. I don’t know that your assistant is not as
1310𓀴remarkable as your advertisement.”
1311𓀴
1312𓀴“Oh, he has his faults, too,” said Mr. Wilson. “Never was such a fellow
1313𓀴for photography. Snapping away with a camera when he ought to be
1314𓀴improving his mind, and then diving down into the cellar like a rabbit
1315𓀴into its hole to develop his pictures. That is his main fault, but on
1316𓀴the whole he’s a good worker. There’s no vice in him.”
1317𓀴
1318𓀴“He is still with you, I presume?”
1319𓀴
1320𓀴“Yes, sir. He and a girl of fourteen, who does a bit of simple cooking
1321𓀴and keeps the place clean—that’s all I have in the house, for I am a
1322𓀴widower and never had any family. We live very quietly, sir, the three
1323𓀴of us; and we keep a roof over our heads and pay our debts, if we do
1324𓀴nothing more.
1325𓀴
1326𓀴“The first thing that put us out was that advertisement. Spaulding, he
1327𓀴came down into the office just this day eight weeks, with this very
1328𓀴paper in his hand, and he says:
1329𓀴
1330𓀴“‘I wish to the Lord, Mr. Wilson, that I was a red-headed man.’
1331𓀴
1332𓀴“‘Why that?’ I asks.
1333𓀴
1334𓀴“‘Why,’ says he, ‘here’s another vacancy on the League of the
1335𓀴Red-headed Men. It’s worth quite a little fortune to any man who gets
1336𓀴it, and I understand that there are more vacancies than there are men,
1337𓀴so that the trustees are at their wits’ end what to do with the money.
1338𓀴If my hair would only change colour, here’s a nice little crib all
1339𓀴ready for me to step into.’
1340𓀴
1341𓀴“‘Why, what is it, then?’ I asked. You see, Mr. Holmes, I am a very
1342𓀴stay-at-home man, and as my business came to me instead of my having to
1343𓀴go to it, I was often weeks on end without putting my foot over the
1344𓀴door-mat. In that way I didn’t know much of what was going on outside,
1345𓀴and I was always glad of a bit of news.
1346𓀴
1347𓀴“‘Have you never heard of the League of the Red-headed Men?’ he asked
1348𓀴with his eyes open.
1349𓀴
1350𓀴“‘Never.’
1351𓀴
1352𓀴“‘Why, I wonder at that, for you are eligible yourself for one of the
1353𓀴vacancies.’
1354𓀴
1355𓀴“‘And what are they worth?’ I asked.
1356𓀴
1357𓀴“‘Oh, merely a couple of hundred a year, but the work is slight, and it
1358𓀴need not interfere very much with one’s other occupations.’
1359𓀴
1360𓀴“Well, you can easily think that that made me prick up my ears, for the
1361𓀴business has not been over good for some years, and an extra couple of
1362𓀴hundred would have been very handy.
1363𓀴
1364𓀴“‘Tell me all about it,’ said I.
1365𓀴
1366𓀴“‘Well,’ said he, showing me the advertisement, ‘you can see for
1367𓀴yourself that the League has a vacancy, and there is the address where
1368𓀴you should apply for particulars. As far as I can make out, the League
1369𓀴was founded by an American millionaire, Ezekiah Hopkins, who was very
1370𓀴peculiar in his ways. He was himself red-headed, and he had a great
1371𓀴sympathy for all red-headed men; so, when he died, it was found that he
1372𓀴had left his enormous fortune in the hands of trustees, with
1373𓀴instructions to apply the interest to the providing of easy berths to
1374𓀴men whose hair is of that colour. From all I hear it is splendid pay
1375𓀴and very little to do.’
1376𓀴
1377𓀴“‘But,’ said I, ‘there would be millions of red-headed men who would
1378𓀴apply.’
1379𓀴
1380𓀴“‘Not so many as you might think,’ he answered. ‘You see it is really
1381𓀴confined to Londoners, and to grown men. This American had started from
1382𓀴London when he was young, and he wanted to do the old town a good turn.
1383𓀴Then, again, I have heard it is no use your applying if your hair is
1384𓀴light red, or dark red, or anything but real bright, blazing, fiery
1385𓀴red. Now, if you cared to apply, Mr. Wilson, you would just walk in;
1386𓀴but perhaps it would hardly be worth your while to put yourself out of
1387𓀴the way for the sake of a few hundred pounds.’
1388𓀴
1389𓀴“Now, it is a fact, gentlemen, as you may see for yourselves, that my
1390𓀴hair is of a very full and rich tint, so that it seemed to me that if
1391𓀴there was to be any competition in the matter I stood as good a chance
1392𓀴as any man that I had ever met. Vincent Spaulding seemed to know so
1393𓀴much about it that I thought he might prove useful, so I just ordered
1394𓀴him to put up the shutters for the day and to come right away with me.
1395𓀴He was very willing to have a holiday, so we shut the business up and
1396𓀴started off for the address that was given us in the advertisement.
1397𓀴
1398𓀴“I never hope to see such a sight as that again, Mr. Holmes. From
1399𓀴north, south, east, and west every man who had a shade of red in his
1400𓀴hair had tramped into the city to answer the advertisement. Fleet
1401𓀴Street was choked with red-headed folk, and Pope’s Court looked like a
1402𓀴coster’s orange barrow. I should not have thought there were so many in
1403𓀴the whole country as were brought together by that single
1404𓀴advertisement. Every shade of colour they were—straw, lemon, orange,
1405𓀴brick, Irish-setter, liver, clay; but, as Spaulding said, there were
1406𓀴not many who had the real vivid flame-coloured tint. When I saw how
1407𓀴many were waiting, I would have given it up in despair; but Spaulding
1408𓀴would not hear of it. How he did it I could not imagine, but he pushed
1409𓀴and pulled and butted until he got me through the crowd, and right up
1410𓀴to the steps which led to the office. There was a double stream upon
1411𓀴the stair, some going up in hope, and some coming back dejected; but we
1412𓀴wedged in as well as we could and soon found ourselves in the office.”
1413𓀴
1414𓀴“Your experience has been a most entertaining one,” remarked Holmes as
1415𓀴his client paused and refreshed his memory with a huge pinch of snuff.
1416𓀴“Pray continue your very interesting statement.”
1417𓀴
1418𓀴“There was nothing in the office but a couple of wooden chairs and a
1419𓀴deal table, behind which sat a small man with a head that was even
1420𓀴redder than mine. He said a few words to each candidate as he came up,
1421𓀴and then he always managed to find some fault in them which would
1422𓀴disqualify them. Getting a vacancy did not seem to be such a very easy
1423𓀴matter, after all. However, when our turn came the little man was much
1424𓀴more favourable to me than to any of the others, and he closed the door
1425𓀴as we entered, so that he might have a private word with us.
1426𓀴
1427𓀴“‘This is Mr. Jabez Wilson,’ said my assistant, ‘and he is willing to
1428𓀴fill a vacancy in the League.’
1429𓀴
1430𓀴“‘And he is admirably suited for it,’ the other answered. ‘He has every
1431𓀴requirement. I cannot recall when I have seen anything so fine.’ He
1432𓀴took a step backward, cocked his head on one side, and gazed at my hair
1433𓀴until I felt quite bashful. Then suddenly he plunged forward, wrung my
1434𓀴hand, and congratulated me warmly on my success.
1435𓀴
1436𓀴“‘It would be injustice to hesitate,’ said he. ‘You will, however, I am
1437𓀴sure, excuse me for taking an obvious precaution.’ With that he seized
1438𓀴my hair in both his hands, and tugged until I yelled with the pain.
1439𓀴‘There is water in your eyes,’ said he as he released me. ‘I perceive
1440𓀴that all is as it should be. But we have to be careful, for we have
1441𓀴twice been deceived by wigs and once by paint. I could tell you tales
1442𓀴of cobbler’s wax which would disgust you with human nature.’ He stepped
1443𓀴over to the window and shouted through it at the top of his voice that
1444𓀴the vacancy was filled. A groan of disappointment came up from below,
1445𓀴and the folk all trooped away in different directions until there was
1446𓀴not a red-head to be seen except my own and that of the manager.
1447𓀴
1448𓀴“‘My name,’ said he, ‘is Mr. Duncan Ross, and I am myself one of the
1449𓀴pensioners upon the fund left by our noble benefactor. Are you a
1450𓀴married man, Mr. Wilson? Have you a family?’
1451𓀴
1452𓀴“I answered that I had not.
1453𓀴
1454𓀴“His face fell immediately.
1455𓀴
1456𓀴“‘Dear me!’ he said gravely, ‘that is very serious indeed! I am sorry
1457𓀴to hear you say that. The fund was, of course, for the propagation and
1458𓀴spread of the red-heads as well as for their maintenance. It is
1459𓀴exceedingly unfortunate that you should be a bachelor.’
1460𓀴
1461𓀴“My face lengthened at this, Mr. Holmes, for I thought that I was not
1462𓀴to have the vacancy after all; but after thinking it over for a few
1463𓀴minutes he said that it would be all right.
1464𓀴
1465𓀴“‘In the case of another,’ said he, ‘the objection might be fatal, but
1466𓀴we must stretch a point in favour of a man with such a head of hair as
1467𓀴yours. When shall you be able to enter upon your new duties?’
1468𓀴
1469𓀴“‘Well, it is a little awkward, for I have a business already,’ said I.
1470𓀴
1471𓀴“‘Oh, never mind about that, Mr. Wilson!’ said Vincent Spaulding. ‘I
1472𓀴should be able to look after that for you.’
1473𓀴
1474𓀴“‘What would be the hours?’ I asked.
1475𓀴
1476𓀴“‘Ten to two.’
1477𓀴
1478𓀴“Now a pawnbroker’s business is mostly done of an evening, Mr. Holmes,
1479𓀴especially Thursday and Friday evening, which is just before pay-day;
1480𓀴so it would suit me very well to earn a little in the mornings.
1481𓀴Besides, I knew that my assistant was a good man, and that he would see
1482𓀴to anything that turned up.
1483𓀴
1484𓀴“‘That would suit me very well,’ said I. ‘And the pay?’
1485𓀴
1486𓀴“‘Is £ 4 a week.’
1487𓀴
1488𓀴“‘And the work?’
1489𓀴
1490𓀴“‘Is purely nominal.’
1491𓀴
1492𓀴“‘What do you call purely nominal?’
1493𓀴
1494𓀴“‘Well, you have to be in the office, or at least in the building, the
1495𓀴whole time. If you leave, you forfeit your whole position forever. The
1496𓀴will is very clear upon that point. You don’t comply with the
1497𓀴conditions if you budge from the office during that time.’
1498𓀴
1499𓀴“‘It’s only four hours a day, and I should not think of leaving,’ said
1500𓀴I.
1501𓀴
1502𓀴“‘No excuse will avail,’ said Mr. Duncan Ross; ‘neither sickness nor
1503𓀴business nor anything else. There you must stay, or you lose your
1504𓀴billet.’
1505𓀴
1506𓀴“‘And the work?’
1507𓀴
1508𓀴“‘Is to copy out the _Encyclopædia Britannica_. There is the first
1509𓀴volume of it in that press. You must find your own ink, pens, and
1510𓀴blotting-paper, but we provide this table and chair. Will you be ready
1511𓀴to-morrow?’
1512𓀴
1513𓀴“‘Certainly,’ I answered.
1514𓀴
1515𓀴“‘Then, good-bye, Mr. Jabez Wilson, and let me congratulate you once
1516𓀴more on the important position which you have been fortunate enough to
1517𓀴gain.’ He bowed me out of the room and I went home with my assistant,
1518𓀴hardly knowing what to say or do, I was so pleased at my own good
1519𓀴fortune.
1520𓀴
1521𓀴“Well, I thought over the matter all day, and by evening I was in low
1522𓀴spirits again; for I had quite persuaded myself that the whole affair
1523𓀴must be some great hoax or fraud, though what its object might be I
1524𓀴could not imagine. It seemed altogether past belief that anyone could
1525𓀴make such a will, or that they would pay such a sum for doing anything
1526𓀴so simple as copying out the _Encyclopædia Britannica_. Vincent
1527𓀴Spaulding did what he could to cheer me up, but by bedtime I had
1528𓀴reasoned myself out of the whole thing. However, in the morning I
1529𓀴determined to have a look at it anyhow, so I bought a penny bottle of
1530𓀴ink, and with a quill-pen, and seven sheets of foolscap paper, I
1531𓀴started off for Pope’s Court.
1532𓀴
1533𓀴“Well, to my surprise and delight, everything was as right as possible.
1534𓀴The table was set out ready for me, and Mr. Duncan Ross was there to
1535𓀴see that I got fairly to work. He started me off upon the letter A, and
1536𓀴then he left me; but he would drop in from time to time to see that all
1537𓀴was right with me. At two o’clock he bade me good-day, complimented me
1538𓀴upon the amount that I had written, and locked the door of the office
1539𓀴after me.
1540𓀴
1541𓀴“This went on day after day, Mr. Holmes, and on Saturday the manager
1542𓀴came in and planked down four golden sovereigns for my week’s work. It
1543𓀴was the same next week, and the same the week after. Every morning I
1544𓀴was there at ten, and every afternoon I left at two. By degrees Mr.
1545𓀴Duncan Ross took to coming in only once of a morning, and then, after a
1546𓀴time, he did not come in at all. Still, of course, I never dared to
1547𓀴leave the room for an instant, for I was not sure when he might come,
1548𓀴and the billet was such a good one, and suited me so well, that I would
1549𓀴not risk the loss of it.
1550𓀴
1551𓀴“Eight weeks passed away like this, and I had written about Abbots and
1552𓀴Archery and Armour and Architecture and Attica, and hoped with
1553𓀴diligence that I might get on to the B’s before very long. It cost me
1554𓀴something in foolscap, and I had pretty nearly filled a shelf with my
1555𓀴writings. And then suddenly the whole business came to an end.”
1556𓀴
1557𓀴“To an end?”
1558𓀴
1559𓀴“Yes, sir. And no later than this morning. I went to my work as usual
1560𓀴at ten o’clock, but the door was shut and locked, with a little square
1561𓀴of cardboard hammered on to the middle of the panel with a tack. Here
1562𓀴it is, and you can read for yourself.”
1563𓀴
1564𓀴He held up a piece of white cardboard about the size of a sheet of
1565𓀴note-paper. It read in this fashion:
1566𓀴
1567𓀴“THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE IS DISSOLVED. October 9, 1890.”
1568𓀴
1569𓀴
1570𓀴Sherlock Holmes and I surveyed this curt announcement and the rueful
1571𓀴face behind it, until the comical side of the affair so completely
1572𓀴overtopped every other consideration that we both burst out into a roar

1574𓀴
1575𓀴“I cannot see that there is anything very funny,” cried our client,
1576𓀴flushing up to the roots of his flaming head. “If you can do nothing
1577𓀴better than laugh at me, I can go elsewhere.”
1578𓀴
1579𓀴“No, no,” cried Holmes, shoving him back into the chair from which he

1581𓀴most refreshingly unusual. But there is, if you will excuse my saying
1582𓀴so, something just a little funny about it. Pray what steps did you
1583𓀴take when you found the card upon the door?”
1584𓀴
1585𓀴“I was staggered, sir. I did not know what to do. Then I called at the
1586𓀴offices round, but none of them seemed to know anything about it.
1587𓀴Finally, I went to the landlord, who is an accountant living on the
1588𓀴ground floor, and I asked him if he could tell me what had become of
1589𓀴the Red-headed League. He said that he had never heard of any such
1590𓀴body. Then I asked him who Mr. Duncan Ross was. He answered that the
1591𓀴name was new to him.
1592𓀴
1593𓀴“‘Well,’ said I, ‘the gentleman at No. 4.’
1594𓀴
1595𓀴“‘What, the red-headed man?’
1596𓀴
1597𓀴“‘Yes.’
1598𓀴
1599𓀴“‘Oh,’ said he, ‘his name was William Morris. He was a solicitor and
1600𓀴was using my room as a temporary convenience until his new premises
1601𓀴were ready. He moved out yesterday.’
1602𓀴
1603𓀴“‘Where could I find him?’
1604𓀴
1605𓀴“‘Oh, at his new offices. He did tell me the address. Yes, 17 King
1606𓀴Edward Street, near St. Paul’s.’
1607𓀴
1608𓀴“I started off, Mr. Holmes, but when I got to that address it was a
1609𓀴manufactory of artificial knee-caps, and no one in it had ever heard of
1610𓀴either Mr. William Morris or Mr. Duncan Ross.”
1611𓀴
1612𓀴“And what did you do then?” asked Holmes.
1613𓀴
1614𓀴“I went home to Saxe-Coburg Square, and I took the advice of my
1615𓀴assistant. But he could not help me in any way. He could only say that
1616𓀴if I waited I should hear by post. But that was not quite good enough,
1617𓀴Mr. Holmes. I did not wish to lose such a place without a struggle, so,
1618𓀴as I had heard that you were good enough to give advice to poor folk

1620𓀴

1622𓀴remarkable one, and I shall be happy to look into it. From what you
1623𓀴have told me I think that it is possible that graver issues hang from

1625𓀴
1626𓀴“Grave enough!” said Mr. Jabez Wilson. “Why, I have lost four pound a
1627𓀴week.”
1628𓀴
1629𓀴“As far as you are personally concerned,” remarked Holmes, “I do not
1630𓀴see that you have any grievance against this extraordinary league. On
1631𓀴the contrary, you are, as I understand, richer by some £ 30, to say

1633𓀴which comes under the letter A. You have lost nothing by them.”
1634𓀴
1635𓀴“No, sir. But I want to find out about them, and who they are, and what
1636𓀴their object was in playing this prank—if it was a prank—upon me. It
1637𓀴was a pretty expensive joke for them, for it cost them two and thirty
1638𓀴pounds.”
1639𓀴
1640𓀴“We shall endeavour to clear up these points for you. And, first, one
1641𓀴or two questions, Mr. Wilson. This assistant of yours who first called
1642𓀴your attention to the advertisement—how long had he been with you?”
1643𓀴
1644𓀴“About a month then.”
1645𓀴
1646𓀴“How did he come?”
1647𓀴
1648𓀴“In answer to an advertisement.”
1649𓀴
1650𓀴“Was he the only applicant?”
1651𓀴
1652𓀴“No, I had a dozen.”
1653𓀴
1654𓀴“Why did you pick him?”
1655𓀴
1656𓀴“Because he was handy and would come cheap.”
1657𓀴
1658𓀴“At half wages, in fact.”
1659𓀴
1660𓀴“Yes.”
1661𓀴
1662𓀴“What is he like, this Vincent Spaulding?”
1663𓀴
1664𓀴“Small, stout-built, very quick in his ways, no hair on his face,
1665𓀴though he’s not short of thirty. Has a white splash of acid upon his

1667𓀴
1668𓀴Holmes sat up in his chair in considerable excitement. “I thought as
1669𓀴much,” said he. “Have you ever observed that his ears are pierced for
1670𓀴earrings?”
1671𓀴
1672𓀴“Yes, sir. He told me that a gipsy had done it for him when he was a
1673𓀴lad.”
1674𓀴

1677𓀴
1678𓀴“Oh, yes, sir; I have only just left him.”
1679𓀴
1680𓀴“And has your business been attended to in your absence?”
1681𓀴
1682𓀴“Nothing to complain of, sir. There’s never very much to do of a
1683𓀴morning.”
1684𓀴
1685𓀴“That will do, Mr. Wilson. I shall be happy to give you an opinion upon
1686𓀴the subject in the course of a day or two. To-day is Saturday, and I
1687𓀴hope that by Monday we may come to a conclusion.”
1688𓀴

1691𓀴

1693𓀴business.”
1694𓀴
1695𓀴“As a rule,” said Holmes, “the more bizarre a thing is the less
1696𓀴mysterious it proves to be. It is your commonplace, featureless crimes
1697𓀴which are really puzzling, just as a commonplace face is the most
1698𓀴difficult to identify. But I must be prompt over this matter.”
1699𓀴
1700𓀴“What are you going to do, then?” I asked.
1701𓀴
1702𓀴“To smoke,” he answered. “It is quite a three pipe problem, and I beg
1703𓀴that you won’t speak to me for fifty minutes.” He curled himself up in
1704𓀴his chair, with his thin knees drawn up to his hawk-like nose, and

1713𓀴hours?”
1714𓀴
1715𓀴“I have nothing to do to-day. My practice is never very absorbing.”
1716𓀴
1717𓀴“Then put on your hat and come. I am going through the City first, and
1718𓀴we can have some lunch on the way. I observe that there is a good deal
1719𓀴of German music on the programme, which is rather more to my taste than

1722𓀴
1723𓀴We travelled by the Underground as far as Aldersgate; and a short walk

1725𓀴had listened to in the morning. It was a poky, little, shabby-genteel
1726𓀴place, where four lines of dingy two-storied brick houses looked out
