1731𓀴place where our red-headed client carried on his business. Sherlock
1732𓀴Holmes stopped in front of it with his head on one side and looked it
1733𓀴all over, with his eyes shining brightly between puckered lids. Then he
1734𓀴walked slowly up the street, and then down again to the corner, still
1735𓀴looking keenly at the houses. Finally he returned to the pawnbroker’s,
1736𓀴and, having thumped vigorously upon the pavement with his stick two or
1737𓀴three times, he went up to the door and knocked. It was instantly
1738𓀴opened by a bright-looking, clean-shaven young fellow, who asked him to
1739𓀴step in.
1740𓀴
1741𓀴“Thank you,” said Holmes, “I only wished to ask you how you would go
1742𓀴from here to the Strand.”
1743𓀴
1744𓀴“Third right, fourth left,” answered the assistant promptly, closing
1745𓀴the door.
1746𓀴
1747𓀴“Smart fellow, that,” observed Holmes as we walked away. “He is, in my
1748𓀴judgment, the fourth smartest man in London, and for daring I am not
1749𓀴sure that he has not a claim to be third. I have known something of him
1750𓀴before.”
1751𓀴
1752𓀴“Evidently,” said I, “Mr. Wilson’s assistant counts for a good deal in
1753𓀴this mystery of the Red-headed League. I am sure that you inquired your
1754𓀴way merely in order that you might see him.”
1755𓀴
1756𓀴“Not him.”
1757𓀴
1758𓀴“What then?”
1759𓀴
1760𓀴“The knees of his trousers.”
1761𓀴
1762𓀴“And what did you see?”
1763𓀴
1764𓀴“What I expected to see.”
1765𓀴
1766𓀴“Why did you beat the pavement?”
1767𓀴
1768𓀴“My dear doctor, this is a time for observation, not for talk. We are
1769𓀴spies in an enemy’s country. We know something of Saxe-Coburg Square.
1770𓀴Let us now explore the parts which lie behind it.”
1771𓀴
1772𓀴The road in which we found ourselves as we turned round the corner from
1773𓀴the retired Saxe-Coburg Square presented as great a contrast to it as
1774𓀴the front of a picture does to the back. It was one of the main
1775𓀴arteries which conveyed the traffic of the City to the north and west.
1776𓀴The roadway was blocked with the immense stream of commerce flowing in
1777𓀴a double tide inward and outward, while the footpaths were black with
1778𓀴the hurrying swarm of pedestrians. It was difficult to realise as we
1779𓀴looked at the line of fine shops and stately business premises that
1780𓀴they really abutted on the other side upon the faded and stagnant
1781𓀴square which we had just quitted.
1782𓀴
1783𓀴“Let me see,” said Holmes, standing at the corner and glancing along
1784𓀴the line, “I should like just to remember the order of the houses here.
1785𓀴It is a hobby of mine to have an exact knowledge of London. There is
1786𓀴Mortimer’s, the tobacconist, the little newspaper shop, the Coburg
1787𓀴branch of the City and Suburban Bank, the Vegetarian Restaurant, and
1788𓀴McFarlane’s carriage-building depot. That carries us right on to the
1789𓀴other block. And now, Doctor, we’ve done our work, so it’s time we had
1790𓀴some play. A sandwich and a cup of coffee, and then off to violin-land,
1791𓀴where all is sweetness and delicacy and harmony, and there are no
1792𓀴red-headed clients to vex us with their conundrums.”
1793𓀴
1794𓀴My friend was an enthusiastic musician, being himself not only a very
1795𓀴capable performer but a composer of no ordinary merit. All the
1796𓀴afternoon he sat in the stalls wrapped in the most perfect happiness,
1797𓀴gently waving his long, thin fingers in time to the music, while his
1798𓀴gently smiling face and his languid, dreamy eyes were as unlike those
1799𓀴of Holmes the sleuth-hound, Holmes the relentless, keen-witted,
1800𓀴ready-handed criminal agent, as it was possible to conceive. In his
1801𓀴singular character the dual nature alternately asserted itself, and his
1802𓀴extreme exactness and astuteness represented, as I have often thought,
1803𓀴the reaction against the poetic and contemplative mood which
1804𓀴occasionally predominated in him. The swing of his nature took him from
1805𓀴extreme languor to devouring energy; and, as I knew well, he was never
1806𓀴so truly formidable as when, for days on end, he had been lounging in
1807𓀴his armchair amid his improvisations and his black-letter editions.
1808𓀴Then it was that the lust of the chase would suddenly come upon him,
1809𓀴and that his brilliant reasoning power would rise to the level of
1810𓀴intuition, until those who were unacquainted with his methods would
1811𓀴look askance at him as on a man whose knowledge was not that of other
1812𓀴mortals. When I saw him that afternoon so enwrapped in the music at St.
1813𓀴James’s Hall I felt that an evil time might be coming upon those whom
1814𓀴he had set himself to hunt down.
1815𓀴
1816𓀴“You want to go home, no doubt, Doctor,” he remarked as we emerged.
1817𓀴
1818𓀴“Yes, it would be as well.”
1819𓀴
1820𓀴“And I have some business to do which will take some hours. This
1821𓀴business at Coburg Square is serious.”
1822𓀴
1823𓀴“Why serious?”
1824𓀴
1825𓀴“A considerable crime is in contemplation. I have every reason to
1826𓀴believe that we shall be in time to stop it. But to-day being Saturday
1827𓀴rather complicates matters. I shall want your help to-night.”
1828𓀴
1829𓀴“At what time?”
1830𓀴
1831𓀴“Ten will be early enough.”
1832𓀴
1833𓀴“I shall be at Baker Street at ten.”
1834𓀴
1835𓀴“Very well. And, I say, Doctor, there may be some little danger, so
1836𓀴kindly put your army revolver in your pocket.” He waved his hand,
1837𓀴turned on his heel, and disappeared in an instant among the crowd.
1838𓀴
1839𓀴I trust that I am not more dense than my neighbours, but I was always
1840𓀴oppressed with a sense of my own stupidity in my dealings with Sherlock
1841𓀴Holmes. Here I had heard what he had heard, I had seen what he had
1842𓀴seen, and yet from his words it was evident that he saw clearly not
1843𓀴only what had happened but what was about to happen, while to me the
1844𓀴whole business was still confused and grotesque. As I drove home to my
1845𓀴house in Kensington I thought over it all, from the extraordinary story
1846𓀴of the red-headed copier of the _Encyclopædia_ down to the visit to
1847𓀴Saxe-Coburg Square, and the ominous words with which he had parted from
1848𓀴me. What was this nocturnal expedition, and why should I go armed?
1849𓀴Where were we going, and what were we to do? I had the hint from Holmes
1850𓀴that this smooth-faced pawnbroker’s assistant was a formidable man—a
1851𓀴man who might play a deep game. I tried to puzzle it out, but gave it
1852𓀴up in despair and set the matter aside until night should bring an
1853𓀴explanation.
1854𓀴
1855𓀴It was a quarter-past nine when I started from home and made my way
1856𓀴across the Park, and so through Oxford Street to Baker Street. Two
1857𓀴hansoms were standing at the door, and as I entered the passage I heard
1858𓀴the sound of voices from above. On entering his room, I found Holmes in
1859𓀴animated conversation with two men, one of whom I recognised as Peter
1860𓀴Jones, the official police agent, while the other was a long, thin,
1861𓀴sad-faced man, with a very shiny hat and oppressively respectable
1862𓀴frock-coat.
1863𓀴
1864𓀴“Ha! Our party is complete,” said Holmes, buttoning up his pea-jacket
1865𓀴and taking his heavy hunting crop from the rack. “Watson, I think you
1866𓀴know Mr. Jones, of Scotland Yard? Let me introduce you to Mr.
1867𓀴Merryweather, who is to be our companion in to-night’s adventure.”
1868𓀴
1869𓀴“We’re hunting in couples again, Doctor, you see,” said Jones in his
1870𓀴consequential way. “Our friend here is a wonderful man for starting a
1871𓀴chase. All he wants is an old dog to help him to do the running down.”
1872𓀴
1873𓀴“I hope a wild goose may not prove to be the end of our chase,”
1874𓀴observed Mr. Merryweather gloomily.
1875𓀴
1876𓀴“You may place considerable confidence in Mr. Holmes, sir,” said the
1877𓀴police agent loftily. “He has his own little methods, which are, if he
1878𓀴won’t mind my saying so, just a little too theoretical and fantastic,
1879𓀴but he has the makings of a detective in him. It is not too much to say
1880𓀴that once or twice, as in that business of the Sholto murder and the
1881𓀴Agra treasure, he has been more nearly correct than the official
1882𓀴force.”
1883𓀴
1884𓀴“Oh, if you say so, Mr. Jones, it is all right,” said the stranger with
1885𓀴deference. “Still, I confess that I miss my rubber. It is the first
1886𓀴Saturday night for seven-and-twenty years that I have not had my
1887𓀴rubber.”
1888𓀴
1889𓀴“I think you will find,” said Sherlock Holmes, “that you will play for
1890𓀴a higher stake to-night than you have ever done yet, and that the play
1891𓀴will be more exciting. For you, Mr. Merryweather, the stake will be
1892𓀴some £ 30,000; and for you, Jones, it will be the man upon whom you
1893𓀴wish to lay your hands.”
1894𓀴
1895𓀴“John Clay, the murderer, thief, smasher, and forger. He’s a young man,
1896𓀴Mr. Merryweather, but he is at the head of his profession, and I would
1897𓀴rather have my bracelets on him than on any criminal in London. He’s a
1898𓀴remarkable man, is young John Clay. His grandfather was a royal duke,
1899𓀴and he himself has been to Eton and Oxford. His brain is as cunning as
1900𓀴his fingers, and though we meet signs of him at every turn, we never
1901𓀴know where to find the man himself. He’ll crack a crib in Scotland one
1902𓀴week, and be raising money to build an orphanage in Cornwall the next.
1903𓀴I’ve been on his track for years and have never set eyes on him yet.”
1904𓀴
1905𓀴“I hope that I may have the pleasure of introducing you to-night. I’ve
1906𓀴had one or two little turns also with Mr. John Clay, and I agree with
1907𓀴you that he is at the head of his profession. It is past ten, however,
1908𓀴and quite time that we started. If you two will take the first hansom,
1909𓀴Watson and I will follow in the second.”
1910𓀴
1911𓀴Sherlock Holmes was not very communicative during the long drive and
1912𓀴lay back in the cab humming the tunes which he had heard in the
1913𓀴afternoon. We rattled through an endless labyrinth of gas-lit streets
1914𓀴until we emerged into Farrington Street.
1915𓀴
1916𓀴“We are close there now,” my friend remarked. “This fellow Merryweather
1917𓀴is a bank director, and personally interested in the matter. I thought
1918𓀴it as well to have Jones with us also. He is not a bad fellow, though
1919𓀴an absolute imbecile in his profession. He has one positive virtue. He
1920𓀴is as brave as a bulldog and as tenacious as a lobster if he gets his
1921𓀴claws upon anyone. Here we are, and they are waiting for us.”
1922𓀴
1923𓀴We had reached the same crowded thoroughfare in which we had found
1924𓀴ourselves in the morning. Our cabs were dismissed, and, following the
1925𓀴guidance of Mr. Merryweather, we passed down a narrow passage and
1926𓀴through a side door, which he opened for us. Within there was a small
1927𓀴corridor, which ended in a very massive iron gate. This also was
1928𓀴opened, and led down a flight of winding stone steps, which terminated
1929𓀴at another formidable gate. Mr. Merryweather stopped to light a
1930𓀴lantern, and then conducted us down a dark, earth-smelling passage, and
1931𓀴so, after opening a third door, into a huge vault or cellar, which was
1932𓀴piled all round with crates and massive boxes.
1933𓀴
1934𓀴“You are not very vulnerable from above,” Holmes remarked as he held up
1935𓀴the lantern and gazed about him.
1936𓀴
1937𓀴“Nor from below,” said Mr. Merryweather, striking his stick upon the
1938𓀴flags which lined the floor. “Why, dear me, it sounds quite hollow!” he
1939𓀴remarked, looking up in surprise.
1940𓀴
1941𓀴“I must really ask you to be a little more quiet!” said Holmes
1942𓀴severely. “You have already imperilled the whole success of our
1943𓀴expedition. Might I beg that you would have the goodness to sit down
1944𓀴upon one of those boxes, and not to interfere?”
1945𓀴
1946𓀴The solemn Mr. Merryweather perched himself upon a crate, with a very
1947𓀴injured expression upon his face, while Holmes fell upon his knees upon
1948𓀴the floor and, with the lantern and a magnifying lens, began to examine
1949𓀴minutely the cracks between the stones. A few seconds sufficed to
1950𓀴satisfy him, for he sprang to his feet again and put his glass in his
1951𓀴pocket.
1952𓀴
1953𓀴“We have at least an hour before us,” he remarked, “for they can hardly
1954𓀴take any steps until the good pawnbroker is safely in bed. Then they
1955𓀴will not lose a minute, for the sooner they do their work the longer
1956𓀴time they will have for their escape. We are at present, Doctor—as no
1957𓀴doubt you have divined—in the cellar of the City branch of one of the
1958𓀴principal London banks. Mr. Merryweather is the chairman of directors,
1959𓀴and he will explain to you that there are reasons why the more daring
1960𓀴criminals of London should take a considerable interest in this cellar
1961𓀴at present.”
1962𓀴
1963𓀴“It is our French gold,” whispered the director. “We have had several
1964𓀴warnings that an attempt might be made upon it.”
1965𓀴
1966𓀴“Your French gold?”
1967𓀴
1968𓀴“Yes. We had occasion some months ago to strengthen our resources and
1969𓀴borrowed for that purpose 30,000 napoleons from the Bank of France. It
1970𓀴has become known that we have never had occasion to unpack the money,
1971𓀴and that it is still lying in our cellar. The crate upon which I sit
1972𓀴contains 2,000 napoleons packed between layers of lead foil. Our
1973𓀴reserve of bullion is much larger at present than is usually kept in a
1974𓀴single branch office, and the directors have had misgivings upon the
1975𓀴subject.”
1976𓀴
1977𓀴“Which were very well justified,” observed Holmes. “And now it is time
1978𓀴that we arranged our little plans. I expect that within an hour matters
1979𓀴will come to a head. In the meantime Mr. Merryweather, we must put the
1980𓀴screen over that dark lantern.”
1981𓀴
1982𓀴“And sit in the dark?”
1983𓀴
1984𓀴“I am afraid so. I had brought a pack of cards in my pocket, and I
1985𓀴thought that, as we were a _partie carrée_, you might have your rubber
1986𓀴after all. But I see that the enemy’s preparations have gone so far
1987𓀴that we cannot risk the presence of a light. And, first of all, we must
1988𓀴choose our positions. These are daring men, and though we shall take
1989𓀴them at a disadvantage, they may do us some harm unless we are careful.
1990𓀴I shall stand behind this crate, and do you conceal yourselves behind
1991𓀴those. Then, when I flash a light upon them, close in swiftly. If they
1992𓀴fire, Watson, have no compunction about shooting them down.”
1993𓀴
1994𓀴I placed my revolver, cocked, upon the top of the wooden case behind
1995𓀴which I crouched. Holmes shot the slide across the front of his lantern
1996𓀴and left us in pitch darkness—such an absolute darkness as I have never
1997𓀴before experienced. The smell of hot metal remained to assure us that
1998𓀴the light was still there, ready to flash out at a moment’s notice. To
1999𓀴me, with my nerves worked up to a pitch of expectancy, there was
2000𓀴something depressing and subduing in the sudden gloom, and in the cold
2001𓀴dank air of the vault.
2002𓀴
2003𓀴“They have but one retreat,” whispered Holmes. “That is back through
2004𓀴the house into Saxe-Coburg Square. I hope that you have done what I
2005𓀴asked you, Jones?”
2006𓀴
2007𓀴“I have an inspector and two officers waiting at the front door.”
2008𓀴
2009𓀴“Then we have stopped all the holes. And now we must be silent and
2010𓀴wait.”
2011𓀴
2012𓀴What a time it seemed! From comparing notes afterwards it was but an
2013𓀴hour and a quarter, yet it appeared to me that the night must have
2014𓀴almost gone, and the dawn be breaking above us. My limbs were weary and
2015𓀴stiff, for I feared to change my position; yet my nerves were worked up
2016𓀴to the highest pitch of tension, and my hearing was so acute that I
2017𓀴could not only hear the gentle breathing of my companions, but I could
2018𓀴distinguish the deeper, heavier in-breath of the bulky Jones from the
2019𓀴thin, sighing note of the bank director. From my position I could look
2020𓀴over the case in the direction of the floor. Suddenly my eyes caught
2021𓀴the glint of a light.
2022𓀴
2023𓀴At first it was but a lurid spark upon the stone pavement. Then it
2024𓀴lengthened out until it became a yellow line, and then, without any
2025𓀴warning or sound, a gash seemed to open and a hand appeared, a white,
2026𓀴almost womanly hand, which felt about in the centre of the little area
2027𓀴of light. For a minute or more the hand, with its writhing fingers,
2028𓀴protruded out of the floor. Then it was withdrawn as suddenly as it
2029𓀴appeared, and all was dark again save the single lurid spark which
2030𓀴marked a chink between the stones.
2031𓀴
2032𓀴Its disappearance, however, was but momentary. With a rending, tearing
2033𓀴sound, one of the broad, white stones turned over upon its side and
2034𓀴left a square, gaping hole, through which streamed the light of a
2035𓀴lantern. Over the edge there peeped a clean-cut, boyish face, which
2036𓀴looked keenly about it, and then, with a hand on either side of the
2037𓀴aperture, drew itself shoulder-high and waist-high, until one knee
2038𓀴rested upon the edge. In another instant he stood at the side of the
2039𓀴hole and was hauling after him a companion, lithe and small like
2040𓀴himself, with a pale face and a shock of very red hair.
2041𓀴
2042𓀴“It’s all clear,” he whispered. “Have you the chisel and the bags?
2043𓀴Great Scott! Jump, Archie, jump, and I’ll swing for it!”
2044𓀴
2045𓀴Sherlock Holmes had sprung out and seized the intruder by the collar.
2046𓀴The other dived down the hole, and I heard the sound of rending cloth
2047𓀴as Jones clutched at his skirts. The light flashed upon the barrel of a
2048𓀴revolver, but Holmes’ hunting crop came down on the man’s wrist, and
2049𓀴the pistol clinked upon the stone floor.
2050𓀴
2051𓀴“It’s no use, John Clay,” said Holmes blandly. “You have no chance at
2052𓀴all.”
2053𓀴
2054𓀴“So I see,” the other answered with the utmost coolness. “I fancy that
2055𓀴my pal is all right, though I see you have got his coat-tails.”
2056𓀴
2057𓀴“There are three men waiting for him at the door,” said Holmes.
2058𓀴
2059𓀴“Oh, indeed! You seem to have done the thing very completely. I must
2060𓀴compliment you.”
2061𓀴
2062𓀴“And I you,” Holmes answered. “Your red-headed idea was very new and
2063𓀴effective.”
2064𓀴
2065𓀴“You’ll see your pal again presently,” said Jones. “He’s quicker at
2066𓀴climbing down holes than I am. Just hold out while I fix the derbies.”
2067𓀴
2068𓀴“I beg that you will not touch me with your filthy hands,” remarked our
2069𓀴prisoner as the handcuffs clattered upon his wrists. “You may not be
2070𓀴aware that I have royal blood in my veins. Have the goodness, also,
2071𓀴when you address me always to say ‘sir’ and ‘please.’”
2072𓀴
2073𓀴“All right,” said Jones with a stare and a snigger. “Well, would you
2074𓀴please, sir, march upstairs, where we can get a cab to carry your
2075𓀴Highness to the police-station?”
2076𓀴
2077𓀴“That is better,” said John Clay serenely. He made a sweeping bow to
2078𓀴the three of us and walked quietly off in the custody of the detective.
2079𓀴
2080𓀴“Really, Mr. Holmes,” said Mr. Merryweather as we followed them from
2081𓀴the cellar, “I do not know how the bank can thank you or repay you.
2082𓀴There is no doubt that you have detected and defeated in the most
2083𓀴complete manner one of the most determined attempts at bank robbery
2084𓀴that have ever come within my experience.”
2085𓀴
2086𓀴“I have had one or two little scores of my own to settle with Mr. John
2087𓀴Clay,” said Holmes. “I have been at some small expense over this
2088𓀴matter, which I shall expect the bank to refund, but beyond that I am
2089𓀴amply repaid by having had an experience which is in many ways unique,
2090𓀴and by hearing the very remarkable narrative of the Red-headed League.”
2091𓀴
2092𓀴“You see, Watson,” he explained in the early hours of the morning as we
2093𓀴sat over a glass of whisky and soda in Baker Street, “it was perfectly
2094𓀴obvious from the first that the only possible object of this rather
2095𓀴fantastic business of the advertisement of the League, and the copying
2096𓀴of the _Encyclopædia_, must be to get this not over-bright pawnbroker
2097𓀴out of the way for a number of hours every day. It was a curious way of
2098𓀴managing it, but, really, it would be difficult to suggest a better.
2099𓀴The method was no doubt suggested to Clay’s ingenious mind by the
2100𓀴colour of his accomplice’s hair. The £ 4 a week was a lure which must
2101𓀴draw him, and what was it to them, who were playing for thousands? They
2102𓀴put in the advertisement, one rogue has the temporary office, the other
2103𓀴rogue incites the man to apply for it, and together they manage to
2104𓀴secure his absence every morning in the week. From the time that I
2105𓀴heard of the assistant having come for half wages, it was obvious to me
2106𓀴that he had some strong motive for securing the situation.”
2107𓀴
2108𓀴“But how could you guess what the motive was?”
2109𓀴
2110𓀴“Had there been women in the house, I should have suspected a mere
2111𓀴vulgar intrigue. That, however, was out of the question. The man’s
2112𓀴business was a small one, and there was nothing in his house which
2113𓀴could account for such elaborate preparations, and such an expenditure
2114𓀴as they were at. It must, then, be something out of the house. What
2115𓀴could it be? I thought of the assistant’s fondness for photography, and
2116𓀴his trick of vanishing into the cellar. The cellar! There was the end
2117𓀴of this tangled clue. Then I made inquiries as to this mysterious
2118𓀴assistant and found that I had to deal with one of the coolest and most
2119𓀴daring criminals in London. He was doing something in the
2120𓀴cellar—something which took many hours a day for months on end. What
2121𓀴could it be, once more? I could think of nothing save that he was
2122𓀴running a tunnel to some other building.
2123𓀴
2124𓀴“So far I had got when we went to visit the scene of action. I
2125𓀴surprised you by beating upon the pavement with my stick. I was
2126𓀴ascertaining whether the cellar stretched out in front or behind. It
2127𓀴was not in front. Then I rang the bell, and, as I hoped, the assistant
2128𓀴answered it. We have had some skirmishes, but we had never set eyes
2129𓀴upon each other before. I hardly looked at his face. His knees were
2130𓀴what I wished to see. You must yourself have remarked how worn,
2131𓀴wrinkled, and stained they were. They spoke of those hours of
2132𓀴burrowing. The only remaining point was what they were burrowing for. I
2133𓀴walked round the corner, saw the City and Suburban Bank abutted on our
2134𓀴friend’s premises, and felt that I had solved my problem. When you
2135𓀴drove home after the concert I called upon Scotland Yard and upon the
2136𓀴chairman of the bank directors, with the result that you have seen.”
2137𓀴
2138𓀴“And how could you tell that they would make their attempt to-night?” I
2139𓀴asked.
2140𓀴
2141𓀴“Well, when they closed their League offices that was a sign that they
2142𓀴cared no longer about Mr. Jabez Wilson’s presence—in other words, that
2143𓀴they had completed their tunnel. But it was essential that they should
2144𓀴use it soon, as it might be discovered, or the bullion might be
2145𓀴removed. Saturday would suit them better than any other day, as it
2146𓀴would give them two days for their escape. For all these reasons I
2147𓀴expected them to come to-night.”
2148𓀴
2149𓀴“You reasoned it out beautifully,” I exclaimed in unfeigned admiration.
2150𓀴“It is so long a chain, and yet every link rings true.”
2151𓀴
2152𓀴“It saved me from ennui,” he answered, yawning. “Alas! I already feel
2153𓀴it closing in upon me. My life is spent in one long effort to escape
2154𓀴from the commonplaces of existence. These little problems help me to do
2155𓀴so.”
2156𓀴
2157𓀴“And you are a benefactor of the race,” said I.
2158𓀴
2159𓀴He shrugged his shoulders. “Well, perhaps, after all, it is of some
2160𓀴little use,” he remarked. “‘_L’homme c’est rien—l’œuvre c’est tout_,’
2161𓀴as Gustave Flaubert wrote to George Sand.”
2162𓀴
2163𓀴
2164𓀴
2165𓀴
2166𓀴III. A CASE OF IDENTITY
2167𓀴
2168𓀴
2169𓀴“My dear fellow,” said Sherlock Holmes as we sat on either side of the
2170𓀴fire in his lodgings at Baker Street, “life is infinitely stranger than
2171𓀴anything which the mind of man could invent. We would not dare to
2172𓀴conceive the things which are really mere commonplaces of existence. If
2173𓀴we could fly out of that window hand in hand, hover over this great
2174𓀴city, gently remove the roofs, and peep in at the queer things which
2175𓀴are going on, the strange coincidences, the plannings, the
2176𓀴cross-purposes, the wonderful chains of events, working through
2177𓀴generations, and leading to the most _outré_ results, it would make all
2178𓀴fiction with its conventionalities and foreseen conclusions most stale
2179𓀴and unprofitable.”
2180𓀴
2181𓀴“And yet I am not convinced of it,” I answered. “The cases which come
2182𓀴to light in the papers are, as a rule, bald enough, and vulgar enough.
2183𓀴We have in our police reports realism pushed to its extreme limits, and
2184𓀴yet the result is, it must be confessed, neither fascinating nor
2185𓀴artistic.”
2186𓀴
2187𓀴“A certain selection and discretion must be used in producing a
2188𓀴realistic effect,” remarked Holmes. “This is wanting in the police
2189𓀴report, where more stress is laid, perhaps, upon the platitudes of the
2190𓀴magistrate than upon the details, which to an observer contain the
2191𓀴vital essence of the whole matter. Depend upon it, there is nothing so
2192𓀴unnatural as the commonplace.”
2193𓀴
2194𓀴I smiled and shook my head. “I can quite understand your thinking so,”
2195𓀴I said. “Of course, in your position of unofficial adviser and helper
2196𓀴to everybody who is absolutely puzzled, throughout three continents,
2197𓀴you are brought in contact with all that is strange and bizarre. But
2198𓀴here”—I picked up the morning paper from the ground—“let us put it to a
2199𓀴practical test. Here is the first heading upon which I come. ‘A
2200𓀴husband’s cruelty to his wife.’ There is half a column of print, but I
2201𓀴know without reading it that it is all perfectly familiar to me. There
2202𓀴is, of course, the other woman, the drink, the push, the blow, the
2203𓀴bruise, the sympathetic sister or landlady. The crudest of writers
2204𓀴could invent nothing more crude.”
2205𓀴
2206𓀴“Indeed, your example is an unfortunate one for your argument,” said
2207𓀴Holmes, taking the paper and glancing his eye down it. “This is the
2208𓀴Dundas separation case, and, as it happens, I was engaged in clearing
2209𓀴up some small points in connection with it. The husband was a
2210𓀴teetotaler, there was no other woman, and the conduct complained of was
2211𓀴that he had drifted into the habit of winding up every meal by taking
2212𓀴out his false teeth and hurling them at his wife, which, you will
2213𓀴allow, is not an action likely to occur to the imagination of the
2214𓀴average story-teller. Take a pinch of snuff, Doctor, and acknowledge
2215𓀴that I have scored over you in your example.”
2216𓀴
2217𓀴He held out his snuffbox of old gold, with a great amethyst in the
2218𓀴centre of the lid. Its splendour was in such contrast to his homely
2219𓀴ways and simple life that I could not help commenting upon it.
2220𓀴
2221𓀴“Ah,” said he, “I forgot that I had not seen you for some weeks. It is
2222𓀴a little souvenir from the King of Bohemia in return for my assistance
2223𓀴in the case of the Irene Adler papers.”
2224𓀴
2225𓀴“And the ring?” I asked, glancing at a remarkable brilliant which
2226𓀴sparkled upon his finger.
2227𓀴
2228𓀴“It was from the reigning family of Holland, though the matter in which
2229𓀴I served them was of such delicacy that I cannot confide it even to
2230𓀴you, who have been good enough to chronicle one or two of my little
2231𓀴problems.”
2232𓀴
2233𓀴“And have you any on hand just now?” I asked with interest.
2234𓀴
2235𓀴“Some ten or twelve, but none which present any feature of interest.
2236𓀴They are important, you understand, without being interesting. Indeed,
2237𓀴I have found that it is usually in unimportant matters that there is a
2238𓀴field for the observation, and for the quick analysis of cause and
2239𓀴effect which gives the charm to an investigation. The larger crimes are
2240𓀴apt to be the simpler, for the bigger the crime the more obvious, as a
2241𓀴rule, is the motive. In these cases, save for one rather intricate
2242𓀴matter which has been referred to me from Marseilles, there is nothing
2243𓀴which presents any features of interest. It is possible, however, that
2244𓀴I may have something better before very many minutes are over, for this
2245𓀴is one of my clients, or I am much mistaken.”
2246𓀴
2247𓀴He had risen from his chair and was standing between the parted blinds
2248𓀴gazing down into the dull neutral-tinted London street. Looking over
2249𓀴his shoulder, I saw that on the pavement opposite there stood a large
2250𓀴woman with a heavy fur boa round her neck, and a large curling red
2251𓀴feather in a broad-brimmed hat which was tilted in a coquettish Duchess
2252𓀴of Devonshire fashion over her ear. From under this great panoply she
2253𓀴peeped up in a nervous, hesitating fashion at our windows, while her
2254𓀴body oscillated backward and forward, and her fingers fidgeted with her
2255𓀴glove buttons. Suddenly, with a plunge, as of the swimmer who leaves
2256𓀴the bank, she hurried across the road, and we heard the sharp clang of
2257𓀴the bell.
2258𓀴
2259𓀴“I have seen those symptoms before,” said Holmes, throwing his
2260𓀴cigarette into the fire. “Oscillation upon the pavement always means an
2261𓀴_affaire de cœur_. She would like advice, but is not sure that the
2262𓀴matter is not too delicate for communication. And yet even here we may
2263𓀴discriminate. When a woman has been seriously wronged by a man she no
2264𓀴longer oscillates, and the usual symptom is a broken bell wire. Here we
2265𓀴may take it that there is a love matter, but that the maiden is not so
2266𓀴much angry as perplexed, or grieved. But here she comes in person to
2267𓀴resolve our doubts.”
2268𓀴
2269𓀴As he spoke there was a tap at the door, and the boy in buttons entered
2270𓀴to announce Miss Mary Sutherland, while the lady herself loomed behind
2271𓀴his small black figure like a full-sailed merchant-man behind a tiny
2272𓀴pilot boat. Sherlock Holmes welcomed her with the easy courtesy for
2273𓀴which he was remarkable, and, having closed the door and bowed her into
2274𓀴an armchair, he looked her over in the minute and yet abstracted
2275𓀴fashion which was peculiar to him.
2276𓀴
2277𓀴“Do you not find,” he said, “that with your short sight it is a little
2278𓀴trying to do so much typewriting?”
2279𓀴
2280𓀴“I did at first,” she answered, “but now I know where the letters are
2281𓀴without looking.” Then, suddenly realising the full purport of his
2282𓀴words, she gave a violent start and looked up, with fear and
2283𓀴astonishment upon her broad, good-humoured face. “You’ve heard about
2284𓀴me, Mr. Holmes,” she cried, “else how could you know all that?”
2285𓀴
2286𓀴“Never mind,” said Holmes, laughing; “it is my business to know things.
2287𓀴Perhaps I have trained myself to see what others overlook. If not, why
2288𓀴should you come to consult me?”
2289𓀴
2290𓀴“I came to you, sir, because I heard of you from Mrs. Etherege, whose
2291𓀴husband you found so easy when the police and everyone had given him up
2292𓀴for dead. Oh, Mr. Holmes, I wish you would do as much for me. I’m not
2293𓀴rich, but still I have a hundred a year in my own right, besides the
2294𓀴little that I make by the machine, and I would give it all to know what
2295𓀴has become of Mr. Hosmer Angel.”
2296𓀴
2297𓀴“Why did you come away to consult me in such a hurry?” asked Sherlock
2298𓀴Holmes, with his finger-tips together and his eyes to the ceiling.
2299𓀴
2300𓀴Again a startled look came over the somewhat vacuous face of Miss Mary
2301𓀴Sutherland. “Yes, I did bang out of the house,” she said, “for it made
2302𓀴me angry to see the easy way in which Mr. Windibank—that is, my
2303𓀴father—took it all. He would not go to the police, and he would not go
2304𓀴to you, and so at last, as he would do nothing and kept on saying that
2305𓀴there was no harm done, it made me mad, and I just on with my things
2306𓀴and came right away to you.”
2307𓀴
2308𓀴“Your father,” said Holmes, “your stepfather, surely, since the name is
2309𓀴different.”
2310𓀴
2311𓀴“Yes, my stepfather. I call him father, though it sounds funny, too,
2312𓀴for he is only five years and two months older than myself.”
2313𓀴
2314𓀴“And your mother is alive?”
2315𓀴
2316𓀴“Oh, yes, mother is alive and well. I wasn’t best pleased, Mr. Holmes,
2317𓀴when she married again so soon after father’s death, and a man who was
2318𓀴nearly fifteen years younger than herself. Father was a plumber in the
2319𓀴Tottenham Court Road, and he left a tidy business behind him, which
2320𓀴mother carried on with Mr. Hardy, the foreman; but when Mr. Windibank
2321𓀴came he made her sell the business, for he was very superior, being a
2322𓀴traveller in wines. They got £ 4700 for the goodwill and interest,
2323𓀴which wasn’t near as much as father could have got if he had been
2324𓀴alive.”
2325𓀴
2326𓀴I had expected to see Sherlock Holmes impatient under this rambling and
2327𓀴inconsequential narrative, but, on the contrary, he had listened with
2328𓀴the greatest concentration of attention.
2329𓀴
2330𓀴“Your own little income,” he asked, “does it come out of the business?”
2331𓀴
2332𓀴“Oh, no, sir. It is quite separate and was left me by my uncle Ned in
2333𓀴Auckland. It is in New Zealand stock, paying 4½ per cent. Two thousand
2334𓀴five hundred pounds was the amount, but I can only touch the interest.”
2335𓀴
2336𓀴“You interest me extremely,” said Holmes. “And since you draw so large
2337𓀴a sum as a hundred a year, with what you earn into the bargain, you no
2338𓀴doubt travel a little and indulge yourself in every way. I believe that
2339𓀴a single lady can get on very nicely upon an income of about £ 60.”
2340𓀴
2341𓀴“I could do with much less than that, Mr. Holmes, but you understand
2342𓀴that as long as I live at home I don’t wish to be a burden to them, and
2343𓀴so they have the use of the money just while I am staying with them. Of
2344𓀴course, that is only just for the time. Mr. Windibank draws my interest
2345𓀴every quarter and pays it over to mother, and I find that I can do
2346𓀴pretty well with what I earn at typewriting. It brings me twopence a
2347𓀴sheet, and I can often do from fifteen to twenty sheets in a day.”
2348𓀴
2349𓀴“You have made your position very clear to me,” said Holmes. “This is
2350𓀴my friend, Dr. Watson, before whom you can speak as freely as before
2351𓀴myself. Kindly tell us now all about your connection with Mr. Hosmer
2352𓀴Angel.”
2353𓀴
2354𓀴A flush stole over Miss Sutherland’s face, and she picked nervously at
2355𓀴the fringe of her jacket. “I met him first at the gasfitters’ ball,”
2356𓀴she said. “They used to send father tickets when he was alive, and then
2357𓀴afterwards they remembered us, and sent them to mother. Mr. Windibank
2358𓀴did not wish us to go. He never did wish us to go anywhere. He would
2359𓀴get quite mad if I wanted so much as to join a Sunday-school treat. But
2360𓀴this time I was set on going, and I would go; for what right had he to
2361𓀴prevent? He said the folk were not fit for us to know, when all
2362𓀴father’s friends were to be there. And he said that I had nothing fit
2363𓀴to wear, when I had my purple plush that I had never so much as taken
2364𓀴out of the drawer. At last, when nothing else would do, he went off to
2365𓀴France upon the business of the firm, but we went, mother and I, with
2366𓀴Mr. Hardy, who used to be our foreman, and it was there I met Mr.
2367𓀴Hosmer Angel.”
2368𓀴
2369𓀴“I suppose,” said Holmes, “that when Mr. Windibank came back from
2370𓀴France he was very annoyed at your having gone to the ball.”
2371𓀴
2372𓀴“Oh, well, he was very good about it. He laughed, I remember, and
2373𓀴shrugged his shoulders, and said there was no use denying anything to a
2374𓀴woman, for she would have her way.”
2375𓀴
2376𓀴“I see. Then at the gasfitters’ ball you met, as I understand, a
2377𓀴gentleman called Mr. Hosmer Angel.”
2378𓀴
2379𓀴“Yes, sir. I met him that night, and he called next day to ask if we
2380𓀴had got home all safe, and after that we met him—that is to say, Mr.
2381𓀴Holmes, I met him twice for walks, but after that father came back
2382𓀴again, and Mr. Hosmer Angel could not come to the house any more.”
2383𓀴
2384𓀴“No?”
2385𓀴
2386𓀴“Well, you know father didn’t like anything of the sort. He wouldn’t
2387𓀴have any visitors if he could help it, and he used to say that a woman
2388𓀴should be happy in her own family circle. But then, as I used to say to
2389𓀴mother, a woman wants her own circle to begin with, and I had not got
2390𓀴mine yet.”
2391𓀴
2392𓀴“But how about Mr. Hosmer Angel? Did he make no attempt to see you?”
2393𓀴

2395𓀴and said that it would be safer and better not to see each other until
2396𓀴he had gone. We could write in the meantime, and he used to write every
2397𓀴day. I took the letters in in the morning, so there was no need for
2398𓀴father to know.”
2399𓀴
2400𓀴“Were you engaged to the gentleman at this time?”
2401𓀴
2402𓀴“Oh, yes, Mr. Holmes. We were engaged after the first walk that we
2403𓀴took. Hosmer—Mr. Angel—was a cashier in an office in Leadenhall
2404𓀴Street—and—”
2405𓀴
2406𓀴“What office?”
2407𓀴
2408𓀴“That’s the worst of it, Mr. Holmes, I don’t know.”
2409𓀴
2410𓀴“Where did he live, then?”
2411𓀴
2412𓀴“He slept on the premises.”
2413𓀴
2414𓀴“And you don’t know his address?”
2415𓀴
2416𓀴“No—except that it was Leadenhall Street.”
2417𓀴
2418𓀴“Where did you address your letters, then?”
2419𓀴
2420𓀴“To the Leadenhall Street Post Office, to be left till called for. He
2421𓀴said that if they were sent to the office he would be chaffed by all
2422𓀴the other clerks about having letters from a lady, so I offered to
2423𓀴typewrite them, like he did his, but he wouldn’t have that, for he said
2424𓀴that when I wrote them they seemed to come from me, but when they were
2425𓀴typewritten he always felt that the machine had come between us. That
2426𓀴will just show you how fond he was of me, Mr. Holmes, and the little
2427𓀴things that he would think of.”
2428𓀴
2429𓀴“It was most suggestive,” said Holmes. “It has long been an axiom of
2430𓀴mine that the little things are infinitely the most important. Can you
2431𓀴remember any other little things about Mr. Hosmer Angel?”
2432𓀴
2433𓀴“He was a very shy man, Mr. Holmes. He would rather walk with me in the
2434𓀴evening than in the daylight, for he said that he hated to be
2435𓀴conspicuous. Very retiring and gentlemanly he was. Even his voice was
2436𓀴gentle. He’d had the quinsy and swollen glands when he was young, he
2437𓀴told me, and it had left him with a weak throat, and a hesitating,
2438𓀴whispering fashion of speech. He was always well dressed, very neat and
2439𓀴plain, but his eyes were weak, just as mine are, and he wore tinted
2440𓀴glasses against the glare.”
2441𓀴
2442𓀴“Well, and what happened when Mr. Windibank, your stepfather, returned
2443𓀴to France?”
2444𓀴
2445𓀴“Mr. Hosmer Angel came to the house again and proposed that we should
2446𓀴marry before father came back. He was in dreadful earnest and made me
2447𓀴swear, with my hands on the Testament, that whatever happened I would
2448𓀴always be true to him. Mother said he was quite right to make me swear,
2449𓀴and that it was a sign of his passion. Mother was all in his favour
2450𓀴from the first and was even fonder of him than I was. Then, when they
2451𓀴talked of marrying within the week, I began to ask about father; but
2452𓀴they both said never to mind about father, but just to tell him
2453𓀴afterwards, and mother said she would make it all right with him. I
2454𓀴didn’t quite like that, Mr. Holmes. It seemed funny that I should ask
2455𓀴his leave, as he was only a few years older than me; but I didn’t want
2456𓀴to do anything on the sly, so I wrote to father at Bordeaux, where the
2457𓀴company has its French offices, but the letter came back to me on the
2458𓀴very morning of the wedding.”
2459𓀴
2460𓀴“It missed him, then?”
2461𓀴
2462𓀴“Yes, sir; for he had started to England just before it arrived.”
2463𓀴
2464𓀴“Ha! that was unfortunate. Your wedding was arranged, then, for the
2465𓀴Friday. Was it to be in church?”
2466𓀴
2467𓀴“Yes, sir, but very quietly. It was to be at St. Saviour’s, near King’s
2468𓀴Cross, and we were to have breakfast afterwards at the St. Pancras
2469𓀴Hotel. Hosmer came for us in a hansom, but as there were two of us he
2470𓀴put us both into it and stepped himself into a four-wheeler, which
2471𓀴happened to be the only other cab in the street. We got to the church
2472𓀴first, and when the four-wheeler drove up we waited for him to step
2473𓀴out, but he never did, and when the cabman got down from the box and
2474𓀴looked there was no one there! The cabman said that he could not
2475𓀴imagine what had become of him, for he had seen him get in with his own

2477𓀴anything since then to throw any light upon what became of him.”
2478𓀴
2479𓀴“It seems to me that you have been very shamefully treated,” said
2480𓀴Holmes.
2481𓀴
2482𓀴“Oh, no, sir! He was too good and kind to leave me so. Why, all the
2483𓀴morning he was saying to me that, whatever happened, I was to be true;
2484𓀴and that even if something quite unforeseen occurred to separate us, I
2485𓀴was always to remember that I was pledged to him, and that he would
2486𓀴claim his pledge sooner or later. It seemed strange talk for a
2487𓀴wedding-morning, but what has happened since gives a meaning to it.”
2488𓀴
2489𓀴“Most certainly it does. Your own opinion is, then, that some
2490𓀴unforeseen catastrophe has occurred to him?”
2491𓀴
2492𓀴“Yes, sir. I believe that he foresaw some danger, or else he would not
2493𓀴have talked so. And then I think that what he foresaw happened.”
2494𓀴
2495𓀴“But you have no notion as to what it could have been?”
2496𓀴
2497𓀴“None.”
2498𓀴
2499𓀴“One more question. How did your mother take the matter?”
2500𓀴
2501𓀴“She was angry, and said that I was never to speak of the matter
2502𓀴again.”
2503𓀴
2504𓀴“And your father? Did you tell him?”
2505𓀴
2506𓀴“Yes; and he seemed to think, with me, that something had happened, and
2507𓀴that I should hear of Hosmer again. As he said, what interest could
2508𓀴anyone have in bringing me to the doors of the church, and then leaving
2509𓀴me? Now, if he had borrowed my money, or if he had married me and got
2510𓀴my money settled on him, there might be some reason, but Hosmer was
2511𓀴very independent about money and never would look at a shilling of
2512𓀴mine. And yet, what could have happened? And why could he not write?
2513𓀴Oh, it drives me half-mad to think of it, and I can’t sleep a wink at
2514𓀴night.” She pulled a little handkerchief out of her muff and began to
2515𓀴sob heavily into it.
2516𓀴
2517𓀴“I shall glance into the case for you,” said Holmes, rising, “and I
2518𓀴have no doubt that we shall reach some definite result. Let the weight
2519𓀴of the matter rest upon me now, and do not let your mind dwell upon it
2520𓀴further. Above all, try to let Mr. Hosmer Angel vanish from your
2521𓀴memory, as he has done from your life.”
2522𓀴
2523𓀴“Then you don’t think I’ll see him again?”
2524𓀴
2525𓀴“I fear not.”
2526𓀴
2527𓀴“Then what has happened to him?”
2528𓀴

2530𓀴description of him and any letters of his which you can spare.”

2534𓀴

2536𓀴

2538𓀴

2540𓀴father’s place of business?”
2541𓀴

2544𓀴

2546𓀴the papers here, and remember the advice which I have given you. Let
2547𓀴the whole incident be a sealed book, and do not allow it to affect your
