2552𓀴
2553𓀴For all the preposterous hat and the vacuous face, there was something
2554𓀴noble in the simple faith of our visitor which compelled our respect.
2555𓀴She laid her little bundle of papers upon the table and went her way,
2556𓀴with a promise to come again whenever she might be summoned.
2557𓀴
2558𓀴Sherlock Holmes sat silent for a few minutes with his fingertips still
2559𓀴pressed together, his legs stretched out in front of him, and his gaze
2560𓀴directed upward to the ceiling. Then he took down from the rack the old
2561𓀴and oily clay pipe, which was to him as a counsellor, and, having lit
2562𓀴it, he leaned back in his chair, with the thick blue cloud-wreaths
2563𓀴spinning up from him, and a look of infinite languor in his face.
2564𓀴
2565𓀴“Quite an interesting study, that maiden,” he observed. “I found her
2566𓀴more interesting than her little problem, which, by the way, is rather
2567𓀴a trite one. You will find parallel cases, if you consult my index, in
2568𓀴Andover in ’77, and there was something of the sort at The Hague last
2569𓀴year. Old as is the idea, however, there were one or two details which
2570𓀴were new to me. But the maiden herself was most instructive.”
2571𓀴
2572𓀴“You appeared to read a good deal upon her which was quite invisible to
2573𓀴me,” I remarked.
2574𓀴
2575𓀴“Not invisible but unnoticed, Watson. You did not know where to look,
2576𓀴and so you missed all that was important. I can never bring you to
2577𓀴realise the importance of sleeves, the suggestiveness of thumb-nails,
2578𓀴or the great issues that may hang from a boot-lace. Now, what did you
2579𓀴gather from that woman’s appearance? Describe it.”
2580𓀴
2581𓀴“Well, she had a slate-coloured, broad-brimmed straw hat, with a
2582𓀴feather of a brickish red. Her jacket was black, with black beads sewn
2583𓀴upon it, and a fringe of little black jet ornaments. Her dress was
2584𓀴brown, rather darker than coffee colour, with a little purple plush at
2585𓀴the neck and sleeves. Her gloves were greyish and were worn through at
2586𓀴the right forefinger. Her boots I didn’t observe. She had small round,
2587𓀴hanging gold earrings, and a general air of being fairly well-to-do in
2588𓀴a vulgar, comfortable, easy-going way.”
2589𓀴
2590𓀴Sherlock Holmes clapped his hands softly together and chuckled.
2591𓀴
2592𓀴“’Pon my word, Watson, you are coming along wonderfully. You have
2593𓀴really done very well indeed. It is true that you have missed
2594𓀴everything of importance, but you have hit upon the method, and you
2595𓀴have a quick eye for colour. Never trust to general impressions, my
2596𓀴boy, but concentrate yourself upon details. My first glance is always
2597𓀴at a woman’s sleeve. In a man it is perhaps better first to take the
2598𓀴knee of the trouser. As you observe, this woman had plush upon her
2599𓀴sleeves, which is a most useful material for showing traces. The double
2600𓀴line a little above the wrist, where the typewritist presses against
2601𓀴the table, was beautifully defined. The sewing-machine, of the hand
2602𓀴type, leaves a similar mark, but only on the left arm, and on the side
2603𓀴of it farthest from the thumb, instead of being right across the
2604𓀴broadest part, as this was. I then glanced at her face, and, observing
2605𓀴the dint of a pince-nez at either side of her nose, I ventured a remark
2606𓀴upon short sight and typewriting, which seemed to surprise her.”
2607𓀴
2608𓀴“It surprised me.”
2609𓀴
2610𓀴“But, surely, it was obvious. I was then much surprised and interested
2611𓀴on glancing down to observe that, though the boots which she was
2612𓀴wearing were not unlike each other, they were really odd ones; the one
2613𓀴having a slightly decorated toe-cap, and the other a plain one. One was
2614𓀴buttoned only in the two lower buttons out of five, and the other at
2615𓀴the first, third, and fifth. Now, when you see that a young lady,
2616𓀴otherwise neatly dressed, has come away from home with odd boots,
2617𓀴half-buttoned, it is no great deduction to say that she came away in a
2618𓀴hurry.”
2619𓀴
2620𓀴“And what else?” I asked, keenly interested, as I always was, by my
2621𓀴friend’s incisive reasoning.
2622𓀴
2623𓀴“I noted, in passing, that she had written a note before leaving home
2624𓀴but after being fully dressed. You observed that her right glove was
2625𓀴torn at the forefinger, but you did not apparently see that both glove
2626𓀴and finger were stained with violet ink. She had written in a hurry and
2627𓀴dipped her pen too deep. It must have been this morning, or the mark
2628𓀴would not remain clear upon the finger. All this is amusing, though
2629𓀴rather elementary, but I must go back to business, Watson. Would you
2630𓀴mind reading me the advertised description of Mr. Hosmer Angel?”
2631𓀴
2632𓀴I held the little printed slip to the light. “Missing,” it said, “on
2633𓀴the morning of the fourteenth, a gentleman named Hosmer Angel. About
2634𓀴five ft. seven in. in height; strongly built, sallow complexion, black
2635𓀴hair, a little bald in the centre, bushy, black side-whiskers and
2636𓀴moustache; tinted glasses, slight infirmity of speech. Was dressed,
2637𓀴when last seen, in black frock-coat faced with silk, black waistcoat,
2638𓀴gold Albert chain, and grey Harris tweed trousers, with brown gaiters
2639𓀴over elastic-sided boots. Known to have been employed in an office in
2640𓀴Leadenhall Street. Anybody bringing,” &c, &c.
2641𓀴
2642𓀴“That will do,” said Holmes. “As to the letters,” he continued,
2643𓀴glancing over them, “they are very commonplace. Absolutely no clue in
2644𓀴them to Mr. Angel, save that he quotes Balzac once. There is one
2645𓀴remarkable point, however, which will no doubt strike you.”
2646𓀴
2647𓀴“They are typewritten,” I remarked.
2648𓀴
2649𓀴“Not only that, but the signature is typewritten. Look at the neat
2650𓀴little ‘Hosmer Angel’ at the bottom. There is a date, you see, but no
2651𓀴superscription except Leadenhall Street, which is rather vague. The
2652𓀴point about the signature is very suggestive—in fact, we may call it
2653𓀴conclusive.”
2654𓀴
2655𓀴“Of what?”
2656𓀴
2657𓀴“My dear fellow, is it possible you do not see how strongly it bears
2658𓀴upon the case?”
2659𓀴
2660𓀴“I cannot say that I do unless it were that he wished to be able to
2661𓀴deny his signature if an action for breach of promise were instituted.”
2662𓀴
2663𓀴“No, that was not the point. However, I shall write two letters, which
2664𓀴should settle the matter. One is to a firm in the City, the other is to
2665𓀴the young lady’s stepfather, Mr. Windibank, asking him whether he could
2666𓀴meet us here at six o’clock to-morrow evening. It is just as well that
2667𓀴we should do business with the male relatives. And now, Doctor, we can
2668𓀴do nothing until the answers to those letters come, so we may put our
2669𓀴little problem upon the shelf for the interim.”
2670𓀴
2671𓀴I had had so many reasons to believe in my friend’s subtle powers of
2672𓀴reasoning and extraordinary energy in action that I felt that he must
2673𓀴have some solid grounds for the assured and easy demeanour with which
2674𓀴he treated the singular mystery which he had been called upon to
2675𓀴fathom. Once only had I known him to fail, in the case of the King of
2676𓀴Bohemia and of the Irene Adler photograph; but when I looked back to
2677𓀴the weird business of the Sign of Four, and the extraordinary
2678𓀴circumstances connected with the Study in Scarlet, I felt that it would
2679𓀴be a strange tangle indeed which he could not unravel.
2680𓀴
2681𓀴I left him then, still puffing at his black clay pipe, with the
2682𓀴conviction that when I came again on the next evening I would find that
2683𓀴he held in his hands all the clues which would lead up to the identity
2684𓀴of the disappearing bridegroom of Miss Mary Sutherland.
2685𓀴
2686𓀴A professional case of great gravity was engaging my own attention at
2687𓀴the time, and the whole of next day I was busy at the bedside of the
2688𓀴sufferer. It was not until close upon six o’clock that I found myself
2689𓀴free and was able to spring into a hansom and drive to Baker Street,
2690𓀴half afraid that I might be too late to assist at the _dénouement_ of
2691𓀴the little mystery. I found Sherlock Holmes alone, however, half
2692𓀴asleep, with his long, thin form curled up in the recesses of his
2693𓀴armchair. A formidable array of bottles and test-tubes, with the
2694𓀴pungent cleanly smell of hydrochloric acid, told me that he had spent
2695𓀴his day in the chemical work which was so dear to him.
2696𓀴
2697𓀴“Well, have you solved it?” I asked as I entered.
2698𓀴
2699𓀴“Yes. It was the bisulphate of baryta.”
2700𓀴
2701𓀴“No, no, the mystery!” I cried.
2702𓀴
2703𓀴“Oh, that! I thought of the salt that I have been working upon. There
2704𓀴was never any mystery in the matter, though, as I said yesterday, some
2705𓀴of the details are of interest. The only drawback is that there is no
2706𓀴law, I fear, that can touch the scoundrel.”
2707𓀴
2708𓀴“Who was he, then, and what was his object in deserting Miss
2709𓀴Sutherland?”
2710𓀴
2711𓀴The question was hardly out of my mouth, and Holmes had not yet opened
2712𓀴his lips to reply, when we heard a heavy footfall in the passage and a
2713𓀴tap at the door.
2714𓀴
2715𓀴“This is the girl’s stepfather, Mr. James Windibank,” said Holmes. “He
2716𓀴has written to me to say that he would be here at six. Come in!”
2717𓀴
2718𓀴The man who entered was a sturdy, middle-sized fellow, some thirty
2719𓀴years of age, clean-shaven, and sallow-skinned, with a bland,
2720𓀴insinuating manner, and a pair of wonderfully sharp and penetrating
2721𓀴grey eyes. He shot a questioning glance at each of us, placed his shiny
2722𓀴top-hat upon the sideboard, and with a slight bow sidled down into the
2723𓀴nearest chair.
2724𓀴
2725𓀴“Good-evening, Mr. James Windibank,” said Holmes. “I think that this
2726𓀴typewritten letter is from you, in which you made an appointment with
2727𓀴me for six o’clock?”
2728𓀴
2729𓀴“Yes, sir. I am afraid that I am a little late, but I am not quite my
2730𓀴own master, you know. I am sorry that Miss Sutherland has troubled you
2731𓀴about this little matter, for I think it is far better not to wash
2732𓀴linen of the sort in public. It was quite against my wishes that she
2733𓀴came, but she is a very excitable, impulsive girl, as you may have
2734𓀴noticed, and she is not easily controlled when she has made up her mind
2735𓀴on a point. Of course, I did not mind you so much, as you are not
2736𓀴connected with the official police, but it is not pleasant to have a
2737𓀴family misfortune like this noised abroad. Besides, it is a useless
2738𓀴expense, for how could you possibly find this Hosmer Angel?”
2739𓀴
2740𓀴“On the contrary,” said Holmes quietly; “I have every reason to believe
2741𓀴that I will succeed in discovering Mr. Hosmer Angel.”
2742𓀴
2743𓀴Mr. Windibank gave a violent start and dropped his gloves. “I am
2744𓀴delighted to hear it,” he said.
2745𓀴
2746𓀴“It is a curious thing,” remarked Holmes, “that a typewriter has really
2747𓀴quite as much individuality as a man’s handwriting. Unless they are
2748𓀴quite new, no two of them write exactly alike. Some letters get more
2749𓀴worn than others, and some wear only on one side. Now, you remark in
2750𓀴this note of yours, Mr. Windibank, that in every case there is some
2751𓀴little slurring over of the ‘e,’ and a slight defect in the tail of the
2752𓀴‘r.’ There are fourteen other characteristics, but those are the more
2753𓀴obvious.”
2754𓀴
2755𓀴“We do all our correspondence with this machine at the office, and no
2756𓀴doubt it is a little worn,” our visitor answered, glancing keenly at
2757𓀴Holmes with his bright little eyes.
2758𓀴
2759𓀴“And now I will show you what is really a very interesting study, Mr.
2760𓀴Windibank,” Holmes continued. “I think of writing another little
2761𓀴monograph some of these days on the typewriter and its relation to
2762𓀴crime. It is a subject to which I have devoted some little attention. I
2763𓀴have here four letters which purport to come from the missing man. They
2764𓀴are all typewritten. In each case, not only are the ‘e’s’ slurred and
2765𓀴the ‘r’s’ tailless, but you will observe, if you care to use my
2766𓀴magnifying lens, that the fourteen other characteristics to which I
2767𓀴have alluded are there as well.”
2768𓀴
2769𓀴Mr. Windibank sprang out of his chair and picked up his hat. “I cannot
2770𓀴waste time over this sort of fantastic talk, Mr. Holmes,” he said. “If
2771𓀴you can catch the man, catch him, and let me know when you have done
2772𓀴it.”
2773𓀴
2774𓀴“Certainly,” said Holmes, stepping over and turning the key in the
2775𓀴door. “I let you know, then, that I have caught him!”
2776𓀴
2777𓀴“What! where?” shouted Mr. Windibank, turning white to his lips and
2778𓀴glancing about him like a rat in a trap.
2779𓀴
2780𓀴“Oh, it won’t do—really it won’t,” said Holmes suavely. “There is no
2781𓀴possible getting out of it, Mr. Windibank. It is quite too transparent,
2782𓀴and it was a very bad compliment when you said that it was impossible
2783𓀴for me to solve so simple a question. That’s right! Sit down and let us
2784𓀴talk it over.”
2785𓀴
2786𓀴Our visitor collapsed into a chair, with a ghastly face and a glitter
2787𓀴of moisture on his brow. “It—it’s not actionable,” he stammered.
2788𓀴
2789𓀴“I am very much afraid that it is not. But between ourselves,
2790𓀴Windibank, it was as cruel and selfish and heartless a trick in a petty
2791𓀴way as ever came before me. Now, let me just run over the course of
2792𓀴events, and you will contradict me if I go wrong.”
2793𓀴
2794𓀴The man sat huddled up in his chair, with his head sunk upon his
2795𓀴breast, like one who is utterly crushed. Holmes stuck his feet up on
2796𓀴the corner of the mantelpiece and, leaning back with his hands in his
2797𓀴pockets, began talking, rather to himself, as it seemed, than to us.
2798𓀴
2799𓀴“The man married a woman very much older than himself for her money,”
2800𓀴said he, “and he enjoyed the use of the money of the daughter as long
2801𓀴as she lived with them. It was a considerable sum, for people in their
2802𓀴position, and the loss of it would have made a serious difference. It
2803𓀴was worth an effort to preserve it. The daughter was of a good, amiable
2804𓀴disposition, but affectionate and warm-hearted in her ways, so that it
2805𓀴was evident that with her fair personal advantages, and her little
2806𓀴income, she would not be allowed to remain single long. Now her
2807𓀴marriage would mean, of course, the loss of a hundred a year, so what
2808𓀴does her stepfather do to prevent it? He takes the obvious course of
2809𓀴keeping her at home and forbidding her to seek the company of people of
2810𓀴her own age. But soon he found that that would not answer forever. She
2811𓀴became restive, insisted upon her rights, and finally announced her
2812𓀴positive intention of going to a certain ball. What does her clever
2813𓀴stepfather do then? He conceives an idea more creditable to his head
2814𓀴than to his heart. With the connivance and assistance of his wife he
2815𓀴disguised himself, covered those keen eyes with tinted glasses, masked
2816𓀴the face with a moustache and a pair of bushy whiskers, sunk that clear
2817𓀴voice into an insinuating whisper, and doubly secure on account of the
2818𓀴girl’s short sight, he appears as Mr. Hosmer Angel, and keeps off other
2819𓀴lovers by making love himself.”
2820𓀴
2821𓀴“It was only a joke at first,” groaned our visitor. “We never thought
2822𓀴that she would have been so carried away.”
2823𓀴
2824𓀴“Very likely not. However that may be, the young lady was very
2825𓀴decidedly carried away, and, having quite made up her mind that her
2826𓀴stepfather was in France, the suspicion of treachery never for an
2827𓀴instant entered her mind. She was flattered by the gentleman’s
2828𓀴attentions, and the effect was increased by the loudly expressed
2829𓀴admiration of her mother. Then Mr. Angel began to call, for it was
2830𓀴obvious that the matter should be pushed as far as it would go if a
2831𓀴real effect were to be produced. There were meetings, and an
2832𓀴engagement, which would finally secure the girl’s affections from
2833𓀴turning towards anyone else. But the deception could not be kept up
2834𓀴forever. These pretended journeys to France were rather cumbrous. The
2835𓀴thing to do was clearly to bring the business to an end in such a
2836𓀴dramatic manner that it would leave a permanent impression upon the
2837𓀴young lady’s mind and prevent her from looking upon any other suitor
2838𓀴for some time to come. Hence those vows of fidelity exacted upon a
2839𓀴Testament, and hence also the allusions to a possibility of something
2840𓀴happening on the very morning of the wedding. James Windibank wished
2841𓀴Miss Sutherland to be so bound to Hosmer Angel, and so uncertain as to
2842𓀴his fate, that for ten years to come, at any rate, she would not listen
2843𓀴to another man. As far as the church door he brought her, and then, as
2844𓀴he could go no farther, he conveniently vanished away by the old trick
2845𓀴of stepping in at one door of a four-wheeler and out at the other. I
2846𓀴think that was the chain of events, Mr. Windibank!”
2847𓀴
2848𓀴Our visitor had recovered something of his assurance while Holmes had
2849𓀴been talking, and he rose from his chair now with a cold sneer upon his
2850𓀴pale face.
2851𓀴
2852𓀴“It may be so, or it may not, Mr. Holmes,” said he, “but if you are so
2853𓀴very sharp you ought to be sharp enough to know that it is you who are
2854𓀴breaking the law now, and not me. I have done nothing actionable from
2855𓀴the first, but as long as you keep that door locked you lay yourself
2856𓀴open to an action for assault and illegal constraint.”
2857𓀴
2858𓀴“The law cannot, as you say, touch you,” said Holmes, unlocking and
2859𓀴throwing open the door, “yet there never was a man who deserved
2860𓀴punishment more. If the young lady has a brother or a friend, he ought
2861𓀴to lay a whip across your shoulders. By Jove!” he continued, flushing
2862𓀴up at the sight of the bitter sneer upon the man’s face, “it is not
2863𓀴part of my duties to my client, but here’s a hunting crop handy, and I
2864𓀴think I shall just treat myself to—” He took two swift steps to the
2865𓀴whip, but before he could grasp it there was a wild clatter of steps
2866𓀴upon the stairs, the heavy hall door banged, and from the window we
2867𓀴could see Mr. James Windibank running at the top of his speed down the
2868𓀴road.
2869𓀴
2870𓀴“There’s a cold-blooded scoundrel!” said Holmes, laughing, as he threw
2871𓀴himself down into his chair once more. “That fellow will rise from
2872𓀴crime to crime until he does something very bad, and ends on a gallows.
2873𓀴The case has, in some respects, been not entirely devoid of interest.”
2874𓀴
2875𓀴“I cannot now entirely see all the steps of your reasoning,” I
2876𓀴remarked.
2877𓀴
2878𓀴“Well, of course it was obvious from the first that this Mr. Hosmer
2879𓀴Angel must have some strong object for his curious conduct, and it was
2880𓀴equally clear that the only man who really profited by the incident, as
2881𓀴far as we could see, was the stepfather. Then the fact that the two men
2882𓀴were never together, but that the one always appeared when the other
2883𓀴was away, was suggestive. So were the tinted spectacles and the curious
2884𓀴voice, which both hinted at a disguise, as did the bushy whiskers. My
2885𓀴suspicions were all confirmed by his peculiar action in typewriting his
2886𓀴signature, which, of course, inferred that his handwriting was so
2887𓀴familiar to her that she would recognise even the smallest sample of
2888𓀴it. You see all these isolated facts, together with many minor ones,
2889𓀴all pointed in the same direction.”
2890𓀴
2891𓀴“And how did you verify them?”
2892𓀴
2893𓀴“Having once spotted my man, it was easy to get corroboration. I knew
2894𓀴the firm for which this man worked. Having taken the printed
2895𓀴description. I eliminated everything from it which could be the result
2896𓀴of a disguise—the whiskers, the glasses, the voice, and I sent it to
2897𓀴the firm, with a request that they would inform me whether it answered
2898𓀴to the description of any of their travellers. I had already noticed
2899𓀴the peculiarities of the typewriter, and I wrote to the man himself at
2900𓀴his business address asking him if he would come here. As I expected,
2901𓀴his reply was typewritten and revealed the same trivial but
2902𓀴characteristic defects. The same post brought me a letter from
2903𓀴Westhouse & Marbank, of Fenchurch Street, to say that the description
2904𓀴tallied in every respect with that of their employé, James Windibank.
2905𓀴_Voilà tout_!”
2906𓀴
2907𓀴“And Miss Sutherland?”
2908𓀴
2909𓀴“If I tell her she will not believe me. You may remember the old
2910𓀴Persian saying, ‘There is danger for him who taketh the tiger cub, and
2911𓀴danger also for whoso snatches a delusion from a woman.’ There is as
2912𓀴much sense in Hafiz as in Horace, and as much knowledge of the world.”
2913𓀴
2914𓀴
2915𓀴
2916𓀴
2917𓀴IV. THE BOSCOMBE VALLEY MYSTERY
2918𓀴
2919𓀴
2920𓀴We were seated at breakfast one morning, my wife and I, when the maid
2921𓀴brought in a telegram. It was from Sherlock Holmes and ran in this way:
2922𓀴
2923𓀴“Have you a couple of days to spare? Have just been wired for from the
2924𓀴west of England in connection with Boscombe Valley tragedy. Shall be
2925𓀴glad if you will come with me. Air and scenery perfect. Leave
2926𓀴Paddington by the 11:15.”
2927𓀴
2928𓀴“What do you say, dear?” said my wife, looking across at me. “Will you
2929𓀴go?”
2930𓀴
2931𓀴“I really don’t know what to say. I have a fairly long list at
2932𓀴present.”
2933𓀴
2934𓀴“Oh, Anstruther would do your work for you. You have been looking a
2935𓀴little pale lately. I think that the change would do you good, and you
2936𓀴are always so interested in Mr. Sherlock Holmes’ cases.”
2937𓀴
2938𓀴“I should be ungrateful if I were not, seeing what I gained through one
2939𓀴of them,” I answered. “But if I am to go, I must pack at once, for I
2940𓀴have only half an hour.”
2941𓀴
2942𓀴My experience of camp life in Afghanistan had at least had the effect
2943𓀴of making me a prompt and ready traveller. My wants were few and
2944𓀴simple, so that in less than the time stated I was in a cab with my
2945𓀴valise, rattling away to Paddington Station. Sherlock Holmes was pacing
2946𓀴up and down the platform, his tall, gaunt figure made even gaunter and
2947𓀴taller by his long grey travelling-cloak and close-fitting cloth cap.
2948𓀴
2949𓀴“It is really very good of you to come, Watson,” said he. “It makes a
2950𓀴considerable difference to me, having someone with me on whom I can
2951𓀴thoroughly rely. Local aid is always either worthless or else biassed.
2952𓀴If you will keep the two corner seats I shall get the tickets.”
2953𓀴
2954𓀴We had the carriage to ourselves save for an immense litter of papers
2955𓀴which Holmes had brought with him. Among these he rummaged and read,
2956𓀴with intervals of note-taking and of meditation, until we were past
2957𓀴Reading. Then he suddenly rolled them all into a gigantic ball and
2958𓀴tossed them up onto the rack.
2959𓀴
2960𓀴“Have you heard anything of the case?” he asked.
2961𓀴
2962𓀴“Not a word. I have not seen a paper for some days.”
2963𓀴
2964𓀴“The London press has not had very full accounts. I have just been
2965𓀴looking through all the recent papers in order to master the
2966𓀴particulars. It seems, from what I gather, to be one of those simple
2967𓀴cases which are so extremely difficult.”
2968𓀴
2969𓀴“That sounds a little paradoxical.”
2970𓀴
2971𓀴“But it is profoundly true. Singularity is almost invariably a clue.
2972𓀴The more featureless and commonplace a crime is, the more difficult it
2973𓀴is to bring it home. In this case, however, they have established a
2974𓀴very serious case against the son of the murdered man.”
2975𓀴
2976𓀴“It is a murder, then?”
2977𓀴
2978𓀴“Well, it is conjectured to be so. I shall take nothing for granted
2979𓀴until I have the opportunity of looking personally into it. I will
2980𓀴explain the state of things to you, as far as I have been able to
2981𓀴understand it, in a very few words.
2982𓀴
2983𓀴“Boscombe Valley is a country district not very far from Ross, in
2984𓀴Herefordshire. The largest landed proprietor in that part is a Mr. John
2985𓀴Turner, who made his money in Australia and returned some years ago to
2986𓀴the old country. One of the farms which he held, that of Hatherley, was
2987𓀴let to Mr. Charles McCarthy, who was also an ex-Australian. The men had
2988𓀴known each other in the colonies, so that it was not unnatural that
2989𓀴when they came to settle down they should do so as near each other as
2990𓀴possible. Turner was apparently the richer man, so McCarthy became his
2991𓀴tenant but still remained, it seems, upon terms of perfect equality, as
2992𓀴they were frequently together. McCarthy had one son, a lad of eighteen,
2993𓀴and Turner had an only daughter of the same age, but neither of them
2994𓀴had wives living. They appear to have avoided the society of the
2995𓀴neighbouring English families and to have led retired lives, though
2996𓀴both the McCarthys were fond of sport and were frequently seen at the
2997𓀴race-meetings of the neighbourhood. McCarthy kept two servants—a man
2998𓀴and a girl. Turner had a considerable household, some half-dozen at the
2999𓀴least. That is as much as I have been able to gather about the
3000𓀴families. Now for the facts.
3001𓀴
3002𓀴“On June 3rd, that is, on Monday last, McCarthy left his house at
3003𓀴Hatherley about three in the afternoon and walked down to the Boscombe
3004𓀴Pool, which is a small lake formed by the spreading out of the stream
3005𓀴which runs down the Boscombe Valley. He had been out with his
3006𓀴serving-man in the morning at Ross, and he had told the man that he
3007𓀴must hurry, as he had an appointment of importance to keep at three.
3008𓀴From that appointment he never came back alive.
3009𓀴
3010𓀴“From Hatherley Farmhouse to the Boscombe Pool is a quarter of a mile,
3011𓀴and two people saw him as he passed over this ground. One was an old
3012𓀴woman, whose name is not mentioned, and the other was William Crowder,
3013𓀴a game-keeper in the employ of Mr. Turner. Both these witnesses depose
3014𓀴that Mr. McCarthy was walking alone. The game-keeper adds that within a
3015𓀴few minutes of his seeing Mr. McCarthy pass he had seen his son, Mr.
3016𓀴James McCarthy, going the same way with a gun under his arm. To the
3017𓀴best of his belief, the father was actually in sight at the time, and
3018𓀴the son was following him. He thought no more of the matter until he
3019𓀴heard in the evening of the tragedy that had occurred.
3020𓀴
3021𓀴“The two McCarthys were seen after the time when William Crowder, the
3022𓀴game-keeper, lost sight of them. The Boscombe Pool is thickly wooded
3023𓀴round, with just a fringe of grass and of reeds round the edge. A girl
3024𓀴of fourteen, Patience Moran, who is the daughter of the lodge-keeper of
3025𓀴the Boscombe Valley estate, was in one of the woods picking flowers.
3026𓀴She states that while she was there she saw, at the border of the wood
3027𓀴and close by the lake, Mr. McCarthy and his son, and that they appeared
3028𓀴to be having a violent quarrel. She heard Mr. McCarthy the elder using
3029𓀴very strong language to his son, and she saw the latter raise up his
3030𓀴hand as if to strike his father. She was so frightened by their
3031𓀴violence that she ran away and told her mother when she reached home
3032𓀴that she had left the two McCarthys quarrelling near Boscombe Pool, and
3033𓀴that she was afraid that they were going to fight. She had hardly said
3034𓀴the words when young Mr. McCarthy came running up to the lodge to say
3035𓀴that he had found his father dead in the wood, and to ask for the help
3036𓀴of the lodge-keeper. He was much excited, without either his gun or his
3037𓀴hat, and his right hand and sleeve were observed to be stained with
3038𓀴fresh blood. On following him they found the dead body stretched out
3039𓀴upon the grass beside the pool. The head had been beaten in by repeated
3040𓀴blows of some heavy and blunt weapon. The injuries were such as might
3041𓀴very well have been inflicted by the butt-end of his son’s gun, which
3042𓀴was found lying on the grass within a few paces of the body. Under
3043𓀴these circumstances the young man was instantly arrested, and a verdict
3044𓀴of ‘wilful murder’ having been returned at the inquest on Tuesday, he
3045𓀴was on Wednesday brought before the magistrates at Ross, who have
3046𓀴referred the case to the next Assizes. Those are the main facts of the
3047𓀴case as they came out before the coroner and the police-court.”
3048𓀴
3049𓀴“I could hardly imagine a more damning case,” I remarked. “If ever
3050𓀴circumstantial evidence pointed to a criminal it does so here.”
3051𓀴
3052𓀴“Circumstantial evidence is a very tricky thing,” answered Holmes
3053𓀴thoughtfully. “It may seem to point very straight to one thing, but if
3054𓀴you shift your own point of view a little, you may find it pointing in
3055𓀴an equally uncompromising manner to something entirely different. It
3056𓀴must be confessed, however, that the case looks exceedingly grave
3057𓀴against the young man, and it is very possible that he is indeed the
3058𓀴culprit. There are several people in the neighbourhood, however, and
3059𓀴among them Miss Turner, the daughter of the neighbouring landowner, who
3060𓀴believe in his innocence, and who have retained Lestrade, whom you may
3061𓀴recollect in connection with the Study in Scarlet, to work out the case
3062𓀴in his interest. Lestrade, being rather puzzled, has referred the case
3063𓀴to me, and hence it is that two middle-aged gentlemen are flying
3064𓀴westward at fifty miles an hour instead of quietly digesting their
3065𓀴breakfasts at home.”
3066𓀴
3067𓀴“I am afraid,” said I, “that the facts are so obvious that you will
3068𓀴find little credit to be gained out of this case.”
3069𓀴
3070𓀴“There is nothing more deceptive than an obvious fact,” he answered,
3071𓀴laughing. “Besides, we may chance to hit upon some other obvious facts
3072𓀴which may have been by no means obvious to Mr. Lestrade. You know me
3073𓀴too well to think that I am boasting when I say that I shall either
3074𓀴confirm or destroy his theory by means which he is quite incapable of
3075𓀴employing, or even of understanding. To take the first example to hand,
3076𓀴I very clearly perceive that in your bedroom the window is upon the
3077𓀴right-hand side, and yet I question whether Mr. Lestrade would have
3078𓀴noted even so self-evident a thing as that.”
3079𓀴
3080𓀴“How on earth—”
3081𓀴
3082𓀴“My dear fellow, I know you well. I know the military neatness which
3083𓀴characterises you. You shave every morning, and in this season you
3084𓀴shave by the sunlight; but since your shaving is less and less complete
3085𓀴as we get farther back on the left side, until it becomes positively
3086𓀴slovenly as we get round the angle of the jaw, it is surely very clear
3087𓀴that that side is less illuminated than the other. I could not imagine
3088𓀴a man of your habits looking at himself in an equal light and being
3089𓀴satisfied with such a result. I only quote this as a trivial example of
3090𓀴observation and inference. Therein lies my _métier_, and it is just
3091𓀴possible that it may be of some service in the investigation which lies
3092𓀴before us. There are one or two minor points which were brought out in
3093𓀴the inquest, and which are worth considering.”
3094𓀴
3095𓀴“What are they?”
3096𓀴
3097𓀴“It appears that his arrest did not take place at once, but after the
3098𓀴return to Hatherley Farm. On the inspector of constabulary informing
3099𓀴him that he was a prisoner, he remarked that he was not surprised to
3100𓀴hear it, and that it was no more than his deserts. This observation of
3101𓀴his had the natural effect of removing any traces of doubt which might
3102𓀴have remained in the minds of the coroner’s jury.”
3103𓀴
3104𓀴“It was a confession,” I ejaculated.
3105𓀴
3106𓀴“No, for it was followed by a protestation of innocence.”
3107𓀴
3108𓀴“Coming on the top of such a damning series of events, it was at least
3109𓀴a most suspicious remark.”
3110𓀴
3111𓀴“On the contrary,” said Holmes, “it is the brightest rift which I can
3112𓀴at present see in the clouds. However innocent he might be, he could
3113𓀴not be such an absolute imbecile as not to see that the circumstances
3114𓀴were very black against him. Had he appeared surprised at his own
3115𓀴arrest, or feigned indignation at it, I should have looked upon it as
3116𓀴highly suspicious, because such surprise or anger would not be natural
3117𓀴under the circumstances, and yet might appear to be the best policy to
3118𓀴a scheming man. His frank acceptance of the situation marks him as
3119𓀴either an innocent man, or else as a man of considerable self-restraint
3120𓀴and firmness. As to his remark about his deserts, it was also not
3121𓀴unnatural if you consider that he stood beside the dead body of his
3122𓀴father, and that there is no doubt that he had that very day so far
3123𓀴forgotten his filial duty as to bandy words with him, and even,
3124𓀴according to the little girl whose evidence is so important, to raise
3125𓀴his hand as if to strike him. The self-reproach and contrition which
3126𓀴are displayed in his remark appear to me to be the signs of a healthy
3127𓀴mind rather than of a guilty one.”
3128𓀴
3129𓀴I shook my head. “Many men have been hanged on far slighter evidence,”
3130𓀴I remarked.
3131𓀴
3132𓀴“So they have. And many men have been wrongfully hanged.”
3133𓀴
3134𓀴“What is the young man’s own account of the matter?”
3135𓀴
3136𓀴“It is, I am afraid, not very encouraging to his supporters, though
3137𓀴there are one or two points in it which are suggestive. You will find
3138𓀴it here, and may read it for yourself.”
3139𓀴
3140𓀴He picked out from his bundle a copy of the local Herefordshire paper,
3141𓀴and having turned down the sheet he pointed out the paragraph in which
3142𓀴the unfortunate young man had given his own statement of what had
3143𓀴occurred. I settled myself down in the corner of the carriage and read
3144𓀴it very carefully. It ran in this way:
3145𓀴
3146𓀴“Mr. James McCarthy, the only son of the deceased, was then called and
3147𓀴gave evidence as follows: ‘I had been away from home for three days at
3148𓀴Bristol, and had only just returned upon the morning of last Monday,
3149𓀴the 3rd. My father was absent from home at the time of my arrival, and
3150𓀴I was informed by the maid that he had driven over to Ross with John
3151𓀴Cobb, the groom. Shortly after my return I heard the wheels of his trap
3152𓀴in the yard, and, looking out of my window, I saw him get out and walk
3153𓀴rapidly out of the yard, though I was not aware in which direction he
3154𓀴was going. I then took my gun and strolled out in the direction of the
3155𓀴Boscombe Pool, with the intention of visiting the rabbit warren which
3156𓀴is upon the other side. On my way I saw William Crowder, the
3157𓀴game-keeper, as he had stated in his evidence; but he is mistaken in
3158𓀴thinking that I was following my father. I had no idea that he was in
3159𓀴front of me. When about a hundred yards from the pool I heard a cry of
3160𓀴“Cooee!” which was a usual signal between my father and myself. I then
3161𓀴hurried forward, and found him standing by the pool. He appeared to be
3162𓀴much surprised at seeing me and asked me rather roughly what I was
3163𓀴doing there. A conversation ensued which led to high words and almost
3164𓀴to blows, for my father was a man of a very violent temper. Seeing that
3165𓀴his passion was becoming ungovernable, I left him and returned towards
3166𓀴Hatherley Farm. I had not gone more than 150 yards, however, when I
3167𓀴heard a hideous outcry behind me, which caused me to run back again. I
3168𓀴found my father expiring upon the ground, with his head terribly
3169𓀴injured. I dropped my gun and held him in my arms, but he almost
3170𓀴instantly expired. I knelt beside him for some minutes, and then made
3171𓀴my way to Mr. Turner’s lodge-keeper, his house being the nearest, to
3172𓀴ask for assistance. I saw no one near my father when I returned, and I
3173𓀴have no idea how he came by his injuries. He was not a popular man,
3174𓀴being somewhat cold and forbidding in his manners, but he had, as far
3175𓀴as I know, no active enemies. I know nothing further of the matter.’
3176𓀴
3177𓀴“The Coroner: Did your father make any statement to you before he died?
3178𓀴
3179𓀴“Witness: He mumbled a few words, but I could only catch some allusion
3180𓀴to a rat.
3181𓀴
3182𓀴“The Coroner: What did you understand by that?
3183𓀴
3184𓀴“Witness: It conveyed no meaning to me. I thought that he was
3185𓀴delirious.
3186𓀴
3187𓀴“The Coroner: What was the point upon which you and your father had
3188𓀴this final quarrel?
3189𓀴
3190𓀴“Witness: I should prefer not to answer.
3191𓀴
3192𓀴“The Coroner: I am afraid that I must press it.
3193𓀴
3194𓀴“Witness: It is really impossible for me to tell you. I can assure you
3195𓀴that it has nothing to do with the sad tragedy which followed.
3196𓀴
3197𓀴“The Coroner: That is for the court to decide. I need not point out to
3198𓀴you that your refusal to answer will prejudice your case considerably
3199𓀴in any future proceedings which may arise.
3200𓀴
3201𓀴“Witness: I must still refuse.
3202𓀴
3203𓀴“The Coroner: I understand that the cry of ‘Cooee’ was a common signal
3204𓀴between you and your father?
3205𓀴
3206𓀴“Witness: It was.
3207𓀴
3208𓀴“The Coroner: How was it, then, that he uttered it before he saw you,
3209𓀴and before he even knew that you had returned from Bristol?
3210𓀴
3211𓀴“Witness (with considerable confusion): I do not know.
3212𓀴
3213𓀴“A Juryman: Did you see nothing which aroused your suspicions when you
3214𓀴returned on hearing the cry and found your father fatally injured?
3215𓀴
3216𓀴“Witness: Nothing definite.
3217𓀴
3218𓀴“The Coroner: What do you mean?
3219𓀴
3220𓀴“Witness: I was so disturbed and excited as I rushed out into the open,
3221𓀴that I could think of nothing except of my father. Yet I have a vague
3222𓀴impression that as I ran forward something lay upon the ground to the
3223𓀴left of me. It seemed to me to be something grey in colour, a coat of
3224𓀴some sort, or a plaid perhaps. When I rose from my father I looked
3225𓀴round for it, but it was gone.
3226𓀴
3227𓀴“‘Do you mean that it disappeared before you went for help?’
3228𓀴
3229𓀴“‘Yes, it was gone.’
3230𓀴
3231𓀴“‘You cannot say what it was?’
3232𓀴
3233𓀴“‘No, I had a feeling something was there.’
3234𓀴
3235𓀴“‘How far from the body?’
3236𓀴
3237𓀴“‘A dozen yards or so.’
3238𓀴
3239𓀴“‘And how far from the edge of the wood?’
3240𓀴
3241𓀴“‘About the same.’
3242𓀴
3243𓀴“‘Then if it was removed it was while you were within a dozen yards of
3244𓀴it?’
3245𓀴
3246𓀴“‘Yes, but with my back towards it.’
3247𓀴
3248𓀴“This concluded the examination of the witness.”
3249𓀴
3250𓀴“I see,” said I as I glanced down the column, “that the coroner in his
3251𓀴concluding remarks was rather severe upon young McCarthy. He calls
3252𓀴attention, and with reason, to the discrepancy about his father having
3253𓀴signalled to him before seeing him, also to his refusal to give details
3254𓀴of his conversation with his father, and his singular account of his
3255𓀴father’s dying words. They are all, as he remarks, very much against
3256𓀴the son.”
3257𓀴
3258𓀴Holmes laughed softly to himself and stretched himself out upon the
3259𓀴cushioned seat. “Both you and the coroner have been at some pains,”
3260𓀴said he, “to single out the very strongest points in the young man’s
3261𓀴favour. Don’t you see that you alternately give him credit for having
3262𓀴too much imagination and too little? Too little, if he could not invent
3263𓀴a cause of quarrel which would give him the sympathy of the jury; too
3264𓀴much, if he evolved from his own inner consciousness anything so
3265𓀴_outré_ as a dying reference to a rat, and the incident of the
3266𓀴vanishing cloth. No, sir, I shall approach this case from the point of
3267𓀴view that what this young man says is true, and we shall see whither
3268𓀴that hypothesis will lead us. And now here is my pocket Petrarch, and
3269𓀴not another word shall I say of this case until we are on the scene of
3270𓀴action. We lunch at Swindon, and I see that we shall be there in twenty
3271𓀴minutes.”
3272𓀴
3273𓀴It was nearly four o’clock when we at last, after passing through the
3274𓀴beautiful Stroud Valley, and over the broad gleaming Severn, found
3275𓀴ourselves at the pretty little country-town of Ross. A lean,
3276𓀴ferret-like man, furtive and sly-looking, was waiting for us upon the
3277𓀴platform. In spite of the light brown dustcoat and leather-leggings
3278𓀴which he wore in deference to his rustic surroundings, I had no
3279𓀴difficulty in recognising Lestrade, of Scotland Yard. With him we drove
3280𓀴to the Hereford Arms where a room had already been engaged for us.
3281𓀴
3282𓀴“I have ordered a carriage,” said Lestrade as we sat over a cup of tea.
3283𓀴“I knew your energetic nature, and that you would not be happy until
3284𓀴you had been on the scene of the crime.”
3285𓀴
3286𓀴“It was very nice and complimentary of you,” Holmes answered. “It is
3287𓀴entirely a question of barometric pressure.”
3288𓀴
3289𓀴Lestrade looked startled. “I do not quite follow,” he said.
3290𓀴
3291𓀴“How is the glass? Twenty-nine, I see. No wind, and not a cloud in the
3292𓀴sky. I have a caseful of cigarettes here which need smoking, and the
3293𓀴sofa is very much superior to the usual country hotel abomination. I do
3294𓀴not think that it is probable that I shall use the carriage to-night.”
3295𓀴
3296𓀴Lestrade laughed indulgently. “You have, no doubt, already formed your
3297𓀴conclusions from the newspapers,” he said. “The case is as plain as a
3298𓀴pikestaff, and the more one goes into it the plainer it becomes. Still,
3299𓀴of course, one can’t refuse a lady, and such a very positive one, too.
3300𓀴She has heard of you, and would have your opinion, though I repeatedly
3301𓀴told her that there was nothing which you could do which I had not
3302𓀴already done. Why, bless my soul! here is her carriage at the door.”
3303𓀴
3304𓀴He had hardly spoken before there rushed into the room one of the most
3305𓀴lovely young women that I have ever seen in my life. Her violet eyes
3306𓀴shining, her lips parted, a pink flush upon her cheeks, all thought of
3307𓀴her natural reserve lost in her overpowering excitement and concern.
3308𓀴

3310𓀴us, and finally, with a woman’s quick intuition, fastening upon my

3312𓀴you so. I know that James didn’t do it. I know it, and I want you to
3313𓀴start upon your work knowing it, too. Never let yourself doubt upon
3314𓀴that point. We have known each other since we were little children, and
3315𓀴I know his faults as no one else does; but he is too tender-hearted to
3316𓀴hurt a fly. Such a charge is absurd to anyone who really knows him.”
3317𓀴
3318𓀴“I hope we may clear him, Miss Turner,” said Sherlock Holmes. “You may
3319𓀴rely upon my doing all that I can.”
3320𓀴
3321𓀴“But you have read the evidence. You have formed some conclusion? Do
3322𓀴you not see some loophole, some flaw? Do you not yourself think that he
3323𓀴is innocent?”
3324𓀴
3325𓀴“I think that it is very probable.”
3326𓀴
3327𓀴“There, now!” she cried, throwing back her head and looking defiantly
3328𓀴at Lestrade. “You hear! He gives me hopes.”
3329𓀴
3330𓀴Lestrade shrugged his shoulders. “I am afraid that my colleague has
3331𓀴been a little quick in forming his conclusions,” he said.
3332𓀴
3333𓀴“But he is right. Oh! I know that he is right. James never did it. And
3334𓀴about his quarrel with his father, I am sure that the reason why he
3335𓀴would not speak about it to the coroner was because I was concerned in
3336𓀴it.”
3337𓀴
3338𓀴“In what way?” asked Holmes.
3339𓀴
3340𓀴“It is no time for me to hide anything. James and his father had many
3341𓀴disagreements about me. Mr. McCarthy was very anxious that there should
3342𓀴be a marriage between us. James and I have always loved each other as
3343𓀴brother and sister; but of course he is young and has seen very little
3344𓀴of life yet, and—and—well, he naturally did not wish to do anything
3345𓀴like that yet. So there were quarrels, and this, I am sure, was one of
3346𓀴them.”
3347𓀴

3349𓀴
3350𓀴“No, he was averse to it also. No one but Mr. McCarthy was in favour of

3353𓀴

3355𓀴call to-morrow?”
3356𓀴
3357𓀴“I am afraid the doctor won’t allow it.”
3358𓀴
3359𓀴“The doctor?”
3360𓀴
3361𓀴“Yes, have you not heard? Poor father has never been strong for years
3362𓀴back, but this has broken him down completely. He has taken to his bed,
3363𓀴and Dr. Willows says that he is a wreck and that his nervous system is
3364𓀴shattered. Mr. McCarthy was the only man alive who had known dad in the
3365𓀴old days in Victoria.”
3366𓀴
3367𓀴“Ha! In Victoria! That is important.”
3368𓀴
3369𓀴“Yes, at the mines.”
3370𓀴
3371𓀴“Quite so; at the gold-mines, where, as I understand, Mr. Turner made
3372𓀴his money.”
3373𓀴
3374𓀴“Yes, certainly.”
3375𓀴
3376𓀴“Thank you, Miss Turner. You have been of material assistance to me.”
3377𓀴

3379𓀴to the prison to see James. Oh, if you do, Mr. Holmes, do tell him that
3380𓀴I know him to be innocent.”
3381𓀴

3383𓀴
3384𓀴“I must go home now, for dad is very ill, and he misses me so if I
3385𓀴leave him. Good-bye, and God help you in your undertaking.” She hurried
3386𓀴from the room as impulsively as she had entered, and we heard the

3388𓀴
3389𓀴“I am ashamed of you, Holmes,” said Lestrade with dignity after a few
3390𓀴minutes’ silence. “Why should you raise up hopes which you are bound to
3391𓀴disappoint? I am not over-tender of heart, but I call it cruel.”
3392𓀴
3393𓀴“I think that I see my way to clearing James McCarthy,” said Holmes.
3394𓀴“Have you an order to see him in prison?”
3395𓀴
3396𓀴“Yes, but only for you and me.”
3397𓀴
3398𓀴“Then I shall reconsider my resolution about going out. We have still
3399𓀴time to take a train to Hereford and see him to-night?”
3400𓀴
3401𓀴“Ample.”
3402𓀴
3403𓀴“Then let us do so. Watson, I fear that you will find it very slow, but
3404𓀴I shall only be away a couple of hours.”
3405𓀴
3406𓀴I walked down to the station with them, and then wandered through the
3407𓀴streets of the little town, finally returning to the hotel, where I lay
3408𓀴upon the sofa and tried to interest myself in a yellow-backed novel.
3409𓀴The puny plot of the story was so thin, however, when compared to the
3410𓀴deep mystery through which we were groping, and I found my attention
3411𓀴wander so continually from the action to the fact, that I at last flung
3412𓀴it across the room and gave myself up entirely to a consideration of
3413𓀴the events of the day. Supposing that this unhappy young man’s story
3414𓀴were absolutely true, then what hellish thing, what absolutely
3415𓀴unforeseen and extraordinary calamity could have occurred between the
3416𓀴time when he parted from his father, and the moment when, drawn back by
3417𓀴his screams, he rushed into the glade? It was something terrible and
3418𓀴deadly. What could it be? Might not the nature of the injuries reveal
3419𓀴something to my medical instincts? I rang the bell and called for the
3420𓀴weekly county paper, which contained a verbatim account of the inquest.
3421𓀴In the surgeon’s deposition it was stated that the posterior third of
3422𓀴the left parietal bone and the left half of the occipital bone had been
3423𓀴shattered by a heavy blow from a blunt weapon. I marked the spot upon
3424𓀴my own head. Clearly such a blow must have been struck from behind.
3425𓀴That was to some extent in favour of the accused, as when seen
3426𓀴quarrelling he was face to face with his father. Still, it did not go
3427𓀴for very much, for the older man might have turned his back before the
3428𓀴blow fell. Still, it might be worth while to call Holmes’ attention to
3429𓀴it. Then there was the peculiar dying reference to a rat. What could
3430𓀴that mean? It could not be delirium. A man dying from a sudden blow
3431𓀴does not commonly become delirious. No, it was more likely to be an
3432𓀴attempt to explain how he met his fate. But what could it indicate? I
3433𓀴cudgelled my brains to find some possible explanation. And then the
3434𓀴incident of the grey cloth seen by young McCarthy. If that were true
3435𓀴the murderer must have dropped some part of his dress, presumably his
3436𓀴overcoat, in his flight, and must have had the hardihood to return and
3437𓀴to carry it away at the instant when the son was kneeling with his back
3438𓀴turned not a dozen paces off. What a tissue of mysteries and
3439𓀴improbabilities the whole thing was! I did not wonder at Lestrade’s
3440𓀴opinion, and yet I had so much faith in Sherlock Holmes’ insight that I
3441𓀴could not lose hope as long as every fresh fact seemed to strengthen
3442𓀴his conviction of young McCarthy’s innocence.
3443𓀴
3444𓀴It was late before Sherlock Holmes returned. He came back alone, for
3445𓀴Lestrade was staying in lodgings in the town.
3446𓀴
3447𓀴“The glass still keeps very high,” he remarked as he sat down. “It is
3448𓀴of importance that it should not rain before we are able to go over the
3449𓀴ground. On the other hand, a man should be at his very best and keenest
3450𓀴for such nice work as that, and I did not wish to do it when fagged by
3451𓀴a long journey. I have seen young McCarthy.”
3452𓀴
3453𓀴“And what did you learn from him?”
3454𓀴
3455𓀴“Nothing.”
3456𓀴
3457𓀴“Could he throw no light?”
3458𓀴
3459𓀴“None at all. I was inclined to think at one time that he knew who had
3460𓀴done it and was screening him or her, but I am convinced now that he is
3461𓀴as puzzled as everyone else. He is not a very quick-witted youth,
3462𓀴though comely to look at and, I should think, sound at heart.”
3463𓀴
3464𓀴“I cannot admire his taste,” I remarked, “if it is indeed a fact that
3465𓀴he was averse to a marriage with so charming a young lady as this Miss
3466𓀴Turner.”
3467𓀴
3468𓀴“Ah, thereby hangs a rather painful tale. This fellow is madly,
3469𓀴insanely, in love with her, but some two years ago, when he was only a
3470𓀴lad, and before he really knew her, for she had been away five years at
3471𓀴a boarding-school, what does the idiot do but get into the clutches of
3472𓀴a barmaid in Bristol and marry her at a registry office? No one knows a
3473𓀴word of the matter, but you can imagine how maddening it must be to him
3474𓀴to be upbraided for not doing what he would give his very eyes to do,
3475𓀴but what he knows to be absolutely impossible. It was sheer frenzy of
3476𓀴this sort which made him throw his hands up into the air when his
3477𓀴father, at their last interview, was goading him on to propose to Miss
3478𓀴Turner. On the other hand, he had no means of supporting himself, and
3479𓀴his father, who was by all accounts a very hard man, would have thrown
3480𓀴him over utterly had he known the truth. It was with his barmaid wife
3481𓀴that he had spent the last three days in Bristol, and his father did
3482𓀴not know where he was. Mark that point. It is of importance. Good has
3483𓀴come out of evil, however, for the barmaid, finding from the papers
3484𓀴that he is in serious trouble and likely to be hanged, has thrown him
3485𓀴over utterly and has written to him to say that she has a husband
3486𓀴already in the Bermuda Dockyard, so that there is really no tie between
3487𓀴them. I think that that bit of news has consoled young McCarthy for all
3488𓀴that he has suffered.”
3489𓀴
3490𓀴“But if he is innocent, who has done it?”
3491𓀴
3492𓀴“Ah! who? I would call your attention very particularly to two points.
3493𓀴One is that the murdered man had an appointment with someone at the
3494𓀴pool, and that the someone could not have been his son, for his son was
3495𓀴away, and he did not know when he would return. The second is that the
3496𓀴murdered man was heard to cry ‘Cooee!’ before he knew that his son had
3497𓀴returned. Those are the crucial points upon which the case depends. And
3498𓀴now let us talk about George Meredith, if you please, and we shall
3499𓀴leave all minor matters until to-morrow.”
3500𓀴
3501𓀴There was no rain, as Holmes had foretold, and the morning broke bright
3502𓀴and cloudless. At nine o’clock Lestrade called for us with the
3503𓀴carriage, and we set off for Hatherley Farm and the Boscombe Pool.
3504𓀴
3505𓀴“There is serious news this morning,” Lestrade observed. “It is said
3506𓀴that Mr. Turner, of the Hall, is so ill that his life is despaired of.”
3507𓀴
3508𓀴“An elderly man, I presume?” said Holmes.
3509𓀴
3510𓀴“About sixty; but his constitution has been shattered by his life
3511𓀴abroad, and he has been in failing health for some time. This business
3512𓀴has had a very bad effect upon him. He was an old friend of McCarthy’s,
3513𓀴and, I may add, a great benefactor to him, for I have learned that he
3514𓀴gave him Hatherley Farm rent free.”
3515𓀴
3516𓀴“Indeed! That is interesting,” said Holmes.
3517𓀴
3518𓀴“Oh, yes! In a hundred other ways he has helped him. Everybody about
3519𓀴here speaks of his kindness to him.”
3520𓀴
3521𓀴“Really! Does it not strike you as a little singular that this
3522𓀴McCarthy, who appears to have had little of his own, and to have been
3523𓀴under such obligations to Turner, should still talk of marrying his son
3524𓀴to Turner’s daughter, who is, presumably, heiress to the estate, and
3525𓀴that in such a very cocksure manner, as if it were merely a case of a
3526𓀴proposal and all else would follow? It is the more strange, since we
3527𓀴know that Turner himself was averse to the idea. The daughter told us
3528𓀴as much. Do you not deduce something from that?”
3529𓀴
3530𓀴“We have got to the deductions and the inferences,” said Lestrade,
3531𓀴winking at me. “I find it hard enough to tackle facts, Holmes, without
3532𓀴flying away after theories and fancies.”
3533𓀴
3534𓀴“You are right,” said Holmes demurely; “you do find it very hard to
3535𓀴tackle the facts.”
3536𓀴
3537𓀴“Anyhow, I have grasped one fact which you seem to find it difficult to
3538𓀴get hold of,” replied Lestrade with some warmth.
3539𓀴
3540𓀴“And that is—”
3541𓀴
3542𓀴“That McCarthy senior met his death from McCarthy junior and that all
3543𓀴theories to the contrary are the merest moonshine.”
3544𓀴
3545𓀴“Well, moonshine is a brighter thing than fog,” said Holmes, laughing.
3546𓀴“But I am very much mistaken if this is not Hatherley Farm upon the
3547𓀴left.”
3548𓀴
3549𓀴“Yes, that is it.” It was a widespread, comfortable-looking building,
3550𓀴two-storied, slate-roofed, with great yellow blotches of lichen upon
3551𓀴the grey walls. The drawn blinds and the smokeless chimneys, however,
3552𓀴gave it a stricken look, as though the weight of this horror still lay
3553𓀴heavy upon it. We called at the door, when the maid, at Holmes’
3554𓀴request, showed us the boots which her master wore at the time of his
3555𓀴death, and also a pair of the son’s, though not the pair which he had
3556𓀴then had. Having measured these very carefully from seven or eight
3557𓀴different points, Holmes desired to be led to the court-yard, from
3558𓀴which we all followed the winding track which led to Boscombe Pool.
3559𓀴
3560𓀴Sherlock Holmes was transformed when he was hot upon such a scent as
3561𓀴this. Men who had only known the quiet thinker and logician of Baker
3562𓀴Street would have failed to recognise him. His face flushed and
3563𓀴darkened. His brows were drawn into two hard black lines, while his
3564𓀴eyes shone out from beneath them with a steely glitter. His face was
3565𓀴bent downward, his shoulders bowed, his lips compressed, and the veins
3566𓀴stood out like whipcord in his long, sinewy neck. His nostrils seemed
3567𓀴to dilate with a purely animal lust for the chase, and his mind was so
3568𓀴absolutely concentrated upon the matter before him that a question or
3569𓀴remark fell unheeded upon his ears, or, at the most, only provoked a
3570𓀴quick, impatient snarl in reply. Swiftly and silently he made his way
3571𓀴along the track which ran through the meadows, and so by way of the
3572𓀴woods to the Boscombe Pool. It was damp, marshy ground, as is all that
3573𓀴district, and there were marks of many feet, both upon the path and
3574𓀴amid the short grass which bounded it on either side. Sometimes Holmes
3575𓀴would hurry on, sometimes stop dead, and once he made quite a little
3576𓀴detour into the meadow. Lestrade and I walked behind him, the detective
3577𓀴indifferent and contemptuous, while I watched my friend with the
3578𓀴interest which sprang from the conviction that every one of his actions
3579𓀴was directed towards a definite end.
3580𓀴
3581𓀴The Boscombe Pool, which is a little reed-girt sheet of water some
3582𓀴fifty yards across, is situated at the boundary between the Hatherley
3583𓀴Farm and the private park of the wealthy Mr. Turner. Above the woods
3584𓀴which lined it upon the farther side we could see the red, jutting
3585𓀴pinnacles which marked the site of the rich landowner’s dwelling. On
3586𓀴the Hatherley side of the pool the woods grew very thick, and there was
3587𓀴a narrow belt of sodden grass twenty paces across between the edge of
3588𓀴the trees and the reeds which lined the lake. Lestrade showed us the
3589𓀴exact spot at which the body had been found, and, indeed, so moist was
3590𓀴the ground, that I could plainly see the traces which had been left by
3591𓀴the fall of the stricken man. To Holmes, as I could see by his eager
3592𓀴face and peering eyes, very many other things were to be read upon the
3593𓀴trampled grass. He ran round, like a dog who is picking up a scent, and
3594𓀴then turned upon my companion.
3595𓀴
3596𓀴“What did you go into the pool for?” he asked.
3597𓀴
3598𓀴“I fished about with a rake. I thought there might be some weapon or
3599𓀴other trace. But how on earth—”
3600𓀴
3601𓀴“Oh, tut, tut! I have no time! That left foot of yours with its inward
3602𓀴twist is all over the place. A mole could trace it, and there it
3603𓀴vanishes among the reeds. Oh, how simple it would all have been had I
3604𓀴been here before they came like a herd of buffalo and wallowed all over
3605𓀴it. Here is where the party with the lodge-keeper came, and they have
3606𓀴covered all tracks for six or eight feet round the body. But here are
3607𓀴three separate tracks of the same feet.” He drew out a lens and lay
3608𓀴down upon his waterproof to have a better view, talking all the time
3609𓀴rather to himself than to us. “These are young McCarthy’s feet. Twice
3610𓀴he was walking, and once he ran swiftly, so that the soles are deeply
3611𓀴marked and the heels hardly visible. That bears out his story. He ran
3612𓀴when he saw his father on the ground. Then here are the father’s feet
3613𓀴as he paced up and down. What is this, then? It is the butt-end of the
3614𓀴gun as the son stood listening. And this? Ha, ha! What have we here?
3615𓀴Tiptoes! tiptoes! Square, too, quite unusual boots! They come, they go,
3616𓀴they come again—of course that was for the cloak. Now where did they
3617𓀴come from?” He ran up and down, sometimes losing, sometimes finding the
3618𓀴track until we were well within the edge of the wood and under the
3619𓀴shadow of a great beech, the largest tree in the neighbourhood. Holmes
3620𓀴traced his way to the farther side of this and lay down once more upon
3621𓀴his face with a little cry of satisfaction. For a long time he remained
3622𓀴there, turning over the leaves and dried sticks, gathering up what
3623𓀴seemed to me to be dust into an envelope and examining with his lens
3624𓀴not only the ground but even the bark of the tree as far as he could
3625𓀴reach. A jagged stone was lying among the moss, and this also he
3626𓀴carefully examined and retained. Then he followed a pathway through the
3627𓀴wood until he came to the high road, where all traces were lost.
3628𓀴
3629𓀴“It has been a case of considerable interest,” he remarked, returning
3630𓀴to his natural manner. “I fancy that this grey house on the right must
3631𓀴be the lodge. I think that I will go in and have a word with Moran, and
3632𓀴perhaps write a little note. Having done that, we may drive back to our
3633𓀴luncheon. You may walk to the cab, and I shall be with you presently.”
3634𓀴
3635𓀴It was about ten minutes before we regained our cab and drove back into
3636𓀴Ross, Holmes still carrying with him the stone which he had picked up
3637𓀴in the wood.
3638𓀴
3639𓀴“This may interest you, Lestrade,” he remarked, holding it out. “The
3640𓀴murder was done with it.”
3641𓀴
3642𓀴“I see no marks.”
3643𓀴
3644𓀴“There are none.”
3645𓀴
3646𓀴“How do you know, then?”
3647𓀴
3648𓀴“The grass was growing under it. It had only lain there a few days.
3649𓀴There was no sign of a place whence it had been taken. It corresponds
3650𓀴with the injuries. There is no sign of any other weapon.”
3651𓀴
3652𓀴“And the murderer?”
3653𓀴
3654𓀴“Is a tall man, left-handed, limps with the right leg, wears
3655𓀴thick-soled shooting-boots and a grey cloak, smokes Indian cigars, uses
3656𓀴a cigar-holder, and carries a blunt pen-knife in his pocket. There are
3657𓀴several other indications, but these may be enough to aid us in our
3658𓀴search.”
3659𓀴
3660𓀴Lestrade laughed. “I am afraid that I am still a sceptic,” he said.
3661𓀴“Theories are all very well, but we have to deal with a hard-headed
3662𓀴British jury.”
3663𓀴
3664𓀴“_Nous verrons_,” answered Holmes calmly. “You work your own method,
3665𓀴and I shall work mine. I shall be busy this afternoon, and shall
3666𓀴probably return to London by the evening train.”
3667𓀴
3668𓀴“And leave your case unfinished?”
3669𓀴
3670𓀴“No, finished.”
3671𓀴
3672𓀴“But the mystery?”
3673𓀴
3674𓀴“It is solved.”
3675𓀴
3676𓀴“Who was the criminal, then?”
3677𓀴
3678𓀴“The gentleman I describe.”
3679𓀴
3680𓀴“But who is he?”
3681𓀴
3682𓀴“Surely it would not be difficult to find out. This is not such a
3683𓀴populous neighbourhood.”
3684𓀴
3685𓀴Lestrade shrugged his shoulders. “I am a practical man,” he said, “and
3686𓀴I really cannot undertake to go about the country looking for a
3687𓀴left-handed gentleman with a game leg. I should become the
3688𓀴laughing-stock of Scotland Yard.”
3689𓀴
3690𓀴“All right,” said Holmes quietly. “I have given you the chance. Here
3691𓀴are your lodgings. Good-bye. I shall drop you a line before I leave.”
3692𓀴
3693𓀴Having left Lestrade at his rooms, we drove to our hotel, where we
3694𓀴found lunch upon the table. Holmes was silent and buried in thought
3695𓀴with a pained expression upon his face, as one who finds himself in a
3696𓀴perplexing position.
3697𓀴
3698𓀴“Look here, Watson,” he said when the cloth was cleared “just sit down
3699𓀴in this chair and let me preach to you for a little. I don’t know quite
3700𓀴what to do, and I should value your advice. Light a cigar and let me
3701𓀴expound.”
3702𓀴
3703𓀴 “Pray do so.”
3704𓀴
3705𓀴“Well, now, in considering this case there are two points about young
3706𓀴McCarthy’s narrative which struck us both instantly, although they
3707𓀴impressed me in his favour and you against him. One was the fact that
3708𓀴his father should, according to his account, cry ‘Cooee!’ before seeing
3709𓀴him. The other was his singular dying reference to a rat. He mumbled
3710𓀴several words, you understand, but that was all that caught the son’s
3711𓀴ear. Now from this double point our research must commence, and we will
3712𓀴begin it by presuming that what the lad says is absolutely true.”
3713𓀴
3714𓀴“What of this ‘Cooee!’ then?”
3715𓀴
3716𓀴“Well, obviously it could not have been meant for the son. The son, as
3717𓀴far as he knew, was in Bristol. It was mere chance that he was within
3718𓀴earshot. The ‘Cooee!’ was meant to attract the attention of whoever it
3719𓀴was that he had the appointment with. But ‘Cooee’ is a distinctly
3720𓀴Australian cry, and one which is used between Australians. There is a
3721𓀴strong presumption that the person whom McCarthy expected to meet him
3722𓀴at Boscombe Pool was someone who had been in Australia.”
3723𓀴
3724𓀴“What of the rat, then?”
3725𓀴
3726𓀴Sherlock Holmes took a folded paper from his pocket and flattened it
3727𓀴out on the table. “This is a map of the Colony of Victoria,” he said.
3728𓀴“I wired to Bristol for it last night.” He put his hand over part of
3729𓀴the map. “What do you read?”
3730𓀴
3731𓀴“ARAT,” I read.
3732𓀴
3733𓀴“And now?” He raised his hand.
3734𓀴
3735𓀴“BALLARAT.”
3736𓀴
3737𓀴“Quite so. That was the word the man uttered, and of which his son only
3738𓀴caught the last two syllables. He was trying to utter the name of his
3739𓀴murderer. So and so, of Ballarat.”
3740𓀴
3741𓀴“It is wonderful!” I exclaimed.
3742𓀴
3743𓀴“It is obvious. And now, you see, I had narrowed the field down
3744𓀴considerably. The possession of a grey garment was a third point which,
3745𓀴granting the son’s statement to be correct, was a certainty. We have
3746𓀴come now out of mere vagueness to the definite conception of an
3747𓀴Australian from Ballarat with a grey cloak.”
3748𓀴
3749𓀴“Certainly.”
3750𓀴
3751𓀴“And one who was at home in the district, for the pool can only be
3752𓀴approached by the farm or by the estate, where strangers could hardly
3753𓀴wander.”
3754𓀴
3755𓀴“Quite so.”
3756𓀴
3757𓀴“Then comes our expedition of to-day. By an examination of the ground I
3758𓀴gained the trifling details which I gave to that imbecile Lestrade, as
3759𓀴to the personality of the criminal.”
3760𓀴
3761𓀴“But how did you gain them?”
3762𓀴
3763𓀴“You know my method. It is founded upon the observation of trifles.”
3764𓀴
3765𓀴“His height I know that you might roughly judge from the length of his
3766𓀴stride. His boots, too, might be told from their traces.”
3767𓀴
3768𓀴“Yes, they were peculiar boots.”
3769𓀴
3770𓀴“But his lameness?”
3771𓀴
3772𓀴“The impression of his right foot was always less distinct than his
3773𓀴left. He put less weight upon it. Why? Because he limped—he was lame.”
3774𓀴
3775𓀴“But his left-handedness.”
3776𓀴
3777𓀴“You were yourself struck by the nature of the injury as recorded by
3778𓀴the surgeon at the inquest. The blow was struck from immediately
3779𓀴behind, and yet was upon the left side. Now, how can that be unless it
3780𓀴were by a left-handed man? He had stood behind that tree during the
3781𓀴interview between the father and son. He had even smoked there. I found
3782𓀴the ash of a cigar, which my special knowledge of tobacco ashes enables
3783𓀴me to pronounce as an Indian cigar. I have, as you know, devoted some
3784𓀴attention to this, and written a little monograph on the ashes of 140
3785𓀴different varieties of pipe, cigar, and cigarette tobacco. Having found
3786𓀴the ash, I then looked round and discovered the stump among the moss
3787𓀴where he had tossed it. It was an Indian cigar, of the variety which
3788𓀴are rolled in Rotterdam.”
3789𓀴
3790𓀴“And the cigar-holder?”
3791𓀴
3792𓀴“I could see that the end had not been in his mouth. Therefore he used
3793𓀴a holder. The tip had been cut off, not bitten off, but the cut was not
3794𓀴a clean one, so I deduced a blunt pen-knife.”
3795𓀴
3796𓀴“Holmes,” I said, “you have drawn a net round this man from which he
3797𓀴cannot escape, and you have saved an innocent human life as truly as if
3798𓀴you had cut the cord which was hanging him. I see the direction in
3799𓀴which all this points. The culprit is—”
3800𓀴
3801𓀴“Mr. John Turner,” cried the hotel waiter, opening the door of our
3802𓀴sitting-room, and ushering in a visitor.
3803𓀴
3804𓀴The man who entered was a strange and impressive figure. His slow,
3805𓀴limping step and bowed shoulders gave the appearance of decrepitude,
3806𓀴and yet his hard, deep-lined, craggy features, and his enormous limbs
3807𓀴showed that he was possessed of unusual strength of body and of
3808𓀴character. His tangled beard, grizzled hair, and outstanding, drooping
3809𓀴eyebrows combined to give an air of dignity and power to his
3810𓀴appearance, but his face was of an ashen white, while his lips and the
3811𓀴corners of his nostrils were tinged with a shade of blue. It was clear
3812𓀴to me at a glance that he was in the grip of some deadly and chronic
3813𓀴disease.
3814𓀴
3815𓀴“Pray sit down on the sofa,” said Holmes gently. “You had my note?”
3816𓀴
3817𓀴“Yes, the lodge-keeper brought it up. You said that you wished to see
3818𓀴me here to avoid scandal.”
3819𓀴
3820𓀴“I thought people would talk if I went to the Hall.”
3821𓀴
3822𓀴“And why did you wish to see me?” He looked across at my companion with
3823𓀴despair in his weary eyes, as though his question was already answered.
3824𓀴
3825𓀴“Yes,” said Holmes, answering the look rather than the words. “It is
3826𓀴so. I know all about McCarthy.”
3827𓀴
3828𓀴The old man sank his face in his hands. “God help me!” he cried. “But I
3829𓀴would not have let the young man come to harm. I give you my word that
3830𓀴I would have spoken out if it went against him at the Assizes.”
3831𓀴
3832𓀴“I am glad to hear you say so,” said Holmes gravely.
3833𓀴
3834𓀴“I would have spoken now had it not been for my dear girl. It would
3835𓀴break her heart—it will break her heart when she hears that I am
3836𓀴arrested.”
3837𓀴
3838𓀴“It may not come to that,” said Holmes.
3839𓀴
3840𓀴“What?”
3841𓀴
3842𓀴“I am no official agent. I understand that it was your daughter who
3843𓀴required my presence here, and I am acting in her interests. Young
3844𓀴McCarthy must be got off, however.”
3845𓀴
3846𓀴“I am a dying man,” said old Turner. “I have had diabetes for years. My
3847𓀴doctor says it is a question whether I shall live a month. Yet I would
3848𓀴rather die under my own roof than in a gaol.”
3849𓀴
3850𓀴Holmes rose and sat down at the table with his pen in his hand and a
3851𓀴bundle of paper before him. “Just tell us the truth,” he said. “I shall
3852𓀴jot down the facts. You will sign it, and Watson here can witness it.
3853𓀴Then I could produce your confession at the last extremity to save
3854𓀴young McCarthy. I promise you that I shall not use it unless it is
3855𓀴absolutely needed.”
3856𓀴
3857𓀴“It’s as well,” said the old man; “it’s a question whether I shall live
3858𓀴to the Assizes, so it matters little to me, but I should wish to spare
3859𓀴Alice the shock. And now I will make the thing clear to you; it has
3860𓀴been a long time in the acting, but will not take me long to tell.
3861𓀴
3862𓀴“You didn’t know this dead man, McCarthy. He was a devil incarnate. I
3863𓀴tell you that. God keep you out of the clutches of such a man as he.
3864𓀴His grip has been upon me these twenty years, and he has blasted my
3865𓀴life. I’ll tell you first how I came to be in his power.
3866𓀴
3867𓀴“It was in the early ’60’s at the diggings. I was a young chap then,
3868𓀴hot-blooded and reckless, ready to turn my hand at anything; I got
3869𓀴among bad companions, took to drink, had no luck with my claim, took to
3870𓀴the bush, and in a word became what you would call over here a highway
3871𓀴robber. There were six of us, and we had a wild, free life of it,
3872𓀴sticking up a station from time to time, or stopping the wagons on the
3873𓀴road to the diggings. Black Jack of Ballarat was the name I went under,
3874𓀴and our party is still remembered in the colony as the Ballarat Gang.
3875𓀴
3876𓀴“One day a gold convoy came down from Ballarat to Melbourne, and we lay
3877𓀴in wait for it and attacked it. There were six troopers and six of us,
3878𓀴so it was a close thing, but we emptied four of their saddles at the
3879𓀴first volley. Three of our boys were killed, however, before we got the
3880𓀴swag. I put my pistol to the head of the wagon-driver, who was this
3881𓀴very man McCarthy. I wish to the Lord that I had shot him then, but I
3882𓀴spared him, though I saw his wicked little eyes fixed on my face, as
3883𓀴though to remember every feature. We got away with the gold, became
3884𓀴wealthy men, and made our way over to England without being suspected.
3885𓀴There I parted from my old pals and determined to settle down to a
3886𓀴quiet and respectable life. I bought this estate, which chanced to be
3887𓀴in the market, and I set myself to do a little good with my money, to
3888𓀴make up for the way in which I had earned it. I married, too, and
3889𓀴though my wife died young she left me my dear little Alice. Even when
3890𓀴she was just a baby her wee hand seemed to lead me down the right path
3891𓀴as nothing else had ever done. In a word, I turned over a new leaf and
3892𓀴did my best to make up for the past. All was going well when McCarthy
3893𓀴laid his grip upon me.
3894𓀴
3895𓀴“I had gone up to town about an investment, and I met him in Regent
3896𓀴Street with hardly a coat to his back or a boot to his foot.
3897𓀴
3898𓀴“‘Here we are, Jack,’ says he, touching me on the arm; ‘we’ll be as
3899𓀴good as a family to you. There’s two of us, me and my son, and you can
3900𓀴have the keeping of us. If you don’t—it’s a fine, law-abiding country
3901𓀴is England, and there’s always a policeman within hail.’
3902𓀴
3903𓀴“Well, down they came to the west country, there was no shaking them
3904𓀴off, and there they have lived rent free on my best land ever since.
3905𓀴There was no rest for me, no peace, no forgetfulness; turn where I
3906𓀴would, there was his cunning, grinning face at my elbow. It grew worse
3907𓀴as Alice grew up, for he soon saw I was more afraid of her knowing my
3908𓀴past than of the police. Whatever he wanted he must have, and whatever
3909𓀴it was I gave him without question, land, money, houses, until at last
3910𓀴he asked a thing which I could not give. He asked for Alice.
3911𓀴
3912𓀴“His son, you see, had grown up, and so had my girl, and as I was known
3913𓀴to be in weak health, it seemed a fine stroke to him that his lad
3914𓀴should step into the whole property. But there I was firm. I would not
3915𓀴have his cursed stock mixed with mine; not that I had any dislike to
3916𓀴the lad, but his blood was in him, and that was enough. I stood firm.
3917𓀴McCarthy threatened. I braved him to do his worst. We were to meet at
3918𓀴the pool midway between our houses to talk it over.
3919𓀴
3920𓀴“When I went down there I found him talking with his son, so I smoked a
3921𓀴cigar and waited behind a tree until he should be alone. But as I
3922𓀴listened to his talk all that was black and bitter in me seemed to come
3923𓀴uppermost. He was urging his son to marry my daughter with as little
3924𓀴regard for what she might think as if she were a slut from off the
3925𓀴streets. It drove me mad to think that I and all that I held most dear
3926𓀴should be in the power of such a man as this. Could I not snap the
3927𓀴bond? I was already a dying and a desperate man. Though clear of mind
3928𓀴and fairly strong of limb, I knew that my own fate was sealed. But my
3929𓀴memory and my girl! Both could be saved if I could but silence that
3930𓀴foul tongue. I did it, Mr. Holmes. I would do it again. Deeply as I
3931𓀴have sinned, I have led a life of martyrdom to atone for it. But that
3932𓀴my girl should be entangled in the same meshes which held me was more
3933𓀴than I could suffer. I struck him down with no more compunction than if
3934𓀴he had been some foul and venomous beast. His cry brought back his son;
3935𓀴but I had gained the cover of the wood, though I was forced to go back
3936𓀴to fetch the cloak which I had dropped in my flight. That is the true
3937𓀴story, gentlemen, of all that occurred.”
3938𓀴
3939𓀴“Well, it is not for me to judge you,” said Holmes as the old man
3940𓀴signed the statement which had been drawn out. “I pray that we may
3941𓀴never be exposed to such a temptation.”
3942𓀴
3943𓀴“I pray not, sir. And what do you intend to do?”
3944𓀴
3945𓀴“In view of your health, nothing. You are yourself aware that you will
3946𓀴soon have to answer for your deed at a higher court than the Assizes. I
3947𓀴will keep your confession, and if McCarthy is condemned I shall be
3948𓀴forced to use it. If not, it shall never be seen by mortal eye; and
3949𓀴your secret, whether you be alive or dead, shall be safe with us.”
3950𓀴
3951𓀴“Farewell, then,” said the old man solemnly. “Your own deathbeds, when
3952𓀴they come, will be the easier for the thought of the peace which you
3953𓀴have given to mine.” Tottering and shaking in all his giant frame, he
3954𓀴stumbled slowly from the room.
3955𓀴
3956𓀴“God help us!” said Holmes after a long silence. “Why does fate play
3957𓀴such tricks with poor, helpless worms? I never hear of such a case as
3958𓀴this that I do not think of Baxter’s words, and say, ‘There, but for
3959𓀴the grace of God, goes Sherlock Holmes.’”
3960𓀴
3961𓀴James McCarthy was acquitted at the Assizes on the strength of a number
3962𓀴of objections which had been drawn out by Holmes and submitted to the
3963𓀴defending counsel. Old Turner lived for seven months after our
3964𓀴interview, but he is now dead; and there is every prospect that the son
3965𓀴and daughter may come to live happily together in ignorance of the
3966𓀴black cloud which rests upon their past.
3967𓀴
3968𓀴
3969𓀴
3970𓀴
3971𓀴V. THE FIVE ORANGE PIPS
3972𓀴
3973𓀴
3974𓀴When I glance over my notes and records of the Sherlock Holmes cases
3975𓀴between the years ’82 and ’90, I am faced by so many which present
3976𓀴strange and interesting features that it is no easy matter to know
3977𓀴which to choose and which to leave. Some, however, have already gained
3978𓀴publicity through the papers, and others have not offered a field for
3979𓀴those peculiar qualities which my friend possessed in so high a degree,
3980𓀴and which it is the object of these papers to illustrate. Some, too,
3981𓀴have baffled his analytical skill, and would be, as narratives,
3982𓀴beginnings without an ending, while others have been but partially
3983𓀴cleared up, and have their explanations founded rather upon conjecture
3984𓀴and surmise than on that absolute logical proof which was so dear to
3985𓀴him. There is, however, one of these last which was so remarkable in
3986𓀴its details and so startling in its results that I am tempted to give
3987𓀴some account of it in spite of the fact that there are points in
3988𓀴connection with it which never have been, and probably never will be,
3989𓀴entirely cleared up.
3990𓀴
3991𓀴The year ’87 furnished us with a long series of cases of greater or
3992𓀴less interest, of which I retain the records. Among my headings under
3993𓀴this one twelve months I find an account of the adventure of the
3994𓀴Paradol Chamber, of the Amateur Mendicant Society, who held a luxurious
3995𓀴club in the lower vault of a furniture warehouse, of the facts
3996𓀴connected with the loss of the British barque _Sophy Anderson_, of the
3997𓀴singular adventures of the Grice Patersons in the island of Uffa, and
3998𓀴finally of the Camberwell poisoning case. In the latter, as may be
3999𓀴remembered, Sherlock Holmes was able, by winding up the dead man’s
4000𓀴watch, to prove that it had been wound up two hours before, and that
4001𓀴therefore the deceased had gone to bed within that time—a deduction
4002𓀴which was of the greatest importance in clearing up the case. All these
4003𓀴I may sketch out at some future date, but none of them present such
4004𓀴singular features as the strange train of circumstances which I have
4005𓀴now taken up my pen to describe.
4006𓀴
4007𓀴It was in the latter days of September, and the equinoctial gales had
4008𓀴set in with exceptional violence. All day the wind had screamed and the
4009𓀴rain had beaten against the windows, so that even here in the heart of
4010𓀴great, hand-made London we were forced to raise our minds for the
4011𓀴instant from the routine of life and to recognise the presence of those
4012𓀴great elemental forces which shriek at mankind through the bars of his
4013𓀴civilisation, like untamed beasts in a cage. As evening drew in, the
4014𓀴storm grew higher and louder, and the wind cried and sobbed like a
