4015𓀴child in the chimney. Sherlock Holmes sat moodily at one side of the
4016𓀴fireplace cross-indexing his records of crime, while I at the other was
4017𓀴deep in one of Clark Russell’s fine sea-stories until the howl of the
4018𓀴gale from without seemed to blend with the text, and the splash of the
4019𓀴rain to lengthen out into the long swash of the sea waves. My wife was
4020𓀴on a visit to her mother’s, and for a few days I was a dweller once
4021𓀴more in my old quarters at Baker Street.
4022𓀴
4023𓀴“Why,” said I, glancing up at my companion, “that was surely the bell.
4024𓀴Who could come to-night? Some friend of yours, perhaps?”
4025𓀴
4026𓀴“Except yourself I have none,” he answered. “I do not encourage
4027𓀴visitors.”
4028𓀴
4029𓀴“A client, then?”
4030𓀴
4031𓀴“If so, it is a serious case. Nothing less would bring a man out on
4032𓀴such a day and at such an hour. But I take it that it is more likely to
4033𓀴be some crony of the landlady’s.”
4034𓀴
4035𓀴Sherlock Holmes was wrong in his conjecture, however, for there came a
4036𓀴step in the passage and a tapping at the door. He stretched out his
4037𓀴long arm to turn the lamp away from himself and towards the vacant
4038𓀴chair upon which a newcomer must sit.
4039𓀴
4040𓀴“Come in!” said he.
4041𓀴
4042𓀴The man who entered was young, some two-and-twenty at the outside,
4043𓀴well-groomed and trimly clad, with something of refinement and delicacy
4044𓀴in his bearing. The streaming umbrella which he held in his hand, and
4045𓀴his long shining waterproof told of the fierce weather through which he
4046𓀴had come. He looked about him anxiously in the glare of the lamp, and I
4047𓀴could see that his face was pale and his eyes heavy, like those of a
4048𓀴man who is weighed down with some great anxiety.
4049𓀴
4050𓀴“I owe you an apology,” he said, raising his golden pince-nez to his
4051𓀴eyes. “I trust that I am not intruding. I fear that I have brought some
4052𓀴traces of the storm and rain into your snug chamber.”
4053𓀴
4054𓀴“Give me your coat and umbrella,” said Holmes. “They may rest here on
4055𓀴the hook and will be dry presently. You have come up from the
4056𓀴south-west, I see.”
4057𓀴
4058𓀴“Yes, from Horsham.”
4059𓀴
4060𓀴“That clay and chalk mixture which I see upon your toe caps is quite
4061𓀴distinctive.”
4062𓀴
4063𓀴“I have come for advice.”
4064𓀴
4065𓀴“That is easily got.”
4066𓀴
4067𓀴“And help.”
4068𓀴
4069𓀴“That is not always so easy.”
4070𓀴
4071𓀴“I have heard of you, Mr. Holmes. I heard from Major Prendergast how
4072𓀴you saved him in the Tankerville Club scandal.”
4073𓀴
4074𓀴“Ah, of course. He was wrongfully accused of cheating at cards.”
4075𓀴
4076𓀴“He said that you could solve anything.”
4077𓀴
4078𓀴“He said too much.”
4079𓀴
4080𓀴“That you are never beaten.”
4081𓀴
4082𓀴“I have been beaten four times—three times by men, and once by a
4083𓀴woman.”
4084𓀴
4085𓀴“But what is that compared with the number of your successes?”
4086𓀴
4087𓀴“It is true that I have been generally successful.”
4088𓀴
4089𓀴“Then you may be so with me.”
4090𓀴
4091𓀴“I beg that you will draw your chair up to the fire and favour me with
4092𓀴some details as to your case.”
4093𓀴
4094𓀴“It is no ordinary one.”
4095𓀴
4096𓀴“None of those which come to me are. I am the last court of appeal.”
4097𓀴
4098𓀴“And yet I question, sir, whether, in all your experience, you have
4099𓀴ever listened to a more mysterious and inexplicable chain of events
4100𓀴than those which have happened in my own family.”
4101𓀴
4102𓀴“You fill me with interest,” said Holmes. “Pray give us the essential
4103𓀴facts from the commencement, and I can afterwards question you as to
4104𓀴those details which seem to me to be most important.”
4105𓀴
4106𓀴The young man pulled his chair up and pushed his wet feet out towards
4107𓀴the blaze.
4108𓀴
4109𓀴“My name,” said he, “is John Openshaw, but my own affairs have, as far
4110𓀴as I can understand, little to do with this awful business. It is a
4111𓀴hereditary matter; so in order to give you an idea of the facts, I must
4112𓀴go back to the commencement of the affair.
4113𓀴
4114𓀴“You must know that my grandfather had two sons—my uncle Elias and my
4115𓀴father Joseph. My father had a small factory at Coventry, which he
4116𓀴enlarged at the time of the invention of bicycling. He was a patentee
4117𓀴of the Openshaw unbreakable tire, and his business met with such
4118𓀴success that he was able to sell it and to retire upon a handsome
4119𓀴competence.
4120𓀴
4121𓀴“My uncle Elias emigrated to America when he was a young man and became
4122𓀴a planter in Florida, where he was reported to have done very well. At
4123𓀴the time of the war he fought in Jackson’s army, and afterwards under
4124𓀴Hood, where he rose to be a colonel. When Lee laid down his arms my
4125𓀴uncle returned to his plantation, where he remained for three or four
4126𓀴years. About 1869 or 1870 he came back to Europe and took a small
4127𓀴estate in Sussex, near Horsham. He had made a very considerable fortune
4128𓀴in the States, and his reason for leaving them was his aversion to the
4129𓀴negroes, and his dislike of the Republican policy in extending the
4130𓀴franchise to them. He was a singular man, fierce and quick-tempered,
4131𓀴very foul-mouthed when he was angry, and of a most retiring
4132𓀴disposition. During all the years that he lived at Horsham, I doubt if
4133𓀴ever he set foot in the town. He had a garden and two or three fields
4134𓀴round his house, and there he would take his exercise, though very
4135𓀴often for weeks on end he would never leave his room. He drank a great
4136𓀴deal of brandy and smoked very heavily, but he would see no society and
4137𓀴did not want any friends, not even his own brother.
4138𓀴
4139𓀴“He didn’t mind me; in fact, he took a fancy to me, for at the time
4140𓀴when he saw me first I was a youngster of twelve or so. This would be
4141𓀴in the year 1878, after he had been eight or nine years in England. He
4142𓀴begged my father to let me live with him and he was very kind to me in
4143𓀴his way. When he was sober he used to be fond of playing backgammon and
4144𓀴draughts with me, and he would make me his representative both with the
4145𓀴servants and with the tradespeople, so that by the time that I was
4146𓀴sixteen I was quite master of the house. I kept all the keys and could
4147𓀴go where I liked and do what I liked, so long as I did not disturb him
4148𓀴in his privacy. There was one singular exception, however, for he had a
4149𓀴single room, a lumber-room up among the attics, which was invariably
4150𓀴locked, and which he would never permit either me or anyone else to
4151𓀴enter. With a boy’s curiosity I have peeped through the keyhole, but I
4152𓀴was never able to see more than such a collection of old trunks and
4153𓀴bundles as would be expected in such a room.
4154𓀴
4155𓀴“One day—it was in March, 1883—a letter with a foreign stamp lay upon
4156𓀴the table in front of the colonel’s plate. It was not a common thing
4157𓀴for him to receive letters, for his bills were all paid in ready money,
4158𓀴and he had no friends of any sort. ‘From India!’ said he as he took it
4159𓀴up, ‘Pondicherry postmark! What can this be?’ Opening it hurriedly, out
4160𓀴there jumped five little dried orange pips, which pattered down upon
4161𓀴his plate. I began to laugh at this, but the laugh was struck from my
4162𓀴lips at the sight of his face. His lip had fallen, his eyes were
4163𓀴protruding, his skin the colour of putty, and he glared at the envelope
4164𓀴which he still held in his trembling hand, ‘K. K. K.!’ he shrieked, and
4165𓀴then, ‘My God, my God, my sins have overtaken me!’
4166𓀴
4167𓀴“‘What is it, uncle?’ I cried.
4168𓀴
4169𓀴“‘Death,’ said he, and rising from the table he retired to his room,
4170𓀴leaving me palpitating with horror. I took up the envelope and saw
4171𓀴scrawled in red ink upon the inner flap, just above the gum, the letter
4172𓀴K three times repeated. There was nothing else save the five dried
4173𓀴pips. What could be the reason of his overpowering terror? I left the
4174𓀴breakfast-table, and as I ascended the stair I met him coming down with
4175𓀴an old rusty key, which must have belonged to the attic, in one hand,
4176𓀴and a small brass box, like a cashbox, in the other.
4177𓀴
4178𓀴“‘They may do what they like, but I’ll checkmate them still,’ said he
4179𓀴with an oath. ‘Tell Mary that I shall want a fire in my room to-day,
4180𓀴and send down to Fordham, the Horsham lawyer.’
4181𓀴
4182𓀴“I did as he ordered, and when the lawyer arrived I was asked to step
4183𓀴up to the room. The fire was burning brightly, and in the grate there
4184𓀴was a mass of black, fluffy ashes, as of burned paper, while the brass
4185𓀴box stood open and empty beside it. As I glanced at the box I noticed,
4186𓀴with a start, that upon the lid was printed the treble K which I had
4187𓀴read in the morning upon the envelope.
4188𓀴
4189𓀴“‘I wish you, John,’ said my uncle, ‘to witness my will. I leave my
4190𓀴estate, with all its advantages and all its disadvantages, to my
4191𓀴brother, your father, whence it will, no doubt, descend to you. If you
4192𓀴can enjoy it in peace, well and good! If you find you cannot, take my
4193𓀴advice, my boy, and leave it to your deadliest enemy. I am sorry to
4194𓀴give you such a two-edged thing, but I can’t say what turn things are
4195𓀴going to take. Kindly sign the paper where Mr. Fordham shows you.’
4196𓀴
4197𓀴“I signed the paper as directed, and the lawyer took it away with him.
4198𓀴The singular incident made, as you may think, the deepest impression
4199𓀴upon me, and I pondered over it and turned it every way in my mind
4200𓀴without being able to make anything of it. Yet I could not shake off
4201𓀴the vague feeling of dread which it left behind, though the sensation
4202𓀴grew less keen as the weeks passed and nothing happened to disturb the
4203𓀴usual routine of our lives. I could see a change in my uncle, however.
4204𓀴He drank more than ever, and he was less inclined for any sort of
4205𓀴society. Most of his time he would spend in his room, with the door
4206𓀴locked upon the inside, but sometimes he would emerge in a sort of
4207𓀴drunken frenzy and would burst out of the house and tear about the
4208𓀴garden with a revolver in his hand, screaming out that he was afraid of
4209𓀴no man, and that he was not to be cooped up, like a sheep in a pen, by
4210𓀴man or devil. When these hot fits were over, however, he would rush
4211𓀴tumultuously in at the door and lock and bar it behind him, like a man
4212𓀴who can brazen it out no longer against the terror which lies at the
4213𓀴roots of his soul. At such times I have seen his face, even on a cold
4214𓀴day, glisten with moisture, as though it were new raised from a basin.
4215𓀴
4216𓀴“Well, to come to an end of the matter, Mr. Holmes, and not to abuse
4217𓀴your patience, there came a night when he made one of those drunken
4218𓀴sallies from which he never came back. We found him, when we went to
4219𓀴search for him, face downward in a little green-scummed pool, which lay
4220𓀴at the foot of the garden. There was no sign of any violence, and the
4221𓀴water was but two feet deep, so that the jury, having regard to his
4222𓀴known eccentricity, brought in a verdict of ‘suicide.’ But I, who knew
4223𓀴how he winced from the very thought of death, had much ado to persuade
4224𓀴myself that he had gone out of his way to meet it. The matter passed,
4225𓀴however, and my father entered into possession of the estate, and of
4226𓀴some £ 14,000, which lay to his credit at the bank.”
4227𓀴
4228𓀴“One moment,” Holmes interposed, “your statement is, I foresee, one of
4229𓀴the most remarkable to which I have ever listened. Let me have the date
4230𓀴of the reception by your uncle of the letter, and the date of his
4231𓀴supposed suicide.”
4232𓀴
4233𓀴“The letter arrived on March 10, 1883. His death was seven weeks later,
4234𓀴upon the night of May 2nd.”
4235𓀴
4236𓀴“Thank you. Pray proceed.”
4237𓀴
4238𓀴“When my father took over the Horsham property, he, at my request, made
4239𓀴a careful examination of the attic, which had been always locked up. We
4240𓀴found the brass box there, although its contents had been destroyed. On
4241𓀴the inside of the cover was a paper label, with the initials of K. K.
4242𓀴K. repeated upon it, and ‘Letters, memoranda, receipts, and a register’
4243𓀴written beneath. These, we presume, indicated the nature of the papers
4244𓀴which had been destroyed by Colonel Openshaw. For the rest, there was
4245𓀴nothing of much importance in the attic save a great many scattered
4246𓀴papers and note-books bearing upon my uncle’s life in America. Some of
4247𓀴them were of the war time and showed that he had done his duty well and
4248𓀴had borne the repute of a brave soldier. Others were of a date during
4249𓀴the reconstruction of the Southern states, and were mostly concerned
4250𓀴with politics, for he had evidently taken a strong part in opposing the
4251𓀴carpet-bag politicians who had been sent down from the North.
4252𓀴
4253𓀴“Well, it was the beginning of ’84 when my father came to live at
4254𓀴Horsham, and all went as well as possible with us until the January of
4255𓀴’85. On the fourth day after the new year I heard my father give a
4256𓀴sharp cry of surprise as we sat together at the breakfast-table. There
4257𓀴he was, sitting with a newly opened envelope in one hand and five dried
4258𓀴orange pips in the outstretched palm of the other one. He had always
4259𓀴laughed at what he called my cock-and-bull story about the colonel, but
4260𓀴he looked very scared and puzzled now that the same thing had come upon
4261𓀴himself.
4262𓀴
4263𓀴“‘Why, what on earth does this mean, John?’ he stammered.
4264𓀴
4265𓀴“My heart had turned to lead. ‘It is K. K. K.,’ said I.
4266𓀴
4267𓀴“He looked inside the envelope. ‘So it is,’ he cried. ‘Here are the
4268𓀴very letters. But what is this written above them?’
4269𓀴
4270𓀴“‘Put the papers on the sundial,’ I read, peeping over his shoulder.
4271𓀴
4272𓀴“‘What papers? What sundial?’ he asked.
4273𓀴
4274𓀴“‘The sundial in the garden. There is no other,’ said I; ‘but the
4275𓀴papers must be those that are destroyed.’
4276𓀴
4277𓀴“‘Pooh!’ said he, gripping hard at his courage. ‘We are in a civilised
4278𓀴land here, and we can’t have tomfoolery of this kind. Where does the
4279𓀴thing come from?’
4280𓀴
4281𓀴“‘From Dundee,’ I answered, glancing at the postmark.
4282𓀴
4283𓀴“‘Some preposterous practical joke,’ said he. ‘What have I to do with
4284𓀴sundials and papers? I shall take no notice of such nonsense.’
4285𓀴
4286𓀴“‘I should certainly speak to the police,’ I said.
4287𓀴
4288𓀴“‘And be laughed at for my pains. Nothing of the sort.’
4289𓀴
4290𓀴“‘Then let me do so?’
4291𓀴
4292𓀴“‘No, I forbid you. I won’t have a fuss made about such nonsense.’
4293𓀴
4294𓀴“It was in vain to argue with him, for he was a very obstinate man. I
4295𓀴went about, however, with a heart which was full of forebodings.
4296𓀴
4297𓀴“On the third day after the coming of the letter my father went from
4298𓀴home to visit an old friend of his, Major Freebody, who is in command
4299𓀴of one of the forts upon Portsdown Hill. I was glad that he should go,
4300𓀴for it seemed to me that he was farther from danger when he was away
4301𓀴from home. In that, however, I was in error. Upon the second day of his
4302𓀴absence I received a telegram from the major, imploring me to come at
4303𓀴once. My father had fallen over one of the deep chalk-pits which abound
4304𓀴in the neighbourhood, and was lying senseless, with a shattered skull.
4305𓀴I hurried to him, but he passed away without having ever recovered his
4306𓀴consciousness. He had, as it appears, been returning from Fareham in
4307𓀴the twilight, and as the country was unknown to him, and the chalk-pit
4308𓀴unfenced, the jury had no hesitation in bringing in a verdict of ‘death
4309𓀴from accidental causes.’ Carefully as I examined every fact connected
4310𓀴with his death, I was unable to find anything which could suggest the
4311𓀴idea of murder. There were no signs of violence, no footmarks, no
4312𓀴robbery, no record of strangers having been seen upon the roads. And
4313𓀴yet I need not tell you that my mind was far from at ease, and that I
4314𓀴was well-nigh certain that some foul plot had been woven round him.
4315𓀴
4316𓀴“In this sinister way I came into my inheritance. You will ask me why I
4317𓀴did not dispose of it? I answer, because I was well convinced that our
4318𓀴troubles were in some way dependent upon an incident in my uncle’s
4319𓀴life, and that the danger would be as pressing in one house as in
4320𓀴another.
4321𓀴
4322𓀴“It was in January, ’85, that my poor father met his end, and two years
4323𓀴and eight months have elapsed since then. During that time I have lived
4324𓀴happily at Horsham, and I had begun to hope that this curse had passed
4325𓀴away from the family, and that it had ended with the last generation. I
4326𓀴had begun to take comfort too soon, however; yesterday morning the blow
4327𓀴fell in the very shape in which it had come upon my father.”
4328𓀴
4329𓀴The young man took from his waistcoat a crumpled envelope, and turning
4330𓀴to the table he shook out upon it five little dried orange pips.
4331𓀴
4332𓀴“This is the envelope,” he continued. “The postmark is London—eastern
4333𓀴division. Within are the very words which were upon my father’s last
4334𓀴message: ‘K. K. K.’; and then ‘Put the papers on the sundial.’”
4335𓀴
4336𓀴“What have you done?” asked Holmes.
4337𓀴
4338𓀴“Nothing.”
4339𓀴
4340𓀴“Nothing?”
4341𓀴
4342𓀴“To tell the truth”—he sank his face into his thin, white hands—“I have
4343𓀴felt helpless. I have felt like one of those poor rabbits when the
4344𓀴snake is writhing towards it. I seem to be in the grasp of some
4345𓀴resistless, inexorable evil, which no foresight and no precautions can
4346𓀴guard against.”
4347𓀴
4348𓀴“Tut! tut!” cried Sherlock Holmes. “You must act, man, or you are lost.
4349𓀴Nothing but energy can save you. This is no time for despair.”
4350𓀴
4351𓀴“I have seen the police.”
4352𓀴
4353𓀴“Ah!”
4354𓀴
4355𓀴“But they listened to my story with a smile. I am convinced that the
4356𓀴inspector has formed the opinion that the letters are all practical
4357𓀴jokes, and that the deaths of my relations were really accidents, as
4358𓀴the jury stated, and were not to be connected with the warnings.”
4359𓀴
4360𓀴Holmes shook his clenched hands in the air. “Incredible imbecility!” he
4361𓀴cried.
4362𓀴
4363𓀴“They have, however, allowed me a policeman, who may remain in the
4364𓀴house with me.”
4365𓀴
4366𓀴“Has he come with you to-night?”
4367𓀴
4368𓀴“No. His orders were to stay in the house.”
4369𓀴
4370𓀴Again Holmes raved in the air.
4371𓀴
4372𓀴“Why did you come to me?” he said, “and, above all, why did you not
4373𓀴come at once?”
4374𓀴
4375𓀴“I did not know. It was only to-day that I spoke to Major Prendergast
4376𓀴about my troubles and was advised by him to come to you.”
4377𓀴
4378𓀴“It is really two days since you had the letter. We should have acted
4379𓀴before this. You have no further evidence, I suppose, than that which
4380𓀴you have placed before us—no suggestive detail which might help us?”
4381𓀴
4382𓀴“There is one thing,” said John Openshaw. He rummaged in his coat
4383𓀴pocket, and, drawing out a piece of discoloured, blue-tinted paper, he
4384𓀴laid it out upon the table. “I have some remembrance,” said he, “that
4385𓀴on the day when my uncle burned the papers I observed that the small,
4386𓀴unburned margins which lay amid the ashes were of this particular
4387𓀴colour. I found this single sheet upon the floor of his room, and I am
4388𓀴inclined to think that it may be one of the papers which has, perhaps,
4389𓀴fluttered out from among the others, and in that way has escaped
4390𓀴destruction. Beyond the mention of pips, I do not see that it helps us
4391𓀴much. I think myself that it is a page from some private diary. The
4392𓀴writing is undoubtedly my uncle’s.”
4393𓀴
4394𓀴Holmes moved the lamp, and we both bent over the sheet of paper, which
4395𓀴showed by its ragged edge that it had indeed been torn from a book. It
4396𓀴was headed, “March, 1869,” and beneath were the following enigmatical
4397𓀴notices:
4398𓀴
4399𓀴“4th. Hudson came. Same old platform.
4400𓀴
4401𓀴“7th. Set the pips on McCauley, Paramore, and John Swain of St.
4402𓀴Augustine.
4403𓀴
4404𓀴“9th. McCauley cleared.
4405𓀴
4406𓀴“10th. John Swain cleared.
4407𓀴
4408𓀴“12th. Visited Paramore. All well.”
4409𓀴
4410𓀴“Thank you!” said Holmes, folding up the paper and returning it to our
4411𓀴visitor. “And now you must on no account lose another instant. We
4412𓀴cannot spare time even to discuss what you have told me. You must get
4413𓀴home instantly and act.”
4414𓀴
4415𓀴“What shall I do?”
4416𓀴
4417𓀴“There is but one thing to do. It must be done at once. You must put
4418𓀴this piece of paper which you have shown us into the brass box which
4419𓀴you have described. You must also put in a note to say that all the
4420𓀴other papers were burned by your uncle, and that this is the only one
4421𓀴which remains. You must assert that in such words as will carry
4422𓀴conviction with them. Having done this, you must at once put the box
4423𓀴out upon the sundial, as directed. Do you understand?”
4424𓀴
4425𓀴“Entirely.”
4426𓀴
4427𓀴“Do not think of revenge, or anything of the sort, at present. I think
4428𓀴that we may gain that by means of the law; but we have our web to
4429𓀴weave, while theirs is already woven. The first consideration is to
4430𓀴remove the pressing danger which threatens you. The second is to clear
4431𓀴up the mystery and to punish the guilty parties.”
4432𓀴
4433𓀴“I thank you,” said the young man, rising and pulling on his overcoat.
4434𓀴“You have given me fresh life and hope. I shall certainly do as you
4435𓀴advise.”
4436𓀴
4437𓀴“Do not lose an instant. And, above all, take care of yourself in the
4438𓀴meanwhile, for I do not think that there can be a doubt that you are
4439𓀴threatened by a very real and imminent danger. How do you go back?”
4440𓀴
4441𓀴“By train from Waterloo.”
4442𓀴
4443𓀴“It is not yet nine. The streets will be crowded, so I trust that you
4444𓀴may be in safety. And yet you cannot guard yourself too closely.”
4445𓀴
4446𓀴“I am armed.”
4447𓀴
4448𓀴“That is well. To-morrow I shall set to work upon your case.”
4449𓀴
4450𓀴“I shall see you at Horsham, then?”
4451𓀴
4452𓀴“No, your secret lies in London. It is there that I shall seek it.”
4453𓀴
4454𓀴“Then I shall call upon you in a day, or in two days, with news as to
4455𓀴the box and the papers. I shall take your advice in every particular.”
4456𓀴He shook hands with us and took his leave. Outside the wind still
4457𓀴screamed and the rain splashed and pattered against the windows. This
4458𓀴strange, wild story seemed to have come to us from amid the mad
4459𓀴elements—blown in upon us like a sheet of sea-weed in a gale—and now to
4460𓀴have been reabsorbed by them once more.
4461𓀴
4462𓀴Sherlock Holmes sat for some time in silence, with his head sunk
4463𓀴forward and his eyes bent upon the red glow of the fire. Then he lit
4464𓀴his pipe, and leaning back in his chair he watched the blue smoke-rings
4465𓀴as they chased each other up to the ceiling.
4466𓀴
4467𓀴“I think, Watson,” he remarked at last, “that of all our cases we have
4468𓀴had none more fantastic than this.”
4469𓀴
4470𓀴“Save, perhaps, the Sign of Four.”
4471𓀴
4472𓀴“Well, yes. Save, perhaps, that. And yet this John Openshaw seems to me
4473𓀴to be walking amid even greater perils than did the Sholtos.”
4474𓀴
4475𓀴“But have you,” I asked, “formed any definite conception as to what
4476𓀴these perils are?”
4477𓀴
4478𓀴“There can be no question as to their nature,” he answered.
4479𓀴
4480𓀴“Then what are they? Who is this K. K. K., and why does he pursue this
4481𓀴unhappy family?”
4482𓀴
4483𓀴Sherlock Holmes closed his eyes and placed his elbows upon the arms of
4484𓀴his chair, with his finger-tips together. “The ideal reasoner,” he
4485𓀴remarked, “would, when he had once been shown a single fact in all its
4486𓀴bearings, deduce from it not only all the chain of events which led up
4487𓀴to it but also all the results which would follow from it. As Cuvier
4488𓀴could correctly describe a whole animal by the contemplation of a
4489𓀴single bone, so the observer who has thoroughly understood one link in
4490𓀴a series of incidents should be able to accurately state all the other
4491𓀴ones, both before and after. We have not yet grasped the results which
4492𓀴the reason alone can attain to. Problems may be solved in the study
4493𓀴which have baffled all those who have sought a solution by the aid of
4494𓀴their senses. To carry the art, however, to its highest pitch, it is
4495𓀴necessary that the reasoner should be able to utilise all the facts
4496𓀴which have come to his knowledge; and this in itself implies, as you
4497𓀴will readily see, a possession of all knowledge, which, even in these
4498𓀴days of free education and encyclopædias, is a somewhat rare
4499𓀴accomplishment. It is not so impossible, however, that a man should
4500𓀴possess all knowledge which is likely to be useful to him in his work,
4501𓀴and this I have endeavoured in my case to do. If I remember rightly,
4502𓀴you on one occasion, in the early days of our friendship, defined my
4503𓀴limits in a very precise fashion.”
4504𓀴
4505𓀴“Yes,” I answered, laughing. “It was a singular document. Philosophy,
4506𓀴astronomy, and politics were marked at zero, I remember. Botany
4507𓀴variable, geology profound as regards the mud-stains from any region
4508𓀴within fifty miles of town, chemistry eccentric, anatomy unsystematic,
4509𓀴sensational literature and crime records unique, violin-player, boxer,
4510𓀴swordsman, lawyer, and self-poisoner by cocaine and tobacco. Those, I
4511𓀴think, were the main points of my analysis.”
4512𓀴
4513𓀴Holmes grinned at the last item. “Well,” he said, “I say now, as I said
4514𓀴then, that a man should keep his little brain-attic stocked with all
4515𓀴the furniture that he is likely to use, and the rest he can put away in
4516𓀴the lumber-room of his library, where he can get it if he wants it.
4517𓀴Now, for such a case as the one which has been submitted to us
4518𓀴to-night, we need certainly to muster all our resources. Kindly hand me
4519𓀴down the letter K of the _American Encyclopædia_ which stands upon the
4520𓀴shelf beside you. Thank you. Now let us consider the situation and see
4521𓀴what may be deduced from it. In the first place, we may start with a
4522𓀴strong presumption that Colonel Openshaw had some very strong reason
4523𓀴for leaving America. Men at his time of life do not change all their
4524𓀴habits and exchange willingly the charming climate of Florida for the
4525𓀴lonely life of an English provincial town. His extreme love of solitude
4526𓀴in England suggests the idea that he was in fear of someone or
4527𓀴something, so we may assume as a working hypothesis that it was fear of
4528𓀴someone or something which drove him from America. As to what it was he
4529𓀴feared, we can only deduce that by considering the formidable letters
4530𓀴which were received by himself and his successors. Did you remark the
4531𓀴postmarks of those letters?”
4532𓀴
4533𓀴“The first was from Pondicherry, the second from Dundee, and the third
4534𓀴from London.”
4535𓀴
4536𓀴“From East London. What do you deduce from that?”
4537𓀴
4538𓀴“They are all seaports. That the writer was on board of a ship.”
4539𓀴
4540𓀴“Excellent. We have already a clue. There can be no doubt that the
4541𓀴probability—the strong probability—is that the writer was on board of a
4542𓀴ship. And now let us consider another point. In the case of
4543𓀴Pondicherry, seven weeks elapsed between the threat and its fulfilment,
4544𓀴in Dundee it was only some three or four days. Does that suggest
4545𓀴anything?”
4546𓀴
4547𓀴“A greater distance to travel.”
4548𓀴
4549𓀴“But the letter had also a greater distance to come.”
4550𓀴
4551𓀴“Then I do not see the point.”
4552𓀴
4553𓀴“There is at least a presumption that the vessel in which the man or
4554𓀴men are is a sailing-ship. It looks as if they always send their
4555𓀴singular warning or token before them when starting upon their mission.
4556𓀴You see how quickly the deed followed the sign when it came from
4557𓀴Dundee. If they had come from Pondicherry in a steamer they would have
4558𓀴arrived almost as soon as their letter. But, as a matter of fact, seven
4559𓀴weeks elapsed. I think that those seven weeks represented the
4560𓀴difference between the mail-boat which brought the letter and the
4561𓀴sailing vessel which brought the writer.”
4562𓀴
4563𓀴“It is possible.”
4564𓀴
4565𓀴“More than that. It is probable. And now you see the deadly urgency of
4566𓀴this new case, and why I urged young Openshaw to caution. The blow has
4567𓀴always fallen at the end of the time which it would take the senders to
4568𓀴travel the distance. But this one comes from London, and therefore we
4569𓀴cannot count upon delay.”
4570𓀴
4571𓀴“Good God!” I cried. “What can it mean, this relentless persecution?”
4572𓀴
4573𓀴“The papers which Openshaw carried are obviously of vital importance to
4574𓀴the person or persons in the sailing-ship. I think that it is quite
4575𓀴clear that there must be more than one of them. A single man could not
4576𓀴have carried out two deaths in such a way as to deceive a coroner’s
4577𓀴jury. There must have been several in it, and they must have been men
4578𓀴of resource and determination. Their papers they mean to have, be the
4579𓀴holder of them who it may. In this way you see K. K. K. ceases to be
4580𓀴the initials of an individual and becomes the badge of a society.”
4581𓀴
4582𓀴“But of what society?”
4583𓀴
4584𓀴“Have you never—” said Sherlock Holmes, bending forward and sinking his
4585𓀴voice—“have you never heard of the Ku Klux Klan?”
4586𓀴
4587𓀴“I never have.”
4588𓀴
4589𓀴Holmes turned over the leaves of the book upon his knee. “Here it is,”
4590𓀴said he presently:
4591𓀴
4592𓀴“‘Ku Klux Klan. A name derived from the fanciful resemblance to the
4593𓀴sound produced by cocking a rifle. This terrible secret society was
4594𓀴formed by some ex-Confederate soldiers in the Southern states after the
4595𓀴Civil War, and it rapidly formed local branches in different parts of
4596𓀴the country, notably in Tennessee, Louisiana, the Carolinas, Georgia,
4597𓀴and Florida. Its power was used for political purposes, principally for
4598𓀴the terrorising of the negro voters and the murdering and driving from
4599𓀴the country of those who were opposed to its views. Its outrages were
4600𓀴usually preceded by a warning sent to the marked man in some fantastic
4601𓀴but generally recognised shape—a sprig of oak-leaves in some parts,
4602𓀴melon seeds or orange pips in others. On receiving this the victim
4603𓀴might either openly abjure his former ways, or might fly from the
4604𓀴country. If he braved the matter out, death would unfailingly come upon
4605𓀴him, and usually in some strange and unforeseen manner. So perfect was
4606𓀴the organisation of the society, and so systematic its methods, that
4607𓀴there is hardly a case upon record where any man succeeded in braving
4608𓀴it with impunity, or in which any of its outrages were traced home to
4609𓀴the perpetrators. For some years the organisation flourished in spite
4610𓀴of the efforts of the United States government and of the better
4611𓀴classes of the community in the South. Eventually, in the year 1869,
4612𓀴the movement rather suddenly collapsed, although there have been
4613𓀴sporadic outbreaks of the same sort since that date.’
4614𓀴
4615𓀴“You will observe,” said Holmes, laying down the volume, “that the
4616𓀴sudden breaking up of the society was coincident with the disappearance
4617𓀴of Openshaw from America with their papers. It may well have been cause
4618𓀴and effect. It is no wonder that he and his family have some of the
4619𓀴more implacable spirits upon their track. You can understand that this
4620𓀴register and diary may implicate some of the first men in the South,
4621𓀴and that there may be many who will not sleep easy at night until it is
4622𓀴recovered.”
4623𓀴
4624𓀴“Then the page we have seen—”
4625𓀴
4626𓀴“Is such as we might expect. It ran, if I remember right, ‘sent the
4627𓀴pips to A, B, and C’—that is, sent the society’s warning to them. Then
4628𓀴there are successive entries that A and B cleared, or left the country,
4629𓀴and finally that C was visited, with, I fear, a sinister result for C.
4630𓀴Well, I think, Doctor, that we may let some light into this dark place,
4631𓀴and I believe that the only chance young Openshaw has in the meantime
4632𓀴is to do what I have told him. There is nothing more to be said or to
4633𓀴be done to-night, so hand me over my violin and let us try to forget
4634𓀴for half an hour the miserable weather and the still more miserable
4635𓀴ways of our fellow men.”
4636𓀴
4637𓀴It had cleared in the morning, and the sun was shining with a subdued
4638𓀴brightness through the dim veil which hangs over the great city.
4639𓀴Sherlock Holmes was already at breakfast when I came down.
4640𓀴
4641𓀴“You will excuse me for not waiting for you,” said he; “I have, I
4642𓀴foresee, a very busy day before me in looking into this case of young
4643𓀴Openshaw’s.”
4644𓀴
4645𓀴“What steps will you take?” I asked.
4646𓀴
4647𓀴“It will very much depend upon the results of my first inquiries. I may
4648𓀴have to go down to Horsham, after all.”
4649𓀴
4650𓀴“You will not go there first?”
4651𓀴
4652𓀴“No, I shall commence with the City. Just ring the bell and the maid
4653𓀴will bring up your coffee.”
4654𓀴
4655𓀴As I waited, I lifted the unopened newspaper from the table and glanced
4656𓀴my eye over it. It rested upon a heading which sent a chill to my
4657𓀴heart.
4658𓀴
4659𓀴“Holmes,” I cried, “you are too late.”
4660𓀴
4661𓀴“Ah!” said he, laying down his cup, “I feared as much. How was it
4662𓀴done?” He spoke calmly, but I could see that he was deeply moved.
4663𓀴
4664𓀴“My eye caught the name of Openshaw, and the heading ‘Tragedy Near
4665𓀴Waterloo Bridge.’ Here is the account:
4666𓀴
4667𓀴“‘Between nine and ten last night Police-Constable Cook, of the H
4668𓀴Division, on duty near Waterloo Bridge, heard a cry for help and a
4669𓀴splash in the water. The night, however, was extremely dark and stormy,
4670𓀴so that, in spite of the help of several passers-by, it was quite
4671𓀴impossible to effect a rescue. The alarm, however, was given, and, by
4672𓀴the aid of the water-police, the body was eventually recovered. It
4673𓀴proved to be that of a young gentleman whose name, as it appears from
4674𓀴an envelope which was found in his pocket, was John Openshaw, and whose
4675𓀴residence is near Horsham. It is conjectured that he may have been
4676𓀴hurrying down to catch the last train from Waterloo Station, and that
4677𓀴in his haste and the extreme darkness he missed his path and walked
4678𓀴over the edge of one of the small landing-places for river steamboats.
4679𓀴The body exhibited no traces of violence, and there can be no doubt
4680𓀴that the deceased had been the victim of an unfortunate accident, which
4681𓀴should have the effect of calling the attention of the authorities to
4682𓀴the condition of the riverside landing-stages.’”
4683𓀴
4684𓀴We sat in silence for some minutes, Holmes more depressed and shaken
4685𓀴than I had ever seen him.
4686𓀴
4687𓀴“That hurts my pride, Watson,” he said at last. “It is a petty feeling,
4688𓀴no doubt, but it hurts my pride. It becomes a personal matter with me
4689𓀴now, and, if God sends me health, I shall set my hand upon this gang.
4690𓀴That he should come to me for help, and that I should send him away to
4691𓀴his death—!” He sprang from his chair and paced about the room in
4692𓀴uncontrollable agitation, with a flush upon his sallow cheeks and a
4693𓀴nervous clasping and unclasping of his long thin hands.
4694𓀴
4695𓀴“They must be cunning devils,” he exclaimed at last. “How could they
4696𓀴have decoyed him down there? The Embankment is not on the direct line
4697𓀴to the station. The bridge, no doubt, was too crowded, even on such a
4698𓀴night, for their purpose. Well, Watson, we shall see who will win in
4699𓀴the long run. I am going out now!”
4700𓀴
4701𓀴“To the police?”
4702𓀴
4703𓀴“No; I shall be my own police. When I have spun the web they may take
4704𓀴the flies, but not before.”
4705𓀴
4706𓀴All day I was engaged in my professional work, and it was late in the
4707𓀴evening before I returned to Baker Street. Sherlock Holmes had not come
4708𓀴back yet. It was nearly ten o’clock before he entered, looking pale and
4709𓀴worn. He walked up to the sideboard, and tearing a piece from the loaf
4710𓀴he devoured it voraciously, washing it down with a long draught of
4711𓀴water.
4712𓀴
4713𓀴“You are hungry,” I remarked.
4714𓀴
4715𓀴“Starving. It had escaped my memory. I have had nothing since
4716𓀴breakfast.”
4717𓀴
4718𓀴“Nothing?”
4719𓀴
4720𓀴“Not a bite. I had no time to think of it.”
4721𓀴
4722𓀴“And how have you succeeded?”
4723𓀴
4724𓀴“Well.”
4725𓀴
4726𓀴“You have a clue?”
4727𓀴
4728𓀴“I have them in the hollow of my hand. Young Openshaw shall not long
4729𓀴remain unavenged. Why, Watson, let us put their own devilish trade-mark
4730𓀴upon them. It is well thought of!”
4731𓀴
4732𓀴“What do you mean?”
4733𓀴
4734𓀴He took an orange from the cupboard, and tearing it to pieces he
4735𓀴squeezed out the pips upon the table. Of these he took five and thrust
4736𓀴them into an envelope. On the inside of the flap he wrote “S. H. for J.
4737𓀴O.” Then he sealed it and addressed it to “Captain James Calhoun,
4738𓀴Barque _Lone Star_, Savannah, Georgia.”
4739𓀴
4740𓀴“That will await him when he enters port,” said he, chuckling. “It may
4741𓀴give him a sleepless night. He will find it as sure a precursor of his
4742𓀴fate as Openshaw did before him.”
4743𓀴
4744𓀴“And who is this Captain Calhoun?”
4745𓀴
4746𓀴“The leader of the gang. I shall have the others, but he first.”
4747𓀴
4748𓀴“How did you trace it, then?”
4749𓀴
4750𓀴He took a large sheet of paper from his pocket, all covered with dates
4751𓀴and names.
4752𓀴
4753𓀴“I have spent the whole day,” said he, “over Lloyd’s registers and
4754𓀴files of the old papers, following the future career of every vessel
4755𓀴which touched at Pondicherry in January and February in ’83. There were
4756𓀴thirty-six ships of fair tonnage which were reported there during those
4757𓀴months. Of these, one, the _Lone Star_, instantly attracted my
4758𓀴attention, since, although it was reported as having cleared from
4759𓀴London, the name is that which is given to one of the states of the
4760𓀴Union.”
4761𓀴
4762𓀴“Texas, I think.”
4763𓀴
4764𓀴“I was not and am not sure which; but I knew that the ship must have an
4765𓀴American origin.”
4766𓀴
4767𓀴“What then?”
4768𓀴
4769𓀴“I searched the Dundee records, and when I found that the barque _Lone
4770𓀴Star_ was there in January, ’85, my suspicion became a certainty. I
4771𓀴then inquired as to the vessels which lay at present in the port of
4772𓀴London.”
4773𓀴
4774𓀴“Yes?”
4775𓀴
4776𓀴“The _Lone Star_ had arrived here last week. I went down to the Albert
4777𓀴Dock and found that she had been taken down the river by the early tide
4778𓀴this morning, homeward bound to Savannah. I wired to Gravesend and
4779𓀴learned that she had passed some time ago, and as the wind is easterly
4780𓀴I have no doubt that she is now past the Goodwins and not very far from
4781𓀴the Isle of Wight.”
4782𓀴
4783𓀴“What will you do, then?”
4784𓀴
4785𓀴“Oh, I have my hand upon him. He and the two mates, are as I learn, the
4786𓀴only native-born Americans in the ship. The others are Finns and
4787𓀴Germans. I know, also, that they were all three away from the ship last
4788𓀴night. I had it from the stevedore who has been loading their cargo. By
4789𓀴the time that their sailing-ship reaches Savannah the mail-boat will
4790𓀴have carried this letter, and the cable will have informed the police
4791𓀴of Savannah that these three gentlemen are badly wanted here upon a
4792𓀴charge of murder.”
4793𓀴
4794𓀴There is ever a flaw, however, in the best laid of human plans, and the
4795𓀴murderers of John Openshaw were never to receive the orange pips which
4796𓀴would show them that another, as cunning and as resolute as themselves,
4797𓀴was upon their track. Very long and very severe were the equinoctial
4798𓀴gales that year. We waited long for news of the _Lone Star_ of
4799𓀴Savannah, but none ever reached us. We did at last hear that somewhere
4800𓀴far out in the Atlantic a shattered stern-post of a boat was seen
4801𓀴swinging in the trough of a wave, with the letters “L. S.” carved upon
4802𓀴it, and that is all which we shall ever know of the fate of the _Lone
4803𓀴Star_.
4804𓀴
4805𓀴
4806𓀴
4807𓀴
4808𓀴VI. THE MAN WITH THE TWISTED LIP
4809𓀴
4810𓀴
4811𓀴Isa Whitney, brother of the late Elias Whitney, D.D., Principal of the
4812𓀴Theological College of St. George’s, was much addicted to opium. The
4813𓀴habit grew upon him, as I understand, from some foolish freak when he
4814𓀴was at college; for having read De Quincey’s description of his dreams
4815𓀴and sensations, he had drenched his tobacco with laudanum in an attempt
4816𓀴to produce the same effects. He found, as so many more have done, that
4817𓀴the practice is easier to attain than to get rid of, and for many years
4818𓀴he continued to be a slave to the drug, an object of mingled horror and
4819𓀴pity to his friends and relatives. I can see him now, with yellow,
4820𓀴pasty face, drooping lids, and pin-point pupils, all huddled in a
4821𓀴chair, the wreck and ruin of a noble man.
4822𓀴
4823𓀴One night—it was in June, ’89—there came a ring to my bell, about the
4824𓀴hour when a man gives his first yawn and glances at the clock. I sat up
4825𓀴in my chair, and my wife laid her needle-work down in her lap and made
4826𓀴a little face of disappointment.
4827𓀴
4828𓀴“A patient!” said she. “You’ll have to go out.”
4829𓀴
4830𓀴I groaned, for I was newly come back from a weary day.
4831𓀴
4832𓀴We heard the door open, a few hurried words, and then quick steps upon
4833𓀴the linoleum. Our own door flew open, and a lady, clad in some
4834𓀴dark-coloured stuff, with a black veil, entered the room.
4835𓀴
4836𓀴“You will excuse my calling so late,” she began, and then, suddenly
4837𓀴losing her self-control, she ran forward, threw her arms about my
4838𓀴wife’s neck, and sobbed upon her shoulder. “Oh, I’m in such trouble!”
4839𓀴she cried; “I do so want a little help.”
4840𓀴
4841𓀴“Why,” said my wife, pulling up her veil, “it is Kate Whitney. How you
4842𓀴startled me, Kate! I had not an idea who you were when you came in.”
4843𓀴
4844𓀴“I didn’t know what to do, so I came straight to you.” That was always
4845𓀴the way. Folk who were in grief came to my wife like birds to a
4846𓀴lighthouse.
4847𓀴
4848𓀴“It was very sweet of you to come. Now, you must have some wine and
4849𓀴water, and sit here comfortably and tell us all about it. Or should you
4850𓀴rather that I sent James off to bed?”
4851𓀴
4852𓀴“Oh, no, no! I want the doctor’s advice and help, too. It’s about Isa.
4853𓀴He has not been home for two days. I am so frightened about him!”
4854𓀴
4855𓀴It was not the first time that she had spoken to us of her husband’s
4856𓀴trouble, to me as a doctor, to my wife as an old friend and school
4857𓀴companion. We soothed and comforted her by such words as we could find.
4858𓀴Did she know where her husband was? Was it possible that we could bring
4859𓀴him back to her?
4860𓀴
4861𓀴It seems that it was. She had the surest information that of late he
4862𓀴had, when the fit was on him, made use of an opium den in the farthest
4863𓀴east of the City. Hitherto his orgies had always been confined to one
4864𓀴day, and he had come back, twitching and shattered, in the evening. But
4865𓀴now the spell had been upon him eight-and-forty hours, and he lay
4866𓀴there, doubtless among the dregs of the docks, breathing in the poison
4867𓀴or sleeping off the effects. There he was to be found, she was sure of
4868𓀴it, at the Bar of Gold, in Upper Swandam Lane. But what was she to do?
4869𓀴How could she, a young and timid woman, make her way into such a place
4870𓀴and pluck her husband out from among the ruffians who surrounded him?
4871𓀴
4872𓀴There was the case, and of course there was but one way out of it.
4873𓀴Might I not escort her to this place? And then, as a second thought,
4874𓀴why should she come at all? I was Isa Whitney’s medical adviser, and as
4875𓀴such I had influence over him. I could manage it better if I were
4876𓀴alone. I promised her on my word that I would send him home in a cab
4877𓀴within two hours if he were indeed at the address which she had given
4878𓀴me. And so in ten minutes I had left my armchair and cheery
4879𓀴sitting-room behind me, and was speeding eastward in a hansom on a
4880𓀴strange errand, as it seemed to me at the time, though the future only
4881𓀴could show how strange it was to be.
4882𓀴
4883𓀴But there was no great difficulty in the first stage of my adventure.
4884𓀴Upper Swandam Lane is a vile alley lurking behind the high wharves
4885𓀴which line the north side of the river to the east of London Bridge.
4886𓀴Between a slop-shop and a gin-shop, approached by a steep flight of
4887𓀴steps leading down to a black gap like the mouth of a cave, I found the
4888𓀴den of which I was in search. Ordering my cab to wait, I passed down
4889𓀴the steps, worn hollow in the centre by the ceaseless tread of drunken
4890𓀴feet; and by the light of a flickering oil-lamp above the door I found
4891𓀴the latch and made my way into a long, low room, thick and heavy with
4892𓀴the brown opium smoke, and terraced with wooden berths, like the
4893𓀴forecastle of an emigrant ship.
4894𓀴
4895𓀴Through the gloom one could dimly catch a glimpse of bodies lying in
4896𓀴strange fantastic poses, bowed shoulders, bent knees, heads thrown
4897𓀴back, and chins pointing upward, with here and there a dark,
4898𓀴lack-lustre eye turned upon the newcomer. Out of the black shadows
4899𓀴there glimmered little red circles of light, now bright, now faint, as
4900𓀴the burning poison waxed or waned in the bowls of the metal pipes. The
4901𓀴most lay silent, but some muttered to themselves, and others talked
4902𓀴together in a strange, low, monotonous voice, their conversation coming
4903𓀴in gushes, and then suddenly tailing off into silence, each mumbling
4904𓀴out his own thoughts and paying little heed to the words of his
4905𓀴neighbour. At the farther end was a small brazier of burning charcoal,
4906𓀴beside which on a three-legged wooden stool there sat a tall, thin old
4907𓀴man, with his jaw resting upon his two fists, and his elbows upon his
4908𓀴knees, staring into the fire.
4909𓀴
4910𓀴As I entered, a sallow Malay attendant had hurried up with a pipe for
4911𓀴me and a supply of the drug, beckoning me to an empty berth.
4912𓀴
4913𓀴“Thank you. I have not come to stay,” said I. “There is a friend of
4914𓀴mine here, Mr. Isa Whitney, and I wish to speak with him.”
4915𓀴
4916𓀴There was a movement and an exclamation from my right, and peering
4917𓀴through the gloom, I saw Whitney, pale, haggard, and unkempt, staring
4918𓀴out at me.
4919𓀴
4920𓀴“My God! It’s Watson,” said he. He was in a pitiable state of reaction,
4921𓀴with every nerve in a twitter. “I say, Watson, what o’clock is it?”
4922𓀴
4923𓀴“Nearly eleven.”
4924𓀴
4925𓀴“Of what day?”
4926𓀴
4927𓀴“Of Friday, June 19th.”
4928𓀴
4929𓀴“Good heavens! I thought it was Wednesday. It is Wednesday. What d’you
4930𓀴want to frighten a chap for?” He sank his face onto his arms and began
4931𓀴to sob in a high treble key.
4932𓀴
4933𓀴“I tell you that it is Friday, man. Your wife has been waiting this two
4934𓀴days for you. You should be ashamed of yourself!”
4935𓀴
4936𓀴“So I am. But you’ve got mixed, Watson, for I have only been here a few
4937𓀴hours, three pipes, four pipes—I forget how many. But I’ll go home with
4938𓀴you. I wouldn’t frighten Kate—poor little Kate. Give me your hand! Have
4939𓀴you a cab?”
4940𓀴
4941𓀴“Yes, I have one waiting.”
4942𓀴
4943𓀴“Then I shall go in it. But I must owe something. Find what I owe,
4944𓀴Watson. I am all off colour. I can do nothing for myself.”
4945𓀴
4946𓀴I walked down the narrow passage between the double row of sleepers,
4947𓀴holding my breath to keep out the vile, stupefying fumes of the drug,
4948𓀴and looking about for the manager. As I passed the tall man who sat by
4949𓀴the brazier I felt a sudden pluck at my skirt, and a low voice
4950𓀴whispered, “Walk past me, and then look back at me.” The words fell
4951𓀴quite distinctly upon my ear. I glanced down. They could only have come
4952𓀴from the old man at my side, and yet he sat now as absorbed as ever,
4953𓀴very thin, very wrinkled, bent with age, an opium pipe dangling down
4954𓀴from between his knees, as though it had dropped in sheer lassitude
4955𓀴from his fingers. I took two steps forward and looked back. It took all
4956𓀴my self-control to prevent me from breaking out into a cry of
4957𓀴astonishment. He had turned his back so that none could see him but I.
4958𓀴His form had filled out, his wrinkles were gone, the dull eyes had
4959𓀴regained their fire, and there, sitting by the fire and grinning at my
4960𓀴surprise, was none other than Sherlock Holmes. He made a slight motion
4961𓀴to me to approach him, and instantly, as he turned his face half round
4962𓀴to the company once more, subsided into a doddering, loose-lipped
4963𓀴senility.
4964𓀴
4965𓀴“Holmes!” I whispered, “what on earth are you doing in this den?”
4966𓀴
4967𓀴“As low as you can,” he answered; “I have excellent ears. If you would
4968𓀴have the great kindness to get rid of that sottish friend of yours I
4969𓀴should be exceedingly glad to have a little talk with you.”
4970𓀴
4971𓀴“I have a cab outside.”
4972𓀴
4973𓀴“Then pray send him home in it. You may safely trust him, for he
4974𓀴appears to be too limp to get into any mischief. I should recommend you
4975𓀴also to send a note by the cabman to your wife to say that you have
4976𓀴thrown in your lot with me. If you will wait outside, I shall be with
4977𓀴you in five minutes.”
4978𓀴
4979𓀴It was difficult to refuse any of Sherlock Holmes’ requests, for they
4980𓀴were always so exceedingly definite, and put forward with such a quiet
4981𓀴air of mastery. I felt, however, that when Whitney was once confined in
4982𓀴the cab my mission was practically accomplished; and for the rest, I
4983𓀴could not wish anything better than to be associated with my friend in
4984𓀴one of those singular adventures which were the normal condition of his
4985𓀴existence. In a few minutes I had written my note, paid Whitney’s bill,
4986𓀴led him out to the cab, and seen him driven through the darkness. In a
4987𓀴very short time a decrepit figure had emerged from the opium den, and I
4988𓀴was walking down the street with Sherlock Holmes. For two streets he
4989𓀴shuffled along with a bent back and an uncertain foot. Then, glancing
4990𓀴quickly round, he straightened himself out and burst into a hearty fit
4991𓀴of laughter.
4992𓀴
4993𓀴“I suppose, Watson,” said he, “that you imagine that I have added
4994𓀴opium-smoking to cocaine injections, and all the other little
4995𓀴weaknesses on which you have favoured me with your medical views.”
4996𓀴
4997𓀴“I was certainly surprised to find you there.”
4998𓀴
4999𓀴“But not more so than I to find you.”
5000𓀴
5001𓀴“I came to find a friend.”
5002𓀴
5003𓀴“And I to find an enemy.”
5004𓀴
5005𓀴“An enemy?”
5006𓀴
5007𓀴“Yes; one of my natural enemies, or, shall I say, my natural prey.
5008𓀴Briefly, Watson, I am in the midst of a very remarkable inquiry, and I
5009𓀴have hoped to find a clue in the incoherent ramblings of these sots, as
5010𓀴I have done before now. Had I been recognised in that den my life would
5011𓀴not have been worth an hour’s purchase; for I have used it before now
5012𓀴for my own purposes, and the rascally Lascar who runs it has sworn to
5013𓀴have vengeance upon me. There is a trap-door at the back of that
5014𓀴building, near the corner of Paul’s Wharf, which could tell some
5015𓀴strange tales of what has passed through it upon the moonless nights.”
5016𓀴
5017𓀴“What! You do not mean bodies?”
5018𓀴
5019𓀴“Ay, bodies, Watson. We should be rich men if we had £ 1000 for every
5020𓀴poor devil who has been done to death in that den. It is the vilest
5021𓀴murder-trap on the whole riverside, and I fear that Neville St. Clair
5022𓀴has entered it never to leave it more. But our trap should be here.” He
5023𓀴put his two forefingers between his teeth and whistled shrilly—a signal
5024𓀴which was answered by a similar whistle from the distance, followed
5025𓀴shortly by the rattle of wheels and the clink of horses’ hoofs.
5026𓀴
5027𓀴“Now, Watson,” said Holmes, as a tall dog-cart dashed up through the
5028𓀴gloom, throwing out two golden tunnels of yellow light from its side
5029𓀴lanterns. “You’ll come with me, won’t you?”
5030𓀴
5031𓀴“If I can be of use.”
5032𓀴
5033𓀴“Oh, a trusty comrade is always of use; and a chronicler still more so.
5034𓀴My room at The Cedars is a double-bedded one.”
5035𓀴
5036𓀴“The Cedars?”
5037𓀴
5038𓀴“Yes; that is Mr. St. Clair’s house. I am staying there while I conduct
5039𓀴the inquiry.”
5040𓀴
5041𓀴“Where is it, then?”
5042𓀴
5043𓀴“Near Lee, in Kent. We have a seven-mile drive before us.”
5044𓀴
5045𓀴“But I am all in the dark.”
5046𓀴
5047𓀴“Of course you are. You’ll know all about it presently. Jump up here.
5048𓀴All right, John; we shall not need you. Here’s half a crown. Look out
5049𓀴for me to-morrow, about eleven. Give her her head. So long, then!”
5050𓀴
5051𓀴He flicked the horse with his whip, and we dashed away through the
5052𓀴endless succession of sombre and deserted streets, which widened
5053𓀴gradually, until we were flying across a broad balustraded bridge, with
5054𓀴the murky river flowing sluggishly beneath us. Beyond lay another dull
5055𓀴wilderness of bricks and mortar, its silence broken only by the heavy,
5056𓀴regular footfall of the policeman, or the songs and shouts of some
5057𓀴belated party of revellers. A dull wrack was drifting slowly across the
5058𓀴sky, and a star or two twinkled dimly here and there through the rifts
5059𓀴of the clouds. Holmes drove in silence, with his head sunk upon his
5060𓀴breast, and the air of a man who is lost in thought, while I sat beside
5061𓀴him, curious to learn what this new quest might be which seemed to tax
5062𓀴his powers so sorely, and yet afraid to break in upon the current of
5063𓀴his thoughts. We had driven several miles, and were beginning to get to
5064𓀴the fringe of the belt of suburban villas, when he shook himself,
5065𓀴shrugged his shoulders, and lit up his pipe with the air of a man who
5066𓀴has satisfied himself that he is acting for the best.
5067𓀴
5068𓀴“You have a grand gift of silence, Watson,” said he. “It makes you
5069𓀴quite invaluable as a companion. ’Pon my word, it is a great thing for
5070𓀴me to have someone to talk to, for my own thoughts are not
5071𓀴over-pleasant. I was wondering what I should say to this dear little
5072𓀴woman to-night when she meets me at the door.”
5073𓀴
5074𓀴“You forget that I know nothing about it.”
5075𓀴
5076𓀴“I shall just have time to tell you the facts of the case before we get
5077𓀴to Lee. It seems absurdly simple, and yet, somehow I can get nothing to
5078𓀴go upon. There’s plenty of thread, no doubt, but I can’t get the end of
5079𓀴it into my hand. Now, I’ll state the case clearly and concisely to you,
5080𓀴Watson, and maybe you can see a spark where all is dark to me.”
5081𓀴
5082𓀴“Proceed, then.”
5083𓀴
5084𓀴“Some years ago—to be definite, in May, 1884—there came to Lee a
5085𓀴gentleman, Neville St. Clair by name, who appeared to have plenty of
5086𓀴money. He took a large villa, laid out the grounds very nicely, and
5087𓀴lived generally in good style. By degrees he made friends in the
5088𓀴neighbourhood, and in 1887 he married the daughter of a local brewer,
5089𓀴by whom he now has two children. He had no occupation, but was
5090𓀴interested in several companies and went into town as a rule in the
5091𓀴morning, returning by the 5:14 from Cannon Street every night. Mr. St.
5092𓀴Clair is now thirty-seven years of age, is a man of temperate habits, a
5093𓀴good husband, a very affectionate father, and a man who is popular with
5094𓀴all who know him. I may add that his whole debts at the present moment,
5095𓀴as far as we have been able to ascertain, amount to £ 88 10_s_., while
5096𓀴he has £ 220 standing to his credit in the Capital and Counties Bank.
5097𓀴There is no reason, therefore, to think that money troubles have been
5098𓀴weighing upon his mind.
5099𓀴
5100𓀴“Last Monday Mr. Neville St. Clair went into town rather earlier than
5101𓀴usual, remarking before he started that he had two important
5102𓀴commissions to perform, and that he would bring his little boy home a
5103𓀴box of bricks. Now, by the merest chance, his wife received a telegram
5104𓀴upon this same Monday, very shortly after his departure, to the effect
5105𓀴that a small parcel of considerable value which she had been expecting
5106𓀴was waiting for her at the offices of the Aberdeen Shipping Company.
5107𓀴Now, if you are well up in your London, you will know that the office
5108𓀴of the company is in Fresno Street, which branches out of Upper Swandam
5109𓀴Lane, where you found me to-night. Mrs. St. Clair had her lunch,
5110𓀴started for the City, did some shopping, proceeded to the company’s
5111𓀴office, got her packet, and found herself at exactly 4:35 walking
5112𓀴through Swandam Lane on her way back to the station. Have you followed
5113𓀴me so far?”
5114𓀴
5115𓀴“It is very clear.”
5116𓀴
5117𓀴“If you remember, Monday was an exceedingly hot day, and Mrs. St. Clair
5118𓀴walked slowly, glancing about in the hope of seeing a cab, as she did
5119𓀴not like the neighbourhood in which she found herself. While she was
5120𓀴walking in this way down Swandam Lane, she suddenly heard an
5121𓀴ejaculation or cry, and was struck cold to see her husband looking down
5122𓀴at her and, as it seemed to her, beckoning to her from a second-floor
5123𓀴window. The window was open, and she distinctly saw his face, which she
5124𓀴describes as being terribly agitated. He waved his hands frantically to
5125𓀴her, and then vanished from the window so suddenly that it seemed to
5126𓀴her that he had been plucked back by some irresistible force from
5127𓀴behind. One singular point which struck her quick feminine eye was that
5128𓀴although he wore some dark coat, such as he had started to town in, he
5129𓀴had on neither collar nor necktie.
5130𓀴
5131𓀴“Convinced that something was amiss with him, she rushed down the
5132𓀴steps—for the house was none other than the opium den in which you
5133𓀴found me to-night—and running through the front room she attempted to
5134𓀴ascend the stairs which led to the first floor. At the foot of the
5135𓀴stairs, however, she met this Lascar scoundrel of whom I have spoken,
5136𓀴who thrust her back and, aided by a Dane, who acts as assistant there,
5137𓀴pushed her out into the street. Filled with the most maddening doubts
5138𓀴and fears, she rushed down the lane and, by rare good-fortune, met in
5139𓀴Fresno Street a number of constables with an inspector, all on their
5140𓀴way to their beat. The inspector and two men accompanied her back, and
5141𓀴in spite of the continued resistance of the proprietor, they made their
5142𓀴way to the room in which Mr. St. Clair had last been seen. There was no
5143𓀴sign of him there. In fact, in the whole of that floor there was no one
5144𓀴to be found save a crippled wretch of hideous aspect, who, it seems,
5145𓀴made his home there. Both he and the Lascar stoutly swore that no one
5146𓀴else had been in the front room during the afternoon. So determined was
5147𓀴their denial that the inspector was staggered, and had almost come to
5148𓀴believe that Mrs. St. Clair had been deluded when, with a cry, she
5149𓀴sprang at a small deal box which lay upon the table and tore the lid
5150𓀴from it. Out there fell a cascade of children’s bricks. It was the toy
5151𓀴which he had promised to bring home.
5152𓀴
5153𓀴“This discovery, and the evident confusion which the cripple showed,
5154𓀴made the inspector realise that the matter was serious. The rooms were
5155𓀴carefully examined, and results all pointed to an abominable crime. The
5156𓀴front room was plainly furnished as a sitting-room and led into a small
5157𓀴bedroom, which looked out upon the back of one of the wharves. Between
5158𓀴the wharf and the bedroom window is a narrow strip, which is dry at low
5159𓀴tide but is covered at high tide with at least four and a half feet of
5160𓀴water. The bedroom window was a broad one and opened from below. On
5161𓀴examination traces of blood were to be seen upon the windowsill, and
5162𓀴several scattered drops were visible upon the wooden floor of the
5163𓀴bedroom. Thrust away behind a curtain in the front room were all the
5164𓀴clothes of Mr. Neville St. Clair, with the exception of his coat. His
5165𓀴boots, his socks, his hat, and his watch—all were there. There were no
5166𓀴signs of violence upon any of these garments, and there were no other
5167𓀴traces of Mr. Neville St. Clair. Out of the window he must apparently
5168𓀴have gone for no other exit could be discovered, and the ominous
5169𓀴bloodstains upon the sill gave little promise that he could save
5170𓀴himself by swimming, for the tide was at its very highest at the moment
5171𓀴of the tragedy.
5172𓀴
5173𓀴“And now as to the villains who seemed to be immediately implicated in
5174𓀴the matter. The Lascar was known to be a man of the vilest antecedents,
5175𓀴but as, by Mrs. St. Clair’s story, he was known to have been at the
5176𓀴foot of the stair within a very few seconds of her husband’s appearance
5177𓀴at the window, he could hardly have been more than an accessory to the
5178𓀴crime. His defence was one of absolute ignorance, and he protested that
5179𓀴he had no knowledge as to the doings of Hugh Boone, his lodger, and
5180𓀴that he could not account in any way for the presence of the missing
5181𓀴gentleman’s clothes.
5182𓀴
5183𓀴“So much for the Lascar manager. Now for the sinister cripple who lives
5184𓀴upon the second floor of the opium den, and who was certainly the last
5185𓀴human being whose eyes rested upon Neville St. Clair. His name is Hugh
5186𓀴Boone, and his hideous face is one which is familiar to every man who
5187𓀴goes much to the City. He is a professional beggar, though in order to
5188𓀴avoid the police regulations he pretends to a small trade in wax
5189𓀴vestas. Some little distance down Threadneedle Street, upon the
5190𓀴left-hand side, there is, as you may have remarked, a small angle in
5191𓀴the wall. Here it is that this creature takes his daily seat,
5192𓀴cross-legged with his tiny stock of matches on his lap, and as he is a
5193𓀴piteous spectacle a small rain of charity descends into the greasy
5194𓀴leather cap which lies upon the pavement beside him. I have watched the
5195𓀴fellow more than once before ever I thought of making his professional
5196𓀴acquaintance, and I have been surprised at the harvest which he has
5197𓀴reaped in a short time. His appearance, you see, is so remarkable that
5198𓀴no one can pass him without observing him. A shock of orange hair, a
5199𓀴pale face disfigured by a horrible scar, which, by its contraction, has
5200𓀴turned up the outer edge of his upper lip, a bulldog chin, and a pair
5201𓀴of very penetrating dark eyes, which present a singular contrast to the
5202𓀴colour of his hair, all mark him out from amid the common crowd of
5203𓀴mendicants and so, too, does his wit, for he is ever ready with a reply
5204𓀴to any piece of chaff which may be thrown at him by the passers-by.
5205𓀴This is the man whom we now learn to have been the lodger at the opium
5206𓀴den, and to have been the last man to see the gentleman of whom we are
5207𓀴in quest.”
5208𓀴
5209𓀴“But a cripple!” said I. “What could he have done single-handed against
5210𓀴a man in the prime of life?”
5211𓀴
5212𓀴“He is a cripple in the sense that he walks with a limp; but in other
5213𓀴respects he appears to be a powerful and well-nurtured man. Surely your
5214𓀴medical experience would tell you, Watson, that weakness in one limb is
5215𓀴often compensated for by exceptional strength in the others.”
5216𓀴
5217𓀴“Pray continue your narrative.”
5218𓀴
5219𓀴“Mrs. St. Clair had fainted at the sight of the blood upon the window,
5220𓀴and she was escorted home in a cab by the police, as her presence could
5221𓀴be of no help to them in their investigations. Inspector Barton, who
5222𓀴had charge of the case, made a very careful examination of the
5223𓀴premises, but without finding anything which threw any light upon the
5224𓀴matter. One mistake had been made in not arresting Boone instantly, as
5225𓀴he was allowed some few minutes during which he might have communicated
5226𓀴with his friend the Lascar, but this fault was soon remedied, and he
5227𓀴was seized and searched, without anything being found which could
5228𓀴incriminate him. There were, it is true, some blood-stains upon his
5229𓀴right shirt-sleeve, but he pointed to his ring-finger, which had been
5230𓀴cut near the nail, and explained that the bleeding came from there,
5231𓀴adding that he had been to the window not long before, and that the
5232𓀴stains which had been observed there came doubtless from the same
5233𓀴source. He denied strenuously having ever seen Mr. Neville St. Clair
5234𓀴and swore that the presence of the clothes in his room was as much a
5235𓀴mystery to him as to the police. As to Mrs. St. Clair’s assertion that
5236𓀴she had actually seen her husband at the window, he declared that she
5237𓀴must have been either mad or dreaming. He was removed, loudly
5238𓀴protesting, to the police-station, while the inspector remained upon
5239𓀴the premises in the hope that the ebbing tide might afford some fresh
5240𓀴clue.
5241𓀴
5242𓀴“And it did, though they hardly found upon the mud-bank what they had
5243𓀴feared to find. It was Neville St. Clair’s coat, and not Neville St.
5244𓀴Clair, which lay uncovered as the tide receded. And what do you think
5245𓀴they found in the pockets?”
5246𓀴
5247𓀴“I cannot imagine.”
5248𓀴
5249𓀴“No, I don’t think you would guess. Every pocket stuffed with pennies
5250𓀴and half-pennies—421 pennies and 270 half-pennies. It was no wonder
5251𓀴that it had not been swept away by the tide. But a human body is a
5252𓀴different matter. There is a fierce eddy between the wharf and the
5253𓀴house. It seemed likely enough that the weighted coat had remained when
5254𓀴the stripped body had been sucked away into the river.”
5255𓀴
5256𓀴“But I understand that all the other clothes were found in the room.
5257𓀴Would the body be dressed in a coat alone?”
5258𓀴
5259𓀴“No, sir, but the facts might be met speciously enough. Suppose that
5260𓀴this man Boone had thrust Neville St. Clair through the window, there
5261𓀴is no human eye which could have seen the deed. What would he do then?
5262𓀴It would of course instantly strike him that he must get rid of the
5263𓀴tell-tale garments. He would seize the coat, then, and be in the act of
5264𓀴throwing it out, when it would occur to him that it would swim and not
5265𓀴sink. He has little time, for he has heard the scuffle downstairs when
5266𓀴the wife tried to force her way up, and perhaps he has already heard
5267𓀴from his Lascar confederate that the police are hurrying up the street.
5268𓀴There is not an instant to be lost. He rushes to some secret hoard,
5269𓀴where he has accumulated the fruits of his beggary, and he stuffs all
5270𓀴the coins upon which he can lay his hands into the pockets to make sure
5271𓀴of the coat’s sinking. He throws it out, and would have done the same
5272𓀴with the other garments had not he heard the rush of steps below, and
5273𓀴only just had time to close the window when the police appeared.”
5274𓀴
5275𓀴“It certainly sounds feasible.”
5276𓀴
5277𓀴“Well, we will take it as a working hypothesis for want of a better.
5278𓀴Boone, as I have told you, was arrested and taken to the station, but
5279𓀴it could not be shown that there had ever before been anything against
5280𓀴him. He had for years been known as a professional beggar, but his life
5281𓀴appeared to have been a very quiet and innocent one. There the matter
5282𓀴stands at present, and the questions which have to be solved—what
5283𓀴Neville St. Clair was doing in the opium den, what happened to him when
5284𓀴there, where is he now, and what Hugh Boone had to do with his
5285𓀴disappearance—are all as far from a solution as ever. I confess that I
5286𓀴cannot recall any case within my experience which looked at the first
5287𓀴glance so simple and yet which presented such difficulties.”
5288𓀴
5289𓀴While Sherlock Holmes had been detailing this singular series of
5290𓀴events, we had been whirling through the outskirts of the great town
5291𓀴until the last straggling houses had been left behind, and we rattled
5292𓀴along with a country hedge upon either side of us. Just as he finished,
5293𓀴however, we drove through two scattered villages, where a few lights
5294𓀴still glimmered in the windows.
5295𓀴
5296𓀴“We are on the outskirts of Lee,” said my companion. “We have touched
5297𓀴on three English counties in our short drive, starting in Middlesex,
5298𓀴passing over an angle of Surrey, and ending in Kent. See that light
5299𓀴among the trees? That is The Cedars, and beside that lamp sits a woman
5300𓀴whose anxious ears have already, I have little doubt, caught the clink
5301𓀴of our horse’s feet.”
5302𓀴
5303𓀴“But why are you not conducting the case from Baker Street?” I asked.
5304𓀴
5305𓀴“Because there are many inquiries which must be made out here. Mrs. St.
5306𓀴Clair has most kindly put two rooms at my disposal, and you may rest
5307𓀴assured that she will have nothing but a welcome for my friend and
5308𓀴colleague. I hate to meet her, Watson, when I have no news of her
5309𓀴husband. Here we are. Whoa, there, whoa!”
5310𓀴
5311𓀴We had pulled up in front of a large villa which stood within its own
5312𓀴grounds. A stable-boy had run out to the horse’s head, and springing
5313𓀴down, I followed Holmes up the small, winding gravel-drive which led to
5314𓀴the house. As we approached, the door flew open, and a little blonde
5315𓀴woman stood in the opening, clad in some sort of light mousseline de
5316𓀴soie, with a touch of fluffy pink chiffon at her neck and wrists. She
5317𓀴stood with her figure outlined against the flood of light, one hand
5318𓀴upon the door, one half-raised in her eagerness, her body slightly
5319𓀴bent, her head and face protruded, with eager eyes and parted lips, a
5320𓀴standing question.
5321𓀴
5322𓀴“Well?” she cried, “well?” And then, seeing that there were two of us,
5323𓀴she gave a cry of hope which sank into a groan as she saw that my
5324𓀴companion shook his head and shrugged his shoulders.
5325𓀴
5326𓀴“No good news?”
5327𓀴
5328𓀴“None.”
5329𓀴
5330𓀴“No bad?”
5331𓀴
5332𓀴“No.”
5333𓀴
5334𓀴“Thank God for that. But come in. You must be weary, for you have had a
5335𓀴long day.”
5336𓀴
5337𓀴“This is my friend, Dr. Watson. He has been of most vital use to me in
5338𓀴several of my cases, and a lucky chance has made it possible for me to
5339𓀴bring him out and associate him with this investigation.”
5340𓀴

5342𓀴will, I am sure, forgive anything that may be wanting in our
5343𓀴arrangements, when you consider the blow which has come so suddenly
5344𓀴upon us.”
5345𓀴
5346𓀴“My dear madam,” said I, “I am an old campaigner, and if I were not I
5347𓀴can very well see that no apology is needed. If I can be of any
5348𓀴assistance, either to you or to my friend here, I shall be indeed
5349𓀴happy.”
5350𓀴
5351𓀴“Now, Mr. Sherlock Holmes,” said the lady as we entered a well-lit
5352𓀴dining-room, upon the table of which a cold supper had been laid out,

5354𓀴which I beg that you will give a plain answer.”
5355𓀴
5356𓀴“Certainly, madam.”
5357𓀴
5358𓀴“Do not trouble about my feelings. I am not hysterical, nor given to
5359𓀴fainting. I simply wish to hear your real, real opinion.”
5360𓀴
5361𓀴“Upon what point?”
5362𓀴

5364𓀴
5365𓀴Sherlock Holmes seemed to be embarrassed by the question. “Frankly,
5366𓀴now!” she repeated, standing upon the rug and looking keenly down at
5367𓀴him as he leaned back in a basket-chair.
5368𓀴

5370𓀴
5371𓀴“You think that he is dead?”
5372𓀴
5373𓀴“I do.”
5374𓀴
5375𓀴“Murdered?”
5376𓀴
5377𓀴“I don’t say that. Perhaps.”
5378𓀴
5379𓀴“And on what day did he meet his death?”
5380𓀴
5381𓀴“On Monday.”
5382𓀴
5383𓀴“Then perhaps, Mr. Holmes, you will be good enough to explain how it is
5384𓀴that I have received a letter from him to-day.”
5385𓀴

5387𓀴

5389𓀴

5392𓀴

5394𓀴
5395𓀴“Certainly.”
5396𓀴
5397𓀴He snatched it from her in his eagerness, and smoothing it out upon the

5399𓀴chair and was gazing at it over his shoulder. The envelope was a very
5400𓀴coarse one and was stamped with the Gravesend postmark and with the
5401𓀴date of that very day, or rather of the day before, for it was
5402𓀴considerably after midnight.
5403𓀴

5405𓀴writing, madam.”
5406𓀴

5408𓀴
5409𓀴“I perceive also that whoever addressed the envelope had to go and
5410𓀴inquire as to the address.”
5411𓀴

5413𓀴
5414𓀴“The name, you see, is in perfectly black ink, which has dried itself.
5415𓀴The rest is of the greyish colour, which shows that blotting-paper has
5416𓀴been used. If it had been written straight off, and then blotted, none
5417𓀴would be of a deep black shade. This man has written the name, and
5418𓀴there has then been a pause before he wrote the address, which can only
5419𓀴mean that he was not familiar with it. It is, of course, a trifle, but
5420𓀴there is nothing so important as trifles. Let us now see the letter.
5421𓀴Ha! there has been an enclosure here!”
5422𓀴

5424𓀴

5428𓀴

5437𓀴octavo size, no water-mark. Hum! Posted to-day in Gravesend by a man

5439𓀴much in error, by a person who had been chewing tobacco. And you have

5441𓀴
5442𓀴“None. Neville wrote those words.”
5443𓀴
5444𓀴“And they were posted to-day at Gravesend. Well, Mrs. St. Clair, the

5447𓀴

5449𓀴

5454𓀴

5457𓀴
5458𓀴“That is possible.”
5459𓀴

5461𓀴

5469𓀴

5475𓀴

5477𓀴

5513𓀴“No, but this horrible man confessed to having been there, and the

5530𓀴I wished to be absolutely clear. We shall now have a little supper and

5532𓀴
5533𓀴A large and comfortable double-bedded room had been placed at our
5534𓀴disposal, and I was quickly between the sheets, for I was weary after
5535𓀴my night of adventure. Sherlock Holmes was a man, however, who, when he
5536𓀴had an unsolved problem upon his mind, would go for days, and even for
5537𓀴a week, without rest, turning it over, rearranging his facts, looking
5538𓀴at it from every point of view until he had either fathomed it or
5539𓀴convinced himself that his data were insufficient. It was soon evident
5540𓀴to me that he was now preparing for an all-night sitting. He took off
5541𓀴his coat and waistcoat, put on a large blue dressing-gown, and then
5542𓀴wandered about the room collecting pillows from his bed and cushions
5543𓀴from the sofa and armchairs. With these he constructed a sort of
5544𓀴Eastern divan, upon which he perched himself cross-legged, with an
5545𓀴ounce of shag tobacco and a box of matches laid out in front of him. In
5546𓀴the dim light of the lamp I saw him sitting there, an old briar pipe
5547𓀴between his lips, his eyes fixed vacantly upon the corner of the
5548𓀴ceiling, the blue smoke curling up from him, silent, motionless, with
5549𓀴the light shining upon his strong-set aquiline features. So he sat as I
5550𓀴dropped off to sleep, and so he sat when a sudden ejaculation caused me
5551𓀴to wake up, and I found the summer sun shining into the apartment. The
5552𓀴pipe was still between his lips, the smoke still curled upward, and the
5553𓀴room was full of a dense tobacco haze, but nothing remained of the heap
5554𓀴of shag which I had seen upon the previous night.
5555𓀴
5556𓀴“Awake, Watson?” he asked.
5557𓀴
5558𓀴“Yes.”
5559𓀴
5560𓀴“Game for a morning drive?”
5561𓀴
5562𓀴“Certainly.”
5563𓀴
5564𓀴“Then dress. No one is stirring yet, but I know where the stable-boy
5565𓀴sleeps, and we shall soon have the trap out.” He chuckled to himself as
5566𓀴he spoke, his eyes twinkled, and he seemed a different man to the
5567𓀴sombre thinker of the previous night.
5568𓀴
5569𓀴As I dressed I glanced at my watch. It was no wonder that no one was
5570𓀴stirring. It was twenty-five minutes past four. I had hardly finished
5571𓀴when Holmes returned with the news that the boy was putting in the
5572𓀴horse.
5573𓀴
5574𓀴“I want to test a little theory of mine,” said he, pulling on his
5575𓀴boots. “I think, Watson, that you are now standing in the presence of
5576𓀴one of the most absolute fools in Europe. I deserve to be kicked from
5577𓀴here to Charing Cross. But I think I have the key of the affair now.”
5578𓀴
5579𓀴“And where is it?” I asked, smiling.
5580𓀴
5581𓀴“In the bathroom,” he answered. “Oh, yes, I am not joking,” he
5582𓀴continued, seeing my look of incredulity. “I have just been there, and
5583𓀴I have taken it out, and I have got it in this Gladstone bag. Come on,
5584𓀴my boy, and we shall see whether it will not fit the lock.”
5585𓀴
5586𓀴We made our way downstairs as quietly as possible, and out into the
5587𓀴bright morning sunshine. In the road stood our horse and trap, with the
5588𓀴half-clad stable-boy waiting at the head. We both sprang in, and away
5589𓀴we dashed down the London Road. A few country carts were stirring,
5590𓀴bearing in vegetables to the metropolis, but the lines of villas on
5591𓀴either side were as silent and lifeless as some city in a dream.
5592𓀴
5593𓀴“It has been in some points a singular case,” said Holmes, flicking the
5594𓀴horse on into a gallop. “I confess that I have been as blind as a mole,
5595𓀴but it is better to learn wisdom late than never to learn it at all.”
5596𓀴
5597𓀴In town the earliest risers were just beginning to look sleepily from
5598𓀴their windows as we drove through the streets of the Surrey side.
5599𓀴Passing down the Waterloo Bridge Road we crossed over the river, and
5600𓀴dashing up Wellington Street wheeled sharply to the right and found
5601𓀴ourselves in Bow Street. Sherlock Holmes was well known to the force,
5602𓀴and the two constables at the door saluted him. One of them held the
5603𓀴horse’s head while the other led us in.
5604𓀴
5605𓀴“Who is on duty?” asked Holmes.
5606𓀴
5607𓀴“Inspector Bradstreet, sir.”
5608𓀴
5609𓀴“Ah, Bradstreet, how are you?” A tall, stout official had come down the
5610𓀴stone-flagged passage, in a peaked cap and frogged jacket. “I wish to
5611𓀴have a quiet word with you, Bradstreet.”
5612𓀴
5613𓀴“Certainly, Mr. Holmes. Step into my room here.”
5614𓀴
5615𓀴It was a small, office-like room, with a huge ledger upon the table,
5616𓀴and a telephone projecting from the wall. The inspector sat down at his
5617𓀴desk.
5618𓀴
5619𓀴“What can I do for you, Mr. Holmes?”
5620𓀴
5621𓀴“I called about that beggarman, Boone—the one who was charged with
5622𓀴being concerned in the disappearance of Mr. Neville St. Clair, of Lee.”
5623𓀴
5624𓀴“Yes. He was brought up and remanded for further inquiries.”
5625𓀴
5626𓀴“So I heard. You have him here?”
5627𓀴
5628𓀴“In the cells.”
5629𓀴
5630𓀴“Is he quiet?”
5631𓀴
5632𓀴“Oh, he gives no trouble. But he is a dirty scoundrel.”
5633𓀴
5634𓀴“Dirty?”
5635𓀴
5636𓀴“Yes, it is all we can do to make him wash his hands, and his face is
5637𓀴as black as a tinker’s. Well, when once his case has been settled, he
5638𓀴will have a regular prison bath; and I think, if you saw him, you would
5639𓀴agree with me that he needed it.”
5640𓀴
5641𓀴“I should like to see him very much.”
5642𓀴
5643𓀴“Would you? That is easily done. Come this way. You can leave your
5644𓀴bag.”
5645𓀴
5646𓀴“No, I think that I’ll take it.”
5647𓀴
