5648𓀴“Very good. Come this way, if you please.” He led us down a passage,
5649𓀴opened a barred door, passed down a winding stair, and brought us to a
5650𓀴whitewashed corridor with a line of doors on each side.
5651𓀴
5652𓀴“The third on the right is his,” said the inspector. “Here it is!” He
5653𓀴quietly shot back a panel in the upper part of the door and glanced
5654𓀴through.
5655𓀴
5656𓀴“He is asleep,” said he. “You can see him very well.”
5657𓀴
5658𓀴We both put our eyes to the grating. The prisoner lay with his face
5659𓀴towards us, in a very deep sleep, breathing slowly and heavily. He was
5660𓀴a middle-sized man, coarsely clad as became his calling, with a
5661𓀴coloured shirt protruding through the rent in his tattered coat. He
5662𓀴was, as the inspector had said, extremely dirty, but the grime which
5663𓀴covered his face could not conceal its repulsive ugliness. A broad
5664𓀴wheal from an old scar ran right across it from eye to chin, and by its
5665𓀴contraction had turned up one side of the upper lip, so that three
5666𓀴teeth were exposed in a perpetual snarl. A shock of very bright red
5667𓀴hair grew low over his eyes and forehead.
5668𓀴
5669𓀴“He’s a beauty, isn’t he?” said the inspector.
5670𓀴
5671𓀴“He certainly needs a wash,” remarked Holmes. “I had an idea that he
5672𓀴might, and I took the liberty of bringing the tools with me.” He opened
5673𓀴the Gladstone bag as he spoke, and took out, to my astonishment, a very
5674𓀴large bath-sponge.
5675𓀴
5676𓀴“He! he! You are a funny one,” chuckled the inspector.
5677𓀴
5678𓀴“Now, if you will have the great goodness to open that door very
5679𓀴quietly, we will soon make him cut a much more respectable figure.”
5680𓀴
5681𓀴“Well, I don’t know why not,” said the inspector. “He doesn’t look a
5682𓀴credit to the Bow Street cells, does he?” He slipped his key into the
5683𓀴lock, and we all very quietly entered the cell. The sleeper half
5684𓀴turned, and then settled down once more into a deep slumber. Holmes
5685𓀴stooped to the water-jug, moistened his sponge, and then rubbed it
5686𓀴twice vigorously across and down the prisoner’s face.
5687𓀴
5688𓀴“Let me introduce you,” he shouted, “to Mr. Neville St. Clair, of Lee,
5689𓀴in the county of Kent.”
5690𓀴
5691𓀴Never in my life have I seen such a sight. The man’s face peeled off
5692𓀴under the sponge like the bark from a tree. Gone was the coarse brown
5693𓀴tint! Gone, too, was the horrid scar which had seamed it across, and
5694𓀴the twisted lip which had given the repulsive sneer to the face! A
5695𓀴twitch brought away the tangled red hair, and there, sitting up in his
5696𓀴bed, was a pale, sad-faced, refined-looking man, black-haired and
5697𓀴smooth-skinned, rubbing his eyes and staring about him with sleepy
5698𓀴bewilderment. Then suddenly realising the exposure, he broke into a
5699𓀴scream and threw himself down with his face to the pillow.
5700𓀴
5701𓀴“Great heavens!” cried the inspector, “it is, indeed, the missing man.
5702𓀴I know him from the photograph.”
5703𓀴
5704𓀴The prisoner turned with the reckless air of a man who abandons himself
5705𓀴to his destiny. “Be it so,” said he. “And pray what am I charged with?”
5706𓀴
5707𓀴“With making away with Mr. Neville St.— Oh, come, you can’t be charged
5708𓀴with that unless they make a case of attempted suicide of it,” said the
5709𓀴inspector with a grin. “Well, I have been twenty-seven years in the
5710𓀴force, but this really takes the cake.”
5711𓀴
5712𓀴“If I am Mr. Neville St. Clair, then it is obvious that no crime has
5713𓀴been committed, and that, therefore, I am illegally detained.”
5714𓀴
5715𓀴“No crime, but a very great error has been committed,” said Holmes.
5716𓀴“You would have done better to have trusted your wife.”
5717𓀴
5718𓀴“It was not the wife; it was the children,” groaned the prisoner. “God
5719𓀴help me, I would not have them ashamed of their father. My God! What an
5720𓀴exposure! What can I do?”
5721𓀴
5722𓀴Sherlock Holmes sat down beside him on the couch and patted him kindly
5723𓀴on the shoulder.
5724𓀴
5725𓀴“If you leave it to a court of law to clear the matter up,” said he,
5726𓀴“of course you can hardly avoid publicity. On the other hand, if you
5727𓀴convince the police authorities that there is no possible case against
5728𓀴you, I do not know that there is any reason that the details should
5729𓀴find their way into the papers. Inspector Bradstreet would, I am sure,
5730𓀴make notes upon anything which you might tell us and submit it to the
5731𓀴proper authorities. The case would then never go into court at all.”
5732𓀴
5733𓀴“God bless you!” cried the prisoner passionately. “I would have endured
5734𓀴imprisonment, ay, even execution, rather than have left my miserable
5735𓀴secret as a family blot to my children.
5736𓀴
5737𓀴“You are the first who have ever heard my story. My father was a
5738𓀴schoolmaster in Chesterfield, where I received an excellent education.
5739𓀴I travelled in my youth, took to the stage, and finally became a
5740𓀴reporter on an evening paper in London. One day my editor wished to
5741𓀴have a series of articles upon begging in the metropolis, and I
5742𓀴volunteered to supply them. There was the point from which all my
5743𓀴adventures started. It was only by trying begging as an amateur that I
5744𓀴could get the facts upon which to base my articles. When an actor I
5745𓀴had, of course, learned all the secrets of making up, and had been
5746𓀴famous in the green-room for my skill. I took advantage now of my
5747𓀴attainments. I painted my face, and to make myself as pitiable as
5748𓀴possible I made a good scar and fixed one side of my lip in a twist by
5749𓀴the aid of a small slip of flesh-coloured plaster. Then with a red head
5750𓀴of hair, and an appropriate dress, I took my station in the business
5751𓀴part of the city, ostensibly as a match-seller but really as a beggar.
5752𓀴For seven hours I plied my trade, and when I returned home in the
5753𓀴evening I found to my surprise that I had received no less than 26_s_.
5754𓀴4_d_.
5755𓀴
5756𓀴“I wrote my articles and thought little more of the matter until, some
5757𓀴time later, I backed a bill for a friend and had a writ served upon me
5758𓀴for £ 25. I was at my wit’s end where to get the money, but a sudden
5759𓀴idea came to me. I begged a fortnight’s grace from the creditor, asked
5760𓀴for a holiday from my employers, and spent the time in begging in the
5761𓀴City under my disguise. In ten days I had the money and had paid the
5762𓀴debt.
5763𓀴
5764𓀴“Well, you can imagine how hard it was to settle down to arduous work
5765𓀴at £ 2 a week when I knew that I could earn as much in a day by
5766𓀴smearing my face with a little paint, laying my cap on the ground, and
5767𓀴sitting still. It was a long fight between my pride and the money, but
5768𓀴the dollars won at last, and I threw up reporting and sat day after day
5769𓀴in the corner which I had first chosen, inspiring pity by my ghastly
5770𓀴face and filling my pockets with coppers. Only one man knew my secret.
5771𓀴He was the keeper of a low den in which I used to lodge in Swandam
5772𓀴Lane, where I could every morning emerge as a squalid beggar and in the
5773𓀴evenings transform myself into a well-dressed man about town. This
5774𓀴fellow, a Lascar, was well paid by me for his rooms, so that I knew
5775𓀴that my secret was safe in his possession.
5776𓀴
5777𓀴“Well, very soon I found that I was saving considerable sums of money.
5778𓀴I do not mean that any beggar in the streets of London could earn £ 700
5779𓀴a year—which is less than my average takings—but I had exceptional
5780𓀴advantages in my power of making up, and also in a facility of
5781𓀴repartee, which improved by practice and made me quite a recognised
5782𓀴character in the City. All day a stream of pennies, varied by silver,
5783𓀴poured in upon me, and it was a very bad day in which I failed to take
5784𓀴£ 2.
5785𓀴
5786𓀴“As I grew richer I grew more ambitious, took a house in the country,
5787𓀴and eventually married, without anyone having a suspicion as to my real
5788𓀴occupation. My dear wife knew that I had business in the City. She
5789𓀴little knew what.
5790𓀴
5791𓀴“Last Monday I had finished for the day and was dressing in my room
5792𓀴above the opium den when I looked out of my window and saw, to my
5793𓀴horror and astonishment, that my wife was standing in the street, with
5794𓀴her eyes fixed full upon me. I gave a cry of surprise, threw up my arms
5795𓀴to cover my face, and, rushing to my confidant, the Lascar, entreated
5796𓀴him to prevent anyone from coming up to me. I heard her voice
5797𓀴downstairs, but I knew that she could not ascend. Swiftly I threw off
5798𓀴my clothes, pulled on those of a beggar, and put on my pigments and
5799𓀴wig. Even a wife’s eyes could not pierce so complete a disguise. But
5800𓀴then it occurred to me that there might be a search in the room, and
5801𓀴that the clothes might betray me. I threw open the window, reopening by
5802𓀴my violence a small cut which I had inflicted upon myself in the
5803𓀴bedroom that morning. Then I seized my coat, which was weighted by the
5804𓀴coppers which I had just transferred to it from the leather bag in
5805𓀴which I carried my takings. I hurled it out of the window, and it
5806𓀴disappeared into the Thames. The other clothes would have followed, but
5807𓀴at that moment there was a rush of constables up the stair, and a few
5808𓀴minutes after I found, rather, I confess, to my relief, that instead of
5809𓀴being identified as Mr. Neville St. Clair, I was arrested as his
5810𓀴murderer.
5811𓀴
5812𓀴“I do not know that there is anything else for me to explain. I was
5813𓀴determined to preserve my disguise as long as possible, and hence my
5814𓀴preference for a dirty face. Knowing that my wife would be terribly
5815𓀴anxious, I slipped off my ring and confided it to the Lascar at a
5816𓀴moment when no constable was watching me, together with a hurried
5817𓀴scrawl, telling her that she had no cause to fear.”
5818𓀴
5819𓀴“That note only reached her yesterday,” said Holmes.
5820𓀴
5821𓀴“Good God! What a week she must have spent!”
5822𓀴
5823𓀴“The police have watched this Lascar,” said Inspector Bradstreet, “and
5824𓀴I can quite understand that he might find it difficult to post a letter
5825𓀴unobserved. Probably he handed it to some sailor customer of his, who
5826𓀴forgot all about it for some days.”
5827𓀴
5828𓀴“That was it,” said Holmes, nodding approvingly; “I have no doubt of
5829𓀴it. But have you never been prosecuted for begging?”
5830𓀴
5831𓀴“Many times; but what was a fine to me?”
5832𓀴
5833𓀴“It must stop here, however,” said Bradstreet. “If the police are to
5834𓀴hush this thing up, there must be no more of Hugh Boone.”
5835𓀴
5836𓀴“I have sworn it by the most solemn oaths which a man can take.”
5837𓀴
5838𓀴“In that case I think that it is probable that no further steps may be
5839𓀴taken. But if you are found again, then all must come out. I am sure,
5840𓀴Mr. Holmes, that we are very much indebted to you for having cleared
5841𓀴the matter up. I wish I knew how you reach your results.”
5842𓀴
5843𓀴“I reached this one,” said my friend, “by sitting upon five pillows and
5844𓀴consuming an ounce of shag. I think, Watson, that if we drive to Baker
5845𓀴Street we shall just be in time for breakfast.”
5846𓀴
5847𓀴
5848𓀴
5849𓀴
5850𓀴VII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BLUE CARBUNCLE
5851𓀴
5852𓀴
5853𓀴I had called upon my friend Sherlock Holmes upon the second morning
5854𓀴after Christmas, with the intention of wishing him the compliments of
5855𓀴the season. He was lounging upon the sofa in a purple dressing-gown, a
5856𓀴pipe-rack within his reach upon the right, and a pile of crumpled
5857𓀴morning papers, evidently newly studied, near at hand. Beside the couch
5858𓀴was a wooden chair, and on the angle of the back hung a very seedy and
5859𓀴disreputable hard-felt hat, much the worse for wear, and cracked in
5860𓀴several places. A lens and a forceps lying upon the seat of the chair
5861𓀴suggested that the hat had been suspended in this manner for the
5862𓀴purpose of examination.
5863𓀴
5864𓀴“You are engaged,” said I; “perhaps I interrupt you.”
5865𓀴
5866𓀴“Not at all. I am glad to have a friend with whom I can discuss my
5867𓀴results. The matter is a perfectly trivial one”—he jerked his thumb in
5868𓀴the direction of the old hat—“but there are points in connection with
5869𓀴it which are not entirely devoid of interest and even of instruction.”
5870𓀴
5871𓀴I seated myself in his armchair and warmed my hands before his
5872𓀴crackling fire, for a sharp frost had set in, and the windows were
5873𓀴thick with the ice crystals. “I suppose,” I remarked, “that, homely as
5874𓀴it looks, this thing has some deadly story linked on to it—that it is
5875𓀴the clue which will guide you in the solution of some mystery and the
5876𓀴punishment of some crime.”
5877𓀴
5878𓀴“No, no. No crime,” said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. “Only one of those
5879𓀴whimsical little incidents which will happen when you have four million
5880𓀴human beings all jostling each other within the space of a few square
5881𓀴miles. Amid the action and reaction of so dense a swarm of humanity,
5882𓀴every possible combination of events may be expected to take place, and
5883𓀴many a little problem will be presented which may be striking and
5884𓀴bizarre without being criminal. We have already had experience of
5885𓀴such.”
5886𓀴
5887𓀴“So much so,” I remarked, “that of the last six cases which I have
5888𓀴added to my notes, three have been entirely free of any legal crime.”
5889𓀴
5890𓀴“Precisely. You allude to my attempt to recover the Irene Adler papers,
5891𓀴to the singular case of Miss Mary Sutherland, and to the adventure of
5892𓀴the man with the twisted lip. Well, I have no doubt that this small
5893𓀴matter will fall into the same innocent category. You know Peterson,
5894𓀴the commissionaire?”
5895𓀴
5896𓀴“Yes.”
5897𓀴
5898𓀴“It is to him that this trophy belongs.”
5899𓀴
5900𓀴“It is his hat.”
5901𓀴
5902𓀴“No, no, he found it. Its owner is unknown. I beg that you will look
5903𓀴upon it not as a battered billycock but as an intellectual problem.
5904𓀴And, first, as to how it came here. It arrived upon Christmas morning,
5905𓀴in company with a good fat goose, which is, I have no doubt, roasting
5906𓀴at this moment in front of Peterson’s fire. The facts are these: about
5907𓀴four o’clock on Christmas morning, Peterson, who, as you know, is a
5908𓀴very honest fellow, was returning from some small jollification and was
5909𓀴making his way homeward down Tottenham Court Road. In front of him he
5910𓀴saw, in the gaslight, a tallish man, walking with a slight stagger, and
5911𓀴carrying a white goose slung over his shoulder. As he reached the
5912𓀴corner of Goodge Street, a row broke out between this stranger and a
5913𓀴little knot of roughs. One of the latter knocked off the man’s hat, on
5914𓀴which he raised his stick to defend himself and, swinging it over his
5915𓀴head, smashed the shop window behind him. Peterson had rushed forward
5916𓀴to protect the stranger from his assailants; but the man, shocked at
5917𓀴having broken the window, and seeing an official-looking person in
5918𓀴uniform rushing towards him, dropped his goose, took to his heels, and
5919𓀴vanished amid the labyrinth of small streets which lie at the back of
5920𓀴Tottenham Court Road. The roughs had also fled at the appearance of
5921𓀴Peterson, so that he was left in possession of the field of battle, and
5922𓀴also of the spoils of victory in the shape of this battered hat and a
5923𓀴most unimpeachable Christmas goose.”
5924𓀴
5925𓀴“Which surely he restored to their owner?”
5926𓀴
5927𓀴“My dear fellow, there lies the problem. It is true that ‘For Mrs.
5928𓀴Henry Baker’ was printed upon a small card which was tied to the bird’s
5929𓀴left leg, and it is also true that the initials ‘H. B.’ are legible
5930𓀴upon the lining of this hat, but as there are some thousands of Bakers,
5931𓀴and some hundreds of Henry Bakers in this city of ours, it is not easy
5932𓀴to restore lost property to any one of them.”
5933𓀴
5934𓀴“What, then, did Peterson do?”
5935𓀴
5936𓀴“He brought round both hat and goose to me on Christmas morning,
5937𓀴knowing that even the smallest problems are of interest to me. The
5938𓀴goose we retained until this morning, when there were signs that, in
5939𓀴spite of the slight frost, it would be well that it should be eaten
5940𓀴without unnecessary delay. Its finder has carried it off, therefore, to
5941𓀴fulfil the ultimate destiny of a goose, while I continue to retain the
5942𓀴hat of the unknown gentleman who lost his Christmas dinner.”
5943𓀴
5944𓀴“Did he not advertise?”
5945𓀴
5946𓀴“No.”
5947𓀴
5948𓀴“Then, what clue could you have as to his identity?”
5949𓀴
5950𓀴“Only as much as we can deduce.”
5951𓀴
5952𓀴“From his hat?”
5953𓀴
5954𓀴“Precisely.”
5955𓀴
5956𓀴“But you are joking. What can you gather from this old battered felt?”
5957𓀴
5958𓀴“Here is my lens. You know my methods. What can you gather yourself as
5959𓀴to the individuality of the man who has worn this article?”
5960𓀴
5961𓀴I took the tattered object in my hands and turned it over rather
5962𓀴ruefully. It was a very ordinary black hat of the usual round shape,
5963𓀴hard and much the worse for wear. The lining had been of red silk, but
5964𓀴was a good deal discoloured. There was no maker’s name; but, as Holmes
5965𓀴had remarked, the initials “H. B.” were scrawled upon one side. It was
5966𓀴pierced in the brim for a hat-securer, but the elastic was missing. For
5967𓀴the rest, it was cracked, exceedingly dusty, and spotted in several
5968𓀴places, although there seemed to have been some attempt to hide the
5969𓀴discoloured patches by smearing them with ink.
5970𓀴
5971𓀴“I can see nothing,” said I, handing it back to my friend.
5972𓀴
5973𓀴“On the contrary, Watson, you can see everything. You fail, however, to
5974𓀴reason from what you see. You are too timid in drawing your
5975𓀴inferences.”
5976𓀴
5977𓀴“Then, pray tell me what it is that you can infer from this hat?”
5978𓀴
5979𓀴He picked it up and gazed at it in the peculiar introspective fashion
5980𓀴which was characteristic of him. “It is perhaps less suggestive than it
5981𓀴might have been,” he remarked, “and yet there are a few inferences
5982𓀴which are very distinct, and a few others which represent at least a
5983𓀴strong balance of probability. That the man was highly intellectual is
5984𓀴of course obvious upon the face of it, and also that he was fairly
5985𓀴well-to-do within the last three years, although he has now fallen upon
5986𓀴evil days. He had foresight, but has less now than formerly, pointing
5987𓀴to a moral retrogression, which, when taken with the decline of his
5988𓀴fortunes, seems to indicate some evil influence, probably drink, at
5989𓀴work upon him. This may account also for the obvious fact that his wife
5990𓀴has ceased to love him.”
5991𓀴
5992𓀴“My dear Holmes!”
5993𓀴
5994𓀴“He has, however, retained some degree of self-respect,” he continued,
5995𓀴disregarding my remonstrance. “He is a man who leads a sedentary life,
5996𓀴goes out little, is out of training entirely, is middle-aged, has
5997𓀴grizzled hair which he has had cut within the last few days, and which
5998𓀴he anoints with lime-cream. These are the more patent facts which are
5999𓀴to be deduced from his hat. Also, by the way, that it is extremely
6000𓀴improbable that he has gas laid on in his house.”
6001𓀴
6002𓀴“You are certainly joking, Holmes.”
6003𓀴
6004𓀴“Not in the least. Is it possible that even now, when I give you these
6005𓀴results, you are unable to see how they are attained?”
6006𓀴
6007𓀴“I have no doubt that I am very stupid, but I must confess that I am
6008𓀴unable to follow you. For example, how did you deduce that this man was
6009𓀴intellectual?”
6010𓀴
6011𓀴For answer Holmes clapped the hat upon his head. It came right over the
6012𓀴forehead and settled upon the bridge of his nose. “It is a question of
6013𓀴cubic capacity,” said he; “a man with so large a brain must have
6014𓀴something in it.”
6015𓀴
6016𓀴“The decline of his fortunes, then?”
6017𓀴
6018𓀴“This hat is three years old. These flat brims curled at the edge came
6019𓀴in then. It is a hat of the very best quality. Look at the band of
6020𓀴ribbed silk and the excellent lining. If this man could afford to buy
6021𓀴so expensive a hat three years ago, and has had no hat since, then he
6022𓀴has assuredly gone down in the world.”
6023𓀴
6024𓀴“Well, that is clear enough, certainly. But how about the foresight and
6025𓀴the moral retrogression?”
6026𓀴
6027𓀴Sherlock Holmes laughed. “Here is the foresight,” said he putting his
6028𓀴finger upon the little disc and loop of the hat-securer. “They are
6029𓀴never sold upon hats. If this man ordered one, it is a sign of a
6030𓀴certain amount of foresight, since he went out of his way to take this
6031𓀴precaution against the wind. But since we see that he has broken the
6032𓀴elastic and has not troubled to replace it, it is obvious that he has
6033𓀴less foresight now than formerly, which is a distinct proof of a
6034𓀴weakening nature. On the other hand, he has endeavoured to conceal some
6035𓀴of these stains upon the felt by daubing them with ink, which is a sign
6036𓀴that he has not entirely lost his self-respect.”
6037𓀴
6038𓀴“Your reasoning is certainly plausible.”
6039𓀴
6040𓀴“The further points, that he is middle-aged, that his hair is grizzled,
6041𓀴that it has been recently cut, and that he uses lime-cream, are all to
6042𓀴be gathered from a close examination of the lower part of the lining.
6043𓀴The lens discloses a large number of hair-ends, clean cut by the
6044𓀴scissors of the barber. They all appear to be adhesive, and there is a
6045𓀴distinct odour of lime-cream. This dust, you will observe, is not the
6046𓀴gritty, grey dust of the street but the fluffy brown dust of the house,
6047𓀴showing that it has been hung up indoors most of the time, while the
6048𓀴marks of moisture upon the inside are proof positive that the wearer
6049𓀴perspired very freely, and could therefore, hardly be in the best of
6050𓀴training.”
6051𓀴
6052𓀴“But his wife—you said that she had ceased to love him.”
6053𓀴
6054𓀴“This hat has not been brushed for weeks. When I see you, my dear
6055𓀴Watson, with a week’s accumulation of dust upon your hat, and when your
6056𓀴wife allows you to go out in such a state, I shall fear that you also
6057𓀴have been unfortunate enough to lose your wife’s affection.”
6058𓀴
6059𓀴“But he might be a bachelor.”
6060𓀴
6061𓀴“Nay, he was bringing home the goose as a peace-offering to his wife.
6062𓀴Remember the card upon the bird’s leg.”
6063𓀴
6064𓀴“You have an answer to everything. But how on earth do you deduce that
6065𓀴the gas is not laid on in his house?”
6066𓀴
6067𓀴“One tallow stain, or even two, might come by chance; but when I see no
6068𓀴less than five, I think that there can be little doubt that the
6069𓀴individual must be brought into frequent contact with burning
6070𓀴tallow—walks upstairs at night probably with his hat in one hand and a
6071𓀴guttering candle in the other. Anyhow, he never got tallow-stains from
6072𓀴a gas-jet. Are you satisfied?”
6073𓀴
6074𓀴“Well, it is very ingenious,” said I, laughing; “but since, as you said
6075𓀴just now, there has been no crime committed, and no harm done save the
6076𓀴loss of a goose, all this seems to be rather a waste of energy.”
6077𓀴
6078𓀴Sherlock Holmes had opened his mouth to reply, when the door flew open,
6079𓀴and Peterson, the commissionaire, rushed into the apartment with
6080𓀴flushed cheeks and the face of a man who is dazed with astonishment.
6081𓀴
6082𓀴“The goose, Mr. Holmes! The goose, sir!” he gasped.
6083𓀴
6084𓀴“Eh? What of it, then? Has it returned to life and flapped off through
6085𓀴the kitchen window?” Holmes twisted himself round upon the sofa to get
6086𓀴a fairer view of the man’s excited face.
6087𓀴
6088𓀴“See here, sir! See what my wife found in its crop!” He held out his
6089𓀴hand and displayed upon the centre of the palm a brilliantly
6090𓀴scintillating blue stone, rather smaller than a bean in size, but of
6091𓀴such purity and radiance that it twinkled like an electric point in the
6092𓀴dark hollow of his hand.
6093𓀴
6094𓀴Sherlock Holmes sat up with a whistle. “By Jove, Peterson!” said he,
6095𓀴“this is treasure trove indeed. I suppose you know what you have got?”
6096𓀴
6097𓀴“A diamond, sir? A precious stone. It cuts into glass as though it were
6098𓀴putty.”
6099𓀴
6100𓀴“It’s more than a precious stone. It is _the_ precious stone.”
6101𓀴
6102𓀴“Not the Countess of Morcar’s blue carbuncle!” I ejaculated.
6103𓀴
6104𓀴“Precisely so. I ought to know its size and shape, seeing that I have
6105𓀴read the advertisement about it in _The Times_ every day lately. It is
6106𓀴absolutely unique, and its value can only be conjectured, but the
6107𓀴reward offered of £ 1000 is certainly not within a twentieth part of
6108𓀴the market price.”
6109𓀴
6110𓀴“A thousand pounds! Great Lord of mercy!” The commissionaire plumped
6111𓀴down into a chair and stared from one to the other of us.
6112𓀴
6113𓀴“That is the reward, and I have reason to know that there are
6114𓀴sentimental considerations in the background which would induce the
6115𓀴Countess to part with half her fortune if she could but recover the
6116𓀴gem.”
6117𓀴
6118𓀴“It was lost, if I remember aright, at the Hotel Cosmopolitan,” I
6119𓀴remarked.
6120𓀴
6121𓀴“Precisely so, on December 22nd, just five days ago. John Horner, a
6122𓀴plumber, was accused of having abstracted it from the lady’s
6123𓀴jewel-case. The evidence against him was so strong that the case has
6124𓀴been referred to the Assizes. I have some account of the matter here, I
6125𓀴believe.” He rummaged amid his newspapers, glancing over the dates,
6126𓀴until at last he smoothed one out, doubled it over, and read the
6127𓀴following paragraph:
6128𓀴
6129𓀴“Hotel Cosmopolitan Jewel Robbery. John Horner, 26, plumber, was
6130𓀴brought up upon the charge of having upon the 22nd inst., abstracted
6131𓀴from the jewel-case of the Countess of Morcar the valuable gem known as
6132𓀴the blue carbuncle. James Ryder, upper-attendant at the hotel, gave his
6133𓀴evidence to the effect that he had shown Horner up to the dressing-room
6134𓀴of the Countess of Morcar upon the day of the robbery in order that he
6135𓀴might solder the second bar of the grate, which was loose. He had
6136𓀴remained with Horner some little time, but had finally been called
6137𓀴away. On returning, he found that Horner had disappeared, that the
6138𓀴bureau had been forced open, and that the small morocco casket in
6139𓀴which, as it afterwards transpired, the Countess was accustomed to keep
6140𓀴her jewel, was lying empty upon the dressing-table. Ryder instantly
6141𓀴gave the alarm, and Horner was arrested the same evening; but the stone
6142𓀴could not be found either upon his person or in his rooms. Catherine
6143𓀴Cusack, maid to the Countess, deposed to having heard Ryder’s cry of
6144𓀴dismay on discovering the robbery, and to having rushed into the room,
6145𓀴where she found matters as described by the last witness. Inspector
6146𓀴Bradstreet, B division, gave evidence as to the arrest of Horner, who
6147𓀴struggled frantically, and protested his innocence in the strongest
6148𓀴terms. Evidence of a previous conviction for robbery having been given
6149𓀴against the prisoner, the magistrate refused to deal summarily with the
6150𓀴offence, but referred it to the Assizes. Horner, who had shown signs of
6151𓀴intense emotion during the proceedings, fainted away at the conclusion
6152𓀴and was carried out of court.”
6153𓀴
6154𓀴“Hum! So much for the police-court,” said Holmes thoughtfully, tossing
6155𓀴aside the paper. “The question for us now to solve is the sequence of
6156𓀴events leading from a rifled jewel-case at one end to the crop of a
6157𓀴goose in Tottenham Court Road at the other. You see, Watson, our little
6158𓀴deductions have suddenly assumed a much more important and less
6159𓀴innocent aspect. Here is the stone; the stone came from the goose, and
6160𓀴the goose came from Mr. Henry Baker, the gentleman with the bad hat and
6161𓀴all the other characteristics with which I have bored you. So now we
6162𓀴must set ourselves very seriously to finding this gentleman and
6163𓀴ascertaining what part he has played in this little mystery. To do
6164𓀴this, we must try the simplest means first, and these lie undoubtedly
6165𓀴in an advertisement in all the evening papers. If this fail, I shall
6166𓀴have recourse to other methods.”
6167𓀴
6168𓀴“What will you say?”
6169𓀴
6170𓀴“Give me a pencil and that slip of paper. Now, then: ‘Found at the
6171𓀴corner of Goodge Street, a goose and a black felt hat. Mr. Henry Baker
6172𓀴can have the same by applying at 6:30 this evening at 221B, Baker
6173𓀴Street.’ That is clear and concise.”
6174𓀴
6175𓀴“Very. But will he see it?”
6176𓀴
6177𓀴“Well, he is sure to keep an eye on the papers, since, to a poor man,
6178𓀴the loss was a heavy one. He was clearly so scared by his mischance in
6179𓀴breaking the window and by the approach of Peterson that he thought of
6180𓀴nothing but flight, but since then he must have bitterly regretted the
6181𓀴impulse which caused him to drop his bird. Then, again, the
6182𓀴introduction of his name will cause him to see it, for everyone who
6183𓀴knows him will direct his attention to it. Here you are, Peterson, run
6184𓀴down to the advertising agency and have this put in the evening
6185𓀴papers.”
6186𓀴
6187𓀴“In which, sir?”
6188𓀴
6189𓀴“Oh, in the _Globe_, _Star_, _Pall Mall_, _St. James’s Gazette_,
6190𓀴_Evening News_, _Standard_, _Echo_, and any others that occur to you.”
6191𓀴
6192𓀴“Very well, sir. And this stone?”
6193𓀴
6194𓀴“Ah, yes, I shall keep the stone. Thank you. And, I say, Peterson, just
6195𓀴buy a goose on your way back and leave it here with me, for we must
6196𓀴have one to give to this gentleman in place of the one which your
6197𓀴family is now devouring.”
6198𓀴
6199𓀴When the commissionaire had gone, Holmes took up the stone and held it
6200𓀴against the light. “It’s a bonny thing,” said he. “Just see how it
6201𓀴glints and sparkles. Of course it is a nucleus and focus of crime.
6202𓀴Every good stone is. They are the devil’s pet baits. In the larger and
6203𓀴older jewels every facet may stand for a bloody deed. This stone is not
6204𓀴yet twenty years old. It was found in the banks of the Amoy River in
6205𓀴southern China and is remarkable in having every characteristic of the
6206𓀴carbuncle, save that it is blue in shade instead of ruby red. In spite
6207𓀴of its youth, it has already a sinister history. There have been two
6208𓀴murders, a vitriol-throwing, a suicide, and several robberies brought
6209𓀴about for the sake of this forty-grain weight of crystallised charcoal.
6210𓀴Who would think that so pretty a toy would be a purveyor to the gallows
6211𓀴and the prison? I’ll lock it up in my strong box now and drop a line to
6212𓀴the Countess to say that we have it.”
6213𓀴
6214𓀴“Do you think that this man Horner is innocent?”
6215𓀴
6216𓀴“I cannot tell.”
6217𓀴
6218𓀴“Well, then, do you imagine that this other one, Henry Baker, had
6219𓀴anything to do with the matter?”
6220𓀴
6221𓀴“It is, I think, much more likely that Henry Baker is an absolutely
6222𓀴innocent man, who had no idea that the bird which he was carrying was
6223𓀴of considerably more value than if it were made of solid gold. That,
6224𓀴however, I shall determine by a very simple test if we have an answer
6225𓀴to our advertisement.”
6226𓀴
6227𓀴“And you can do nothing until then?”
6228𓀴
6229𓀴“Nothing.”
6230𓀴
6231𓀴“In that case I shall continue my professional round. But I shall come
6232𓀴back in the evening at the hour you have mentioned, for I should like
6233𓀴to see the solution of so tangled a business.”
6234𓀴
6235𓀴“Very glad to see you. I dine at seven. There is a woodcock, I believe.
6236𓀴By the way, in view of recent occurrences, perhaps I ought to ask Mrs.
6237𓀴Hudson to examine its crop.”
6238𓀴
6239𓀴I had been delayed at a case, and it was a little after half-past six
6240𓀴when I found myself in Baker Street once more. As I approached the
6241𓀴house I saw a tall man in a Scotch bonnet with a coat which was
6242𓀴buttoned up to his chin waiting outside in the bright semicircle which
6243𓀴was thrown from the fanlight. Just as I arrived the door was opened,
6244𓀴and we were shown up together to Holmes’ room.
6245𓀴
6246𓀴“Mr. Henry Baker, I believe,” said he, rising from his armchair and
6247𓀴greeting his visitor with the easy air of geniality which he could so
6248𓀴readily assume. “Pray take this chair by the fire, Mr. Baker. It is a
6249𓀴cold night, and I observe that your circulation is more adapted for
6250𓀴summer than for winter. Ah, Watson, you have just come at the right
6251𓀴time. Is that your hat, Mr. Baker?”
6252𓀴
6253𓀴“Yes, sir, that is undoubtedly my hat.”
6254𓀴
6255𓀴He was a large man with rounded shoulders, a massive head, and a broad,
6256𓀴intelligent face, sloping down to a pointed beard of grizzled brown. A
6257𓀴touch of red in nose and cheeks, with a slight tremor of his extended
6258𓀴hand, recalled Holmes’ surmise as to his habits. His rusty black
6259𓀴frock-coat was buttoned right up in front, with the collar turned up,
6260𓀴and his lank wrists protruded from his sleeves without a sign of cuff
6261𓀴or shirt. He spoke in a slow staccato fashion, choosing his words with
6262𓀴care, and gave the impression generally of a man of learning and
6263𓀴letters who had had ill-usage at the hands of fortune.
6264𓀴
6265𓀴“We have retained these things for some days,” said Holmes, “because we
6266𓀴expected to see an advertisement from you giving your address. I am at
6267𓀴a loss to know now why you did not advertise.”
6268𓀴
6269𓀴Our visitor gave a rather shamefaced laugh. “Shillings have not been so
6270𓀴plentiful with me as they once were,” he remarked. “I had no doubt that
6271𓀴the gang of roughs who assaulted me had carried off both my hat and the
6272𓀴bird. I did not care to spend more money in a hopeless attempt at
6273𓀴recovering them.”
6274𓀴
6275𓀴“Very naturally. By the way, about the bird, we were compelled to eat
6276𓀴it.”
6277𓀴
6278𓀴“To eat it!” Our visitor half rose from his chair in his excitement.
6279𓀴
6280𓀴“Yes, it would have been of no use to anyone had we not done so. But I
6281𓀴presume that this other goose upon the sideboard, which is about the
6282𓀴same weight and perfectly fresh, will answer your purpose equally
6283𓀴well?”
6284𓀴
6285𓀴“Oh, certainly, certainly,” answered Mr. Baker with a sigh of relief.
6286𓀴
6287𓀴“Of course, we still have the feathers, legs, crop, and so on of your
6288𓀴own bird, so if you wish—”
6289𓀴
6290𓀴The man burst into a hearty laugh. “They might be useful to me as
6291𓀴relics of my adventure,” said he, “but beyond that I can hardly see
6292𓀴what use the _disjecta membra_ of my late acquaintance are going to be
6293𓀴to me. No, sir, I think that, with your permission, I will confine my
6294𓀴attentions to the excellent bird which I perceive upon the sideboard.”
6295𓀴
6296𓀴Sherlock Holmes glanced sharply across at me with a slight shrug of his
6297𓀴shoulders.
6298𓀴
6299𓀴“There is your hat, then, and there your bird,” said he. “By the way,
6300𓀴would it bore you to tell me where you got the other one from? I am
6301𓀴somewhat of a fowl fancier, and I have seldom seen a better grown
6302𓀴goose.”
6303𓀴
6304𓀴“Certainly, sir,” said Baker, who had risen and tucked his newly gained
6305𓀴property under his arm. “There are a few of us who frequent the Alpha
6306𓀴Inn, near the Museum—we are to be found in the Museum itself during the
6307𓀴day, you understand. This year our good host, Windigate by name,
6308𓀴instituted a goose club, by which, on consideration of some few pence
6309𓀴every week, we were each to receive a bird at Christmas. My pence were
6310𓀴duly paid, and the rest is familiar to you. I am much indebted to you,
6311𓀴sir, for a Scotch bonnet is fitted neither to my years nor my gravity.”
6312𓀴With a comical pomposity of manner he bowed solemnly to both of us and
6313𓀴strode off upon his way.
6314𓀴
6315𓀴“So much for Mr. Henry Baker,” said Holmes when he had closed the door
6316𓀴behind him. “It is quite certain that he knows nothing whatever about
6317𓀴the matter. Are you hungry, Watson?”
6318𓀴
6319𓀴“Not particularly.”
6320𓀴
6321𓀴“Then I suggest that we turn our dinner into a supper and follow up
6322𓀴this clue while it is still hot.”
6323𓀴
6324𓀴“By all means.”
6325𓀴
6326𓀴It was a bitter night, so we drew on our ulsters and wrapped cravats
6327𓀴about our throats. Outside, the stars were shining coldly in a
6328𓀴cloudless sky, and the breath of the passers-by blew out into smoke
6329𓀴like so many pistol shots. Our footfalls rang out crisply and loudly as
6330𓀴we swung through the doctors’ quarter, Wimpole Street, Harley Street,
6331𓀴and so through Wigmore Street into Oxford Street. In a quarter of an
6332𓀴hour we were in Bloomsbury at the Alpha Inn, which is a small
6333𓀴public-house at the corner of one of the streets which runs down into
6334𓀴Holborn. Holmes pushed open the door of the private bar and ordered two
6335𓀴glasses of beer from the ruddy-faced, white-aproned landlord.
6336𓀴
6337𓀴“Your beer should be excellent if it is as good as your geese,” said
6338𓀴he.
6339𓀴
6340𓀴“My geese!” The man seemed surprised.
6341𓀴
6342𓀴“Yes. I was speaking only half an hour ago to Mr. Henry Baker, who was
6343𓀴a member of your goose club.”
6344𓀴
6345𓀴“Ah! yes, I see. But you see, sir, them’s not _our_ geese.”
6346𓀴
6347𓀴“Indeed! Whose, then?”
6348𓀴
6349𓀴“Well, I got the two dozen from a salesman in Covent Garden.”
6350𓀴
6351𓀴“Indeed? I know some of them. Which was it?”
6352𓀴
6353𓀴“Breckinridge is his name.”
6354𓀴
6355𓀴“Ah! I don’t know him. Well, here’s your good health landlord, and
6356𓀴prosperity to your house. Good-night.”
6357𓀴
6358𓀴“Now for Mr. Breckinridge,” he continued, buttoning up his coat as we
6359𓀴came out into the frosty air. “Remember, Watson that though we have so
6360𓀴homely a thing as a goose at one end of this chain, we have at the
6361𓀴other a man who will certainly get seven years’ penal servitude unless
6362𓀴we can establish his innocence. It is possible that our inquiry may but
6363𓀴confirm his guilt; but, in any case, we have a line of investigation
6364𓀴which has been missed by the police, and which a singular chance has
6365𓀴placed in our hands. Let us follow it out to the bitter end. Faces to
6366𓀴the south, then, and quick march!”
6367𓀴
6368𓀴We passed across Holborn, down Endell Street, and so through a zigzag
6369𓀴of slums to Covent Garden Market. One of the largest stalls bore the
6370𓀴name of Breckinridge upon it, and the proprietor a horsey-looking man,
6371𓀴with a sharp face and trim side-whiskers was helping a boy to put up
6372𓀴the shutters.
6373𓀴
6374𓀴“Good-evening. It’s a cold night,” said Holmes.
6375𓀴
6376𓀴The salesman nodded and shot a questioning glance at my companion.
6377𓀴
6378𓀴“Sold out of geese, I see,” continued Holmes, pointing at the bare
6379𓀴slabs of marble.
6380𓀴
6381𓀴“Let you have five hundred to-morrow morning.”
6382𓀴
6383𓀴“That’s no good.”
6384𓀴
6385𓀴“Well, there are some on the stall with the gas-flare.”
6386𓀴
6387𓀴“Ah, but I was recommended to you.”
6388𓀴
6389𓀴“Who by?”
6390𓀴
6391𓀴“The landlord of the Alpha.”
6392𓀴
6393𓀴“Oh, yes; I sent him a couple of dozen.”
6394𓀴
6395𓀴“Fine birds they were, too. Now where did you get them from?”
6396𓀴
6397𓀴To my surprise the question provoked a burst of anger from the
6398𓀴salesman.
6399𓀴
6400𓀴“Now, then, mister,” said he, with his head cocked and his arms akimbo,
6401𓀴“what are you driving at? Let’s have it straight, now.”
6402𓀴
6403𓀴“It is straight enough. I should like to know who sold you the geese
6404𓀴which you supplied to the Alpha.”
6405𓀴
6406𓀴“Well then, I shan’t tell you. So now!”
6407𓀴
6408𓀴“Oh, it is a matter of no importance; but I don’t know why you should
6409𓀴be so warm over such a trifle.”
6410𓀴
6411𓀴“Warm! You’d be as warm, maybe, if you were as pestered as I am. When I
6412𓀴pay good money for a good article there should be an end of the
6413𓀴business; but it’s ‘Where are the geese?’ and ‘Who did you sell the
6414𓀴geese to?’ and ‘What will you take for the geese?’ One would think they
6415𓀴were the only geese in the world, to hear the fuss that is made over
6416𓀴them.”
6417𓀴
6418𓀴“Well, I have no connection with any other people who have been making
6419𓀴inquiries,” said Holmes carelessly. “If you won’t tell us the bet is
6420𓀴off, that is all. But I’m always ready to back my opinion on a matter
6421𓀴of fowls, and I have a fiver on it that the bird I ate is country
6422𓀴bred.”
6423𓀴
6424𓀴“Well, then, you’ve lost your fiver, for it’s town bred,” snapped the
6425𓀴salesman.
6426𓀴
6427𓀴“It’s nothing of the kind.”
6428𓀴
6429𓀴“I say it is.”
6430𓀴
6431𓀴“I don’t believe it.”
6432𓀴
6433𓀴“D’you think you know more about fowls than I, who have handled them
6434𓀴ever since I was a nipper? I tell you, all those birds that went to the
6435𓀴Alpha were town bred.”
6436𓀴
6437𓀴“You’ll never persuade me to believe that.”
6438𓀴
6439𓀴“Will you bet, then?”
6440𓀴
6441𓀴“It’s merely taking your money, for I know that I am right. But I’ll
6442𓀴have a sovereign on with you, just to teach you not to be obstinate.”
6443𓀴
6444𓀴The salesman chuckled grimly. “Bring me the books, Bill,” said he.
6445𓀴
6446𓀴The small boy brought round a small thin volume and a great
6447𓀴greasy-backed one, laying them out together beneath the hanging lamp.
6448𓀴
6449𓀴“Now then, Mr. Cocksure,” said the salesman, “I thought that I was out
6450𓀴of geese, but before I finish you’ll find that there is still one left
6451𓀴in my shop. You see this little book?”
6452𓀴
6453𓀴“Well?”
6454𓀴
6455𓀴“That’s the list of the folk from whom I buy. D’you see? Well, then,
6456𓀴here on this page are the country folk, and the numbers after their
6457𓀴names are where their accounts are in the big ledger. Now, then! You
6458𓀴see this other page in red ink? Well, that is a list of my town
6459𓀴suppliers. Now, look at that third name. Just read it out to me.”
6460𓀴
6461𓀴“Mrs. Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road—249,” read Holmes.
6462𓀴
6463𓀴“Quite so. Now turn that up in the ledger.”
6464𓀴
6465𓀴Holmes turned to the page indicated. “Here you are, ‘Mrs. Oakshott,
6466𓀴117, Brixton Road, egg and poultry supplier.’”
6467𓀴
6468𓀴“Now, then, what’s the last entry?”
6469𓀴
6470𓀴“‘December 22nd. Twenty-four geese at 7_s_. 6_d_.’”
6471𓀴
6472𓀴“Quite so. There you are. And underneath?”
6473𓀴
6474𓀴“‘Sold to Mr. Windigate of the Alpha, at 12_s_.’”
6475𓀴
6476𓀴“What have you to say now?”
6477𓀴
6478𓀴Sherlock Holmes looked deeply chagrined. He drew a sovereign from his
6479𓀴pocket and threw it down upon the slab, turning away with the air of a
6480𓀴man whose disgust is too deep for words. A few yards off he stopped
6481𓀴under a lamp-post and laughed in the hearty, noiseless fashion which
6482𓀴was peculiar to him.
6483𓀴
6484𓀴“When you see a man with whiskers of that cut and the ‘Pink ’un’
6485𓀴protruding out of his pocket, you can always draw him by a bet,” said
6486𓀴he. “I daresay that if I had put £ 100 down in front of him, that man
6487𓀴would not have given me such complete information as was drawn from him
6488𓀴by the idea that he was doing me on a wager. Well, Watson, we are, I
6489𓀴fancy, nearing the end of our quest, and the only point which remains
6490𓀴to be determined is whether we should go on to this Mrs. Oakshott
6491𓀴to-night, or whether we should reserve it for to-morrow. It is clear
6492𓀴from what that surly fellow said that there are others besides
6493𓀴ourselves who are anxious about the matter, and I should—”
6494𓀴
6495𓀴His remarks were suddenly cut short by a loud hubbub which broke out
6496𓀴from the stall which we had just left. Turning round we saw a little
6497𓀴rat-faced fellow standing in the centre of the circle of yellow light
6498𓀴which was thrown by the swinging lamp, while Breckinridge, the
6499𓀴salesman, framed in the door of his stall, was shaking his fists
6500𓀴fiercely at the cringing figure.
6501𓀴
6502𓀴“I’ve had enough of you and your geese,” he shouted. “I wish you were
6503𓀴all at the devil together. If you come pestering me any more with your
6504𓀴silly talk I’ll set the dog at you. You bring Mrs. Oakshott here and
6505𓀴I’ll answer her, but what have you to do with it? Did I buy the geese
6506𓀴off you?”
6507𓀴
6508𓀴“No; but one of them was mine all the same,” whined the little man.
6509𓀴
6510𓀴“Well, then, ask Mrs. Oakshott for it.”
6511𓀴
6512𓀴“She told me to ask you.”
6513𓀴
6514𓀴“Well, you can ask the King of Proosia, for all I care. I’ve had enough
6515𓀴of it. Get out of this!” He rushed fiercely forward, and the inquirer
6516𓀴flitted away into the darkness.
6517𓀴
6518𓀴“Ha! this may save us a visit to Brixton Road,” whispered Holmes. “Come
6519𓀴with me, and we will see what is to be made of this fellow.” Striding
6520𓀴through the scattered knots of people who lounged round the flaring
6521𓀴stalls, my companion speedily overtook the little man and touched him
6522𓀴upon the shoulder. He sprang round, and I could see in the gas-light
6523𓀴that every vestige of colour had been driven from his face.
6524𓀴
6525𓀴“Who are you, then? What do you want?” he asked in a quavering voice.
6526𓀴
6527𓀴“You will excuse me,” said Holmes blandly, “but I could not help
6528𓀴overhearing the questions which you put to the salesman just now. I
6529𓀴think that I could be of assistance to you.”
6530𓀴
6531𓀴“You? Who are you? How could you know anything of the matter?”
6532𓀴
6533𓀴“My name is Sherlock Holmes. It is my business to know what other
6534𓀴people don’t know.”
6535𓀴
6536𓀴“But you can know nothing of this?”
6537𓀴
6538𓀴“Excuse me, I know everything of it. You are endeavouring to trace some
6539𓀴geese which were sold by Mrs. Oakshott, of Brixton Road, to a salesman
6540𓀴named Breckinridge, by him in turn to Mr. Windigate, of the Alpha, and
6541𓀴by him to his club, of which Mr. Henry Baker is a member.”
6542𓀴
6543𓀴“Oh, sir, you are the very man whom I have longed to meet,” cried the
6544𓀴little fellow with outstretched hands and quivering fingers. “I can
6545𓀴hardly explain to you how interested I am in this matter.”
6546𓀴
6547𓀴Sherlock Holmes hailed a four-wheeler which was passing. “In that case
6548𓀴we had better discuss it in a cosy room rather than in this wind-swept
6549𓀴market-place,” said he. “But pray tell me, before we go farther, who it
6550𓀴is that I have the pleasure of assisting.”
6551𓀴
6552𓀴The man hesitated for an instant. “My name is John Robinson,” he
6553𓀴answered with a sidelong glance.
6554𓀴
6555𓀴“No, no; the real name,” said Holmes sweetly. “It is always awkward
6556𓀴doing business with an alias.”
6557𓀴
6558𓀴A flush sprang to the white cheeks of the stranger. “Well then,” said
6559𓀴he, “my real name is James Ryder.”
6560𓀴
6561𓀴“Precisely so. Head attendant at the Hotel Cosmopolitan. Pray step into
6562𓀴the cab, and I shall soon be able to tell you everything which you
6563𓀴would wish to know.”
6564𓀴
6565𓀴The little man stood glancing from one to the other of us with
6566𓀴half-frightened, half-hopeful eyes, as one who is not sure whether he
6567𓀴is on the verge of a windfall or of a catastrophe. Then he stepped into
6568𓀴the cab, and in half an hour we were back in the sitting-room at Baker
6569𓀴Street. Nothing had been said during our drive, but the high, thin
6570𓀴breathing of our new companion, and the claspings and unclaspings of
6571𓀴his hands, spoke of the nervous tension within him.
6572𓀴
6573𓀴“Here we are!” said Holmes cheerily as we filed into the room. “The
6574𓀴fire looks very seasonable in this weather. You look cold, Mr. Ryder.
6575𓀴Pray take the basket-chair. I will just put on my slippers before we
6576𓀴settle this little matter of yours. Now, then! You want to know what
6577𓀴became of those geese?”
6578𓀴
6579𓀴“Yes, sir.”
6580𓀴
6581𓀴“Or rather, I fancy, of that goose. It was one bird, I imagine in which
6582𓀴you were interested—white, with a black bar across the tail.”
6583𓀴
6584𓀴Ryder quivered with emotion. “Oh, sir,” he cried, “can you tell me
6585𓀴where it went to?”
6586𓀴
6587𓀴“It came here.”
6588𓀴
6589𓀴“Here?”
6590𓀴
6591𓀴“Yes, and a most remarkable bird it proved. I don’t wonder that you
6592𓀴should take an interest in it. It laid an egg after it was dead—the
6593𓀴bonniest, brightest little blue egg that ever was seen. I have it here
6594𓀴in my museum.”
6595𓀴
6596𓀴Our visitor staggered to his feet and clutched the mantelpiece with his
6597𓀴right hand. Holmes unlocked his strong-box and held up the blue
6598𓀴carbuncle, which shone out like a star, with a cold, brilliant,
6599𓀴many-pointed radiance. Ryder stood glaring with a drawn face, uncertain
6600𓀴whether to claim or to disown it.
6601𓀴
6602𓀴“The game’s up, Ryder,” said Holmes quietly. “Hold up, man, or you’ll
6603𓀴be into the fire! Give him an arm back into his chair, Watson. He’s not
6604𓀴got blood enough to go in for felony with impunity. Give him a dash of
6605𓀴brandy. So! Now he looks a little more human. What a shrimp it is, to
6606𓀴be sure!”
6607𓀴
6608𓀴For a moment he had staggered and nearly fallen, but the brandy brought
6609𓀴a tinge of colour into his cheeks, and he sat staring with frightened
6610𓀴eyes at his accuser.
6611𓀴
6612𓀴“I have almost every link in my hands, and all the proofs which I could
6613𓀴possibly need, so there is little which you need tell me. Still, that
6614𓀴little may as well be cleared up to make the case complete. You had
6615𓀴heard, Ryder, of this blue stone of the Countess of Morcar’s?”
6616𓀴
6617𓀴“It was Catherine Cusack who told me of it,” said he in a crackling
6618𓀴voice.
6619𓀴
6620𓀴“I see—her ladyship’s waiting-maid. Well, the temptation of sudden
6621𓀴wealth so easily acquired was too much for you, as it has been for
6622𓀴better men before you; but you were not very scrupulous in the means
6623𓀴you used. It seems to me, Ryder, that there is the making of a very
6624𓀴pretty villain in you. You knew that this man Horner, the plumber, had
6625𓀴been concerned in some such matter before, and that suspicion would
6626𓀴rest the more readily upon him. What did you do, then? You made some
6627𓀴small job in my lady’s room—you and your confederate Cusack—and you
6628𓀴managed that he should be the man sent for. Then, when he had left, you
6629𓀴rifled the jewel-case, raised the alarm, and had this unfortunate man
6630𓀴arrested. You then—”
6631𓀴
6632𓀴Ryder threw himself down suddenly upon the rug and clutched at my
6633𓀴companion’s knees. “For God’s sake, have mercy!” he shrieked. “Think of
6634𓀴my father! Of my mother! It would break their hearts. I never went
6635𓀴wrong before! I never will again. I swear it. I’ll swear it on a Bible.
6636𓀴Oh, don’t bring it into court! For Christ’s sake, don’t!”
6637𓀴
6638𓀴“Get back into your chair!” said Holmes sternly. “It is very well to
6639𓀴cringe and crawl now, but you thought little enough of this poor Horner
6640𓀴in the dock for a crime of which he knew nothing.”
6641𓀴
6642𓀴“I will fly, Mr. Holmes. I will leave the country, sir. Then the charge
6643𓀴against him will break down.”
6644𓀴
6645𓀴“Hum! We will talk about that. And now let us hear a true account of
6646𓀴the next act. How came the stone into the goose, and how came the goose
6647𓀴into the open market? Tell us the truth, for there lies your only hope
6648𓀴of safety.”
6649𓀴
6650𓀴Ryder passed his tongue over his parched lips. “I will tell you it just
6651𓀴as it happened, sir,” said he. “When Horner had been arrested, it
6652𓀴seemed to me that it would be best for me to get away with the stone at
6653𓀴once, for I did not know at what moment the police might not take it
6654𓀴into their heads to search me and my room. There was no place about the
6655𓀴hotel where it would be safe. I went out, as if on some commission, and
6656𓀴I made for my sister’s house. She had married a man named Oakshott, and
6657𓀴lived in Brixton Road, where she fattened fowls for the market. All the
6658𓀴way there every man I met seemed to me to be a policeman or a
6659𓀴detective; and, for all that it was a cold night, the sweat was pouring
6660𓀴down my face before I came to the Brixton Road. My sister asked me what
6661𓀴was the matter, and why I was so pale; but I told her that I had been
6662𓀴upset by the jewel robbery at the hotel. Then I went into the back yard
6663𓀴and smoked a pipe and wondered what it would be best to do.
6664𓀴
6665𓀴“I had a friend once called Maudsley, who went to the bad, and has just
6666𓀴been serving his time in Pentonville. One day he had met me, and fell
6667𓀴into talk about the ways of thieves, and how they could get rid of what
6668𓀴they stole. I knew that he would be true to me, for I knew one or two
6669𓀴things about him; so I made up my mind to go right on to Kilburn, where
6670𓀴he lived, and take him into my confidence. He would show me how to turn
6671𓀴the stone into money. But how to get to him in safety? I thought of the
6672𓀴agonies I had gone through in coming from the hotel. I might at any
6673𓀴moment be seized and searched, and there would be the stone in my
6674𓀴waistcoat pocket. I was leaning against the wall at the time and
6675𓀴looking at the geese which were waddling about round my feet, and
6676𓀴suddenly an idea came into my head which showed me how I could beat the
6677𓀴best detective that ever lived.
6678𓀴
6679𓀴“My sister had told me some weeks before that I might have the pick of
6680𓀴her geese for a Christmas present, and I knew that she was always as
6681𓀴good as her word. I would take my goose now, and in it I would carry my
6682𓀴stone to Kilburn. There was a little shed in the yard, and behind this
6683𓀴I drove one of the birds—a fine big one, white, with a barred tail. I
6684𓀴caught it, and prying its bill open, I thrust the stone down its throat
6685𓀴as far as my finger could reach. The bird gave a gulp, and I felt the
6686𓀴stone pass along its gullet and down into its crop. But the creature
6687𓀴flapped and struggled, and out came my sister to know what was the
6688𓀴matter. As I turned to speak to her the brute broke loose and fluttered
6689𓀴off among the others.
6690𓀴
6691𓀴“‘Whatever were you doing with that bird, Jem?’ says she.
6692𓀴
6693𓀴“‘Well,’ said I, ‘you said you’d give me one for Christmas, and I was
6694𓀴feeling which was the fattest.’
6695𓀴
6696𓀴“‘Oh,’ says she, ‘we’ve set yours aside for you—Jem’s bird, we call it.
6697𓀴It’s the big white one over yonder. There’s twenty-six of them, which
6698𓀴makes one for you, and one for us, and two dozen for the market.’
6699𓀴
6700𓀴“‘Thank you, Maggie,’ says I; ‘but if it is all the same to you, I’d
6701𓀴rather have that one I was handling just now.’
6702𓀴
6703𓀴“‘The other is a good three pound heavier,’ said she, ‘and we fattened
6704𓀴it expressly for you.’
6705𓀴
6706𓀴“‘Never mind. I’ll have the other, and I’ll take it now,’ said I.
6707𓀴
6708𓀴“‘Oh, just as you like,’ said she, a little huffed. ‘Which is it you
6709𓀴want, then?’
6710𓀴
6711𓀴“‘That white one with the barred tail, right in the middle of the
6712𓀴flock.’
6713𓀴
6714𓀴“‘Oh, very well. Kill it and take it with you.’
6715𓀴
6716𓀴“Well, I did what she said, Mr. Holmes, and I carried the bird all the
6717𓀴way to Kilburn. I told my pal what I had done, for he was a man that it
6718𓀴was easy to tell a thing like that to. He laughed until he choked, and
6719𓀴we got a knife and opened the goose. My heart turned to water, for
6720𓀴there was no sign of the stone, and I knew that some terrible mistake
6721𓀴had occurred. I left the bird, rushed back to my sister’s, and hurried
6722𓀴into the back yard. There was not a bird to be seen there.
6723𓀴
6724𓀴“‘Where are they all, Maggie?’ I cried.
6725𓀴
6726𓀴“‘Gone to the dealer’s, Jem.’
6727𓀴
6728𓀴“‘Which dealer’s?’
6729𓀴
6730𓀴“‘Breckinridge, of Covent Garden.’
6731𓀴
6732𓀴“‘But was there another with a barred tail?’ I asked, ‘the same as the
6733𓀴one I chose?’
6734𓀴
6735𓀴“‘Yes, Jem; there were two barred-tailed ones, and I could never tell
6736𓀴them apart.’
6737𓀴
6738𓀴“Well, then, of course I saw it all, and I ran off as hard as my feet
6739𓀴would carry me to this man Breckinridge; but he had sold the lot at
6740𓀴once, and not one word would he tell me as to where they had gone. You
6741𓀴heard him yourselves to-night. Well, he has always answered me like
6742𓀴that. My sister thinks that I am going mad. Sometimes I think that I am
6743𓀴myself. And now—and now I am myself a branded thief, without ever
6744𓀴having touched the wealth for which I sold my character. God help me!
6745𓀴God help me!” He burst into convulsive sobbing, with his face buried in
6746𓀴his hands.
6747𓀴
6748𓀴There was a long silence, broken only by his heavy breathing and by the
6749𓀴measured tapping of Sherlock Holmes’ finger-tips upon the edge of the
6750𓀴table. Then my friend rose and threw open the door.
6751𓀴
6752𓀴“Get out!” said he.
6753𓀴
6754𓀴“What, sir! Oh, Heaven bless you!”
6755𓀴
6756𓀴“No more words. Get out!”
6757𓀴
6758𓀴And no more words were needed. There was a rush, a clatter upon the
6759𓀴stairs, the bang of a door, and the crisp rattle of running footfalls
6760𓀴from the street.
6761𓀴
6762𓀴“After all, Watson,” said Holmes, reaching up his hand for his clay
6763𓀴pipe, “I am not retained by the police to supply their deficiencies. If
6764𓀴Horner were in danger it would be another thing; but this fellow will
6765𓀴not appear against him, and the case must collapse. I suppose that I am
6766𓀴commuting a felony, but it is just possible that I am saving a soul.
6767𓀴This fellow will not go wrong again; he is too terribly frightened.
6768𓀴Send him to gaol now, and you make him a gaol-bird for life. Besides,
6769𓀴it is the season of forgiveness. Chance has put in our way a most
6770𓀴singular and whimsical problem, and its solution is its own reward. If
6771𓀴you will have the goodness to touch the bell, Doctor, we will begin
6772𓀴another investigation, in which, also a bird will be the chief
6773𓀴feature.”
6774𓀴
6775𓀴
6776𓀴
6777𓀴
6778𓀴VIII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE SPECKLED BAND
6779𓀴
6780𓀴
6781𓀴On glancing over my notes of the seventy odd cases in which I have
6782𓀴during the last eight years studied the methods of my friend Sherlock
6783𓀴Holmes, I find many tragic, some comic, a large number merely strange,
6784𓀴but none commonplace; for, working as he did rather for the love of his
6785𓀴art than for the acquirement of wealth, he refused to associate himself
6786𓀴with any investigation which did not tend towards the unusual, and even
6787𓀴the fantastic. Of all these varied cases, however, I cannot recall any
6788𓀴which presented more singular features than that which was associated
6789𓀴with the well-known Surrey family of the Roylotts of Stoke Moran. The
6790𓀴events in question occurred in the early days of my association with
6791𓀴Holmes, when we were sharing rooms as bachelors in Baker Street. It is
6792𓀴possible that I might have placed them upon record before, but a
6793𓀴promise of secrecy was made at the time, from which I have only been
6794𓀴freed during the last month by the untimely death of the lady to whom
6795𓀴the pledge was given. It is perhaps as well that the facts should now
6796𓀴come to light, for I have reasons to know that there are widespread
6797𓀴rumours as to the death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott which tend to make the
6798𓀴matter even more terrible than the truth.
6799𓀴
6800𓀴It was early in April in the year ’83 that I woke one morning to find
6801𓀴Sherlock Holmes standing, fully dressed, by the side of my bed. He was
6802𓀴a late riser, as a rule, and as the clock on the mantelpiece showed me
6803𓀴that it was only a quarter-past seven, I blinked up at him in some
6804𓀴surprise, and perhaps just a little resentment, for I was myself
6805𓀴regular in my habits.
6806𓀴
6807𓀴“Very sorry to knock you up, Watson,” said he, “but it’s the common lot
6808𓀴this morning. Mrs. Hudson has been knocked up, she retorted upon me,
6809𓀴and I on you.”
6810𓀴
6811𓀴“What is it, then—a fire?”
6812𓀴
6813𓀴“No; a client. It seems that a young lady has arrived in a considerable
6814𓀴state of excitement, who insists upon seeing me. She is waiting now in
6815𓀴the sitting-room. Now, when young ladies wander about the metropolis at
6816𓀴this hour of the morning, and knock sleepy people up out of their beds,
6817𓀴I presume that it is something very pressing which they have to
6818𓀴communicate. Should it prove to be an interesting case, you would, I am
6819𓀴sure, wish to follow it from the outset. I thought, at any rate, that I
6820𓀴should call you and give you the chance.”
6821𓀴
6822𓀴“My dear fellow, I would not miss it for anything.”
6823𓀴
6824𓀴I had no keener pleasure than in following Holmes in his professional
6825𓀴investigations, and in admiring the rapid deductions, as swift as
6826𓀴intuitions, and yet always founded on a logical basis with which he
6827𓀴unravelled the problems which were submitted to him. I rapidly threw on
6828𓀴my clothes and was ready in a few minutes to accompany my friend down
6829𓀴to the sitting-room. A lady dressed in black and heavily veiled, who
6830𓀴had been sitting in the window, rose as we entered.
6831𓀴
6832𓀴“Good-morning, madam,” said Holmes cheerily. “My name is Sherlock
6833𓀴Holmes. This is my intimate friend and associate, Dr. Watson, before
6834𓀴whom you can speak as freely as before myself. Ha! I am glad to see
6835𓀴that Mrs. Hudson has had the good sense to light the fire. Pray draw up
6836𓀴to it, and I shall order you a cup of hot coffee, for I observe that
6837𓀴you are shivering.”
6838𓀴
6839𓀴“It is not cold which makes me shiver,” said the woman in a low voice,
6840𓀴changing her seat as requested.
6841𓀴
6842𓀴“What, then?”
6843𓀴
6844𓀴“It is fear, Mr. Holmes. It is terror.” She raised her veil as she
6845𓀴spoke, and we could see that she was indeed in a pitiable state of
6846𓀴agitation, her face all drawn and grey, with restless frightened eyes,
6847𓀴like those of some hunted animal. Her features and figure were those of
6848𓀴a woman of thirty, but her hair was shot with premature grey, and her
6849𓀴expression was weary and haggard. Sherlock Holmes ran her over with one
6850𓀴of his quick, all-comprehensive glances.
6851𓀴
6852𓀴“You must not fear,” said he soothingly, bending forward and patting
6853𓀴her forearm. “We shall soon set matters right, I have no doubt. You
6854𓀴have come in by train this morning, I see.”
6855𓀴
6856𓀴“You know me, then?”
6857𓀴
6858𓀴“No, but I observe the second half of a return ticket in the palm of
6859𓀴your left glove. You must have started early, and yet you had a good
6860𓀴drive in a dog-cart, along heavy roads, before you reached the
6861𓀴station.”
6862𓀴
6863𓀴The lady gave a violent start and stared in bewilderment at my
6864𓀴companion.
6865𓀴
6866𓀴“There is no mystery, my dear madam,” said he, smiling. “The left arm
6867𓀴of your jacket is spattered with mud in no less than seven places. The
6868𓀴marks are perfectly fresh. There is no vehicle save a dog-cart which
6869𓀴throws up mud in that way, and then only when you sit on the left-hand
6870𓀴side of the driver.”
6871𓀴
6872𓀴“Whatever your reasons may be, you are perfectly correct,” said she. “I
6873𓀴started from home before six, reached Leatherhead at twenty past, and
6874𓀴came in by the first train to Waterloo. Sir, I can stand this strain no
6875𓀴longer; I shall go mad if it continues. I have no one to turn to—none,
6876𓀴save only one, who cares for me, and he, poor fellow, can be of little
6877𓀴aid. I have heard of you, Mr. Holmes; I have heard of you from Mrs.
6878𓀴Farintosh, whom you helped in the hour of her sore need. It was from
6879𓀴her that I had your address. Oh, sir, do you not think that you could
6880𓀴help me, too, and at least throw a little light through the dense
6881𓀴darkness which surrounds me? At present it is out of my power to reward
6882𓀴you for your services, but in a month or six weeks I shall be married,
6883𓀴with the control of my own income, and then at least you shall not find
6884𓀴me ungrateful.”
6885𓀴
6886𓀴Holmes turned to his desk and, unlocking it, drew out a small
6887𓀴case-book, which he consulted.
6888𓀴
6889𓀴“Farintosh,” said he. “Ah yes, I recall the case; it was concerned with
6890𓀴an opal tiara. I think it was before your time, Watson. I can only say,
6891𓀴madam, that I shall be happy to devote the same care to your case as I
6892𓀴did to that of your friend. As to reward, my profession is its own
6893𓀴reward; but you are at liberty to defray whatever expenses I may be put
6894𓀴to, at the time which suits you best. And now I beg that you will lay
6895𓀴before us everything that may help us in forming an opinion upon the
6896𓀴matter.”
6897𓀴
6898𓀴“Alas!” replied our visitor, “the very horror of my situation lies in
6899𓀴the fact that my fears are so vague, and my suspicions depend so
6900𓀴entirely upon small points, which might seem trivial to another, that
6901𓀴even he to whom of all others I have a right to look for help and
6902𓀴advice looks upon all that I tell him about it as the fancies of a
6903𓀴nervous woman. He does not say so, but I can read it from his soothing
6904𓀴answers and averted eyes. But I have heard, Mr. Holmes, that you can
6905𓀴see deeply into the manifold wickedness of the human heart. You may
6906𓀴advise me how to walk amid the dangers which encompass me.”
6907𓀴
6908𓀴“I am all attention, madam.”
6909𓀴
6910𓀴“My name is Helen Stoner, and I am living with my stepfather, who is
6911𓀴the last survivor of one of the oldest Saxon families in England, the
6912𓀴Roylotts of Stoke Moran, on the western border of Surrey.”
6913𓀴
6914𓀴Holmes nodded his head. “The name is familiar to me,” said he.
6915𓀴
6916𓀴“The family was at one time among the richest in England, and the
6917𓀴estates extended over the borders into Berkshire in the north, and
6918𓀴Hampshire in the west. In the last century, however, four successive
6919𓀴heirs were of a dissolute and wasteful disposition, and the family ruin
6920𓀴was eventually completed by a gambler in the days of the Regency.
6921𓀴Nothing was left save a few acres of ground, and the
6922𓀴two-hundred-year-old house, which is itself crushed under a heavy
6923𓀴mortgage. The last squire dragged out his existence there, living the
6924𓀴horrible life of an aristocratic pauper; but his only son, my
6925𓀴stepfather, seeing that he must adapt himself to the new conditions,
6926𓀴obtained an advance from a relative, which enabled him to take a
6927𓀴medical degree and went out to Calcutta, where, by his professional
6928𓀴skill and his force of character, he established a large practice. In a
6929𓀴fit of anger, however, caused by some robberies which had been
6930𓀴perpetrated in the house, he beat his native butler to death and
6931𓀴narrowly escaped a capital sentence. As it was, he suffered a long term
6932𓀴of imprisonment and afterwards returned to England a morose and
6933𓀴disappointed man.
6934𓀴
6935𓀴“When Dr. Roylott was in India he married my mother, Mrs. Stoner, the
6936𓀴young widow of Major-General Stoner, of the Bengal Artillery. My sister
6937𓀴Julia and I were twins, and we were only two years old at the time of
6938𓀴my mother’s re-marriage. She had a considerable sum of money—not less
6939𓀴than £ 1000 a year—and this she bequeathed to Dr. Roylott entirely
6940𓀴while we resided with him, with a provision that a certain annual sum
6941𓀴should be allowed to each of us in the event of our marriage. Shortly
6942𓀴after our return to England my mother died—she was killed eight years
6943𓀴ago in a railway accident near Crewe. Dr. Roylott then abandoned his
6944𓀴attempts to establish himself in practice in London and took us to live
6945𓀴with him in the old ancestral house at Stoke Moran. The money which my
6946𓀴mother had left was enough for all our wants, and there seemed to be no
6947𓀴obstacle to our happiness.
6948𓀴
6949𓀴“But a terrible change came over our stepfather about this time.
6950𓀴Instead of making friends and exchanging visits with our neighbours,
6951𓀴who had at first been overjoyed to see a Roylott of Stoke Moran back in
6952𓀴the old family seat, he shut himself up in his house and seldom came
6953𓀴out save to indulge in ferocious quarrels with whoever might cross his
6954𓀴path. Violence of temper approaching to mania has been hereditary in
6955𓀴the men of the family, and in my stepfather’s case it had, I believe,
6956𓀴been intensified by his long residence in the tropics. A series of
6957𓀴disgraceful brawls took place, two of which ended in the police-court,
6958𓀴until at last he became the terror of the village, and the folks would
6959𓀴fly at his approach, for he is a man of immense strength, and
6960𓀴absolutely uncontrollable in his anger.
6961𓀴
6962𓀴“Last week he hurled the local blacksmith over a parapet into a stream,
6963𓀴and it was only by paying over all the money which I could gather
6964𓀴together that I was able to avert another public exposure. He had no
6965𓀴friends at all save the wandering gipsies, and he would give these
6966𓀴vagabonds leave to encamp upon the few acres of bramble-covered land
6967𓀴which represent the family estate, and would accept in return the
6968𓀴hospitality of their tents, wandering away with them sometimes for
6969𓀴weeks on end. He has a passion also for Indian animals, which are sent
6970𓀴over to him by a correspondent, and he has at this moment a cheetah and
6971𓀴a baboon, which wander freely over his grounds and are feared by the
6972𓀴villagers almost as much as their master.
6973𓀴
6974𓀴“You can imagine from what I say that my poor sister Julia and I had no
6975𓀴great pleasure in our lives. No servant would stay with us, and for a
6976𓀴long time we did all the work of the house. She was but thirty at the
6977𓀴time of her death, and yet her hair had already begun to whiten, even
6978𓀴as mine has.”
6979𓀴
6980𓀴“Your sister is dead, then?”
6981𓀴
6982𓀴“She died just two years ago, and it is of her death that I wish to
6983𓀴speak to you. You can understand that, living the life which I have
6984𓀴described, we were little likely to see anyone of our own age and
6985𓀴position. We had, however, an aunt, my mother’s maiden sister, Miss
6986𓀴Honoria Westphail, who lives near Harrow, and we were occasionally
6987𓀴allowed to pay short visits at this lady’s house. Julia went there at
6988𓀴Christmas two years ago, and met there a half-pay major of marines, to
6989𓀴whom she became engaged. My stepfather learned of the engagement when
6990𓀴my sister returned and offered no objection to the marriage; but within
6991𓀴a fortnight of the day which had been fixed for the wedding, the
6992𓀴terrible event occurred which has deprived me of my only companion.”
6993𓀴
6994𓀴Sherlock Holmes had been leaning back in his chair with his eyes closed
6995𓀴and his head sunk in a cushion, but he half opened his lids now and
6996𓀴glanced across at his visitor.
6997𓀴
6998𓀴“Pray be precise as to details,” said he.
6999𓀴
7000𓀴“It is easy for me to be so, for every event of that dreadful time is
7001𓀴seared into my memory. The manor-house is, as I have already said, very
7002𓀴old, and only one wing is now inhabited. The bedrooms in this wing are
7003𓀴on the ground floor, the sitting-rooms being in the central block of
7004𓀴the buildings. Of these bedrooms the first is Dr. Roylott’s, the second
7005𓀴my sister’s, and the third my own. There is no communication between
7006𓀴them, but they all open out into the same corridor. Do I make myself
7007𓀴plain?”
7008𓀴
7009𓀴“Perfectly so.”
7010𓀴
7011𓀴“The windows of the three rooms open out upon the lawn. That fatal
7012𓀴night Dr. Roylott had gone to his room early, though we knew that he
7013𓀴had not retired to rest, for my sister was troubled by the smell of the
7014𓀴strong Indian cigars which it was his custom to smoke. She left her
7015𓀴room, therefore, and came into mine, where she sat for some time,
7016𓀴chatting about her approaching wedding. At eleven o’clock she rose to
7017𓀴leave me, but she paused at the door and looked back.
7018𓀴
7019𓀴“‘Tell me, Helen,’ said she, ‘have you ever heard anyone whistle in the
7020𓀴dead of the night?’
7021𓀴
7022𓀴“‘Never,’ said I.
7023𓀴
7024𓀴“‘I suppose that you could not possibly whistle, yourself, in your
7025𓀴sleep?’
7026𓀴
7027𓀴“‘Certainly not. But why?’
7028𓀴
7029𓀴“‘Because during the last few nights I have always, about three in the
7030𓀴morning, heard a low, clear whistle. I am a light sleeper, and it has
7031𓀴awakened me. I cannot tell where it came from—perhaps from the next
7032𓀴room, perhaps from the lawn. I thought that I would just ask you
7033𓀴whether you had heard it.’
7034𓀴
7035𓀴“‘No, I have not. It must be those wretched gipsies in the plantation.’
7036𓀴
7037𓀴“‘Very likely. And yet if it were on the lawn, I wonder that you did
7038𓀴not hear it also.’
7039𓀴
7040𓀴“‘Ah, but I sleep more heavily than you.’
7041𓀴
7042𓀴“‘Well, it is of no great consequence, at any rate.’ She smiled back at
7043𓀴me, closed my door, and a few moments later I heard her key turn in the
7044𓀴lock.”
7045𓀴
7046𓀴“Indeed,” said Holmes. “Was it your custom always to lock yourselves in
7047𓀴at night?”
7048𓀴
7049𓀴“Always.”
7050𓀴
7051𓀴“And why?”
7052𓀴
7053𓀴“I think that I mentioned to you that the Doctor kept a cheetah and a
7054𓀴baboon. We had no feeling of security unless our doors were locked.”
7055𓀴
7056𓀴“Quite so. Pray proceed with your statement.”
7057𓀴
7058𓀴“I could not sleep that night. A vague feeling of impending misfortune
7059𓀴impressed me. My sister and I, you will recollect, were twins, and you
7060𓀴know how subtle are the links which bind two souls which are so closely
7061𓀴allied. It was a wild night. The wind was howling outside, and the rain
7062𓀴was beating and splashing against the windows. Suddenly, amid all the
7063𓀴hubbub of the gale, there burst forth the wild scream of a terrified
7064𓀴woman. I knew that it was my sister’s voice. I sprang from my bed,
7065𓀴wrapped a shawl round me, and rushed into the corridor. As I opened my
7066𓀴door I seemed to hear a low whistle, such as my sister described, and a
7067𓀴few moments later a clanging sound, as if a mass of metal had fallen.
7068𓀴As I ran down the passage, my sister’s door was unlocked, and revolved
7069𓀴slowly upon its hinges. I stared at it horror-stricken, not knowing
7070𓀴what was about to issue from it. By the light of the corridor-lamp I
7071𓀴saw my sister appear at the opening, her face blanched with terror, her
7072𓀴hands groping for help, her whole figure swaying to and fro like that
7073𓀴of a drunkard. I ran to her and threw my arms round her, but at that
7074𓀴moment her knees seemed to give way and she fell to the ground. She
7075𓀴writhed as one who is in terrible pain, and her limbs were dreadfully
7076𓀴convulsed. At first I thought that she had not recognised me, but as I
7077𓀴bent over her she suddenly shrieked out in a voice which I shall never
7078𓀴forget, ‘Oh, my God! Helen! It was the band! The speckled band!’ There
7079𓀴was something else which she would fain have said, and she stabbed with
7080𓀴her finger into the air in the direction of the Doctor’s room, but a
7081𓀴fresh convulsion seized her and choked her words. I rushed out, calling
7082𓀴loudly for my stepfather, and I met him hastening from his room in his
7083𓀴dressing-gown. When he reached my sister’s side she was unconscious,
7084𓀴and though he poured brandy down her throat and sent for medical aid
7085𓀴from the village, all efforts were in vain, for she slowly sank and
7086𓀴died without having recovered her consciousness. Such was the dreadful
7087𓀴end of my beloved sister.”
7088𓀴
7089𓀴“One moment,” said Holmes, “are you sure about this whistle and
7090𓀴metallic sound? Could you swear to it?”
7091𓀴
7092𓀴“That was what the county coroner asked me at the inquiry. It is my
7093𓀴strong impression that I heard it, and yet, among the crash of the gale
7094𓀴and the creaking of an old house, I may possibly have been deceived.”
7095𓀴
7096𓀴“Was your sister dressed?”
7097𓀴
7098𓀴“No, she was in her night-dress. In her right hand was found the
7099𓀴charred stump of a match, and in her left a match-box.”
7100𓀴
7101𓀴“Showing that she had struck a light and looked about her when the
7102𓀴alarm took place. That is important. And what conclusions did the
7103𓀴coroner come to?”
7104𓀴
7105𓀴“He investigated the case with great care, for Dr. Roylott’s conduct
7106𓀴had long been notorious in the county, but he was unable to find any
7107𓀴satisfactory cause of death. My evidence showed that the door had been
7108𓀴fastened upon the inner side, and the windows were blocked by
7109𓀴old-fashioned shutters with broad iron bars, which were secured every
7110𓀴night. The walls were carefully sounded, and were shown to be quite
7111𓀴solid all round, and the flooring was also thoroughly examined, with
7112𓀴the same result. The chimney is wide, but is barred up by four large
7113𓀴staples. It is certain, therefore, that my sister was quite alone when
7114𓀴she met her end. Besides, there were no marks of any violence upon
7115𓀴her.”
7116𓀴
7117𓀴“How about poison?”
7118𓀴
7119𓀴“The doctors examined her for it, but without success.”
7120𓀴
7121𓀴“What do you think that this unfortunate lady died of, then?”
7122𓀴
7123𓀴“It is my belief that she died of pure fear and nervous shock, though
7124𓀴what it was that frightened her I cannot imagine.”
7125𓀴
7126𓀴“Were there gipsies in the plantation at the time?”
7127𓀴
7128𓀴“Yes, there are nearly always some there.”
7129𓀴
7130𓀴“Ah, and what did you gather from this allusion to a band—a speckled
7131𓀴band?”
7132𓀴
7133𓀴“Sometimes I have thought that it was merely the wild talk of delirium,
7134𓀴sometimes that it may have referred to some band of people, perhaps to
7135𓀴these very gipsies in the plantation. I do not know whether the spotted
7136𓀴handkerchiefs which so many of them wear over their heads might have
7137𓀴suggested the strange adjective which she used.”
7138𓀴
7139𓀴Holmes shook his head like a man who is far from being satisfied.
7140𓀴
7141𓀴“These are very deep waters,” said he; “pray go on with your
7142𓀴narrative.”
7143𓀴
7144𓀴“Two years have passed since then, and my life has been until lately
7145𓀴lonelier than ever. A month ago, however, a dear friend, whom I have
7146𓀴known for many years, has done me the honour to ask my hand in
7147𓀴marriage. His name is Armitage—Percy Armitage—the second son of Mr.
7148𓀴Armitage, of Crane Water, near Reading. My stepfather has offered no
7149𓀴opposition to the match, and we are to be married in the course of the
7150𓀴spring. Two days ago some repairs were started in the west wing of the
7151𓀴building, and my bedroom wall has been pierced, so that I have had to
7152𓀴move into the chamber in which my sister died, and to sleep in the very
7153𓀴bed in which she slept. Imagine, then, my thrill of terror when last
7154𓀴night, as I lay awake, thinking over her terrible fate, I suddenly
7155𓀴heard in the silence of the night the low whistle which had been the
7156𓀴herald of her own death. I sprang up and lit the lamp, but nothing was
7157𓀴to be seen in the room. I was too shaken to go to bed again, however,
7158𓀴so I dressed, and as soon as it was daylight I slipped down, got a
7159𓀴dog-cart at the Crown Inn, which is opposite, and drove to Leatherhead,
7160𓀴from whence I have come on this morning with the one object of seeing
7161𓀴you and asking your advice.”
7162𓀴
7163𓀴“You have done wisely,” said my friend. “But have you told me all?”
7164𓀴
7165𓀴“Yes, all.”
7166𓀴
7167𓀴“Miss Roylott, you have not. You are screening your stepfather.”
7168𓀴
7169𓀴“Why, what do you mean?”
7170𓀴
7171𓀴For answer Holmes pushed back the frill of black lace which fringed the
7172𓀴hand that lay upon our visitor’s knee. Five little livid spots, the
7173𓀴marks of four fingers and a thumb, were printed upon the white wrist.
7174𓀴
7175𓀴“You have been cruelly used,” said Holmes.
7176𓀴
