7177𓀴The lady coloured deeply and covered over her injured wrist. “He is a
7178𓀴hard man,” she said, “and perhaps he hardly knows his own strength.”
7179𓀴
7180𓀴There was a long silence, during which Holmes leaned his chin upon his
7181𓀴hands and stared into the crackling fire.
7182𓀴
7183𓀴“This is a very deep business,” he said at last. “There are a thousand
7184𓀴details which I should desire to know before I decide upon our course
7185𓀴of action. Yet we have not a moment to lose. If we were to come to
7186𓀴Stoke Moran to-day, would it be possible for us to see over these rooms
7187𓀴without the knowledge of your stepfather?”
7188𓀴
7189𓀴“As it happens, he spoke of coming into town to-day upon some most
7190𓀴important business. It is probable that he will be away all day, and
7191𓀴that there would be nothing to disturb you. We have a housekeeper now,
7192𓀴but she is old and foolish, and I could easily get her out of the way.”
7193𓀴
7194𓀴“Excellent. You are not averse to this trip, Watson?”
7195𓀴
7196𓀴“By no means.”
7197𓀴
7198𓀴“Then we shall both come. What are you going to do yourself?”
7199𓀴
7200𓀴“I have one or two things which I would wish to do now that I am in
7201𓀴town. But I shall return by the twelve o’clock train, so as to be there
7202𓀴in time for your coming.”
7203𓀴
7204𓀴“And you may expect us early in the afternoon. I have myself some small
7205𓀴business matters to attend to. Will you not wait and breakfast?”
7206𓀴
7207𓀴“No, I must go. My heart is lightened already since I have confided my
7208𓀴trouble to you. I shall look forward to seeing you again this
7209𓀴afternoon.” She dropped her thick black veil over her face and glided
7210𓀴from the room.
7211𓀴
7212𓀴“And what do you think of it all, Watson?” asked Sherlock Holmes,
7213𓀴leaning back in his chair.
7214𓀴
7215𓀴“It seems to me to be a most dark and sinister business.”
7216𓀴
7217𓀴“Dark enough and sinister enough.”
7218𓀴
7219𓀴“Yet if the lady is correct in saying that the flooring and walls are
7220𓀴sound, and that the door, window, and chimney are impassable, then her
7221𓀴sister must have been undoubtedly alone when she met her mysterious
7222𓀴end.”
7223𓀴
7224𓀴“What becomes, then, of these nocturnal whistles, and what of the very
7225𓀴peculiar words of the dying woman?”
7226𓀴
7227𓀴“I cannot think.”
7228𓀴
7229𓀴“When you combine the ideas of whistles at night, the presence of a
7230𓀴band of gipsies who are on intimate terms with this old doctor, the
7231𓀴fact that we have every reason to believe that the doctor has an
7232𓀴interest in preventing his stepdaughter’s marriage, the dying allusion
7233𓀴to a band, and, finally, the fact that Miss Helen Stoner heard a
7234𓀴metallic clang, which might have been caused by one of those metal bars
7235𓀴that secured the shutters falling back into its place, I think that
7236𓀴there is good ground to think that the mystery may be cleared along
7237𓀴those lines.”
7238𓀴
7239𓀴“But what, then, did the gipsies do?”
7240𓀴
7241𓀴“I cannot imagine.”
7242𓀴
7243𓀴“I see many objections to any such theory.”
7244𓀴
7245𓀴“And so do I. It is precisely for that reason that we are going to
7246𓀴Stoke Moran this day. I want to see whether the objections are fatal,
7247𓀴or if they may be explained away. But what in the name of the devil!”
7248𓀴
7249𓀴The ejaculation had been drawn from my companion by the fact that our
7250𓀴door had been suddenly dashed open, and that a huge man had framed
7251𓀴himself in the aperture. His costume was a peculiar mixture of the
7252𓀴professional and of the agricultural, having a black top-hat, a long
7253𓀴frock-coat, and a pair of high gaiters, with a hunting-crop swinging in
7254𓀴his hand. So tall was he that his hat actually brushed the cross bar of
7255𓀴the doorway, and his breadth seemed to span it across from side to
7256𓀴side. A large face, seared with a thousand wrinkles, burned yellow with
7257𓀴the sun, and marked with every evil passion, was turned from one to the
7258𓀴other of us, while his deep-set, bile-shot eyes, and his high, thin,
7259𓀴fleshless nose, gave him somewhat the resemblance to a fierce old bird
7260𓀴of prey.
7261𓀴
7262𓀴“Which of you is Holmes?” asked this apparition.
7263𓀴
7264𓀴“My name, sir; but you have the advantage of me,” said my companion
7265𓀴quietly.
7266𓀴
7267𓀴“I am Dr. Grimesby Roylott, of Stoke Moran.”
7268𓀴
7269𓀴“Indeed, Doctor,” said Holmes blandly. “Pray take a seat.”
7270𓀴
7271𓀴“I will do nothing of the kind. My stepdaughter has been here. I have
7272𓀴traced her. What has she been saying to you?”
7273𓀴
7274𓀴“It is a little cold for the time of the year,” said Holmes.
7275𓀴
7276𓀴“What has she been saying to you?” screamed the old man furiously.
7277𓀴
7278𓀴“But I have heard that the crocuses promise well,” continued my
7279𓀴companion imperturbably.
7280𓀴
7281𓀴“Ha! You put me off, do you?” said our new visitor, taking a step
7282𓀴forward and shaking his hunting-crop. “I know you, you scoundrel! I
7283𓀴have heard of you before. You are Holmes, the meddler.”
7284𓀴
7285𓀴My friend smiled.
7286𓀴
7287𓀴“Holmes, the busybody!”
7288𓀴
7289𓀴His smile broadened.
7290𓀴
7291𓀴“Holmes, the Scotland Yard Jack-in-office!”
7292𓀴
7293𓀴Holmes chuckled heartily. “Your conversation is most entertaining,”
7294𓀴said he. “When you go out close the door, for there is a decided
7295𓀴draught.”
7296𓀴
7297𓀴“I will go when I have had my say. Don’t you dare to meddle with my
7298𓀴affairs. I know that Miss Stoner has been here. I traced her! I am a
7299𓀴dangerous man to fall foul of! See here.” He stepped swiftly forward,
7300𓀴seized the poker, and bent it into a curve with his huge brown hands.
7301𓀴
7302𓀴“See that you keep yourself out of my grip,” he snarled, and hurling
7303𓀴the twisted poker into the fireplace he strode out of the room.
7304𓀴
7305𓀴“He seems a very amiable person,” said Holmes, laughing. “I am not
7306𓀴quite so bulky, but if he had remained I might have shown him that my
7307𓀴grip was not much more feeble than his own.” As he spoke he picked up
7308𓀴the steel poker and, with a sudden effort, straightened it out again.
7309𓀴
7310𓀴“Fancy his having the insolence to confound me with the official
7311𓀴detective force! This incident gives zest to our investigation,
7312𓀴however, and I only trust that our little friend will not suffer from
7313𓀴her imprudence in allowing this brute to trace her. And now, Watson, we
7314𓀴shall order breakfast, and afterwards I shall walk down to Doctors’
7315𓀴Commons, where I hope to get some data which may help us in this
7316𓀴matter.”
7317𓀴
7318𓀴It was nearly one o’clock when Sherlock Holmes returned from his
7319𓀴excursion. He held in his hand a sheet of blue paper, scrawled over
7320𓀴with notes and figures.
7321𓀴
7322𓀴“I have seen the will of the deceased wife,” said he. “To determine its
7323𓀴exact meaning I have been obliged to work out the present prices of the
7324𓀴investments with which it is concerned. The total income, which at the
7325𓀴time of the wife’s death was little short of £ 1,100, is now, through
7326𓀴the fall in agricultural prices, not more than £ 750. Each daughter can
7327𓀴claim an income of £ 250, in case of marriage. It is evident,
7328𓀴therefore, that if both girls had married, this beauty would have had a
7329𓀴mere pittance, while even one of them would cripple him to a very
7330𓀴serious extent. My morning’s work has not been wasted, since it has
7331𓀴proved that he has the very strongest motives for standing in the way
7332𓀴of anything of the sort. And now, Watson, this is too serious for
7333𓀴dawdling, especially as the old man is aware that we are interesting
7334𓀴ourselves in his affairs; so if you are ready, we shall call a cab and
7335𓀴drive to Waterloo. I should be very much obliged if you would slip your
7336𓀴revolver into your pocket. An Eley’s No. 2 is an excellent argument
7337𓀴with gentlemen who can twist steel pokers into knots. That and a
7338𓀴tooth-brush are, I think, all that we need.”
7339𓀴
7340𓀴At Waterloo we were fortunate in catching a train for Leatherhead,
7341𓀴where we hired a trap at the station inn and drove for four or five
7342𓀴miles through the lovely Surrey lanes. It was a perfect day, with a
7343𓀴bright sun and a few fleecy clouds in the heavens. The trees and
7344𓀴wayside hedges were just throwing out their first green shoots, and the
7345𓀴air was full of the pleasant smell of the moist earth. To me at least
7346𓀴there was a strange contrast between the sweet promise of the spring
7347𓀴and this sinister quest upon which we were engaged. My companion sat in
7348𓀴the front of the trap, his arms folded, his hat pulled down over his
7349𓀴eyes, and his chin sunk upon his breast, buried in the deepest thought.
7350𓀴Suddenly, however, he started, tapped me on the shoulder, and pointed
7351𓀴over the meadows.
7352𓀴
7353𓀴“Look there!” said he.
7354𓀴
7355𓀴A heavily timbered park stretched up in a gentle slope, thickening into
7356𓀴a grove at the highest point. From amid the branches there jutted out
7357𓀴the grey gables and high roof-tree of a very old mansion.
7358𓀴
7359𓀴“Stoke Moran?” said he.
7360𓀴
7361𓀴“Yes, sir, that be the house of Dr. Grimesby Roylott,” remarked the
7362𓀴driver.
7363𓀴
7364𓀴“There is some building going on there,” said Holmes; “that is where we
7365𓀴are going.”
7366𓀴
7367𓀴“There’s the village,” said the driver, pointing to a cluster of roofs
7368𓀴some distance to the left; “but if you want to get to the house, you’ll
7369𓀴find it shorter to get over this stile, and so by the footpath over the
7370𓀴fields. There it is, where the lady is walking.”
7371𓀴
7372𓀴“And the lady, I fancy, is Miss Stoner,” observed Holmes, shading his
7373𓀴eyes. “Yes, I think we had better do as you suggest.”
7374𓀴
7375𓀴We got off, paid our fare, and the trap rattled back on its way to
7376𓀴Leatherhead.
7377𓀴
7378𓀴“I thought it as well,” said Holmes as we climbed the stile, “that this
7379𓀴fellow should think we had come here as architects, or on some definite
7380𓀴business. It may stop his gossip. Good-afternoon, Miss Stoner. You see
7381𓀴that we have been as good as our word.”
7382𓀴
7383𓀴Our client of the morning had hurried forward to meet us with a face
7384𓀴which spoke her joy. “I have been waiting so eagerly for you,” she
7385𓀴cried, shaking hands with us warmly. “All has turned out splendidly.
7386𓀴Dr. Roylott has gone to town, and it is unlikely that he will be back
7387𓀴before evening.”
7388𓀴
7389𓀴“We have had the pleasure of making the Doctor’s acquaintance,” said
7390𓀴Holmes, and in a few words he sketched out what had occurred. Miss
7391𓀴Stoner turned white to the lips as she listened.
7392𓀴
7393𓀴“Good heavens!” she cried, “he has followed me, then.”
7394𓀴
7395𓀴“So it appears.”
7396𓀴
7397𓀴“He is so cunning that I never know when I am safe from him. What will
7398𓀴he say when he returns?”
7399𓀴
7400𓀴“He must guard himself, for he may find that there is someone more
7401𓀴cunning than himself upon his track. You must lock yourself up from him
7402𓀴to-night. If he is violent, we shall take you away to your aunt’s at
7403𓀴Harrow. Now, we must make the best use of our time, so kindly take us
7404𓀴at once to the rooms which we are to examine.”
7405𓀴
7406𓀴The building was of grey, lichen-blotched stone, with a high central
7407𓀴portion and two curving wings, like the claws of a crab, thrown out on
7408𓀴each side. In one of these wings the windows were broken and blocked
7409𓀴with wooden boards, while the roof was partly caved in, a picture of
7410𓀴ruin. The central portion was in little better repair, but the
7411𓀴right-hand block was comparatively modern, and the blinds in the
7412𓀴windows, with the blue smoke curling up from the chimneys, showed that
7413𓀴this was where the family resided. Some scaffolding had been erected
7414𓀴against the end wall, and the stone-work had been broken into, but
7415𓀴there were no signs of any workmen at the moment of our visit. Holmes
7416𓀴walked slowly up and down the ill-trimmed lawn and examined with deep
7417𓀴attention the outsides of the windows.
7418𓀴
7419𓀴“This, I take it, belongs to the room in which you used to sleep, the
7420𓀴centre one to your sister’s, and the one next to the main building to
7421𓀴Dr. Roylott’s chamber?”
7422𓀴
7423𓀴“Exactly so. But I am now sleeping in the middle one.”
7424𓀴
7425𓀴“Pending the alterations, as I understand. By the way, there does not
7426𓀴seem to be any very pressing need for repairs at that end wall.”
7427𓀴
7428𓀴“There were none. I believe that it was an excuse to move me from my
7429𓀴room.”
7430𓀴
7431𓀴“Ah! that is suggestive. Now, on the other side of this narrow wing
7432𓀴runs the corridor from which these three rooms open. There are windows
7433𓀴in it, of course?”
7434𓀴
7435𓀴“Yes, but very small ones. Too narrow for anyone to pass through.”
7436𓀴
7437𓀴“As you both locked your doors at night, your rooms were unapproachable
7438𓀴from that side. Now, would you have the kindness to go into your room
7439𓀴and bar your shutters?”
7440𓀴
7441𓀴Miss Stoner did so, and Holmes, after a careful examination through the
7442𓀴open window, endeavoured in every way to force the shutter open, but
7443𓀴without success. There was no slit through which a knife could be
7444𓀴passed to raise the bar. Then with his lens he tested the hinges, but
7445𓀴they were of solid iron, built firmly into the massive masonry. “Hum!”
7446𓀴said he, scratching his chin in some perplexity, “my theory certainly
7447𓀴presents some difficulties. No one could pass these shutters if they
7448𓀴were bolted. Well, we shall see if the inside throws any light upon the
7449𓀴matter.”
7450𓀴
7451𓀴A small side door led into the whitewashed corridor from which the
7452𓀴three bedrooms opened. Holmes refused to examine the third chamber, so
7453𓀴we passed at once to the second, that in which Miss Stoner was now
7454𓀴sleeping, and in which her sister had met with her fate. It was a
7455𓀴homely little room, with a low ceiling and a gaping fireplace, after
7456𓀴the fashion of old country-houses. A brown chest of drawers stood in
7457𓀴one corner, a narrow white-counterpaned bed in another, and a
7458𓀴dressing-table on the left-hand side of the window. These articles,
7459𓀴with two small wicker-work chairs, made up all the furniture in the
7460𓀴room save for a square of Wilton carpet in the centre. The boards round
7461𓀴and the panelling of the walls were of brown, worm-eaten oak, so old
7462𓀴and discoloured that it may have dated from the original building of
7463𓀴the house. Holmes drew one of the chairs into a corner and sat silent,
7464𓀴while his eyes travelled round and round and up and down, taking in
7465𓀴every detail of the apartment.
7466𓀴
7467𓀴“Where does that bell communicate with?” he asked at last pointing to a
7468𓀴thick bell-rope which hung down beside the bed, the tassel actually
7469𓀴lying upon the pillow.
7470𓀴
7471𓀴“It goes to the housekeeper’s room.”
7472𓀴
7473𓀴“It looks newer than the other things?”
7474𓀴
7475𓀴“Yes, it was only put there a couple of years ago.”
7476𓀴
7477𓀴“Your sister asked for it, I suppose?”
7478𓀴
7479𓀴“No, I never heard of her using it. We used always to get what we
7480𓀴wanted for ourselves.”
7481𓀴
7482𓀴“Indeed, it seemed unnecessary to put so nice a bell-pull there. You
7483𓀴will excuse me for a few minutes while I satisfy myself as to this
7484𓀴floor.” He threw himself down upon his face with his lens in his hand
7485𓀴and crawled swiftly backward and forward, examining minutely the cracks
7486𓀴between the boards. Then he did the same with the wood-work with which
7487𓀴the chamber was panelled. Finally he walked over to the bed and spent
7488𓀴some time in staring at it and in running his eye up and down the wall.
7489𓀴Finally he took the bell-rope in his hand and gave it a brisk tug.
7490𓀴
7491𓀴“Why, it’s a dummy,” said he.
7492𓀴
7493𓀴“Won’t it ring?”
7494𓀴
7495𓀴“No, it is not even attached to a wire. This is very interesting. You
7496𓀴can see now that it is fastened to a hook just above where the little
7497𓀴opening for the ventilator is.”
7498𓀴
7499𓀴“How very absurd! I never noticed that before.”
7500𓀴
7501𓀴“Very strange!” muttered Holmes, pulling at the rope. “There are one or
7502𓀴two very singular points about this room. For example, what a fool a
7503𓀴builder must be to open a ventilator into another room, when, with the
7504𓀴same trouble, he might have communicated with the outside air!”
7505𓀴
7506𓀴“That is also quite modern,” said the lady.
7507𓀴
7508𓀴“Done about the same time as the bell-rope?” remarked Holmes.
7509𓀴
7510𓀴“Yes, there were several little changes carried out about that time.”
7511𓀴
7512𓀴“They seem to have been of a most interesting character—dummy
7513𓀴bell-ropes, and ventilators which do not ventilate. With your
7514𓀴permission, Miss Stoner, we shall now carry our researches into the
7515𓀴inner apartment.”
7516𓀴
7517𓀴Dr. Grimesby Roylott’s chamber was larger than that of his
7518𓀴step-daughter, but was as plainly furnished. A camp-bed, a small wooden
7519𓀴shelf full of books, mostly of a technical character, an armchair
7520𓀴beside the bed, a plain wooden chair against the wall, a round table,
7521𓀴and a large iron safe were the principal things which met the eye.
7522𓀴Holmes walked slowly round and examined each and all of them with the
7523𓀴keenest interest.
7524𓀴
7525𓀴“What’s in here?” he asked, tapping the safe.
7526𓀴
7527𓀴“My stepfather’s business papers.”
7528𓀴
7529𓀴“Oh! you have seen inside, then?”
7530𓀴
7531𓀴“Only once, some years ago. I remember that it was full of papers.”
7532𓀴
7533𓀴“There isn’t a cat in it, for example?”
7534𓀴
7535𓀴“No. What a strange idea!”
7536𓀴
7537𓀴“Well, look at this!” He took up a small saucer of milk which stood on
7538𓀴the top of it.
7539𓀴
7540𓀴“No; we don’t keep a cat. But there is a cheetah and a baboon.”
7541𓀴
7542𓀴“Ah, yes, of course! Well, a cheetah is just a big cat, and yet a
7543𓀴saucer of milk does not go very far in satisfying its wants, I daresay.
7544𓀴There is one point which I should wish to determine.” He squatted down
7545𓀴in front of the wooden chair and examined the seat of it with the
7546𓀴greatest attention.
7547𓀴
7548𓀴“Thank you. That is quite settled,” said he, rising and putting his
7549𓀴lens in his pocket. “Hullo! Here is something interesting!”
7550𓀴
7551𓀴The object which had caught his eye was a small dog lash hung on one
7552𓀴corner of the bed. The lash, however, was curled upon itself and tied
7553𓀴so as to make a loop of whipcord.
7554𓀴
7555𓀴“What do you make of that, Watson?”
7556𓀴
7557𓀴“It’s a common enough lash. But I don’t know why it should be tied.”
7558𓀴
7559𓀴“That is not quite so common, is it? Ah, me! it’s a wicked world, and
7560𓀴when a clever man turns his brains to crime it is the worst of all. I
7561𓀴think that I have seen enough now, Miss Stoner, and with your
7562𓀴permission we shall walk out upon the lawn.”
7563𓀴
7564𓀴I had never seen my friend’s face so grim or his brow so dark as it was
7565𓀴when we turned from the scene of this investigation. We had walked
7566𓀴several times up and down the lawn, neither Miss Stoner nor myself
7567𓀴liking to break in upon his thoughts before he roused himself from his
7568𓀴reverie.
7569𓀴
7570𓀴“It is very essential, Miss Stoner,” said he, “that you should
7571𓀴absolutely follow my advice in every respect.”
7572𓀴
7573𓀴“I shall most certainly do so.”
7574𓀴
7575𓀴“The matter is too serious for any hesitation. Your life may depend
7576𓀴upon your compliance.”
7577𓀴
7578𓀴“I assure you that I am in your hands.”
7579𓀴
7580𓀴“In the first place, both my friend and I must spend the night in your
7581𓀴room.”
7582𓀴
7583𓀴Both Miss Stoner and I gazed at him in astonishment.
7584𓀴
7585𓀴“Yes, it must be so. Let me explain. I believe that that is the village
7586𓀴inn over there?”
7587𓀴
7588𓀴“Yes, that is the Crown.”
7589𓀴
7590𓀴“Very good. Your windows would be visible from there?”
7591𓀴
7592𓀴“Certainly.”
7593𓀴
7594𓀴“You must confine yourself to your room, on pretence of a headache,
7595𓀴when your stepfather comes back. Then when you hear him retire for the
7596𓀴night, you must open the shutters of your window, undo the hasp, put
7597𓀴your lamp there as a signal to us, and then withdraw quietly with
7598𓀴everything which you are likely to want into the room which you used to
7599𓀴occupy. I have no doubt that, in spite of the repairs, you could manage
7600𓀴there for one night.”
7601𓀴
7602𓀴“Oh, yes, easily.”
7603𓀴
7604𓀴“The rest you will leave in our hands.”
7605𓀴
7606𓀴“But what will you do?”
7607𓀴
7608𓀴“We shall spend the night in your room, and we shall investigate the
7609𓀴cause of this noise which has disturbed you.”
7610𓀴
7611𓀴“I believe, Mr. Holmes, that you have already made up your mind,” said
7612𓀴Miss Stoner, laying her hand upon my companion’s sleeve.
7613𓀴
7614𓀴“Perhaps I have.”
7615𓀴
7616𓀴“Then, for pity’s sake, tell me what was the cause of my sister’s
7617𓀴death.”
7618𓀴
7619𓀴“I should prefer to have clearer proofs before I speak.”
7620𓀴
7621𓀴“You can at least tell me whether my own thought is correct, and if she
7622𓀴died from some sudden fright.”
7623𓀴
7624𓀴“No, I do not think so. I think that there was probably some more
7625𓀴tangible cause. And now, Miss Stoner, we must leave you for if Dr.
7626𓀴Roylott returned and saw us our journey would be in vain. Good-bye, and
7627𓀴be brave, for if you will do what I have told you, you may rest assured
7628𓀴that we shall soon drive away the dangers that threaten you.”
7629𓀴
7630𓀴Sherlock Holmes and I had no difficulty in engaging a bedroom and
7631𓀴sitting-room at the Crown Inn. They were on the upper floor, and from
7632𓀴our window we could command a view of the avenue gate, and of the
7633𓀴inhabited wing of Stoke Moran Manor House. At dusk we saw Dr. Grimesby
7634𓀴Roylott drive past, his huge form looming up beside the little figure
7635𓀴of the lad who drove him. The boy had some slight difficulty in undoing
7636𓀴the heavy iron gates, and we heard the hoarse roar of the Doctor’s
7637𓀴voice and saw the fury with which he shook his clinched fists at him.
7638𓀴The trap drove on, and a few minutes later we saw a sudden light spring
7639𓀴up among the trees as the lamp was lit in one of the sitting-rooms.
7640𓀴
7641𓀴“Do you know, Watson,” said Holmes as we sat together in the gathering
7642𓀴darkness, “I have really some scruples as to taking you to-night. There
7643𓀴is a distinct element of danger.”
7644𓀴
7645𓀴“Can I be of assistance?”
7646𓀴
7647𓀴“Your presence might be invaluable.”
7648𓀴
7649𓀴“Then I shall certainly come.”
7650𓀴
7651𓀴“It is very kind of you.”
7652𓀴
7653𓀴“You speak of danger. You have evidently seen more in these rooms than
7654𓀴was visible to me.”
7655𓀴
7656𓀴“No, but I fancy that I may have deduced a little more. I imagine that
7657𓀴you saw all that I did.”
7658𓀴
7659𓀴“I saw nothing remarkable save the bell-rope, and what purpose that
7660𓀴could answer I confess is more than I can imagine.”
7661𓀴
7662𓀴“You saw the ventilator, too?”
7663𓀴
7664𓀴“Yes, but I do not think that it is such a very unusual thing to have a
7665𓀴small opening between two rooms. It was so small that a rat could
7666𓀴hardly pass through.”
7667𓀴
7668𓀴“I knew that we should find a ventilator before ever we came to Stoke
7669𓀴Moran.”
7670𓀴
7671𓀴“My dear Holmes!”
7672𓀴
7673𓀴“Oh, yes, I did. You remember in her statement she said that her sister
7674𓀴could smell Dr. Roylott’s cigar. Now, of course that suggested at once
7675𓀴that there must be a communication between the two rooms. It could only
7676𓀴be a small one, or it would have been remarked upon at the coroner’s
7677𓀴inquiry. I deduced a ventilator.”
7678𓀴
7679𓀴“But what harm can there be in that?”
7680𓀴
7681𓀴“Well, there is at least a curious coincidence of dates. A ventilator
7682𓀴is made, a cord is hung, and a lady who sleeps in the bed dies. Does
7683𓀴not that strike you?”
7684𓀴
7685𓀴“I cannot as yet see any connection.”
7686𓀴
7687𓀴“Did you observe anything very peculiar about that bed?”
7688𓀴
7689𓀴“No.”
7690𓀴
7691𓀴“It was clamped to the floor. Did you ever see a bed fastened like that
7692𓀴before?”
7693𓀴
7694𓀴“I cannot say that I have.”
7695𓀴
7696𓀴“The lady could not move her bed. It must always be in the same
7697𓀴relative position to the ventilator and to the rope—or so we may call
7698𓀴it, since it was clearly never meant for a bell-pull.”
7699𓀴
7700𓀴“Holmes,” I cried, “I seem to see dimly what you are hinting at. We are
7701𓀴only just in time to prevent some subtle and horrible crime.”
7702𓀴
7703𓀴“Subtle enough and horrible enough. When a doctor does go wrong he is
7704𓀴the first of criminals. He has nerve and he has knowledge. Palmer and
7705𓀴Pritchard were among the heads of their profession. This man strikes
7706𓀴even deeper, but I think, Watson, that we shall be able to strike
7707𓀴deeper still. But we shall have horrors enough before the night is
7708𓀴over; for goodness’ sake let us have a quiet pipe and turn our minds
7709𓀴for a few hours to something more cheerful.”
7710𓀴
7711𓀴About nine o’clock the light among the trees was extinguished, and all
7712𓀴was dark in the direction of the Manor House. Two hours passed slowly
7713𓀴away, and then, suddenly, just at the stroke of eleven, a single bright
7714𓀴light shone out right in front of us.
7715𓀴
7716𓀴“That is our signal,” said Holmes, springing to his feet; “it comes
7717𓀴from the middle window.”
7718𓀴
7719𓀴As we passed out he exchanged a few words with the landlord, explaining
7720𓀴that we were going on a late visit to an acquaintance, and that it was
7721𓀴possible that we might spend the night there. A moment later we were
7722𓀴out on the dark road, a chill wind blowing in our faces, and one yellow
7723𓀴light twinkling in front of us through the gloom to guide us on our
7724𓀴sombre errand.
7725𓀴
7726𓀴There was little difficulty in entering the grounds, for unrepaired
7727𓀴breaches gaped in the old park wall. Making our way among the trees, we
7728𓀴reached the lawn, crossed it, and were about to enter through the
7729𓀴window when out from a clump of laurel bushes there darted what seemed
7730𓀴to be a hideous and distorted child, who threw itself upon the grass
7731𓀴with writhing limbs and then ran swiftly across the lawn into the
7732𓀴darkness.
7733𓀴
7734𓀴“My God!” I whispered; “did you see it?”
7735𓀴
7736𓀴Holmes was for the moment as startled as I. His hand closed like a vice
7737𓀴upon my wrist in his agitation. Then he broke into a low laugh and put
7738𓀴his lips to my ear.
7739𓀴
7740𓀴“It is a nice household,” he murmured. “That is the baboon.”
7741𓀴
7742𓀴I had forgotten the strange pets which the Doctor affected. There was a
7743𓀴cheetah, too; perhaps we might find it upon our shoulders at any
7744𓀴moment. I confess that I felt easier in my mind when, after following
7745𓀴Holmes’ example and slipping off my shoes, I found myself inside the
7746𓀴bedroom. My companion noiselessly closed the shutters, moved the lamp
7747𓀴onto the table, and cast his eyes round the room. All was as we had
7748𓀴seen it in the daytime. Then creeping up to me and making a trumpet of
7749𓀴his hand, he whispered into my ear again so gently that it was all that
7750𓀴I could do to distinguish the words:
7751𓀴
7752𓀴“The least sound would be fatal to our plans.”
7753𓀴
7754𓀴I nodded to show that I had heard.
7755𓀴
7756𓀴“We must sit without light. He would see it through the ventilator.”
7757𓀴
7758𓀴I nodded again.
7759𓀴
7760𓀴“Do not go asleep; your very life may depend upon it. Have your pistol
7761𓀴ready in case we should need it. I will sit on the side of the bed, and
7762𓀴you in that chair.”
7763𓀴
7764𓀴I took out my revolver and laid it on the corner of the table.
7765𓀴
7766𓀴Holmes had brought up a long thin cane, and this he placed upon the bed
7767𓀴beside him. By it he laid the box of matches and the stump of a candle.
7768𓀴Then he turned down the lamp, and we were left in darkness.
7769𓀴
7770𓀴How shall I ever forget that dreadful vigil? I could not hear a sound,
7771𓀴not even the drawing of a breath, and yet I knew that my companion sat
7772𓀴open-eyed, within a few feet of me, in the same state of nervous
7773𓀴tension in which I was myself. The shutters cut off the least ray of
7774𓀴light, and we waited in absolute darkness.
7775𓀴
7776𓀴From outside came the occasional cry of a night-bird, and once at our
7777𓀴very window a long drawn catlike whine, which told us that the cheetah
7778𓀴was indeed at liberty. Far away we could hear the deep tones of the
7779𓀴parish clock, which boomed out every quarter of an hour. How long they
7780𓀴seemed, those quarters! Twelve struck, and one and two and three, and
7781𓀴still we sat waiting silently for whatever might befall.
7782𓀴
7783𓀴Suddenly there was the momentary gleam of a light up in the direction
7784𓀴of the ventilator, which vanished immediately, but was succeeded by a
7785𓀴strong smell of burning oil and heated metal. Someone in the next room
7786𓀴had lit a dark-lantern. I heard a gentle sound of movement, and then
7787𓀴all was silent once more, though the smell grew stronger. For half an
7788𓀴hour I sat with straining ears. Then suddenly another sound became
7789𓀴audible—a very gentle, soothing sound, like that of a small jet of
7790𓀴steam escaping continually from a kettle. The instant that we heard it,
7791𓀴Holmes sprang from the bed, struck a match, and lashed furiously with
7792𓀴his cane at the bell-pull.
7793𓀴
7794𓀴“You see it, Watson?” he yelled. “You see it?”
7795𓀴
7796𓀴But I saw nothing. At the moment when Holmes struck the light I heard a
7797𓀴low, clear whistle, but the sudden glare flashing into my weary eyes
7798𓀴made it impossible for me to tell what it was at which my friend lashed
7799𓀴so savagely. I could, however, see that his face was deadly pale and
7800𓀴filled with horror and loathing. He had ceased to strike and was gazing
7801𓀴up at the ventilator when suddenly there broke from the silence of the
7802𓀴night the most horrible cry to which I have ever listened. It swelled
7803𓀴up louder and louder, a hoarse yell of pain and fear and anger all
7804𓀴mingled in the one dreadful shriek. They say that away down in the
7805𓀴village, and even in the distant parsonage, that cry raised the
7806𓀴sleepers from their beds. It struck cold to our hearts, and I stood
7807𓀴gazing at Holmes, and he at me, until the last echoes of it had died
7808𓀴away into the silence from which it rose.
7809𓀴
7810𓀴“What can it mean?” I gasped.
7811𓀴
7812𓀴“It means that it is all over,” Holmes answered. “And perhaps, after
7813𓀴all, it is for the best. Take your pistol, and we will enter Dr.
7814𓀴Roylott’s room.”
7815𓀴
7816𓀴With a grave face he lit the lamp and led the way down the corridor.
7817𓀴Twice he struck at the chamber door without any reply from within. Then
7818𓀴he turned the handle and entered, I at his heels, with the cocked
7819𓀴pistol in my hand.
7820𓀴
7821𓀴It was a singular sight which met our eyes. On the table stood a
7822𓀴dark-lantern with the shutter half open, throwing a brilliant beam of
7823𓀴light upon the iron safe, the door of which was ajar. Beside this
7824𓀴table, on the wooden chair, sat Dr. Grimesby Roylott clad in a long
7825𓀴grey dressing-gown, his bare ankles protruding beneath, and his feet
7826𓀴thrust into red heelless Turkish slippers. Across his lap lay the short
7827𓀴stock with the long lash which we had noticed during the day. His chin
7828𓀴was cocked upward and his eyes were fixed in a dreadful, rigid stare at
7829𓀴the corner of the ceiling. Round his brow he had a peculiar yellow
7830𓀴band, with brownish speckles, which seemed to be bound tightly round
7831𓀴his head. As we entered he made neither sound nor motion.
7832𓀴
7833𓀴“The band! the speckled band!” whispered Holmes.
7834𓀴
7835𓀴I took a step forward. In an instant his strange headgear began to
7836𓀴move, and there reared itself from among his hair the squat
7837𓀴diamond-shaped head and puffed neck of a loathsome serpent.
7838𓀴
7839𓀴“It is a swamp adder!” cried Holmes; “the deadliest snake in India. He
7840𓀴has died within ten seconds of being bitten. Violence does, in truth,
7841𓀴recoil upon the violent, and the schemer falls into the pit which he
7842𓀴digs for another. Let us thrust this creature back into its den, and we
7843𓀴can then remove Miss Stoner to some place of shelter and let the county
7844𓀴police know what has happened.”
7845𓀴
7846𓀴As he spoke he drew the dog-whip swiftly from the dead man’s lap, and
7847𓀴throwing the noose round the reptile’s neck he drew it from its horrid
7848𓀴perch and, carrying it at arm’s length, threw it into the iron safe,
7849𓀴which he closed upon it.
7850𓀴
7851𓀴Such are the true facts of the death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott, of Stoke
7852𓀴Moran. It is not necessary that I should prolong a narrative which has
7853𓀴already run to too great a length by telling how we broke the sad news
7854𓀴to the terrified girl, how we conveyed her by the morning train to the
7855𓀴care of her good aunt at Harrow, of how the slow process of official
7856𓀴inquiry came to the conclusion that the doctor met his fate while
7857𓀴indiscreetly playing with a dangerous pet. The little which I had yet
7858𓀴to learn of the case was told me by Sherlock Holmes as we travelled
7859𓀴back next day.
7860𓀴
7861𓀴“I had,” said he, “come to an entirely erroneous conclusion which
7862𓀴shows, my dear Watson, how dangerous it always is to reason from
7863𓀴insufficient data. The presence of the gipsies, and the use of the word
7864𓀴‘band,’ which was used by the poor girl, no doubt, to explain the
7865𓀴appearance which she had caught a hurried glimpse of by the light of
7866𓀴her match, were sufficient to put me upon an entirely wrong scent. I
7867𓀴can only claim the merit that I instantly reconsidered my position
7868𓀴when, however, it became clear to me that whatever danger threatened an
7869𓀴occupant of the room could not come either from the window or the door.
7870𓀴My attention was speedily drawn, as I have already remarked to you, to
7871𓀴this ventilator, and to the bell-rope which hung down to the bed. The
7872𓀴discovery that this was a dummy, and that the bed was clamped to the
7873𓀴floor, instantly gave rise to the suspicion that the rope was there as
7874𓀴a bridge for something passing through the hole and coming to the bed.
7875𓀴The idea of a snake instantly occurred to me, and when I coupled it
7876𓀴with my knowledge that the doctor was furnished with a supply of
7877𓀴creatures from India, I felt that I was probably on the right track.
7878𓀴The idea of using a form of poison which could not possibly be
7879𓀴discovered by any chemical test was just such a one as would occur to a
7880𓀴clever and ruthless man who had had an Eastern training. The rapidity
7881𓀴with which such a poison would take effect would also, from his point
7882𓀴of view, be an advantage. It would be a sharp-eyed coroner, indeed, who
7883𓀴could distinguish the two little dark punctures which would show where
7884𓀴the poison fangs had done their work. Then I thought of the whistle. Of
7885𓀴course he must recall the snake before the morning light revealed it to
7886𓀴the victim. He had trained it, probably by the use of the milk which we
7887𓀴saw, to return to him when summoned. He would put it through this
7888𓀴ventilator at the hour that he thought best, with the certainty that it
7889𓀴would crawl down the rope and land on the bed. It might or might not
7890𓀴bite the occupant, perhaps she might escape every night for a week, but
7891𓀴sooner or later she must fall a victim.
7892𓀴
7893𓀴“I had come to these conclusions before ever I had entered his room. An
7894𓀴inspection of his chair showed me that he had been in the habit of
7895𓀴standing on it, which of course would be necessary in order that he
7896𓀴should reach the ventilator. The sight of the safe, the saucer of milk,
7897𓀴and the loop of whipcord were enough to finally dispel any doubts which
7898𓀴may have remained. The metallic clang heard by Miss Stoner was
7899𓀴obviously caused by her stepfather hastily closing the door of his safe
7900𓀴upon its terrible occupant. Having once made up my mind, you know the
7901𓀴steps which I took in order to put the matter to the proof. I heard the
7902𓀴creature hiss as I have no doubt that you did also, and I instantly lit
7903𓀴the light and attacked it.”
7904𓀴
7905𓀴“With the result of driving it through the ventilator.”
7906𓀴
7907𓀴“And also with the result of causing it to turn upon its master at the
7908𓀴other side. Some of the blows of my cane came home and roused its
7909𓀴snakish temper, so that it flew upon the first person it saw. In this
7910𓀴way I am no doubt indirectly responsible for Dr. Grimesby Roylott’s
7911𓀴death, and I cannot say that it is likely to weigh very heavily upon my
7912𓀴conscience.”
7913𓀴
7914𓀴
7915𓀴
7916𓀴
7917𓀴IX. THE ADVENTURE OF THE ENGINEER’S THUMB
7918𓀴
7919𓀴
7920𓀴Of all the problems which have been submitted to my friend, Mr.
7921𓀴Sherlock Holmes, for solution during the years of our intimacy, there
7922𓀴were only two which I was the means of introducing to his notice—that
7923𓀴of Mr. Hatherley’s thumb, and that of Colonel Warburton’s madness. Of
7924𓀴these the latter may have afforded a finer field for an acute and
7925𓀴original observer, but the other was so strange in its inception and so
7926𓀴dramatic in its details that it may be the more worthy of being placed
7927𓀴upon record, even if it gave my friend fewer openings for those
7928𓀴deductive methods of reasoning by which he achieved such remarkable
7929𓀴results. The story has, I believe, been told more than once in the
7930𓀴newspapers, but, like all such narratives, its effect is much less
7931𓀴striking when set forth _en bloc_ in a single half-column of print than
7932𓀴when the facts slowly evolve before your own eyes, and the mystery
7933𓀴clears gradually away as each new discovery furnishes a step which
7934𓀴leads on to the complete truth. At the time the circumstances made a
7935𓀴deep impression upon me, and the lapse of two years has hardly served
7936𓀴to weaken the effect.
7937𓀴
7938𓀴It was in the summer of ’89, not long after my marriage, that the
7939𓀴events occurred which I am now about to summarise. I had returned to
7940𓀴civil practice and had finally abandoned Holmes in his Baker Street
7941𓀴rooms, although I continually visited him and occasionally even
7942𓀴persuaded him to forgo his Bohemian habits so far as to come and visit
7943𓀴us. My practice had steadily increased, and as I happened to live at no
7944𓀴very great distance from Paddington Station, I got a few patients from
7945𓀴among the officials. One of these, whom I had cured of a painful and
7946𓀴lingering disease, was never weary of advertising my virtues and of
7947𓀴endeavouring to send me on every sufferer over whom he might have any
7948𓀴influence.
7949𓀴
7950𓀴One morning, at a little before seven o’clock, I was awakened by the
7951𓀴maid tapping at the door to announce that two men had come from
7952𓀴Paddington and were waiting in the consulting-room. I dressed
7953𓀴hurriedly, for I knew by experience that railway cases were seldom
7954𓀴trivial, and hastened downstairs. As I descended, my old ally, the
7955𓀴guard, came out of the room and closed the door tightly behind him.
7956𓀴
7957𓀴“I’ve got him here,” he whispered, jerking his thumb over his shoulder;
7958𓀴“he’s all right.”
7959𓀴
7960𓀴“What is it, then?” I asked, for his manner suggested that it was some
7961𓀴strange creature which he had caged up in my room.
7962𓀴
7963𓀴“It’s a new patient,” he whispered. “I thought I’d bring him round
7964𓀴myself; then he couldn’t slip away. There he is, all safe and sound. I
7965𓀴must go now, Doctor; I have my dooties, just the same as you.” And off
7966𓀴he went, this trusty tout, without even giving me time to thank him.
7967𓀴
7968𓀴I entered my consulting-room and found a gentleman seated by the table.
7969𓀴He was quietly dressed in a suit of heather tweed with a soft cloth cap
7970𓀴which he had laid down upon my books. Round one of his hands he had a
7971𓀴handkerchief wrapped, which was mottled all over with bloodstains. He
7972𓀴was young, not more than five-and-twenty, I should say, with a strong,
7973𓀴masculine face; but he was exceedingly pale and gave me the impression
7974𓀴of a man who was suffering from some strong agitation, which it took
7975𓀴all his strength of mind to control.
7976𓀴
7977𓀴“I am sorry to knock you up so early, Doctor,” said he, “but I have had
7978𓀴a very serious accident during the night. I came in by train this
7979𓀴morning, and on inquiring at Paddington as to where I might find a
7980𓀴doctor, a worthy fellow very kindly escorted me here. I gave the maid a
7981𓀴card, but I see that she has left it upon the side-table.”
7982𓀴
7983𓀴I took it up and glanced at it. “Mr. Victor Hatherley, hydraulic
7984𓀴engineer, 16A, Victoria Street (3rd floor).” That was the name, style,
7985𓀴and abode of my morning visitor. “I regret that I have kept you
7986𓀴waiting,” said I, sitting down in my library-chair. “You are fresh from
7987𓀴a night journey, I understand, which is in itself a monotonous
7988𓀴occupation.”
7989𓀴
7990𓀴“Oh, my night could not be called monotonous,” said he, and laughed. He
7991𓀴laughed very heartily, with a high, ringing note, leaning back in his
7992𓀴chair and shaking his sides. All my medical instincts rose up against
7993𓀴that laugh.
7994𓀴
7995𓀴“Stop it!” I cried; “pull yourself together!” and I poured out some
7996𓀴water from a caraffe.
7997𓀴
7998𓀴It was useless, however. He was off in one of those hysterical
7999𓀴outbursts which come upon a strong nature when some great crisis is
8000𓀴over and gone. Presently he came to himself once more, very weary and
8001𓀴pale-looking.
8002𓀴
8003𓀴“I have been making a fool of myself,” he gasped.
8004𓀴
8005𓀴“Not at all. Drink this.” I dashed some brandy into the water, and the
8006𓀴colour began to come back to his bloodless cheeks.
8007𓀴
8008𓀴“That’s better!” said he. “And now, Doctor, perhaps you would kindly
8009𓀴attend to my thumb, or rather to the place where my thumb used to be.”
8010𓀴
8011𓀴He unwound the handkerchief and held out his hand. It gave even my
8012𓀴hardened nerves a shudder to look at it. There were four protruding
8013𓀴fingers and a horrid red, spongy surface where the thumb should have
8014𓀴been. It had been hacked or torn right out from the roots.
8015𓀴
8016𓀴“Good heavens!” I cried, “this is a terrible injury. It must have bled
8017𓀴considerably.”
8018𓀴
8019𓀴“Yes, it did. I fainted when it was done, and I think that I must have
8020𓀴been senseless for a long time. When I came to I found that it was
8021𓀴still bleeding, so I tied one end of my handkerchief very tightly round
8022𓀴the wrist and braced it up with a twig.”
8023𓀴
8024𓀴“Excellent! You should have been a surgeon.”
8025𓀴
8026𓀴“It is a question of hydraulics, you see, and came within my own
8027𓀴province.”
8028𓀴
8029𓀴“This has been done,” said I, examining the wound, “by a very heavy and
8030𓀴sharp instrument.”
8031𓀴
8032𓀴“A thing like a cleaver,” said he.
8033𓀴
8034𓀴“An accident, I presume?”
8035𓀴
8036𓀴“By no means.”
8037𓀴
8038𓀴“What! a murderous attack?”
8039𓀴
8040𓀴“Very murderous indeed.”
8041𓀴
8042𓀴“You horrify me.”
8043𓀴
8044𓀴I sponged the wound, cleaned it, dressed it, and finally covered it
8045𓀴over with cotton wadding and carbolised bandages. He lay back without
8046𓀴wincing, though he bit his lip from time to time.
8047𓀴
8048𓀴“How is that?” I asked when I had finished.
8049𓀴
8050𓀴“Capital! Between your brandy and your bandage, I feel a new man. I was
8051𓀴very weak, but I have had a good deal to go through.”
8052𓀴
8053𓀴“Perhaps you had better not speak of the matter. It is evidently trying
8054𓀴to your nerves.”
8055𓀴
8056𓀴“Oh, no, not now. I shall have to tell my tale to the police; but,
8057𓀴between ourselves, if it were not for the convincing evidence of this
8058𓀴wound of mine, I should be surprised if they believed my statement, for
8059𓀴it is a very extraordinary one, and I have not much in the way of proof
8060𓀴with which to back it up; and, even if they believe me, the clues which
8061𓀴I can give them are so vague that it is a question whether justice will
8062𓀴be done.”
8063𓀴
8064𓀴“Ha!” cried I, “if it is anything in the nature of a problem which you
8065𓀴desire to see solved, I should strongly recommend you to come to my
8066𓀴friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, before you go to the official police.”
8067𓀴
8068𓀴“Oh, I have heard of that fellow,” answered my visitor, “and I should
8069𓀴be very glad if he would take the matter up, though of course I must
8070𓀴use the official police as well. Would you give me an introduction to
8071𓀴him?”
8072𓀴
8073𓀴“I’ll do better. I’ll take you round to him myself.”
8074𓀴
8075𓀴“I should be immensely obliged to you.”
8076𓀴
8077𓀴“We’ll call a cab and go together. We shall just be in time to have a
8078𓀴little breakfast with him. Do you feel equal to it?”
8079𓀴
8080𓀴“Yes; I shall not feel easy until I have told my story.”
8081𓀴
8082𓀴“Then my servant will call a cab, and I shall be with you in an
8083𓀴instant.” I rushed upstairs, explained the matter shortly to my wife,
8084𓀴and in five minutes was inside a hansom, driving with my new
8085𓀴acquaintance to Baker Street.
8086𓀴
8087𓀴Sherlock Holmes was, as I expected, lounging about his sitting-room in
8088𓀴his dressing-gown, reading the agony column of _The Times_ and smoking
8089𓀴his before-breakfast pipe, which was composed of all the plugs and
8090𓀴dottles left from his smokes of the day before, all carefully dried and
8091𓀴collected on the corner of the mantelpiece. He received us in his
8092𓀴quietly genial fashion, ordered fresh rashers and eggs, and joined us
8093𓀴in a hearty meal. When it was concluded he settled our new acquaintance
8094𓀴upon the sofa, placed a pillow beneath his head, and laid a glass of
8095𓀴brandy and water within his reach.
8096𓀴
8097𓀴“It is easy to see that your experience has been no common one, Mr.
8098𓀴Hatherley,” said he. “Pray, lie down there and make yourself absolutely
8099𓀴at home. Tell us what you can, but stop when you are tired and keep up
8100𓀴your strength with a little stimulant.”
8101𓀴
8102𓀴“Thank you,” said my patient, “but I have felt another man since the
8103𓀴doctor bandaged me, and I think that your breakfast has completed the
8104𓀴cure. I shall take up as little of your valuable time as possible, so I
8105𓀴shall start at once upon my peculiar experiences.”
8106𓀴
8107𓀴Holmes sat in his big armchair with the weary, heavy-lidded expression
8108𓀴which veiled his keen and eager nature, while I sat opposite to him,
8109𓀴and we listened in silence to the strange story which our visitor
8110𓀴detailed to us.
8111𓀴
8112𓀴“You must know,” said he, “that I am an orphan and a bachelor, residing
8113𓀴alone in lodgings in London. By profession I am a hydraulic engineer,
8114𓀴and I have had considerable experience of my work during the seven
8115𓀴years that I was apprenticed to Venner & Matheson, the well-known firm,
8116𓀴of Greenwich. Two years ago, having served my time, and having also
8117𓀴come into a fair sum of money through my poor father’s death, I
8118𓀴determined to start in business for myself and took professional
8119𓀴chambers in Victoria Street.
8120𓀴
8121𓀴“I suppose that everyone finds his first independent start in business
8122𓀴a dreary experience. To me it has been exceptionally so. During two
8123𓀴years I have had three consultations and one small job, and that is
8124𓀴absolutely all that my profession has brought me. My gross takings
8125𓀴amount to £ 27 10_s_. Every day, from nine in the morning until four in
8126𓀴the afternoon, I waited in my little den, until at last my heart began
8127𓀴to sink, and I came to believe that I should never have any practice at
8128𓀴all.
8129𓀴
8130𓀴“Yesterday, however, just as I was thinking of leaving the office, my
8131𓀴clerk entered to say there was a gentleman waiting who wished to see me
8132𓀴upon business. He brought up a card, too, with the name of ‘Colonel
8133𓀴Lysander Stark’ engraved upon it. Close at his heels came the colonel
8134𓀴himself, a man rather over the middle size, but of an exceeding
8135𓀴thinness. I do not think that I have ever seen so thin a man. His whole
8136𓀴face sharpened away into nose and chin, and the skin of his cheeks was
8137𓀴drawn quite tense over his outstanding bones. Yet this emaciation
8138𓀴seemed to be his natural habit, and due to no disease, for his eye was
8139𓀴bright, his step brisk, and his bearing assured. He was plainly but
8140𓀴neatly dressed, and his age, I should judge, would be nearer forty than
8141𓀴thirty.
8142𓀴
8143𓀴“‘Mr. Hatherley?’ said he, with something of a German accent. ‘You have
8144𓀴been recommended to me, Mr. Hatherley, as being a man who is not only
8145𓀴proficient in his profession but is also discreet and capable of
8146𓀴preserving a secret.’
8147𓀴
8148𓀴“I bowed, feeling as flattered as any young man would at such an
8149𓀴address. ‘May I ask who it was who gave me so good a character?’
8150𓀴
8151𓀴“‘Well, perhaps it is better that I should not tell you that just at
8152𓀴this moment. I have it from the same source that you are both an orphan
8153𓀴and a bachelor and are residing alone in London.’
8154𓀴
8155𓀴“‘That is quite correct,’ I answered; ‘but you will excuse me if I say
8156𓀴that I cannot see how all this bears upon my professional
8157𓀴qualifications. I understand that it was on a professional matter that
8158𓀴you wished to speak to me?’
8159𓀴
8160𓀴“‘Undoubtedly so. But you will find that all I say is really to the
8161𓀴point. I have a professional commission for you, but absolute secrecy
8162𓀴is quite essential—absolute secrecy, you understand, and of course we
8163𓀴may expect that more from a man who is alone than from one who lives in
8164𓀴the bosom of his family.’
8165𓀴
8166𓀴“‘If I promise to keep a secret,’ said I, ‘you may absolutely depend
8167𓀴upon my doing so.’
8168𓀴
8169𓀴“He looked very hard at me as I spoke, and it seemed to me that I had
8170𓀴never seen so suspicious and questioning an eye.
8171𓀴
8172𓀴“‘Do you promise, then?’ said he at last.
8173𓀴
8174𓀴“‘Yes, I promise.’
8175𓀴
8176𓀴“‘Absolute and complete silence before, during, and after? No reference
8177𓀴to the matter at all, either in word or writing?’
8178𓀴
8179𓀴“‘I have already given you my word.’
8180𓀴
8181𓀴“‘Very good.’ He suddenly sprang up, and darting like lightning across
8182𓀴the room he flung open the door. The passage outside was empty.
8183𓀴
8184𓀴“‘That’s all right,’ said he, coming back. ‘I know that clerks are
8185𓀴sometimes curious as to their master’s affairs. Now we can talk in
8186𓀴safety.’ He drew up his chair very close to mine and began to stare at
8187𓀴me again with the same questioning and thoughtful look.
8188𓀴
8189𓀴“A feeling of repulsion, and of something akin to fear had begun to
8190𓀴rise within me at the strange antics of this fleshless man. Even my
8191𓀴dread of losing a client could not restrain me from showing my
8192𓀴impatience.
8193𓀴
8194𓀴“‘I beg that you will state your business, sir,’ said I; ‘my time is of
8195𓀴value.’ Heaven forgive me for that last sentence, but the words came to
8196𓀴my lips.
8197𓀴
8198𓀴“‘How would fifty guineas for a night’s work suit you?’ he asked.
8199𓀴
8200𓀴“‘Most admirably.’
8201𓀴
8202𓀴“‘I say a night’s work, but an hour’s would be nearer the mark. I
8203𓀴simply want your opinion about a hydraulic stamping machine which has
8204𓀴got out of gear. If you show us what is wrong we shall soon set it
8205𓀴right ourselves. What do you think of such a commission as that?’
8206𓀴
8207𓀴“‘The work appears to be light and the pay munificent.’
8208𓀴
8209𓀴“‘Precisely so. We shall want you to come to-night by the last train.’
8210𓀴
8211𓀴“‘Where to?’
8212𓀴
8213𓀴“‘To Eyford, in Berkshire. It is a little place near the borders of
8214𓀴Oxfordshire, and within seven miles of Reading. There is a train from
8215𓀴Paddington which would bring you there at about 11:15.’
8216𓀴
8217𓀴“‘Very good.’
8218𓀴
8219𓀴“‘I shall come down in a carriage to meet you.’
8220𓀴
8221𓀴“‘There is a drive, then?’
8222𓀴
8223𓀴“‘Yes, our little place is quite out in the country. It is a good seven
8224𓀴miles from Eyford Station.’
8225𓀴
8226𓀴“‘Then we can hardly get there before midnight. I suppose there would
8227𓀴be no chance of a train back. I should be compelled to stop the night.’
8228𓀴
8229𓀴“‘Yes, we could easily give you a shake-down.’
8230𓀴
8231𓀴“‘That is very awkward. Could I not come at some more convenient hour?’
8232𓀴
8233𓀴“‘We have judged it best that you should come late. It is to recompense
8234𓀴you for any inconvenience that we are paying to you, a young and
8235𓀴unknown man, a fee which would buy an opinion from the very heads of
8236𓀴your profession. Still, of course, if you would like to draw out of the
8237𓀴business, there is plenty of time to do so.’
8238𓀴
8239𓀴“I thought of the fifty guineas, and of how very useful they would be
8240𓀴to me. ‘Not at all,’ said I, ‘I shall be very happy to accommodate
8241𓀴myself to your wishes. I should like, however, to understand a little
8242𓀴more clearly what it is that you wish me to do.’
8243𓀴
8244𓀴“‘Quite so. It is very natural that the pledge of secrecy which we have
8245𓀴exacted from you should have aroused your curiosity. I have no wish to
8246𓀴commit you to anything without your having it all laid before you. I
8247𓀴suppose that we are absolutely safe from eavesdroppers?’
8248𓀴
8249𓀴“‘Entirely.’
8250𓀴
8251𓀴“‘Then the matter stands thus. You are probably aware that
8252𓀴fuller’s-earth is a valuable product, and that it is only found in one
8253𓀴or two places in England?’
8254𓀴
8255𓀴“‘I have heard so.’
8256𓀴
8257𓀴“‘Some little time ago I bought a small place—a very small place—within
8258𓀴ten miles of Reading. I was fortunate enough to discover that there was
8259𓀴a deposit of fuller’s-earth in one of my fields. On examining it,
8260𓀴however, I found that this deposit was a comparatively small one, and
8261𓀴that it formed a link between two very much larger ones upon the right
8262𓀴and left—both of them, however, in the grounds of my neighbours. These
8263𓀴good people were absolutely ignorant that their land contained that
8264𓀴which was quite as valuable as a gold-mine. Naturally, it was to my
8265𓀴interest to buy their land before they discovered its true value, but
8266𓀴unfortunately I had no capital by which I could do this. I took a few
8267𓀴of my friends into the secret, however, and they suggested that we
8268𓀴should quietly and secretly work our own little deposit and that in
8269𓀴this way we should earn the money which would enable us to buy the
8270𓀴neighbouring fields. This we have now been doing for some time, and in
8271𓀴order to help us in our operations we erected a hydraulic press. This
8272𓀴press, as I have already explained, has got out of order, and we wish
8273𓀴your advice upon the subject. We guard our secret very jealously,
8274𓀴however, and if it once became known that we had hydraulic engineers
8275𓀴coming to our little house, it would soon rouse inquiry, and then, if
8276𓀴the facts came out, it would be good-bye to any chance of getting these
8277𓀴fields and carrying out our plans. That is why I have made you promise
8278𓀴me that you will not tell a human being that you are going to Eyford
8279𓀴to-night. I hope that I make it all plain?’
8280𓀴
8281𓀴“‘I quite follow you,’ said I. ‘The only point which I could not quite
8282𓀴understand was what use you could make of a hydraulic press in
8283𓀴excavating fuller’s-earth, which, as I understand, is dug out like
8284𓀴gravel from a pit.’
8285𓀴
8286𓀴“‘Ah!’ said he carelessly, ‘we have our own process. We compress the
8287𓀴earth into bricks, so as to remove them without revealing what they
8288𓀴are. But that is a mere detail. I have taken you fully into my
8289𓀴confidence now, Mr. Hatherley, and I have shown you how I trust you.’
8290𓀴He rose as he spoke. ‘I shall expect you, then, at Eyford at 11:15.’
8291𓀴
8292𓀴“‘I shall certainly be there.’
8293𓀴
8294𓀴“‘And not a word to a soul.’ He looked at me with a last long,
8295𓀴questioning gaze, and then, pressing my hand in a cold, dank grasp, he
8296𓀴hurried from the room.
8297𓀴
8298𓀴“Well, when I came to think it all over in cool blood I was very much
8299𓀴astonished, as you may both think, at this sudden commission which had
8300𓀴been intrusted to me. On the one hand, of course, I was glad, for the
8301𓀴fee was at least tenfold what I should have asked had I set a price
8302𓀴upon my own services, and it was possible that this order might lead to
8303𓀴other ones. On the other hand, the face and manner of my patron had
8304𓀴made an unpleasant impression upon me, and I could not think that his
8305𓀴explanation of the fuller’s-earth was sufficient to explain the
8306𓀴necessity for my coming at midnight, and his extreme anxiety lest I
8307𓀴should tell anyone of my errand. However, I threw all fears to the
8308𓀴winds, ate a hearty supper, drove to Paddington, and started off,
8309𓀴having obeyed to the letter the injunction as to holding my tongue.
8310𓀴
8311𓀴“At Reading I had to change not only my carriage but my station.
8312𓀴However, I was in time for the last train to Eyford, and I reached the
8313𓀴little dim-lit station after eleven o’clock. I was the only passenger
8314𓀴who got out there, and there was no one upon the platform save a single
8315𓀴sleepy porter with a lantern. As I passed out through the wicket gate,
8316𓀴however, I found my acquaintance of the morning waiting in the shadow
8317𓀴upon the other side. Without a word he grasped my arm and hurried me
8318𓀴into a carriage, the door of which was standing open. He drew up the
8319𓀴windows on either side, tapped on the wood-work, and away we went as
8320𓀴fast as the horse could go.”
8321𓀴
8322𓀴“One horse?” interjected Holmes.
8323𓀴
8324𓀴“Yes, only one.”
8325𓀴
8326𓀴“Did you observe the colour?”
8327𓀴
8328𓀴“Yes, I saw it by the side-lights when I was stepping into the
8329𓀴carriage. It was a chestnut.”
8330𓀴
8331𓀴“Tired-looking or fresh?”
8332𓀴
8333𓀴“Oh, fresh and glossy.”
8334𓀴
8335𓀴“Thank you. I am sorry to have interrupted you. Pray continue your most
8336𓀴interesting statement.”
8337𓀴
8338𓀴“Away we went then, and we drove for at least an hour. Colonel Lysander
8339𓀴Stark had said that it was only seven miles, but I should think, from
8340𓀴the rate that we seemed to go, and from the time that we took, that it
8341𓀴must have been nearer twelve. He sat at my side in silence all the
8342𓀴time, and I was aware, more than once when I glanced in his direction,
8343𓀴that he was looking at me with great intensity. The country roads seem
8344𓀴to be not very good in that part of the world, for we lurched and
8345𓀴jolted terribly. I tried to look out of the windows to see something of
8346𓀴where we were, but they were made of frosted glass, and I could make
8347𓀴out nothing save the occasional bright blur of a passing light. Now and
8348𓀴then I hazarded some remark to break the monotony of the journey, but
8349𓀴the colonel answered only in monosyllables, and the conversation soon
8350𓀴flagged. At last, however, the bumping of the road was exchanged for
8351𓀴the crisp smoothness of a gravel-drive, and the carriage came to a
8352𓀴stand. Colonel Lysander Stark sprang out, and, as I followed after him,
8353𓀴pulled me swiftly into a porch which gaped in front of us. We stepped,
8354𓀴as it were, right out of the carriage and into the hall, so that I
8355𓀴failed to catch the most fleeting glance of the front of the house. The
8356𓀴instant that I had crossed the threshold the door slammed heavily
8357𓀴behind us, and I heard faintly the rattle of the wheels as the carriage
8358𓀴drove away.
8359𓀴
8360𓀴“It was pitch dark inside the house, and the colonel fumbled about
8361𓀴looking for matches and muttering under his breath. Suddenly a door
8362𓀴opened at the other end of the passage, and a long, golden bar of light
8363𓀴shot out in our direction. It grew broader, and a woman appeared with a
8364𓀴lamp in her hand, which she held above her head, pushing her face
8365𓀴forward and peering at us. I could see that she was pretty, and from
8366𓀴the gloss with which the light shone upon her dark dress I knew that it
8367𓀴was a rich material. She spoke a few words in a foreign tongue in a
8368𓀴tone as though asking a question, and when my companion answered in a
8369𓀴gruff monosyllable she gave such a start that the lamp nearly fell from
8370𓀴her hand. Colonel Stark went up to her, whispered something in her ear,
8371𓀴and then, pushing her back into the room from whence she had come, he
8372𓀴walked towards me again with the lamp in his hand.
8373𓀴
8374𓀴“‘Perhaps you will have the kindness to wait in this room for a few
8375𓀴minutes,’ said he, throwing open another door. It was a quiet, little,
8376𓀴plainly furnished room, with a round table in the centre, on which
8377𓀴several German books were scattered. Colonel Stark laid down the lamp
8378𓀴on the top of a harmonium beside the door. ‘I shall not keep you
8379𓀴waiting an instant,’ said he, and vanished into the darkness.
8380𓀴
8381𓀴“I glanced at the books upon the table, and in spite of my ignorance of
8382𓀴German I could see that two of them were treatises on science, the
8383𓀴others being volumes of poetry. Then I walked across to the window,
8384𓀴hoping that I might catch some glimpse of the country-side, but an oak
8385𓀴shutter, heavily barred, was folded across it. It was a wonderfully
8386𓀴silent house. There was an old clock ticking loudly somewhere in the
8387𓀴passage, but otherwise everything was deadly still. A vague feeling of
8388𓀴uneasiness began to steal over me. Who were these German people, and
8389𓀴what were they doing living in this strange, out-of-the-way place? And
8390𓀴where was the place? I was ten miles or so from Eyford, that was all I
8391𓀴knew, but whether north, south, east, or west I had no idea. For that
8392𓀴matter, Reading, and possibly other large towns, were within that
8393𓀴radius, so the place might not be so secluded, after all. Yet it was
8394𓀴quite certain, from the absolute stillness, that we were in the
8395𓀴country. I paced up and down the room, humming a tune under my breath
8396𓀴to keep up my spirits and feeling that I was thoroughly earning my
8397𓀴fifty-guinea fee.
8398𓀴
8399𓀴“Suddenly, without any preliminary sound in the midst of the utter
8400𓀴stillness, the door of my room swung slowly open. The woman was
8401𓀴standing in the aperture, the darkness of the hall behind her, the
8402𓀴yellow light from my lamp beating upon her eager and beautiful face. I
8403𓀴could see at a glance that she was sick with fear, and the sight sent a
8404𓀴chill to my own heart. She held up one shaking finger to warn me to be
8405𓀴silent, and she shot a few whispered words of broken English at me, her
8406𓀴eyes glancing back, like those of a frightened horse, into the gloom
8407𓀴behind her.
8408𓀴
8409𓀴“‘I would go,’ said she, trying hard, as it seemed to me, to speak
8410𓀴calmly; ‘I would go. I should not stay here. There is no good for you
8411𓀴to do.’
8412𓀴
8413𓀴“‘But, madam,’ said I, ‘I have not yet done what I came for. I cannot
8414𓀴possibly leave until I have seen the machine.’
8415𓀴
8416𓀴“‘It is not worth your while to wait,’ she went on. ‘You can pass
8417𓀴through the door; no one hinders.’ And then, seeing that I smiled and
8418𓀴shook my head, she suddenly threw aside her constraint and made a step
8419𓀴forward, with her hands wrung together. ‘For the love of Heaven!’ she
8420𓀴whispered, ‘get away from here before it is too late!’
8421𓀴
8422𓀴“But I am somewhat headstrong by nature, and the more ready to engage
8423𓀴in an affair when there is some obstacle in the way. I thought of my
8424𓀴fifty-guinea fee, of my wearisome journey, and of the unpleasant night
8425𓀴which seemed to be before me. Was it all to go for nothing? Why should
8426𓀴I slink away without having carried out my commission, and without the
8427𓀴payment which was my due? This woman might, for all I knew, be a
8428𓀴monomaniac. With a stout bearing, therefore, though her manner had
8429𓀴shaken me more than I cared to confess, I still shook my head and
8430𓀴declared my intention of remaining where I was. She was about to renew
8431𓀴her entreaties when a door slammed overhead, and the sound of several
8432𓀴footsteps was heard upon the stairs. She listened for an instant, threw
8433𓀴up her hands with a despairing gesture, and vanished as suddenly and as
8434𓀴noiselessly as she had come.
8435𓀴
8436𓀴“The newcomers were Colonel Lysander Stark and a short thick man with a
8437𓀴chinchilla beard growing out of the creases of his double chin, who was
8438𓀴introduced to me as Mr. Ferguson.
8439𓀴
8440𓀴“‘This is my secretary and manager,’ said the colonel. ‘By the way, I
8441𓀴was under the impression that I left this door shut just now. I fear
8442𓀴that you have felt the draught.’
8443𓀴
8444𓀴“‘On the contrary,’ said I, ‘I opened the door myself because I felt
8445𓀴the room to be a little close.’
8446𓀴
8447𓀴“He shot one of his suspicious looks at me. ‘Perhaps we had better
8448𓀴proceed to business, then,’ said he. ‘Mr. Ferguson and I will take you
8449𓀴up to see the machine.’
8450𓀴
8451𓀴“‘I had better put my hat on, I suppose.’
8452𓀴
8453𓀴“‘Oh, no, it is in the house.’
8454𓀴
8455𓀴“‘What, you dig fuller’s-earth in the house?’
8456𓀴
8457𓀴“‘No, no. This is only where we compress it. But never mind that. All
8458𓀴we wish you to do is to examine the machine and to let us know what is
8459𓀴wrong with it.’
8460𓀴
8461𓀴“We went upstairs together, the colonel first with the lamp, the fat
8462𓀴manager and I behind him. It was a labyrinth of an old house, with
8463𓀴corridors, passages, narrow winding staircases, and little low doors,
8464𓀴the thresholds of which were hollowed out by the generations who had
8465𓀴crossed them. There were no carpets and no signs of any furniture above
8466𓀴the ground floor, while the plaster was peeling off the walls, and the
8467𓀴damp was breaking through in green, unhealthy blotches. I tried to put
8468𓀴on as unconcerned an air as possible, but I had not forgotten the
8469𓀴warnings of the lady, even though I disregarded them, and I kept a keen
8470𓀴eye upon my two companions. Ferguson appeared to be a morose and silent
8471𓀴man, but I could see from the little that he said that he was at least
8472𓀴a fellow-countryman.
8473𓀴
8474𓀴“Colonel Lysander Stark stopped at last before a low door, which he
8475𓀴unlocked. Within was a small, square room, in which the three of us
8476𓀴could hardly get at one time. Ferguson remained outside, and the
8477𓀴colonel ushered me in.
8478𓀴
8479𓀴“‘We are now,’ said he, ‘actually within the hydraulic press, and it
8480𓀴would be a particularly unpleasant thing for us if anyone were to turn
8481𓀴it on. The ceiling of this small chamber is really the end of the
8482𓀴descending piston, and it comes down with the force of many tons upon
8483𓀴this metal floor. There are small lateral columns of water outside
8484𓀴which receive the force, and which transmit and multiply it in the
8485𓀴manner which is familiar to you. The machine goes readily enough, but
8486𓀴there is some stiffness in the working of it, and it has lost a little
8487𓀴of its force. Perhaps you will have the goodness to look it over and to
8488𓀴show us how we can set it right.’
8489𓀴
8490𓀴“I took the lamp from him, and I examined the machine very thoroughly.
8491𓀴It was indeed a gigantic one, and capable of exercising enormous
8492𓀴pressure. When I passed outside, however, and pressed down the levers
8493𓀴which controlled it, I knew at once by the whishing sound that there
8494𓀴was a slight leakage, which allowed a regurgitation of water through
8495𓀴one of the side cylinders. An examination showed that one of the
8496𓀴india-rubber bands which was round the head of a driving-rod had shrunk
8497𓀴so as not quite to fill the socket along which it worked. This was
8498𓀴clearly the cause of the loss of power, and I pointed it out to my
8499𓀴companions, who followed my remarks very carefully and asked several
8500𓀴practical questions as to how they should proceed to set it right. When
8501𓀴I had made it clear to them, I returned to the main chamber of the
8502𓀴machine and took a good look at it to satisfy my own curiosity. It was
8503𓀴obvious at a glance that the story of the fuller’s-earth was the merest
8504𓀴fabrication, for it would be absurd to suppose that so powerful an
8505𓀴engine could be designed for so inadequate a purpose. The walls were of
8506𓀴wood, but the floor consisted of a large iron trough, and when I came
8507𓀴to examine it I could see a crust of metallic deposit all over it. I
8508𓀴had stooped and was scraping at this to see exactly what it was when I
8509𓀴heard a muttered exclamation in German and saw the cadaverous face of
8510𓀴the colonel looking down at me.
8511𓀴
8512𓀴“‘What are you doing there?’ he asked.
8513𓀴
8514𓀴“I felt angry at having been tricked by so elaborate a story as that
8515𓀴which he had told me. ‘I was admiring your fuller’s-earth,’ said I; ‘I
8516𓀴think that I should be better able to advise you as to your machine if
8517𓀴I knew what the exact purpose was for which it was used.’
8518𓀴
8519𓀴“The instant that I uttered the words I regretted the rashness of my
8520𓀴speech. His face set hard, and a baleful light sprang up in his grey
8521𓀴eyes.
8522𓀴
8523𓀴“‘Very well,’ said he, ‘you shall know all about the machine.’ He took
8524𓀴a step backward, slammed the little door, and turned the key in the
8525𓀴lock. I rushed towards it and pulled at the handle, but it was quite
8526𓀴secure, and did not give in the least to my kicks and shoves. ‘Hullo!’
8527𓀴I yelled. ‘Hullo! Colonel! Let me out!’
8528𓀴
8529𓀴“And then suddenly in the silence I heard a sound which sent my heart
8530𓀴into my mouth. It was the clank of the levers and the swish of the
8531𓀴leaking cylinder. He had set the engine at work. The lamp still stood
8532𓀴upon the floor where I had placed it when examining the trough. By its
8533𓀴light I saw that the black ceiling was coming down upon me, slowly,
8534𓀴jerkily, but, as none knew better than myself, with a force which must
8535𓀴within a minute grind me to a shapeless pulp. I threw myself,
8536𓀴screaming, against the door, and dragged with my nails at the lock. I
8537𓀴implored the colonel to let me out, but the remorseless clanking of the
8538𓀴levers drowned my cries. The ceiling was only a foot or two above my
8539𓀴head, and with my hand upraised I could feel its hard, rough surface.
8540𓀴Then it flashed through my mind that the pain of my death would depend
8541𓀴very much upon the position in which I met it. If I lay on my face the
8542𓀴weight would come upon my spine, and I shuddered to think of that
8543𓀴dreadful snap. Easier the other way, perhaps; and yet, had I the nerve
8544𓀴to lie and look up at that deadly black shadow wavering down upon me?
8545𓀴Already I was unable to stand erect, when my eye caught something which
8546𓀴brought a gush of hope back to my heart.
8547𓀴
8548𓀴“I have said that though the floor and ceiling were of iron, the walls
8549𓀴were of wood. As I gave a last hurried glance around, I saw a thin line
8550𓀴of yellow light between two of the boards, which broadened and
8551𓀴broadened as a small panel was pushed backward. For an instant I could
8552𓀴hardly believe that here was indeed a door which led away from death.
8553𓀴The next instant I threw myself through, and lay half-fainting upon the
8554𓀴other side. The panel had closed again behind me, but the crash of the
8555𓀴lamp, and a few moments afterwards the clang of the two slabs of metal,
8556𓀴told me how narrow had been my escape.
8557𓀴
8558𓀴“I was recalled to myself by a frantic plucking at my wrist, and I
8559𓀴found myself lying upon the stone floor of a narrow corridor, while a
8560𓀴woman bent over me and tugged at me with her left hand, while she held
8561𓀴a candle in her right. It was the same good friend whose warning I had
8562𓀴so foolishly rejected.
8563𓀴
8564𓀴“‘Come! come!’ she cried breathlessly. ‘They will be here in a moment.
8565𓀴They will see that you are not there. Oh, do not waste the so-precious
8566𓀴time, but come!’
8567𓀴
8568𓀴“This time, at least, I did not scorn her advice. I staggered to my
8569𓀴feet and ran with her along the corridor and down a winding stair. The
8570𓀴latter led to another broad passage, and just as we reached it we heard
8571𓀴the sound of running feet and the shouting of two voices, one answering
8572𓀴the other from the floor on which  we were and from the one beneath. My
8573𓀴guide stopped and looked about her like one  who is at her wit’s end.
8574𓀴Then she threw open a door which led into a bedroom, through the window
8575𓀴of which the moon was shining brightly.
8576𓀴
8577𓀴“‘It is your only chance,’ said she. ‘It is high, but it may be that
8578𓀴you can jump it.’
8579𓀴
8580𓀴“As she spoke a light sprang into view at the further end of the
8581𓀴passage, and I saw the lean figure of Colonel Lysander Stark rushing
8582𓀴forward with a lantern in one hand and a weapon like a butcher’s
8583𓀴cleaver in the other. I rushed across the bedroom, flung open the
8584𓀴window, and looked out. How quiet and sweet and wholesome the garden
8585𓀴looked in the moonlight, and it could not be more than thirty feet
8586𓀴down. I clambered out upon the sill, but I hesitated to jump until I
8587𓀴should have heard what passed between my saviour and the ruffian who
8588𓀴pursued me. If she were ill-used, then at any risks I was determined to
8589𓀴go back to her assistance. The thought had hardly flashed through my
8590𓀴mind before he was at the door, pushing his way past her; but she threw
8591𓀴her arms round him and tried to hold him back.
8592𓀴
8593𓀴“‘Fritz! Fritz!’ she cried in English, ‘remember your promise after the
8594𓀴last time. You said it should not be again. He will be silent! Oh, he
8595𓀴will be silent!’
8596𓀴
8597𓀴“‘You are mad, Elise!’ he shouted, struggling to break away from her.
8598𓀴‘You will be the ruin of us. He has seen too much. Let me pass, I say!’
8599𓀴He dashed her to one side, and, rushing to the window, cut at me with
8600𓀴his heavy weapon. I had let myself go, and was hanging by the hands to
8601𓀴the sill, when his blow fell. I was conscious of a dull pain, my grip
8602𓀴loosened, and I fell into the garden below.
8603𓀴
8604𓀴“I was shaken but not hurt by the fall; so I picked myself up and
8605𓀴rushed off among the bushes as hard as I could run, for I understood
8606𓀴that I was far from being out of danger yet. Suddenly, however, as I
8607𓀴ran, a deadly dizziness and sickness came over me. I glanced down at my
8608𓀴hand, which was throbbing painfully, and then, for the first time, saw
8609𓀴that my thumb had been cut off and that the blood was pouring from my
8610𓀴wound. I endeavoured to tie my handkerchief round it, but there came a
8611𓀴sudden buzzing in my ears, and next moment I fell in a dead faint among
8612𓀴the rose-bushes.
8613𓀴
8614𓀴“How long I remained unconscious I cannot tell. It must have been a
8615𓀴very long time, for the moon had sunk, and a bright morning was
8616𓀴breaking when I came to myself. My clothes were all sodden with dew,
8617𓀴and my coat-sleeve was drenched with blood from my wounded thumb. The
8618𓀴smarting of it recalled in an instant all the particulars of my night’s
8619𓀴adventure, and I sprang to my feet with the feeling that I might hardly
8620𓀴yet be safe from my pursuers. But to my astonishment, when I came to
8621𓀴look round me, neither house nor garden were to be seen. I had been
8622𓀴lying in an angle of the hedge close by the high road, and just a little
8623𓀴lower down was a long building, which proved, upon my approaching it,
8624𓀴to be the very station at which I had arrived upon the previous night.
8625𓀴Were it not for the ugly wound upon my hand, all that had passed during
8626𓀴those dreadful hours might have been an evil dream.
8627𓀴
8628𓀴“Half dazed, I went into the station and asked about the morning train.
8629𓀴There would be one to Reading in less than an hour. The same porter was
8630𓀴on duty, I found, as had been there when I arrived. I inquired of him
8631𓀴whether he had ever heard of Colonel Lysander Stark. The name was
8632𓀴strange to him. Had he observed a carriage the night before waiting for
8633𓀴me? No, he had not. Was there a police-station anywhere near? There was
8634𓀴one about three miles off.
8635𓀴
8636𓀴“It was too far for me to go, weak and ill as I was. I determined to
8637𓀴wait until I got back to town before telling my story to the police. It
8638𓀴was a little past six when I arrived, so I went first to have my wound
8639𓀴dressed, and then the doctor was kind enough to bring me along here. I
8640𓀴put the case into your hands and shall do exactly what you advise.”
8641𓀴
8642𓀴We both sat in silence for some little time after listening to this
8643𓀴extraordinary narrative. Then Sherlock Holmes pulled down from the
8644𓀴shelf one of the ponderous commonplace books in which he placed his
8645𓀴cuttings.
8646𓀴
8647𓀴“Here is an advertisement which will interest you,” said he. “It
8648𓀴appeared in all the papers about a year ago. Listen to this: ‘Lost, on
8649𓀴the 9th inst., Mr. Jeremiah Hayling, aged twenty-six, a hydraulic
8650𓀴engineer. Left his lodgings at ten o’clock at night, and has not been
8651𓀴heard of since. Was dressed in,’ etc., etc. Ha! That represents the
8652𓀴last time that the colonel needed to have his machine overhauled, I
8653𓀴fancy.”
8654𓀴
8655𓀴“Good heavens!” cried my patient. “Then that explains what the girl
8656𓀴said.”
8657𓀴
8658𓀴“Undoubtedly. It is quite clear that the colonel was a cool and
8659𓀴desperate man, who was absolutely determined that nothing should stand
8660𓀴in the way of his little game, like those out-and-out pirates who will
8661𓀴leave no survivor from a captured ship. Well, every moment now is
8662𓀴precious, so if you feel equal to it we shall go down to Scotland Yard
8663𓀴at once as a preliminary to starting for Eyford.”
8664𓀴
8665𓀴Some three hours or so afterwards we were all in the train together,
8666𓀴bound from Reading to the little Berkshire village. There were Sherlock
8667𓀴Holmes, the hydraulic engineer, Inspector Bradstreet, of Scotland Yard,
8668𓀴a plain-clothes man, and myself. Bradstreet had spread an ordnance map
8669𓀴of the county out upon the seat and was busy with his compasses drawing
8670𓀴a circle with Eyford for its centre.
8671𓀴
8672𓀴“There you are,” said he. “That circle is drawn at a radius of ten
8673𓀴miles from the village. The place we want must be somewhere near that
8674𓀴line. You said ten miles, I think, sir.”
8675𓀴
8676𓀴“It was an hour’s good drive.”
8677𓀴
8678𓀴“And you think that they brought you back all that way when you were
8679𓀴unconscious?”
8680𓀴
8681𓀴“They must have done so. I have a confused memory, too, of having been
8682𓀴lifted and conveyed somewhere.”
8683𓀴
8684𓀴“What I cannot understand,” said I, “is why they should have spared you
8685𓀴when they found you lying fainting in the garden. Perhaps the villain
8686𓀴was softened by the woman’s entreaties.”
8687𓀴
8688𓀴“I hardly think that likely. I never saw a more inexorable face in my
8689𓀴life.”
8690𓀴
8691𓀴“Oh, we shall soon clear up all that,” said Bradstreet. “Well, I have
8692𓀴drawn my circle, and I only wish I knew at what point upon it the folk
8693𓀴that we are in search of are to be found.”
8694𓀴
8695𓀴“I think I could lay my finger on it,” said Holmes quietly.
8696𓀴
8697𓀴“Really, now!” cried the inspector, “you have formed your opinion!
8698𓀴Come, now, we shall see who agrees with you. I say it is south, for the
8699𓀴country is more deserted there.”
8700𓀴
8701𓀴“And I say east,” said my patient.
8702𓀴
8703𓀴“I am for west,” remarked the plain-clothes man. “There are several
8704𓀴quiet little villages up there.”
8705𓀴
8706𓀴“And I am for north,” said I, “because there are no hills there, and
