8707𓀴our friend says that he did not notice the carriage go up any.”
8708𓀴
8709𓀴“Come,” cried the inspector, laughing; “it’s a very pretty diversity of
8710𓀴opinion. We have boxed the compass among us. Who do you give your
8711𓀴casting vote to?”
8712𓀴
8713𓀴“You are all wrong.”
8714𓀴
8715𓀴“But we can’t all be.”
8716𓀴
8717𓀴“Oh, yes, you can. This is my point.” He placed his finger in the
8718𓀴centre of the circle. “This is where we shall find them.”
8719𓀴
8720𓀴“But the twelve-mile drive?” gasped Hatherley.
8721𓀴
8722𓀴“Six out and six back. Nothing simpler. You say yourself that the horse
8723𓀴was fresh and glossy when you got in. How could it be that if it had
8724𓀴gone twelve miles over heavy roads?”
8725𓀴
8726𓀴“Indeed, it is a likely ruse enough,” observed Bradstreet thoughtfully.
8727𓀴“Of course there can be no doubt as to the nature of this gang.”
8728𓀴
8729𓀴“None at all,” said Holmes. “They are coiners on a large scale, and
8730𓀴have used the machine to form the amalgam which has taken the place of
8731𓀴silver.”
8732𓀴
8733𓀴“We have known for some time that a clever gang was at work,” said the
8734𓀴inspector. “They have been turning out half-crowns by the thousand. We
8735𓀴even traced them as far as Reading, but could get no farther, for they
8736𓀴had covered their traces in a way that showed that they were very old
8737𓀴hands. But now, thanks to this lucky chance, I think that we have got
8738𓀴them right enough.”
8739𓀴
8740𓀴But the inspector was mistaken, for those criminals were not destined
8741𓀴to fall into the hands of justice. As we rolled into Eyford Station we
8742𓀴saw a gigantic column of smoke which streamed up from behind a small
8743𓀴clump of trees in the neighbourhood and hung like an immense ostrich
8744𓀴feather over the landscape.
8745𓀴
8746𓀴“A house on fire?” asked Bradstreet as the train steamed off again on
8747𓀴its way.
8748𓀴
8749𓀴“Yes, sir!” said the station-master.
8750𓀴
8751𓀴“When did it break out?”
8752𓀴
8753𓀴“I hear that it was during the night, sir, but it has got worse, and
8754𓀴the whole place is in a blaze.”
8755𓀴
8756𓀴“Whose house is it?”
8757𓀴
8758𓀴“Dr. Becher’s.”
8759𓀴
8760𓀴“Tell me,” broke in the engineer, “is Dr. Becher a German, very thin,
8761𓀴with a long, sharp nose?”
8762𓀴
8763𓀴The station-master laughed heartily. “No, sir, Dr. Becher is an
8764𓀴Englishman, and there isn’t a man in the parish who has a better-lined
8765𓀴waistcoat. But he has a gentleman staying with him, a patient, as I
8766𓀴understand, who is a foreigner, and he looks as if a little good
8767𓀴Berkshire beef would do him no harm.”
8768𓀴
8769𓀴The station-master had not finished his speech before we were all
8770𓀴hastening in the direction of the fire. The road topped a low hill, and
8771𓀴there was a great widespread whitewashed building in front of us,
8772𓀴spouting fire at every chink and window, while in the garden in front
8773𓀴three fire-engines were vainly striving to keep the flames under.
8774𓀴
8775𓀴“That’s it!” cried Hatherley, in intense excitement. “There is the
8776𓀴gravel-drive, and there are the rose-bushes where I lay. That second
8777𓀴window is the one that I jumped from.”
8778𓀴
8779𓀴“Well, at least,” said Holmes, “you have had your revenge upon them.
8780𓀴There can be no question that it was your oil-lamp which, when it was
8781𓀴crushed in the press, set fire to the wooden walls, though no doubt
8782𓀴they were too excited in the chase after you to observe it at the time.
8783𓀴Now keep your eyes open in this crowd for your friends of last night,
8784𓀴though I very much fear that they are a good hundred miles off by now.”
8785𓀴
8786𓀴And Holmes’ fears came to be realised, for from that day to this no
8787𓀴word has ever been heard either of the beautiful woman, the sinister
8788𓀴German, or the morose Englishman. Early that morning a peasant had met
8789𓀴a cart containing several people and some very bulky boxes driving
8790𓀴rapidly in the direction of Reading, but there all traces of the
8791𓀴fugitives disappeared, and even Holmes’ ingenuity failed ever to
8792𓀴discover the least clue as to their whereabouts.
8793𓀴
8794𓀴The firemen had been much perturbed at the strange arrangements which
8795𓀴they had found within, and still more so by discovering a newly severed
8796𓀴human thumb upon a window-sill of the second floor. About sunset,
8797𓀴however, their efforts were at last successful, and they subdued the
8798𓀴flames, but not before the roof had fallen in, and the whole place been
8799𓀴reduced to such absolute ruin that, save some twisted cylinders and
8800𓀴iron piping, not a trace remained of the machinery which had cost our
8801𓀴unfortunate acquaintance so dearly. Large masses of nickel and of tin
8802𓀴were discovered stored in an out-house, but no coins were to be found,
8803𓀴which may have explained the presence of those bulky boxes which have
8804𓀴been already referred to.
8805𓀴
8806𓀴How our hydraulic engineer had been conveyed from the garden to the
8807𓀴spot where he recovered his senses might have remained forever a
8808𓀴mystery were it not for the soft mould, which told us a very plain
8809𓀴tale. He had evidently been carried down by two persons, one of whom
8810𓀴had remarkably small feet and the other unusually large ones. On the
8811𓀴whole, it was most probable that the silent Englishman, being less bold
8812𓀴or less murderous than his companion, had assisted the woman to bear
8813𓀴the unconscious man out of the way of danger.
8814𓀴
8815𓀴“Well,” said our engineer ruefully as we took our seats to return once
8816𓀴more to London, “it has been a pretty business for me! I have lost my
8817𓀴thumb and I have lost a fifty-guinea fee, and what have I gained?”
8818𓀴
8819𓀴“Experience,” said Holmes, laughing. “Indirectly it may be of value,
8820𓀴you know; you have only to put it into words to gain the reputation of
8821𓀴being excellent company for the remainder of your existence.”
8822𓀴
8823𓀴
8824𓀴
8825𓀴
8826𓀴X. THE ADVENTURE OF THE NOBLE BACHELOR
8827𓀴
8828𓀴
8829𓀴The Lord St. Simon marriage, and its curious termination, have long
8830𓀴ceased to be a subject of interest in those exalted circles in which
8831𓀴the unfortunate bridegroom moves. Fresh scandals have eclipsed it, and
8832𓀴their more piquant details have drawn the gossips away from this
8833𓀴four-year-old drama. As I have reason to believe, however, that the
8834𓀴full facts have never been revealed to the general public, and as my
8835𓀴friend Sherlock Holmes had a considerable share in clearing the matter
8836𓀴up, I feel that no memoir of him would be complete without some little
8837𓀴sketch of this remarkable episode.
8838𓀴
8839𓀴It was a few weeks before my own marriage, during the days when I was
8840𓀴still sharing rooms with Holmes in Baker Street, that he came home from
8841𓀴an afternoon stroll to find a letter on the table waiting for him. I
8842𓀴had remained indoors all day, for the weather had taken a sudden turn
8843𓀴to rain, with high autumnal winds, and the jezail bullet which I had
8844𓀴brought back in one of my limbs as a relic of my Afghan campaign
8845𓀴throbbed with dull persistence. With my body in one easy-chair and my
8846𓀴legs upon another, I had surrounded myself with a cloud of newspapers
8847𓀴until at last, saturated with the news of the day, I tossed them all
8848𓀴aside and lay listless, watching the huge crest and monogram upon the
8849𓀴envelope upon the table and wondering lazily who my friend’s noble
8850𓀴correspondent could be.
8851𓀴
8852𓀴“Here is a very fashionable epistle,” I remarked as he entered. “Your
8853𓀴morning letters, if I remember right, were from a fish-monger and a
8854𓀴tide-waiter.”
8855𓀴
8856𓀴“Yes, my correspondence has certainly the charm of variety,” he
8857𓀴answered, smiling, “and the humbler are usually the more interesting.
8858𓀴This looks like one of those unwelcome social summonses which call upon
8859𓀴a man either to be bored or to lie.”
8860𓀴
8861𓀴He broke the seal and glanced over the contents.
8862𓀴
8863𓀴“Oh, come, it may prove to be something of interest, after all.”
8864𓀴
8865𓀴“Not social, then?”
8866𓀴
8867𓀴“No, distinctly professional.”
8868𓀴
8869𓀴“And from a noble client?”
8870𓀴
8871𓀴“One of the highest in England.”
8872𓀴
8873𓀴“My dear fellow, I congratulate you.”
8874𓀴
8875𓀴“I assure you, Watson, without affectation, that the status of my
8876𓀴client is a matter of less moment to me than the interest of his case.
8877𓀴It is just possible, however, that that also may not be wanting in this
8878𓀴new investigation. You have been reading the papers diligently of late,
8879𓀴have you not?”
8880𓀴
8881𓀴“It looks like it,” said I ruefully, pointing to a huge bundle in the
8882𓀴corner. “I have had nothing else to do.”
8883𓀴
8884𓀴“It is fortunate, for you will perhaps be able to post me up. I read
8885𓀴nothing except the criminal news and the agony column. The latter is
8886𓀴always instructive. But if you have followed recent events so closely
8887𓀴you must have read about Lord St. Simon and his wedding?”
8888𓀴
8889𓀴“Oh, yes, with the deepest interest.”
8890𓀴
8891𓀴“That is well. The letter which I hold in my hand is from Lord St.
8892𓀴Simon. I will read it to you, and in return you must turn over these
8893𓀴papers and let me have whatever bears upon the matter. This is what he
8894𓀴says:
8895𓀴
8896𓀴    “‘MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES,—Lord Backwater tells me that I may
8897𓀴    place implicit reliance upon your judgment and discretion. I have
8898𓀴    determined, therefore, to call upon you and to consult you in
8899𓀴    reference to the very painful event which has occurred in
8900𓀴    connection with my wedding. Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, is
8901𓀴    acting already in the matter, but he assures me that he sees no
8902𓀴    objection to your co-operation, and that he even thinks that it
8903𓀴    might be of some assistance. I will call at four o’clock in the
8904𓀴    afternoon, and, should you have any other engagement at that time,
8905𓀴    I hope that you will postpone it, as this matter is of paramount
8906𓀴    importance. Yours faithfully,
8907𓀴
8908𓀴
8909𓀴    “‘ROBERT ST. SIMON.’
8910𓀴
8911𓀴
8912𓀴“It is dated from Grosvenor Mansions, written with a quill pen, and the
8913𓀴noble lord has had the misfortune to get a smear of ink upon the outer
8914𓀴side of his right little finger,” remarked Holmes as he folded up the
8915𓀴epistle.
8916𓀴
8917𓀴“He says four o’clock. It is three now. He will be here in an hour.”
8918𓀴
8919𓀴“Then I have just time, with your assistance, to get clear upon the
8920𓀴subject. Turn over those papers and arrange the extracts in their order
8921𓀴of time, while I take a glance as to who our client is.” He picked a
8922𓀴red-covered volume from a line of books of reference beside the
8923𓀴mantelpiece. “Here he is,” said he, sitting down and flattening it out
8924𓀴upon his knee. “‘Lord Robert Walsingham de Vere St. Simon, second son
8925𓀴of the Duke of Balmoral.’ Hum! ‘Arms: Azure, three caltrops in chief
8926𓀴over a fess sable. Born in 1846.’ He’s forty-one years of age, which is
8927𓀴mature for marriage. Was Under-Secretary for the colonies in a late
8928𓀴administration. The Duke, his father, was at one time Secretary for
8929𓀴Foreign Affairs. They inherit Plantagenet blood by direct descent, and
8930𓀴Tudor on the distaff side. Ha! Well, there is nothing very instructive
8931𓀴in all this. I think that I must turn to you Watson, for something more
8932𓀴solid.”
8933𓀴
8934𓀴“I have very little difficulty in finding what I want,” said I, “for
8935𓀴the facts are quite recent, and the matter struck me as remarkable. I
8936𓀴feared to refer them to you, however, as I knew that you had an inquiry
8937𓀴on hand and that you disliked the intrusion of other matters.”
8938𓀴
8939𓀴“Oh, you mean the little problem of the Grosvenor Square furniture van.
8940𓀴That is quite cleared up now—though, indeed, it was obvious from the
8941𓀴first. Pray give me the results of your newspaper selections.”
8942𓀴
8943𓀴“Here is the first notice which I can find. It is in the personal
8944𓀴column of the _Morning Post_, and dates, as you see, some weeks back:
8945𓀴‘A marriage has been arranged,’ it says, ‘and will, if rumour is
8946𓀴correct, very shortly take place, between Lord Robert St. Simon, second
8947𓀴son of the Duke of Balmoral, and Miss Hatty Doran, the only daughter of
8948𓀴Aloysius Doran. Esq., of San Francisco, Cal., U.S.A.’ That is all.”
8949𓀴
8950𓀴“Terse and to the point,” remarked Holmes, stretching his long, thin
8951𓀴legs towards the fire.
8952𓀴
8953𓀴“There was a paragraph amplifying this in one of the society papers of
8954𓀴the same week. Ah, here it is: ‘There will soon be a call for
8955𓀴protection in the marriage  market, for the present free-trade
8956𓀴principle appears to tell heavily against our home product. One by one
8957𓀴the management of the noble houses of Great Britain is passing into the
8958𓀴hands of our fair cousins from across the Atlantic. An important
8959𓀴addition has been made during the last week to the list of the prizes
8960𓀴which have been borne away by these charming invaders. Lord St. Simon,
8961𓀴who has shown himself for over twenty years proof against the little
8962𓀴god’s arrows, has now definitely announced his approaching marriage
8963𓀴with Miss Hatty Doran, the fascinating daughter of a California
8964𓀴millionaire. Miss Doran, whose graceful figure and striking face
8965𓀴attracted much attention at the Westbury House festivities, is an only
8966𓀴child, and it is currently reported that her dowry will run to
8967𓀴considerably over the six figures, with expectancies for the future. As
8968𓀴it is an open secret that the Duke of Balmoral has been compelled to
8969𓀴sell his pictures within the last few years, and as Lord St. Simon has
8970𓀴no property of his own save the small estate of Birchmoor, it is
8971𓀴obvious that the Californian heiress is not the only gainer by an
8972𓀴alliance which will enable her to make the easy and common transition
8973𓀴from a Republican lady to a British peeress.’”
8974𓀴
8975𓀴“Anything else?” asked Holmes, yawning.
8976𓀴
8977𓀴“Oh, yes; plenty. Then there is another note in the _Morning Post_ to
8978𓀴say that the marriage would be an absolutely quiet one, that it would
8979𓀴be at St. George’s, Hanover Square, that only half a dozen intimate
8980𓀴friends would be invited, and that the party would return to the
8981𓀴furnished house at Lancaster Gate which has been taken by Mr. Aloysius
8982𓀴Doran. Two days later—that is, on Wednesday last—there is a curt
8983𓀴announcement that the wedding had taken place, and that the honeymoon
8984𓀴would be passed at Lord Backwater’s place, near Petersfield. Those are
8985𓀴all the notices which appeared before the disappearance of the bride.”
8986𓀴
8987𓀴“Before the what?” asked Holmes with a start.
8988𓀴
8989𓀴“The vanishing of the lady.”
8990𓀴
8991𓀴“When did she vanish, then?”
8992𓀴
8993𓀴“At the wedding breakfast.”
8994𓀴
8995𓀴“Indeed. This is more interesting than it promised to be; quite
8996𓀴dramatic, in fact.”
8997𓀴
8998𓀴“Yes; it struck me as being a little out of the common.”
8999𓀴
9000𓀴“They often vanish before the ceremony, and occasionally during the
9001𓀴honeymoon; but I cannot call to mind anything quite so prompt as this.
9002𓀴Pray let me have the details.”
9003𓀴
9004𓀴“I warn you that they are very incomplete.”
9005𓀴
9006𓀴“Perhaps we may make them less so.”
9007𓀴
9008𓀴“Such as they are, they are set forth in a single article of a morning
9009𓀴paper of yesterday, which I will read to you. It is headed, ‘Singular
9010𓀴Occurrence at a Fashionable Wedding’:
9011𓀴
9012𓀴“‘The family of Lord Robert St. Simon has been thrown into the greatest
9013𓀴consternation by the strange and painful episodes which have taken
9014𓀴place in connection with his wedding. The ceremony, as shortly
9015𓀴announced in the papers of yesterday, occurred on the previous morning;
9016𓀴but it is only now that it has been possible to confirm the strange
9017𓀴rumours which have been so persistently floating about. In spite of the
9018𓀴attempts of the friends to hush the matter up, so much public attention
9019𓀴has now been drawn to it that no good purpose can be served by
9020𓀴affecting to disregard what is a common subject for conversation.
9021𓀴
9022𓀴“‘The ceremony, which was performed at St. George’s, Hanover Square,
9023𓀴was a very quiet one, no one being present save the father of the
9024𓀴bride, Mr. Aloysius Doran, the Duchess of Balmoral, Lord Backwater,
9025𓀴Lord Eustace and Lady Clara St. Simon (the younger brother and sister
9026𓀴of the bridegroom), and Lady Alicia Whittington. The whole party
9027𓀴proceeded afterwards to the house of Mr. Aloysius Doran, at Lancaster
9028𓀴Gate, where breakfast had been prepared. It appears that some little
9029𓀴trouble was caused by a woman, whose name has not been ascertained, who
9030𓀴endeavoured to force her way into the house after the bridal party,
9031𓀴alleging that she had some claim upon Lord St. Simon. It was only after
9032𓀴a painful and prolonged scene that she was ejected by the butler and
9033𓀴the footman. The bride, who had fortunately entered the house before
9034𓀴this unpleasant interruption, had sat down to breakfast with the rest,
9035𓀴when she complained of a sudden indisposition and retired to her room.
9036𓀴Her prolonged absence having caused some comment, her father followed
9037𓀴her, but learned from her maid that she had only come up to her chamber
9038𓀴for an instant, caught up an ulster and bonnet, and hurried down to the
9039𓀴passage. One of the footmen declared that he had seen a lady leave the
9040𓀴house thus apparelled, but had refused to credit that it was his
9041𓀴mistress, believing her to be with the company. On ascertaining that
9042𓀴his daughter had disappeared, Mr. Aloysius Doran, in conjunction with
9043𓀴the bridegroom, instantly put themselves in communication with the
9044𓀴police, and very energetic inquiries are being made, which will
9045𓀴probably result in a speedy clearing up of this very singular business.
9046𓀴Up to a late hour last night, however, nothing had transpired as to the
9047𓀴whereabouts of the missing lady. There are rumours of foul play in the
9048𓀴matter, and it is said that the police have caused the arrest of the
9049𓀴woman who had caused the original disturbance, in the belief that, from
9050𓀴jealousy or some other motive, she may have been concerned in the
9051𓀴strange disappearance of the bride.’”
9052𓀴
9053𓀴“And is that all?”
9054𓀴
9055𓀴“Only one little item in another of the morning papers, but it is a
9056𓀴suggestive one.”
9057𓀴
9058𓀴“And it is—”
9059𓀴
9060𓀴“That Miss Flora Millar, the lady who had caused the disturbance, has
9061𓀴actually been arrested. It appears that she was formerly a _danseuse_
9062𓀴at the Allegro, and that she has known the bridegroom for some years.
9063𓀴There are no further particulars, and the whole case is in your hands
9064𓀴now—so far as it has been set forth in the public press.”
9065𓀴
9066𓀴“And an exceedingly interesting case it appears to be. I would not have
9067𓀴missed it for worlds. But there is a ring at the bell, Watson, and as
9068𓀴the clock makes it a few minutes after four, I have no doubt that this
9069𓀴will prove to be our noble client. Do not dream of going, Watson, for I
9070𓀴very much prefer having a witness, if only as a check to my own
9071𓀴memory.”
9072𓀴
9073𓀴“Lord Robert St. Simon,” announced our page-boy, throwing open the
9074𓀴door. A gentleman entered, with a pleasant, cultured face, high-nosed
9075𓀴and pale, with something perhaps of petulance about the mouth, and with
9076𓀴the steady, well-opened eye of a man whose pleasant lot it had ever
9077𓀴been to command and to be obeyed. His manner was brisk, and yet his
9078𓀴general appearance gave an undue impression of age, for he had a slight
9079𓀴forward stoop and a little bend of the knees as he walked. His hair,
9080𓀴too, as he swept off his very curly-brimmed hat, was grizzled round the
9081𓀴edges and thin upon the top. As to his dress, it was careful to the
9082𓀴verge of foppishness, with high collar, black frock-coat, white
9083𓀴waistcoat, yellow gloves, patent-leather shoes, and light-coloured
9084𓀴gaiters. He advanced slowly into the room, turning his head from left
9085𓀴to right, and swinging in his right hand the cord which held his golden
9086𓀴eyeglasses.
9087𓀴
9088𓀴“Good-day, Lord St. Simon,” said Holmes, rising and bowing. “Pray take
9089𓀴the basket-chair. This is my friend and colleague, Dr. Watson. Draw up
9090𓀴a little to the fire, and we will talk this matter over.”
9091𓀴
9092𓀴“A most painful matter to me, as you can most readily imagine, Mr.
9093𓀴Holmes. I have been cut to the quick. I understand that you have
9094𓀴already managed several delicate cases of this sort, sir, though I
9095𓀴presume that they were hardly from the same class of society.”
9096𓀴
9097𓀴“No, I am descending.”
9098𓀴
9099𓀴“I beg pardon.”
9100𓀴
9101𓀴“My last client of the sort was a king.”
9102𓀴
9103𓀴“Oh, really! I had no idea. And which king?”
9104𓀴
9105𓀴“The King of Scandinavia.”
9106𓀴
9107𓀴“What! Had he lost his wife?”
9108𓀴
9109𓀴“You can understand,” said Holmes suavely, “that I extend to the
9110𓀴affairs of my other clients the same secrecy which I promise to you in
9111𓀴yours.”
9112𓀴
9113𓀴“Of course! Very right! very right! I’m sure I beg pardon. As to my own
9114𓀴case, I am ready to give you any information which may assist you in
9115𓀴forming an opinion.”
9116𓀴
9117𓀴“Thank you. I have already learned all that is in the public prints,
9118𓀴nothing more. I presume that I may take it as correct—this article, for
9119𓀴example, as to the disappearance of the bride.”
9120𓀴
9121𓀴Lord St. Simon glanced over it. “Yes, it is correct, as far as it
9122𓀴goes.”
9123𓀴
9124𓀴“But it needs a great deal of supplementing before anyone could offer
9125𓀴an opinion. I think that I may arrive at my facts most directly by
9126𓀴questioning you.”
9127𓀴
9128𓀴“Pray do so.”
9129𓀴
9130𓀴“When did you first meet Miss Hatty Doran?”
9131𓀴
9132𓀴“In San Francisco, a year ago.”
9133𓀴
9134𓀴“You were travelling in the States?”
9135𓀴
9136𓀴“Yes.”
9137𓀴
9138𓀴“Did you become engaged then?”
9139𓀴
9140𓀴“No.”
9141𓀴
9142𓀴“But you were on a friendly footing?”
9143𓀴
9144𓀴“I was amused by her society, and she could see that I was amused.”
9145𓀴
9146𓀴“Her father is very rich?”
9147𓀴
9148𓀴“He is said to be the richest man on the Pacific slope.”
9149𓀴
9150𓀴“And how did he make his money?”
9151𓀴
9152𓀴“In mining. He had nothing a few years ago. Then he struck gold,
9153𓀴invested it, and came up by leaps and bounds.”
9154𓀴
9155𓀴“Now, what is your own impression as to the young lady’s—your wife’s
9156𓀴character?”
9157𓀴
9158𓀴The nobleman swung his glasses a little faster and stared down into the
9159𓀴fire. “You see, Mr. Holmes,” said he, “my wife was twenty before her
9160𓀴father became a rich man. During that time she ran free in a mining
9161𓀴camp and wandered through woods or mountains, so that her education has
9162𓀴come from Nature rather than from the schoolmaster. She is what we call
9163𓀴in England a tomboy, with a strong nature, wild and free, unfettered by
9164𓀴any sort of traditions. She is impetuous—volcanic, I was about to say.
9165𓀴She is swift in making up her mind and fearless in carrying out her
9166𓀴resolutions. On the other hand, I would not have given her the name
9167𓀴which I have the honour to bear”—he gave a little stately cough—“had I
9168𓀴not thought her to be at bottom a noble woman. I believe that she is
9169𓀴capable of heroic self-sacrifice and that anything dishonourable would
9170𓀴be repugnant to her.”
9171𓀴
9172𓀴“Have you her photograph?”
9173𓀴
9174𓀴“I brought this with me.” He opened a locket and showed us the full
9175𓀴face of a very lovely woman. It was not a photograph but an ivory
9176𓀴miniature, and the artist had brought out the full effect of the
9177𓀴lustrous black hair, the large dark eyes, and the exquisite mouth.
9178𓀴Holmes gazed long and earnestly at it. Then he closed the locket and
9179𓀴handed it back to Lord St. Simon.
9180𓀴
9181𓀴“The young lady came to London, then, and you renewed your
9182𓀴acquaintance?”
9183𓀴
9184𓀴“Yes, her father brought her over for this last London season. I met
9185𓀴her several times, became engaged to her, and have now married her.”
9186𓀴
9187𓀴“She brought, I understand, a considerable dowry?”
9188𓀴
9189𓀴“A fair dowry. Not more than is usual in my family.”
9190𓀴
9191𓀴“And this, of course, remains to you, since the marriage is a _fait
9192𓀴accompli_?”
9193𓀴
9194𓀴“I really have made no inquiries on the subject.”
9195𓀴
9196𓀴“Very naturally not. Did you see Miss Doran on the day before the
9197𓀴wedding?”
9198𓀴
9199𓀴“Yes.”
9200𓀴
9201𓀴“Was she in good spirits?”
9202𓀴
9203𓀴“Never better. She kept talking of what we should do in our future
9204𓀴lives.”
9205𓀴
9206𓀴“Indeed! That is very interesting. And on the morning of the wedding?”
9207𓀴
9208𓀴“She was as bright as possible—at least until after the ceremony.”
9209𓀴
9210𓀴“And did you observe any change in her then?”
9211𓀴
9212𓀴“Well, to tell the truth, I saw then the first signs that I had ever
9213𓀴seen that her temper was just a little sharp. The incident however, was
9214𓀴too trivial to relate and can have no possible bearing upon the case.”
9215𓀴
9216𓀴“Pray let us have it, for all that.”
9217𓀴
9218𓀴“Oh, it is childish. She dropped her bouquet as we went towards the
9219𓀴vestry. She was passing the front pew at the time, and it fell over
9220𓀴into the pew. There was a moment’s delay, but the gentleman in the pew
9221𓀴handed it up to her again, and it did not appear to be the worse for
9222𓀴the fall. Yet when I spoke to her of the matter, she answered me
9223𓀴abruptly; and in the carriage, on our way home, she seemed absurdly
9224𓀴agitated over this trifling cause.”
9225𓀴
9226𓀴“Indeed! You say that there was a gentleman in the pew. Some of the
9227𓀴general public were present, then?”
9228𓀴
9229𓀴“Oh, yes. It is impossible to exclude them when the church is open.”
9230𓀴
9231𓀴“This gentleman was not one of your wife’s friends?”
9232𓀴
9233𓀴“No, no; I call him a gentleman by courtesy, but he was quite a
9234𓀴common-looking person. I hardly noticed his appearance. But really I
9235𓀴think that we are wandering rather far from the point.”
9236𓀴
9237𓀴“Lady St. Simon, then, returned from the wedding in a less cheerful
9238𓀴frame of mind than she had gone to it. What did she do on re-entering
9239𓀴her father’s house?”
9240𓀴
9241𓀴“I saw her in conversation with her maid.”
9242𓀴
9243𓀴“And who is her maid?”
9244𓀴
9245𓀴“Alice is her name. She is an American and came from California with
9246𓀴her.”
9247𓀴
9248𓀴“A confidential servant?”
9249𓀴
9250𓀴“A little too much so. It seemed to me that her mistress allowed her to
9251𓀴take great liberties. Still, of course, in America they look upon these
9252𓀴things in a different way.”
9253𓀴
9254𓀴“How long did she speak to this Alice?”
9255𓀴
9256𓀴“Oh, a few minutes. I had something else to think of.”
9257𓀴
9258𓀴“You did not overhear what they said?”
9259𓀴
9260𓀴“Lady St. Simon said something about ‘jumping a claim.’ She was
9261𓀴accustomed to use slang of the kind. I have no idea what she meant.”
9262𓀴
9263𓀴“American slang is very expressive sometimes. And what did your wife do
9264𓀴when she finished speaking to her maid?”
9265𓀴
9266𓀴“She walked into the breakfast-room.”
9267𓀴
9268𓀴“On your arm?”
9269𓀴
9270𓀴“No, alone. She was very independent in little matters like that. Then,
9271𓀴after we had sat down for ten minutes or so, she rose hurriedly,
9272𓀴muttered some words of apology, and left the room. She never came
9273𓀴back.”
9274𓀴
9275𓀴“But this maid, Alice, as I understand, deposes that she went to her
9276𓀴room, covered her bride’s dress with a long ulster, put on a bonnet,
9277𓀴and went out.”
9278𓀴
9279𓀴“Quite so. And she was afterwards seen walking into Hyde Park in
9280𓀴company with Flora Millar, a woman who is now in custody, and who had
9281𓀴already made a disturbance at Mr. Doran’s house that morning.”
9282𓀴
9283𓀴“Ah, yes. I should like a few particulars as to this young lady, and
9284𓀴your relations to her.”
9285𓀴
9286𓀴Lord St. Simon shrugged his shoulders and raised his eyebrows. “We have
9287𓀴been on a friendly footing for some years—I may say on a _very_
9288𓀴friendly footing. She used to be at the Allegro. I have not treated her
9289𓀴ungenerously, and she had no just cause of complaint against me, but
9290𓀴you know what women are, Mr. Holmes. Flora was a dear little thing, but
9291𓀴exceedingly hot-headed and devotedly attached to me. She wrote me
9292𓀴dreadful letters when she heard that I was about to be married, and, to
9293𓀴tell the truth, the reason why I had the marriage celebrated so quietly
9294𓀴was that I feared lest there might be a scandal in the church. She came
9295𓀴to Mr. Doran’s door just after we returned, and she endeavoured to push
9296𓀴her way in, uttering very abusive expressions towards my wife, and even
9297𓀴threatening her, but I had foreseen the possibility of something of the
9298𓀴sort, and I had two police fellows there in private clothes, who soon
9299𓀴pushed her out again. She was quiet when she saw that there was no good
9300𓀴in making a row.”
9301𓀴
9302𓀴“Did your wife hear all this?”
9303𓀴
9304𓀴“No, thank goodness, she did not.”
9305𓀴
9306𓀴“And she was seen walking with this very woman afterwards?”
9307𓀴
9308𓀴“Yes. That is what Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, looks upon as so
9309𓀴serious. It is thought that Flora decoyed my wife out and laid some
9310𓀴terrible trap for her.”
9311𓀴
9312𓀴“Well, it is a possible supposition.”
9313𓀴
9314𓀴“You think so, too?”
9315𓀴
9316𓀴“I did not say a probable one. But you do not yourself look upon this
9317𓀴as likely?”
9318𓀴
9319𓀴“I do not think Flora would hurt a fly.”
9320𓀴
9321𓀴“Still, jealousy is a strange transformer of characters. Pray what is
9322𓀴your own theory as to what took place?”
9323𓀴
9324𓀴“Well, really, I came to seek a theory, not to propound one. I have
9325𓀴given you all the facts. Since you ask me, however, I may say that it
9326𓀴has occurred to me as possible that the excitement of this affair, the
9327𓀴consciousness that she had made so immense a social stride, had the
9328𓀴effect of causing some little nervous disturbance in my wife.”
9329𓀴
9330𓀴“In short, that she had become suddenly deranged?”
9331𓀴
9332𓀴“Well, really, when I consider that she has turned her back—I will not
9333𓀴say upon me, but upon so much that many have aspired to without
9334𓀴success—I can hardly explain it in any other fashion.”
9335𓀴
9336𓀴“Well, certainly that is also a conceivable hypothesis,” said Holmes,
9337𓀴smiling. “And now, Lord St. Simon, I think that I have nearly all my
9338𓀴data. May I ask whether you were seated at the breakfast-table so that
9339𓀴you could see out of the window?”
9340𓀴
9341𓀴“We could see the other side of the road and the Park.”
9342𓀴
9343𓀴“Quite so. Then I do not think that I need to detain you longer. I
9344𓀴shall communicate with you.”
9345𓀴
9346𓀴“Should you be fortunate enough to solve this problem,” said our
9347𓀴client, rising.
9348𓀴
9349𓀴“I have solved it.”
9350𓀴
9351𓀴“Eh? What was that?”
9352𓀴
9353𓀴“I say that I have solved it.”
9354𓀴
9355𓀴“Where, then, is my wife?”
9356𓀴
9357𓀴“That is a detail which I shall speedily supply.”
9358𓀴
9359𓀴Lord St. Simon shook his head. “I am afraid that it will take wiser
9360𓀴heads than yours or mine,” he remarked, and bowing in a stately,
9361𓀴old-fashioned manner he departed.
9362𓀴
9363𓀴“It is very good of Lord St. Simon to honour my head by putting it on a
9364𓀴level with his own,” said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. “I think that I
9365𓀴shall have a whisky and soda and a cigar after all this
9366𓀴cross-questioning. I had formed my conclusions as to the case before
9367𓀴our client came into the room.”
9368𓀴
9369𓀴“My dear Holmes!”
9370𓀴
9371𓀴“I have notes of several similar cases, though none, as I remarked
9372𓀴before, which were quite as prompt. My whole examination served to turn
9373𓀴my conjecture into a certainty. Circumstantial evidence is occasionally
9374𓀴very convincing, as when you find a trout in the milk, to quote
9375𓀴Thoreau’s example.”
9376𓀴
9377𓀴“But I have heard all that you have heard.”
9378𓀴
9379𓀴“Without, however, the knowledge of pre-existing cases which serves me
9380𓀴so well. There was a parallel instance in Aberdeen some years back, and
9381𓀴something on very much the same lines at Munich the year after the
9382𓀴Franco-Prussian War. It is one of these cases—but, hullo, here is
9383𓀴Lestrade! Good-afternoon, Lestrade! You will find an extra tumbler upon
9384𓀴the sideboard, and there are cigars in the box.”
9385𓀴
9386𓀴The official detective was attired in a pea-jacket and cravat, which
9387𓀴gave him a decidedly nautical appearance, and he carried a black canvas
9388𓀴bag in his hand. With a short greeting he seated himself and lit the
9389𓀴cigar which had been offered to him.
9390𓀴
9391𓀴“What’s up, then?” asked Holmes with a twinkle in his eye. “You look
9392𓀴dissatisfied.”
9393𓀴
9394𓀴“And I feel dissatisfied. It is this infernal St. Simon marriage case.
9395𓀴I can make neither head nor tail of the business.”
9396𓀴
9397𓀴“Really! You surprise me.”
9398𓀴
9399𓀴“Who ever heard of such a mixed affair? Every clue seems to slip
9400𓀴through my fingers. I have been at work upon it all day.”
9401𓀴
9402𓀴“And very wet it seems to have made you,” said Holmes laying his hand
9403𓀴upon the arm of the pea-jacket.
9404𓀴
9405𓀴“Yes, I have been dragging the Serpentine.”
9406𓀴
9407𓀴“In Heaven’s name, what for?”
9408𓀴
9409𓀴“In search of the body of Lady St. Simon.”
9410𓀴
9411𓀴Sherlock Holmes leaned back in his chair and laughed heartily.
9412𓀴
9413𓀴“Have you dragged the basin of Trafalgar Square fountain?” he asked.
9414𓀴
9415𓀴“Why? What do you mean?”
9416𓀴
9417𓀴“Because you have just as good a chance of finding this lady in the one
9418𓀴as in the other.”
9419𓀴
9420𓀴Lestrade shot an angry glance at my companion. “I suppose you know all
9421𓀴about it,” he snarled.
9422𓀴
9423𓀴“Well, I have only just heard the facts, but my mind is made up.”
9424𓀴
9425𓀴“Oh, indeed! Then you think that the Serpentine plays no part in the
9426𓀴matter?”
9427𓀴
9428𓀴“I think it very unlikely.”
9429𓀴
9430𓀴“Then perhaps you will kindly explain how it is that we found this in
9431𓀴it?” He opened his bag as he spoke, and tumbled onto the floor a
9432𓀴wedding-dress of watered silk, a pair of white satin shoes and a
9433𓀴bride’s wreath and veil, all discoloured and soaked in water. “There,”
9434𓀴said he, putting a new wedding-ring upon the top of the pile. “There is
9435𓀴a little nut for you to crack, Master Holmes.”
9436𓀴
9437𓀴“Oh, indeed!” said my friend, blowing blue rings into the air. “You
9438𓀴dragged them from the Serpentine?”
9439𓀴
9440𓀴“No. They were found floating near the margin by a park-keeper. They
9441𓀴have been identified as her clothes, and it seemed to me that if the
9442𓀴clothes were there the body would not be far off.”
9443𓀴
9444𓀴“By the same brilliant reasoning, every man’s body is to be found in
9445𓀴the neighbourhood of his wardrobe. And pray what did you hope to arrive
9446𓀴at through this?”
9447𓀴
9448𓀴“At some evidence implicating Flora Millar in the disappearance.”
9449𓀴
9450𓀴“I am afraid that you will find it difficult.”
9451𓀴
9452𓀴“Are you, indeed, now?” cried Lestrade with some bitterness. “I am
9453𓀴afraid, Holmes, that you are not very practical with your deductions
9454𓀴and your inferences. You have made two blunders in as many minutes.
9455𓀴This dress does implicate Miss Flora Millar.”
9456𓀴
9457𓀴“And how?”
9458𓀴
9459𓀴“In the dress is a pocket. In the pocket is a card-case. In the
9460𓀴card-case is a note. And here is the very note.” He slapped it down
9461𓀴upon the table in front of him. “Listen to this: ‘You will see me when
9462𓀴all is ready. Come at once. F. H. M.’ Now my theory all along has been
9463𓀴that Lady St. Simon was decoyed away by Flora Millar, and that she,
9464𓀴with confederates, no doubt, was responsible for her disappearance.
9465𓀴Here, signed with her initials, is the very note which was no doubt
9466𓀴quietly slipped into her hand at the door and which lured her within
9467𓀴their reach.”
9468𓀴
9469𓀴“Very good, Lestrade,” said Holmes, laughing. “You really are very fine
9470𓀴indeed. Let me see it.” He took up the paper in a listless way, but his
9471𓀴attention instantly became riveted, and he gave a little cry of
9472𓀴satisfaction. “This is indeed important,” said he.
9473𓀴
9474𓀴“Ha! you find it so?”
9475𓀴
9476𓀴“Extremely so. I congratulate you warmly.”
9477𓀴
9478𓀴Lestrade rose in his triumph and bent his head to look. “Why,” he
9479𓀴shrieked, “you’re looking at the wrong side!”
9480𓀴
9481𓀴“On the contrary, this is the right side.”
9482𓀴
9483𓀴“The right side? You’re mad! Here is the note written in pencil over
9484𓀴here.”
9485𓀴
9486𓀴“And over here is what appears to be the fragment of a hotel bill,
9487𓀴which interests me deeply.”
9488𓀴
9489𓀴“There’s nothing in it. I looked at it before,” said Lestrade. “‘Oct.
9490𓀴4th, rooms 8_s_., breakfast 2_s_. 6_d_., cocktail 1_s_., lunch 2_s_.
9491𓀴6_d_., glass sherry, 8_d_.’ I see nothing in that.”
9492𓀴
9493𓀴“Very likely not. It is most important, all the same. As to the note,
9494𓀴it is important also, or at least the initials are, so I congratulate
9495𓀴you again.”
9496𓀴
9497𓀴“I’ve wasted time enough,” said Lestrade, rising. “I believe in hard
9498𓀴work and not in sitting by the fire spinning fine theories. Good-day,
9499𓀴Mr. Holmes, and we shall see which gets to the bottom of the matter
9500𓀴first.” He gathered up the garments, thrust them into the bag, and made
9501𓀴for the door.
9502𓀴
9503𓀴“Just one hint to you, Lestrade,” drawled Holmes before his rival
9504𓀴vanished; “I will tell you the true solution of the matter. Lady St.
9505𓀴Simon is a myth. There is not, and there never has been, any such
9506𓀴person.”
9507𓀴
9508𓀴Lestrade looked sadly at my companion. Then he turned to me, tapped his
9509𓀴forehead three times, shook his head solemnly, and hurried away.
9510𓀴
9511𓀴He had hardly shut the door behind him when Holmes rose to put on his
9512𓀴overcoat. “There is something in what the fellow says about outdoor
9513𓀴work,” he remarked, “so I think, Watson, that I must leave you to your
9514𓀴papers for a little.”
9515𓀴
9516𓀴It was after five o’clock when Sherlock Holmes left me, but I had no
9517𓀴time to be lonely, for within an hour there arrived a confectioner’s
9518𓀴man with a very large flat box. This he unpacked with the help of a
9519𓀴youth whom he had brought with him, and presently, to my very great
9520𓀴astonishment, a quite epicurean little cold supper began to be laid out
9521𓀴upon our humble lodging-house mahogany. There were a couple of brace of
9522𓀴cold woodcock, a pheasant, a _pâté de foie gras_ pie with a group of
9523𓀴ancient and cobwebby bottles. Having laid out all these luxuries, my
9524𓀴two visitors vanished away, like the genii of the Arabian Nights, with
9525𓀴no explanation save that the things had been paid for and were ordered
9526𓀴to this address.
9527𓀴
9528𓀴Just before nine o’clock Sherlock Holmes stepped briskly into the room.
9529𓀴His features were gravely set, but there was a light in his eye which
9530𓀴made me think that he had not been disappointed in his conclusions.
9531𓀴
9532𓀴“They have laid the supper, then,” he said, rubbing his hands.
9533𓀴
9534𓀴“You seem to expect company. They have laid for five.”
9535𓀴
9536𓀴“Yes, I fancy we may have some company dropping in,” said he. “I am
9537𓀴surprised that Lord St. Simon has not already arrived. Ha! I fancy that
9538𓀴I hear his step now upon the stairs.”
9539𓀴
9540𓀴It was indeed our visitor of the afternoon who came bustling in,
9541𓀴dangling his glasses more vigorously than ever, and with a very
9542𓀴perturbed expression upon his aristocratic features.
9543𓀴
9544𓀴“My messenger reached you, then?” asked Holmes.
9545𓀴
9546𓀴“Yes, and I confess that the contents startled me beyond measure. Have
9547𓀴you good authority for what you say?”
9548𓀴
9549𓀴“The best possible.”
9550𓀴
9551𓀴Lord St. Simon sank into a chair and passed his hand over his forehead.
9552𓀴
9553𓀴“What will the Duke say,” he murmured, “when he hears that one of the
9554𓀴family has been subjected to such humiliation?”
9555𓀴
9556𓀴“It is the purest accident. I cannot allow that there is any
9557𓀴humiliation.”
9558𓀴
9559𓀴“Ah, you look on these things from another standpoint.”
9560𓀴
9561𓀴“I fail to see that anyone is to blame. I can hardly see how the lady
9562𓀴could have acted otherwise, though her abrupt method of doing it was
9563𓀴undoubtedly to be regretted. Having no mother, she had no one to advise
9564𓀴her at such a crisis.”
9565𓀴
9566𓀴“It was a slight, sir, a public slight,” said Lord St. Simon, tapping
9567𓀴his fingers upon the table.
9568𓀴
9569𓀴“You must make allowance for this poor girl, placed in so unprecedented
9570𓀴a position.”
9571𓀴
9572𓀴“I will make no allowance. I am very angry indeed, and I have been
9573𓀴shamefully used.”
9574𓀴
9575𓀴“I think that I heard a ring,” said Holmes. “Yes, there are steps on
9576𓀴the landing. If I cannot persuade you to take a lenient view of the
9577𓀴matter, Lord St. Simon, I have brought an advocate here who may be more
9578𓀴successful.” He opened the door and ushered in a lady and gentleman.
9579𓀴“Lord St. Simon,” said he “allow me to introduce you to Mr. and Mrs.
9580𓀴Francis Hay Moulton. The lady, I think, you have already met.”
9581𓀴
9582𓀴At the sight of these newcomers our client had sprung from his seat and
9583𓀴stood very erect, with his eyes cast down and his hand thrust into the
9584𓀴breast of his frock-coat, a picture of offended dignity. The lady had
9585𓀴taken a quick step forward and had held out her hand to him, but he
9586𓀴still refused to raise his eyes. It was as well for his resolution,
9587𓀴perhaps, for her pleading face was one which it was hard to resist.
9588𓀴
9589𓀴“You’re angry, Robert,” said she. “Well, I guess you have every cause
9590𓀴to be.”
9591𓀴
9592𓀴“Pray make no apology to me,” said Lord St. Simon bitterly.
9593𓀴
9594𓀴“Oh, yes, I know that I have treated you real bad and that I should
9595𓀴have spoken to you before I went; but I was kind of rattled, and from
9596𓀴the time when I saw Frank here again I just didn’t know what I was
9597𓀴doing or saying. I only wonder I didn’t fall down and do a faint right
9598𓀴there before the altar.”
9599𓀴
9600𓀴“Perhaps, Mrs. Moulton, you would like my friend and me to leave the
9601𓀴room while you explain this matter?”
9602𓀴
9603𓀴“If I may give an opinion,” remarked the strange gentleman, “we’ve had
9604𓀴just a little too much secrecy over this business already. For my part,
9605𓀴I should like all Europe and America to hear the rights of it.” He was
9606𓀴a small, wiry, sunburnt man, clean-shaven, with a sharp face and alert
9607𓀴manner.
9608𓀴
9609𓀴“Then I’ll tell our story right away,” said the lady. “Frank here and I
9610𓀴met in ’84, in McQuire’s camp, near the Rockies, where Pa was working a
9611𓀴claim. We were engaged to each other, Frank and I; but then one day
9612𓀴father struck a rich pocket and made a pile, while poor Frank here had
9613𓀴a claim that petered out and came to nothing. The richer Pa grew the
9614𓀴poorer was Frank; so at last Pa wouldn’t hear of our engagement lasting
9615𓀴any longer, and he took me away to ’Frisco. Frank wouldn’t throw up his
9616𓀴hand, though; so he followed me there, and he saw me without Pa knowing
9617𓀴anything about it. It would only have made him mad to know, so we just
9618𓀴fixed it all up for ourselves. Frank said that he would go and make his
9619𓀴pile, too, and never come back to claim me until he had as much as Pa.
9620𓀴So then I promised to wait for him to the end of time and pledged
9621𓀴myself not to marry anyone else while he lived. ‘Why shouldn’t we be
9622𓀴married right away, then,’ said he, ‘and then I will feel sure of you;
9623𓀴and I won’t claim to be your husband until I come back?’ Well, we
9624𓀴talked it over, and he had fixed it all up so nicely, with a clergyman
9625𓀴all ready in waiting, that we just did it right there; and then Frank
9626𓀴went off to seek his fortune, and I went back to Pa.
9627𓀴
9628𓀴“The next I heard of Frank was that he was in Montana, and then he went
9629𓀴prospecting in Arizona, and then I heard of him from New Mexico. After
9630𓀴that came a long newspaper story about how a miners’ camp had been
9631𓀴attacked by Apache Indians, and there was my Frank’s name among the
9632𓀴killed. I fainted dead away, and I was very sick for months after. Pa
9633𓀴thought I had a decline and took me to half the doctors in ’Frisco. Not
9634𓀴a word of news came for a year and more, so that I never doubted that
9635𓀴Frank was really dead. Then Lord St. Simon came to ’Frisco, and we came
9636𓀴to London, and a marriage was arranged, and Pa was very pleased, but I
9637𓀴felt all the time that no man on this earth would ever take the place
9638𓀴in my heart that had been given to my poor Frank.
9639𓀴
9640𓀴“Still, if I had married Lord St. Simon, of course I’d have done my
9641𓀴duty by him. We can’t command our love, but we can our actions. I went
9642𓀴to the altar with him with the intention to make him just as good a
9643𓀴wife as it was in me to be. But you may imagine what I felt when, just
9644𓀴as I came to the altar rails, I glanced back and saw Frank standing and
9645𓀴looking at me out of the first pew. I thought it was his ghost at
9646𓀴first; but when I looked again there he was still, with a kind of
9647𓀴question in his eyes, as if to ask me whether I were glad or sorry to
9648𓀴see him. I wonder I didn’t drop. I know that everything was turning
9649𓀴round, and the words of the clergyman were just like the buzz of a bee
9650𓀴in my ear. I didn’t know what to do. Should I stop the service and make
9651𓀴a scene in the church? I glanced at him again, and he seemed to know
9652𓀴what I was thinking, for he raised his finger to his lips to tell me to
9653𓀴be still. Then I saw him scribble on a piece of paper, and I knew that
9654𓀴he was writing me a note. As I passed his pew on the way out I dropped
9655𓀴my bouquet over to him, and he slipped the note into my hand when he
9656𓀴returned me the flowers. It was only a line asking me to join him when
9657𓀴he made the sign to me to do so. Of course I never doubted for a moment
9658𓀴that my first duty was now to him, and I determined to do just whatever
9659𓀴he might direct.
9660𓀴
9661𓀴“When I got back I told my maid, who had known him in California, and
9662𓀴had always been his friend. I ordered her to say nothing, but to get a
9663𓀴few things packed and my ulster ready. I know I ought to have spoken to
9664𓀴Lord St. Simon, but it was dreadful hard before his mother and all
9665𓀴those great people. I just made up my mind to run away and explain
9666𓀴afterwards. I hadn’t been at the table ten minutes before I saw Frank
9667𓀴out of the window at the other side of the road. He beckoned to me and
9668𓀴then began walking into the Park. I slipped out, put on my things, and
9669𓀴followed him. Some woman came talking something or other about Lord St.
9670𓀴Simon to me—seemed to me from the little I heard as if he had a little
9671𓀴secret of his own before marriage also—but I managed to get away from
9672𓀴her and soon overtook Frank. We got into a cab together, and away we
9673𓀴drove to some lodgings he had taken in Gordon Square, and that was my
9674𓀴true wedding after all those years of waiting. Frank had been a
9675𓀴prisoner among the Apaches, had escaped, came on to ’Frisco, found that
9676𓀴I had given him up for dead and had gone to England, followed me there,
9677𓀴and had come upon me at last on the very morning of my second wedding.”
9678𓀴
9679𓀴“I saw it in a paper,” explained the American. “It gave the name and
9680𓀴the church but not where the lady lived.”
9681𓀴
9682𓀴“Then we had a talk as to what we should do, and Frank was all for
9683𓀴openness, but I was so ashamed of it all that I felt as if I should
9684𓀴like to vanish away and never see any of them again—just sending a line
9685𓀴to Pa, perhaps, to show him that I was alive. It was awful to me to
9686𓀴think of all those lords and ladies sitting round that breakfast-table
9687𓀴and waiting for me to come back. So Frank took my wedding-clothes and
9688𓀴things and made a bundle of them, so that I should not be traced, and
9689𓀴dropped them away somewhere where no one could find them. It is likely
9690𓀴that we should have gone on to Paris to-morrow, only that this good
9691𓀴gentleman, Mr. Holmes, came round to us this evening, though how he
9692𓀴found us is more than I can think, and he showed us very clearly and
9693𓀴kindly that I was wrong and that Frank was right, and that we should be
9694𓀴putting ourselves in the wrong if we were so secret. Then he offered to
9695𓀴give us a chance of talking to Lord St. Simon alone, and so we came
9696𓀴right away round to his rooms at once. Now, Robert, you have heard it
9697𓀴all, and I am very sorry if I have given you pain, and I hope that you
9698𓀴do not think very meanly of me.”
9699𓀴
9700𓀴Lord St. Simon had by no means relaxed his rigid attitude, but had
9701𓀴listened with a frowning brow and a compressed lip to this long
9702𓀴narrative.
9703𓀴
9704𓀴“Excuse me,” he said, “but it is not my custom to discuss my most
9705𓀴intimate personal affairs in this public manner.”
9706𓀴
9707𓀴“Then you won’t forgive me? You won’t shake hands before I go?”
9708𓀴
9709𓀴“Oh, certainly, if it would give you any pleasure.” He put out his hand
9710𓀴and coldly grasped that which she extended to him.
9711𓀴
9712𓀴“I had hoped,” suggested Holmes, “that you would have joined us in a
9713𓀴friendly supper.”
9714𓀴
9715𓀴“I think that there you ask a little too much,” responded his Lordship.
9716𓀴“I may be forced to acquiesce in these recent developments, but I can
9717𓀴hardly be expected to make merry over them. I think that with your
9718𓀴permission I will now wish you all a very good-night.” He included us
9719𓀴all in a sweeping bow and stalked out of the room.
9720𓀴
9721𓀴“Then I trust that you at least will honour me with your company,” said
9722𓀴Sherlock Holmes. “It is always a joy to meet an American, Mr. Moulton,
9723𓀴for I am one of those who believe that the folly of a monarch and the
9724𓀴blundering of a minister in far-gone years will not prevent our
9725𓀴children from being some day citizens of the same world-wide country
9726𓀴under a flag which shall be a quartering of the Union Jack with the
9727𓀴Stars and Stripes.”
9728𓀴
9729𓀴“The case has been an interesting one,” remarked Holmes when our
9730𓀴visitors had left us, “because it serves to show very clearly how
9731𓀴simple the explanation may be of an affair which at first sight seems
9732𓀴to be almost inexplicable. Nothing could be more natural than the
9733𓀴sequence of events as narrated by this lady, and nothing stranger than
9734𓀴the result when viewed, for instance, by Mr. Lestrade of Scotland
9735𓀴Yard.”
9736𓀴
9737𓀴“You were not yourself at fault at all, then?”
9738𓀴
9739𓀴“From the first, two facts were very obvious to me, the one that the
9740𓀴lady had been quite willing to undergo the wedding ceremony, the other
9741𓀴that she had repented of it within a few minutes of returning home.
9742𓀴Obviously something had occurred during the morning, then, to cause her
9743𓀴to change her mind. What could that something be? She could not have
9744𓀴spoken to anyone when she was out, for she had been in the company of
9745𓀴the bridegroom. Had she seen someone, then? If she had, it must be
9746𓀴someone from America because she had spent so short a time in this
9747𓀴country that she could hardly have allowed anyone to acquire so deep an
9748𓀴influence over her that the mere sight of him would induce her to
9749𓀴change her plans so completely. You see we have already arrived, by a
9750𓀴process of exclusion, at the idea that she might have seen an American.
9751𓀴Then who could this American be, and why should he possess so much
9752𓀴influence over her? It might be a lover; it might be a husband. Her
9753𓀴young womanhood had, I knew, been spent in rough scenes and under
9754𓀴strange conditions. So far I had got before I ever heard Lord St.
9755𓀴Simon’s narrative. When he told us of a man in a pew, of the change in
9756𓀴the bride’s manner, of so transparent a device for obtaining a note as
9757𓀴the dropping of a bouquet, of her resort to her confidential maid, and
9758𓀴of her very significant allusion to claim-jumping—which in miners’
9759𓀴parlance means taking possession of that which another person has a
9760𓀴prior claim to—the whole situation became absolutely clear. She had
9761𓀴gone off with a man, and the man was either a lover or was a previous
9762𓀴husband—the chances being in favour of the latter.”
9763𓀴
9764𓀴“And how in the world did you find them?”
9765𓀴
9766𓀴“It might have been difficult, but friend Lestrade held information in
9767𓀴his hands the value of which he did not himself know. The initials
9768𓀴were, of course, of the highest importance, but more valuable still was
9769𓀴it to know that within a week he had settled his bill at one of the
9770𓀴most select London hotels.”
9771𓀴
9772𓀴“How did you deduce the select?”
9773𓀴
9774𓀴“By the select prices. Eight shillings for a bed and eightpence for a
9775𓀴glass of sherry pointed to one of the most expensive hotels. There are
9776𓀴not many in London which charge at that rate. In the second one which I
9777𓀴visited in Northumberland Avenue, I learned by an inspection of the
9778𓀴book that Francis H. Moulton, an American gentleman, had left only the
9779𓀴day before, and on looking over the entries against him, I came upon
9780𓀴the very items which I had seen in the duplicate bill. His letters were
9781𓀴to be forwarded to 226 Gordon Square; so thither I travelled, and being
9782𓀴fortunate enough to find the loving couple at home, I ventured to give
9783𓀴them some paternal advice and to point out to them that it would be
9784𓀴better in every way that they should make their position a little
9785𓀴clearer both to the general public and to Lord St. Simon in particular.
9786𓀴I invited them to meet him here, and, as you see, I made him keep the
9787𓀴appointment.”
9788𓀴
9789𓀴“But with no very good result,” I remarked. “His conduct was certainly
9790𓀴not very gracious.”
9791𓀴
9792𓀴“Ah, Watson,” said Holmes, smiling, “perhaps you would not be very
9793𓀴gracious either, if, after all the trouble of wooing and wedding, you
9794𓀴found yourself deprived in an instant of wife and of fortune. I think
9795𓀴that we may judge Lord St. Simon very mercifully and thank our stars
9796𓀴that we are never likely to find ourselves in the same position. Draw
9797𓀴your chair up and hand me my violin, for the only problem we have still
9798𓀴to solve is how to while away these bleak autumnal evenings.”
9799𓀴
9800𓀴
9801𓀴
9802𓀴
9803𓀴XI. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BERYL CORONET
9804𓀴
9805𓀴
9806𓀴“Holmes,” said I as I stood one morning in our bow-window looking down
9807𓀴the street, “here is a madman coming along. It seems rather sad that
9808𓀴his relatives should allow him to come out alone.”
9809𓀴
9810𓀴My friend rose lazily from his armchair and stood with his hands in the
9811𓀴pockets of his dressing-gown, looking over my shoulder. It was a
9812𓀴bright, crisp February morning, and the snow of the day before still
9813𓀴lay deep upon the ground, shimmering brightly in the wintry sun. Down
9814𓀴the centre of Baker Street it had been ploughed into a brown crumbly
9815𓀴band by the traffic, but at either side and on the heaped-up edges of
9816𓀴the footpaths it still lay as white as when it fell. The grey pavement
9817𓀴had been cleaned and scraped, but was still dangerously slippery, so
9818𓀴that there were fewer passengers than usual. Indeed, from the direction
9819𓀴of the Metropolitan Station no one was coming save the single gentleman
9820𓀴whose eccentric conduct had drawn my attention.
9821𓀴
9822𓀴He was a man of about fifty, tall, portly, and imposing, with a
9823𓀴massive, strongly marked face and a commanding figure. He was dressed
9824𓀴in a sombre yet rich style, in black frock-coat, shining hat, neat
9825𓀴brown gaiters, and well-cut pearl-grey trousers. Yet his actions were
9826𓀴in absurd contrast to the dignity of his dress and features, for he was
9827𓀴running hard, with occasional little springs, such as a weary man gives
9828𓀴who is little accustomed to set any tax upon his legs. As he ran he
9829𓀴jerked his hands up and down, waggled his head, and writhed his face
9830𓀴into the most extraordinary contortions.
9831𓀴
9832𓀴“What on earth can be the matter with him?” I asked. “He is looking up
9833𓀴at the numbers of the houses.”
9834𓀴
9835𓀴“I believe that he is coming here,” said Holmes, rubbing his hands.
9836𓀴
9837𓀴“Here?”
9838𓀴
9839𓀴“Yes; I rather think he is coming to consult me professionally. I think
9840𓀴that I recognise the symptoms. Ha! did I not tell you?” As he spoke,
9841𓀴the man, puffing and blowing, rushed at our door and pulled at our bell
9842𓀴until the whole house resounded with the clanging.
9843𓀴
9844𓀴A few moments later he was in our room, still puffing, still
9845𓀴gesticulating, but with so fixed a look of grief and despair in his
9846𓀴eyes that our smiles were turned in an instant to horror and pity. For
9847𓀴a while he could not get his words out, but swayed his body and plucked
9848𓀴at his hair like one who has been driven to the extreme limits of his
9849𓀴reason. Then, suddenly springing to his feet, he beat his head against
9850𓀴the wall with such force that we both rushed upon him and tore him away
9851𓀴to the centre of the room. Sherlock Holmes pushed him down into the
9852𓀴easy-chair and, sitting beside him, patted his hand and chatted with
9853𓀴him in the easy, soothing tones which he knew so well how to employ.
9854𓀴
9855𓀴“You have come to me to tell your story, have you not?” said he. “You
9856𓀴are fatigued with your haste. Pray wait until you have recovered
9857𓀴yourself, and then I shall be most happy to look into any little
9858𓀴problem which you may submit to me.”
9859𓀴
9860𓀴The man sat for a minute or more with a heaving chest, fighting against
9861𓀴his emotion. Then he passed his handkerchief over his brow, set his
9862𓀴lips tight, and turned his face towards us.
9863𓀴
9864𓀴“No doubt you think me mad?” said he.
9865𓀴
9866𓀴“I see that you have had some great trouble,” responded Holmes.
9867𓀴
9868𓀴“God knows I have!—a trouble which is enough to unseat my reason, so
9869𓀴sudden and so terrible is it. Public disgrace I might have faced,
9870𓀴although I am a man whose character has never yet borne a stain.
9871𓀴Private affliction also is the lot of every man; but the two coming
9872𓀴together, and in so frightful a form, have been enough to shake my very
9873𓀴soul. Besides, it is not I alone. The very noblest in the land may
9874𓀴suffer unless some way be found out of this horrible affair.”
9875𓀴
9876𓀴“Pray compose yourself, sir,” said Holmes, “and let me have a clear
9877𓀴account of who you are and what it is that has befallen you.”
9878𓀴
9879𓀴“My name,” answered our visitor, “is probably familiar to your ears. I
9880𓀴am Alexander Holder, of the banking firm of Holder & Stevenson, of
9881𓀴Threadneedle Street.”
9882𓀴
9883𓀴The name was indeed well known to us as belonging to the senior partner
9884𓀴in the second largest private banking concern in the City of London.
9885𓀴What could have happened, then, to bring one of the foremost citizens
9886𓀴of London to this most pitiable pass? We waited, all curiosity, until
9887𓀴with another effort he braced himself to tell his story.
9888𓀴
9889𓀴“I feel that time is of value,” said he; “that is why I hastened here
9890𓀴when the police inspector suggested that I should secure your
9891𓀴co-operation. I came to Baker Street by the Underground and hurried
9892𓀴from there on foot, for the cabs go slowly through this snow. That is
9893𓀴why I was so out of breath, for I am a man who takes very little
9894𓀴exercise. I feel better now, and I will put the facts before you as
9895𓀴shortly and yet as clearly as I can.
9896𓀴
9897𓀴“It is, of course, well known to you that in a successful banking
9898𓀴business as much depends upon our being able to find remunerative
9899𓀴investments for our funds as upon our increasing our connection and the
9900𓀴number of our depositors. One of our most lucrative means of laying out
9901𓀴money is in the shape of loans, where the security is unimpeachable. We
9902𓀴have done a good deal in this direction during the last few years, and
9903𓀴there are many noble families to whom we have advanced large sums upon
9904𓀴the security of their pictures, libraries, or plate.
9905𓀴
9906𓀴“Yesterday morning I was seated in my office at the bank when a card
9907𓀴was brought in to me by one of the clerks. I started when I saw the
9908𓀴name, for it was that of none other than—well, perhaps even to you I
9909𓀴had better say no more than that it was a name which is a household
9910𓀴word all over the earth—one of the highest, noblest, most exalted names
9911𓀴in England. I was overwhelmed by the honour and attempted, when he
9912𓀴entered, to say so, but he plunged at once into business with the air
9913𓀴of a man who wishes to hurry quickly through a disagreeable task.
9914𓀴
9915𓀴“‘Mr. Holder,’ said he, ‘I have been informed that you are in the habit
9916𓀴of advancing money.’
9917𓀴
9918𓀴“‘The firm does so when the security is good.’ I answered.
9919𓀴
9920𓀴“‘It is absolutely essential to me,’ said he, ‘that I should have £
9921𓀴50,000 at once. I could, of course, borrow so trifling a sum ten times
9922𓀴over from my friends, but I much prefer to make it a matter of business
9923𓀴and to carry out that business myself. In my position you can readily
9924𓀴understand that it is unwise to place one’s self under obligations.’
9925𓀴
9926𓀴“‘For how long, may I ask, do you want this sum?’ I asked.
9927𓀴
9928𓀴“‘Next Monday I have a large sum due to me, and I shall then most
9929𓀴certainly repay what you advance, with whatever interest you think it
9930𓀴right to charge. But it is very essential to me that the money should
9931𓀴be paid at once.’
9932𓀴
9933𓀴“‘I should be happy to advance it without further parley from my own
9934𓀴private purse,’ said I, ‘were it not that the strain would be rather
9935𓀴more than it could bear. If, on the other hand, I am to do it in the
9936𓀴name of the firm, then in justice to my partner I must insist that,
9937𓀴even in your case, every businesslike precaution should be taken.’
9938𓀴
9939𓀴“‘I should much prefer to have it so,’ said he, raising up a square,
9940𓀴black morocco case which he had laid beside his chair. ‘You have
9941𓀴doubtless heard of the Beryl Coronet?’
9942𓀴
9943𓀴“‘One of the most precious public possessions of the empire,’ said I.
9944𓀴
9945𓀴“‘Precisely.’ He opened the case, and there, imbedded in soft,
9946𓀴flesh-coloured velvet, lay the magnificent piece of jewellery which he
9947𓀴had named. ‘There are thirty-nine enormous beryls,’ said he, ‘and the
9948𓀴price of the gold chasing is incalculable. The lowest estimate would
9949𓀴put the worth of the coronet at double the sum which I have asked. I am
9950𓀴prepared to leave it with you as my security.’
9951𓀴
9952𓀴“I took the precious case into my hands and looked in some perplexity
9953𓀴from it to my illustrious client.
9954𓀴
9955𓀴“‘You doubt its value?’ he asked.
9956𓀴
9957𓀴“‘Not at all. I only doubt—’
9958𓀴
9959𓀴“‘The propriety of my leaving it. You may set your mind at rest about
9960𓀴that. I should not dream of doing so were it not absolutely certain
9961𓀴that I should be able in four days to reclaim it. It is a pure matter
9962𓀴of form. Is the security sufficient?’
9963𓀴
9964𓀴“‘Ample.’
9965𓀴
9966𓀴“‘You understand, Mr. Holder, that I am giving you a strong proof of
9967𓀴the confidence which I have in you, founded upon all that I have heard
9968𓀴of you. I rely upon you not only to be discreet and to refrain from all
9969𓀴gossip upon the matter but, above all, to preserve this coronet with
9970𓀴every possible precaution because I need not say that a great public
9971𓀴scandal would be caused if any harm were to befall it. Any injury to it
9972𓀴would be almost as serious as its complete loss, for there are no
9973𓀴beryls in the world to match these, and it would be impossible to
9974𓀴replace them. I leave it with you, however, with every confidence, and
9975𓀴I shall call for it in person on Monday morning.’
9976𓀴
9977𓀴“Seeing that my client was anxious to leave, I said no more but,
9978𓀴calling for my cashier, I ordered him to pay over fifty £ 1000 notes.
9979𓀴When I was alone once more, however, with the precious case lying upon
9980𓀴the table in front of me, I could not but think with some misgivings of
9981𓀴the immense responsibility which it entailed upon me. There could be no
9982𓀴doubt that, as it was a national possession, a horrible scandal would
9983𓀴ensue if any misfortune should occur to it. I already regretted having
9984𓀴ever consented to take charge of it. However, it was too late to alter
9985𓀴the matter now, so I locked it up in my private safe and turned once
9986𓀴more to my work.
9987𓀴
9988𓀴“When evening came I felt that it would be an imprudence to leave so
9989𓀴precious a thing in the office behind me. Bankers’ safes had been
9990𓀴forced before now, and why should not mine be? If so, how terrible
9991𓀴would be the position in which I should find myself! I determined,
9992𓀴therefore, that for the next few days I would always carry the case
9993𓀴backward and forward with me, so that it might never be really out of
9994𓀴my reach. With this intention, I called a cab and drove out to my house
9995𓀴at Streatham, carrying the jewel with me. I did not breathe freely
9996𓀴until I had taken it upstairs and locked it in the bureau of my
9997𓀴dressing-room.
9998𓀴
9999𓀴“And now a word as to my household, Mr. Holmes, for I wish you to
10000𓀴thoroughly understand the situation. My groom and my page sleep out of
10001𓀴the house, and may be set aside altogether. I have three maid-servants
10002𓀴who have been with me a number of years and whose absolute reliability
10003𓀴is quite above suspicion. Another, Lucy Parr, the second waiting-maid,
10004𓀴has only been in my service a few months. She came with an excellent
10005𓀴character, however, and has always given me satisfaction. She is a very
10006𓀴pretty girl and has attracted admirers who have occasionally hung about
10007𓀴the place. That is the only drawback which we have found to her, but we
10008𓀴believe her to be a thoroughly good girl in every way.
10009𓀴
10010𓀴“So much for the servants. My family itself is so small that it will
10011𓀴not take me long to describe it. I am a widower and have an only son,
10012𓀴Arthur. He has been a disappointment to me, Mr. Holmes—a grievous
10013𓀴disappointment. I have no doubt that I am myself to blame. People tell
10014𓀴me that I have spoiled him. Very likely I have. When my dear wife died
10015𓀴I felt that he was all I had to love. I could not bear to see the smile
10016𓀴fade even for a moment from his face. I have never denied him a wish.
10017𓀴Perhaps it would have been better for both of us had I been sterner,
10018𓀴but I meant it for the best.
10019𓀴
10020𓀴“It was naturally my intention that he should succeed me in my
10021𓀴business, but he was not of a business turn. He was wild, wayward, and,
10022𓀴to speak the truth, I could not trust him in the handling of large sums
10023𓀴of money. When he was young he became a member of an aristocratic club,
10024𓀴and there, having charming manners, he was soon the intimate of a
10025𓀴number of men with long purses and expensive habits. He learned to play
10026𓀴heavily at cards and to squander money on the turf, until he had again
10027𓀴and again to come to me and implore me to give him an advance upon his
10028𓀴allowance, that he might settle his debts of honour. He tried more than
10029𓀴once to break away from the dangerous company which he was keeping, but
10030𓀴each time the influence of his friend, Sir George Burnwell, was enough
10031𓀴to draw him back again.
10032𓀴
10033𓀴“And, indeed, I could not wonder that such a man as Sir George Burnwell
10034𓀴should gain an influence over him, for he has frequently brought him to
10035𓀴my house, and I have found myself that I could hardly resist the
10036𓀴fascination of his manner. He is older than Arthur, a man of the world
10037𓀴to his finger-tips, one who had been everywhere, seen everything, a
10038𓀴brilliant talker, and a man of great personal beauty. Yet when I think
10039𓀴of him in cold blood, far away from the glamour of his presence, I am
10040𓀴convinced from his cynical speech and the look which I have caught in
10041𓀴his eyes that he is one who should be deeply distrusted. So I think,
10042𓀴and so, too, thinks my little Mary, who has a woman’s quick insight
10043𓀴into character.
10044𓀴
10045𓀴“And now there is only she to be described. She is my niece; but when
10046𓀴my brother died five years ago and left her alone in the world I
10047𓀴adopted her, and have looked upon her ever since as my daughter. She is
10048𓀴a sunbeam in my house—sweet, loving, beautiful, a wonderful manager and
10049𓀴housekeeper, yet as tender and quiet and gentle as a woman could be.
10050𓀴She is my right hand. I do not know what I could do without her. In
10051𓀴only one matter has she ever gone against my wishes. Twice my boy has
10052𓀴asked her to marry him, for he loves her devotedly, but each time she
10053𓀴has refused him. I think that if anyone could have drawn him into the
10054𓀴right path it would have been she, and that his marriage might have
10055𓀴changed his whole life; but now, alas! it is too late—forever too late!
10056𓀴
10057𓀴“Now, Mr. Holmes, you know the people who live under my roof, and I
10058𓀴shall continue with my miserable story.
10059𓀴
10060𓀴“When we were taking coffee in the drawing-room that night after
10061𓀴dinner, I told Arthur and Mary my experience, and of the precious
10062𓀴treasure which we had under our roof, suppressing only the name of my
10063𓀴client. Lucy Parr, who had brought in the coffee, had, I am sure, left
10064𓀴the room; but I cannot swear that the door was closed. Mary and Arthur
10065𓀴were much interested and wished to see the famous coronet, but I
10066𓀴thought it better not to disturb it.
10067𓀴
10068𓀴“‘Where have you put it?’ asked Arthur.
10069𓀴
10070𓀴“‘In my own bureau.’
10071𓀴
10072𓀴“‘Well, I hope to goodness the house won’t be burgled during the
10073𓀴night.’ said he.
10074𓀴
10075𓀴“‘It is locked up,’ I answered.
10076𓀴
10077𓀴“‘Oh, any old key will fit that bureau. When I was a youngster I have
10078𓀴opened it myself with the key of the box-room cupboard.’
10079𓀴
10080𓀴“He often had a wild way of talking, so that I thought little of what
10081𓀴he said. He followed me to my room, however, that night with a very
10082𓀴grave face.
10083𓀴
10084𓀴“‘Look here, dad,’ said he with his eyes cast down, ‘can you let me
10085𓀴have £ 200?’
10086𓀴
10087𓀴“‘No, I cannot!’ I answered sharply. ‘I have been far too generous with
10088𓀴you in money matters.’
10089𓀴
10090𓀴“‘You have been very kind,’ said he, ‘but I must have this money, or
10091𓀴else I can never show my face inside the club again.’
10092𓀴
10093𓀴“‘And a very good thing, too!’ I cried.
10094𓀴
10095𓀴“‘Yes, but you would not have me leave it a dishonoured man,’ said he.
10096𓀴‘I could not bear the disgrace. I must raise the money in some way, and
10097𓀴if you will not let me have it, then I must try other means.’
10098𓀴
10099𓀴“I was very angry, for this was the third demand during the month. ‘You
10100𓀴shall not have a farthing from me,’ I cried, on which he bowed and left
10101𓀴the room without another word.
10102𓀴
10103𓀴“When he was gone I unlocked my bureau, made sure that my treasure was
10104𓀴safe, and locked it again. Then I started to go round the house to see
10105𓀴that all was secure—a duty which I usually leave to Mary but which I
10106𓀴thought it well to perform myself that night. As I came down the stairs
10107𓀴I saw Mary herself at the side window of the hall, which she closed and
10108𓀴fastened as I approached.
10109𓀴
10110𓀴“‘Tell me, dad,’ said she, looking, I thought, a little disturbed, ‘did
10111𓀴you give Lucy, the maid, leave to go out to-night?’
10112𓀴
10113𓀴“‘Certainly not.’
10114𓀴
10115𓀴“‘She came in just now by the back door. I have no doubt that she has
10116𓀴only been to the side gate to see someone, but I think that it is
10117𓀴hardly safe and should be stopped.’
10118𓀴

10121𓀴

10123𓀴
10124𓀴“‘Then, good-night.’ I kissed her and went up to my bedroom again,
10125𓀴where I was soon asleep.
10126𓀴
10127𓀴“I am endeavouring to tell you everything, Mr. Holmes, which may have
10128𓀴any bearing upon the case, but I beg that you will question me upon any
10129𓀴point which I do not make clear.”
10130𓀴
10131𓀴“On the contrary, your statement is singularly lucid.”
10132𓀴
10133𓀴“I come to a part of my story now in which I should wish to be
10134𓀴particularly so. I am not a very heavy sleeper, and the anxiety in my
10135𓀴mind tended, no doubt, to make me even less so than usual. About two in
10136𓀴the morning, then, I was awakened by some sound in the house. It had
10137𓀴ceased ere I was wide awake, but it had left an impression behind it as

10140𓀴footsteps moving softly in the next room. I slipped out of bed, all
10141𓀴palpitating with fear, and peeped round the corner of my dressing-room

10143𓀴
10144𓀴“‘Arthur!’ I screamed, ‘you villain! you thief! How dare you touch that

10146𓀴
10147𓀴“The gas was half up, as I had left it, and my unhappy boy, dressed
10148𓀴only in his shirt and trousers, was standing beside the light, holding
10149𓀴the coronet in his hands. He appeared to be wrenching at it, or bending
10150𓀴it with all his strength. At my cry he dropped it from his grasp and
10151𓀴turned as pale as death. I snatched it up and examined it. One of the
10152𓀴gold corners, with three of the beryls in it, was missing.
10153𓀴
10154𓀴“‘You blackguard!’ I shouted, beside myself with rage. ‘You have
10155𓀴destroyed it! You have dishonoured me forever! Where are the jewels
10156𓀴which you have stolen?’
10157𓀴
10158𓀴“‘Stolen!’ he cried.
10159𓀴
10160𓀴“‘Yes, thief!’ I roared, shaking him by the shoulder.
10161𓀴
10162𓀴“‘There are none missing. There cannot be any missing,’ said he.
10163𓀴
10164𓀴“‘There are three missing. And you know where they are. Must I call you
10165𓀴a liar as well as a thief? Did I not see you trying to tear off another
10166𓀴piece?’
10167𓀴
10168𓀴“‘You have called me names enough,’ said he, ‘I will not stand it any
10169𓀴longer. I shall not say another word about this business, since you
10170𓀴have chosen to insult me. I will leave your house in the morning and
10171𓀴make my own way in the world.’
10172𓀴
10173𓀴“‘You shall leave it in the hands of the police!’ I cried half-mad with
10174𓀴grief and rage. ‘I shall have this matter probed to the bottom.’
10175𓀴
10176𓀴“‘You shall learn nothing from me,’ said he with a passion such as I
10177𓀴should not have thought was in his nature. ‘If you choose to call the
10178𓀴police, let the police find what they can.’
10179𓀴
10180𓀴“By this time the whole house was astir, for I had raised my voice in
10181𓀴my anger. Mary was the first to rush into my room, and, at the sight of
10182𓀴the coronet and of Arthur’s face, she read the whole story and, with a
10183𓀴scream, fell down senseless on the ground. I sent the housemaid for the
10184𓀴police and put the investigation into their hands at once. When the
10185𓀴inspector and a constable entered the house, Arthur, who had stood
10186𓀴sullenly with his arms folded, asked me whether it was my intention to
10187𓀴charge him with theft. I answered that it had ceased to be a private
10188𓀴matter, but had become a public one, since the ruined coronet was
10189𓀴national property. I was determined that the law should have its way in
10190𓀴everything.
10191𓀴
10192𓀴“‘At least,’ said he, ‘you will not have me arrested at once. It would
10193𓀴be to your advantage as well as mine if I might leave the house for
10194𓀴five minutes.’
10195𓀴
10196𓀴“‘That you may get away, or perhaps that you may conceal what you have
10197𓀴stolen,’ said I. And then, realising the dreadful position in which I
10198𓀴was placed, I implored him to remember that not only my honour but that
10199𓀴of one who was far greater than I was at stake; and that he threatened
10200𓀴to raise a scandal which would convulse the nation. He might avert it
10201𓀴all if he would but tell me what he had done with the three missing
10202𓀴stones.
10203𓀴
10204𓀴“‘You may as well face the matter,’ said I; ‘you have been caught in
10205𓀴the act, and no confession could make your guilt more heinous. If you
10206𓀴but make such reparation as is in your power, by telling us where the
10207𓀴beryls are, all shall be forgiven and forgotten.’
10208𓀴
10209𓀴“‘Keep your forgiveness for those who ask for it,’ he answered, turning
10210𓀴away from me with a sneer. I saw that he was too hardened for any words
10211𓀴of mine to influence him. There was but one way for it. I called in the
10212𓀴inspector and gave him into custody. A search was made at once not only
10213𓀴of his person but of his room and of every portion of the house where
10214𓀴he could possibly have concealed the gems; but no trace of them could
10215𓀴be found, nor would the wretched boy open his mouth for all our
10216𓀴persuasions and our threats. This morning he was removed to a cell, and
10217𓀴I, after going through all the police formalities, have hurried round
10218𓀴to you to implore you to use your skill in unravelling the matter. The
10219𓀴police have openly confessed that they can at present make nothing of
10220𓀴it. You may go to any expense which you think necessary. I have already
10221𓀴offered a reward of £ 1000. My God, what shall I do! I have lost my
10222𓀴honour, my gems, and my son in one night. Oh, what shall I do!”
10223𓀴
10224𓀴He put a hand on either side of his head and rocked himself to and fro,
10225𓀴droning to himself like a child whose grief has got beyond words.
10226𓀴
10227𓀴Sherlock Holmes sat silent for some few minutes, with his brows knitted
10228𓀴and his eyes fixed upon the fire.
10229𓀴
10230𓀴“Do you receive much company?” he asked.
10231𓀴
10232𓀴“None save my partner with his family and an occasional friend of
10233𓀴Arthur’s. Sir George Burnwell has been several times lately. No one
10234𓀴else, I think.”
10235𓀴
10236𓀴“Do you go out much in society?”
10237𓀴
10238𓀴“Arthur does. Mary and I stay at home. We neither of us care for it.”
10239𓀴
10240𓀴“That is unusual in a young girl.”
10241𓀴
10242𓀴“She is of a quiet nature. Besides, she is not so very young. She is
10243𓀴four-and-twenty.”
10244𓀴
10245𓀴“This matter, from what you say, seems to have been a shock to her
10246𓀴also.”
10247𓀴
10248𓀴“Terrible! She is even more affected than I.”
10249𓀴
10250𓀴“You have neither of you any doubt as to your son’s guilt?”
10251𓀴
10252𓀴“How can we have when I saw him with my own eyes with the coronet in
10253𓀴his hands.”
10254𓀴
10255𓀴“I hardly consider that a conclusive proof. Was the remainder of the
10256𓀴coronet at all injured?”
10257𓀴
10258𓀴“Yes, it was twisted.”
10259𓀴
10260𓀴“Do you not think, then, that he might have been trying to straighten
10261𓀴it?”
10262𓀴
10263𓀴“God bless you! You are doing what you can for him and for me. But it
10264𓀴is too heavy a task. What was he doing there at all? If his purpose
10265𓀴were innocent, why did he not say so?”
10266𓀴
10267𓀴“Precisely. And if it were guilty, why did he not invent a lie? His
10268𓀴silence appears to me to cut both ways. There are several singular
10269𓀴points about the case. What did the police think of the noise which
10270𓀴awoke you from your sleep?”
10271𓀴
