10272𓀴“They considered that it might be caused by Arthur’s closing his
10273𓀴bedroom door.”
10274𓀴
10275𓀴“A likely story! As if a man bent on felony would slam his door so as
10276𓀴to wake a household. What did they say, then, of the disappearance of
10277𓀴these gems?”
10278𓀴
10279𓀴“They are still sounding the planking and probing the furniture in the
10280𓀴hope of finding them.”
10281𓀴
10282𓀴“Have they thought of looking outside the house?”
10283𓀴
10284𓀴“Yes, they have shown extraordinary energy. The whole garden has
10285𓀴already been minutely examined.”
10286𓀴
10287𓀴“Now, my dear sir,” said Holmes, “is it not obvious to you now that
10288𓀴this matter really strikes very much deeper than either you or the
10289𓀴police were at first inclined to think? It appeared to you to be a
10290𓀴simple case; to me it seems exceedingly complex. Consider what is
10291𓀴involved by your theory. You suppose that your son came down from his
10292𓀴bed, went, at great risk, to your dressing-room, opened your bureau,
10293𓀴took out your coronet, broke off by main force a small portion of it,
10294𓀴went off to some other place, concealed three gems out of the
10295𓀴thirty-nine, with such skill that nobody can find them, and then
10296𓀴returned with the other thirty-six into the room in which he exposed
10297𓀴himself to the greatest danger of being discovered. I ask you now, is
10298𓀴such a theory tenable?”
10299𓀴
10300𓀴“But what other is there?” cried the banker with a gesture of despair.
10301𓀴“If his motives were innocent, why does he not explain them?”
10302𓀴
10303𓀴“It is our task to find that out,” replied Holmes; “so now, if you
10304𓀴please, Mr. Holder, we will set off for Streatham together, and devote
10305𓀴an hour to glancing a little more closely into details.”
10306𓀴
10307𓀴My friend insisted upon my accompanying them in their expedition, which
10308𓀴I was eager enough to do, for my curiosity and sympathy were deeply
10309𓀴stirred by the story to which we had listened. I confess that the guilt
10310𓀴of the banker’s son appeared to me to be as obvious as it did to his
10311𓀴unhappy father, but still I had such faith in Holmes’ judgment that I
10312𓀴felt that there must be some grounds for hope as long as he was
10313𓀴dissatisfied with the accepted explanation. He hardly spoke a word the
10314𓀴whole way out to the southern suburb, but sat with his chin upon his
10315𓀴breast and his hat drawn over his eyes, sunk in the deepest thought.
10316𓀴Our client appeared to have taken fresh heart at the little glimpse of
10317𓀴hope which had been presented to him, and he even broke into a
10318𓀴desultory chat with me over his business affairs. A short railway
10319𓀴journey and a shorter walk brought us to Fairbank, the modest residence
10320𓀴of the great financier.
10321𓀴
10322𓀴Fairbank was a good-sized square house of white stone, standing back a
10323𓀴little from the road. A double carriage-sweep, with a snow-clad lawn,
10324𓀴stretched down in front to two large iron gates which closed the
10325𓀴entrance. On the right side was a small wooden thicket, which led into
10326𓀴a narrow path between two neat hedges stretching from the road to the
10327𓀴kitchen door, and forming the tradesmen’s entrance. On the left ran a
10328𓀴lane which led to the stables, and was not itself within the grounds at
10329𓀴all, being a public, though little used, thoroughfare. Holmes left us
10330𓀴standing at the door and walked slowly all round the house, across the
10331𓀴front, down the tradesmen’s path, and so round by the garden behind
10332𓀴into the stable lane. So long was he that Mr. Holder and I went into
10333𓀴the dining-room and waited by the fire until he should return. We were
10334𓀴sitting there in silence when the door opened and a young lady came in.
10335𓀴She was rather above the middle height, slim, with dark hair and eyes,
10336𓀴which seemed the darker against the absolute pallor of her skin. I do
10337𓀴not think that I have ever seen such deadly paleness in a woman’s face.
10338𓀴Her lips, too, were bloodless, but her eyes were flushed with crying.
10339𓀴As she swept silently into the room she impressed me with a greater
10340𓀴sense of grief than the banker had done in the morning, and it was the
10341𓀴more striking in her as she was evidently a woman of strong character,
10342𓀴with immense capacity for self-restraint. Disregarding my presence, she
10343𓀴went straight to her uncle and passed her hand over his head with a
10344𓀴sweet womanly caress.
10345𓀴
10346𓀴“You have given orders that Arthur should be liberated, have you not,
10347𓀴dad?” she asked.
10348𓀴
10349𓀴“No, no, my girl, the matter must be probed to the bottom.”
10350𓀴
10351𓀴“But I am so sure that he is innocent. You know what woman’s instincts
10352𓀴are. I know that he has done no harm and that you will be sorry for
10353𓀴having acted so harshly.”
10354𓀴
10355𓀴“Why is he silent, then, if he is innocent?”
10356𓀴
10357𓀴“Who knows? Perhaps because he was so angry that you should suspect
10358𓀴him.”
10359𓀴
10360𓀴“How could I help suspecting him, when I actually saw him with the
10361𓀴coronet in his hand?”
10362𓀴
10363𓀴“Oh, but he had only picked it up to look at it. Oh, do, do take my
10364𓀴word for it that he is innocent. Let the matter drop and say no more.
10365𓀴It is so dreadful to think of our dear Arthur in prison!”
10366𓀴
10367𓀴“I shall never let it drop until the gems are found—never, Mary! Your
10368𓀴affection for Arthur blinds you as to the awful consequences to me. Far
10369𓀴from hushing the thing up, I have brought a gentleman down from London
10370𓀴to inquire more deeply into it.”
10371𓀴
10372𓀴“This gentleman?” she asked, facing round to me.
10373𓀴
10374𓀴“No, his friend. He wished us to leave him alone. He is round in the
10375𓀴stable lane now.”
10376𓀴
10377𓀴“The stable lane?” She raised her dark eyebrows. “What can he hope to
10378𓀴find there? Ah! this, I suppose, is he. I trust, sir, that you will
10379𓀴succeed in proving, what I feel sure is the truth, that my cousin
10380𓀴Arthur is innocent of this crime.”
10381𓀴
10382𓀴“I fully share your opinion, and I trust, with you, that we may prove
10383𓀴it,” returned Holmes, going back to the mat to knock the snow from his
10384𓀴shoes. “I believe I have the honour of addressing Miss Mary Holder.
10385𓀴Might I ask you a question or two?”
10386𓀴
10387𓀴“Pray do, sir, if it may help to clear this horrible affair up.”
10388𓀴
10389𓀴“You heard nothing yourself last night?”
10390𓀴
10391𓀴“Nothing, until my uncle here began to speak loudly. I heard that, and
10392𓀴I came down.”
10393𓀴
10394𓀴“You shut up the windows and doors the night before. Did you fasten all
10395𓀴the windows?”
10396𓀴
10397𓀴“Yes.”
10398𓀴
10399𓀴“Were they all fastened this morning?”
10400𓀴
10401𓀴“Yes.”
10402𓀴
10403𓀴“You have a maid who has a sweetheart? I think that you remarked to
10404𓀴your uncle last night that she had been out to see him?”
10405𓀴
10406𓀴“Yes, and she was the girl who waited in the drawing-room, and who may
10407𓀴have heard uncle’s remarks about the coronet.”
10408𓀴
10409𓀴“I see. You infer that she may have gone out to tell her sweetheart,
10410𓀴and that the two may have planned the robbery.”
10411𓀴
10412𓀴“But what is the good of all these vague theories,” cried the banker
10413𓀴impatiently, “when I have told you that I saw Arthur with the coronet
10414𓀴in his hands?”
10415𓀴
10416𓀴“Wait a little, Mr. Holder. We must come back to that. About this girl,
10417𓀴Miss Holder. You saw her return by the kitchen door, I presume?”
10418𓀴
10419𓀴“Yes; when I went to see if the door was fastened for the night I met
10420𓀴her slipping in. I saw the man, too, in the gloom.”
10421𓀴
10422𓀴“Do you know him?”
10423𓀴
10424𓀴“Oh, yes! he is the greengrocer who brings our vegetables round. His
10425𓀴name is Francis Prosper.”
10426𓀴
10427𓀴“He stood,” said Holmes, “to the left of the door—that is to say,
10428𓀴farther up the path than is necessary to reach the door?”
10429𓀴
10430𓀴“Yes, he did.”
10431𓀴
10432𓀴“And he is a man with a wooden leg?”
10433𓀴
10434𓀴Something like fear sprang up in the young lady’s expressive black
10435𓀴eyes. “Why, you are like a magician,” said she. “How do you know that?”
10436𓀴She smiled, but there was no answering smile in Holmes’ thin, eager
10437𓀴face.
10438𓀴
10439𓀴“I should be very glad now to go upstairs,” said he. “I shall probably
10440𓀴wish to go over the outside of the house again. Perhaps I had better
10441𓀴take a look at the lower windows before I go up.”
10442𓀴
10443𓀴He walked swiftly round from one to the other, pausing only at the
10444𓀴large one which looked from the hall onto the stable lane. This he
10445𓀴opened and made a very careful examination of the sill with his
10446𓀴powerful magnifying lens. “Now we shall go upstairs,” said he at last.
10447𓀴
10448𓀴The banker’s dressing-room was a plainly furnished little chamber, with
10449𓀴a grey carpet, a large bureau, and a long mirror. Holmes went to the
10450𓀴bureau first and looked hard at the lock.
10451𓀴
10452𓀴“Which key was used to open it?” he asked.
10453𓀴
10454𓀴“That which my son himself indicated—that of the cupboard of the
10455𓀴lumber-room.”
10456𓀴
10457𓀴“Have you it here?”
10458𓀴
10459𓀴“That is it on the dressing-table.”
10460𓀴
10461𓀴Sherlock Holmes took it up and opened the bureau.
10462𓀴
10463𓀴“It is a noiseless lock,” said he. “It is no wonder that it did not
10464𓀴wake you. This case, I presume, contains the coronet. We must have a
10465𓀴look at it.” He opened the case, and taking out the diadem he laid it
10466𓀴upon the table. It was a magnificent specimen of the jeweller’s art,
10467𓀴and the thirty-six stones were the finest that I have ever seen. At one
10468𓀴side of the coronet was a cracked edge, where a corner holding three
10469𓀴gems had been torn away.
10470𓀴
10471𓀴“Now, Mr. Holder,” said Holmes, “here is the corner which corresponds
10472𓀴to that which has been so unfortunately lost. Might I beg that you will
10473𓀴break it off.”
10474𓀴
10475𓀴The banker recoiled in horror. “I should not dream of trying,” said he.
10476𓀴
10477𓀴“Then I will.” Holmes suddenly bent his strength upon it, but without
10478𓀴result. “I feel it give a little,” said he; “but, though I am
10479𓀴exceptionally strong in the fingers, it would take me all my time to
10480𓀴break it. An ordinary man could not do it. Now, what do you think would
10481𓀴happen if I did break it, Mr. Holder? There would be a noise like a
10482𓀴pistol shot. Do you tell me that all this happened within a few yards
10483𓀴of your bed and that you heard nothing of it?”
10484𓀴
10485𓀴“I do not know what to think. It is all dark to me.”
10486𓀴
10487𓀴“But perhaps it may grow lighter as we go. What do you think, Miss
10488𓀴Holder?”
10489𓀴
10490𓀴“I confess that I still share my uncle’s perplexity.”
10491𓀴
10492𓀴“Your son had no shoes or slippers on when you saw him?”
10493𓀴
10494𓀴“He had nothing on save only his trousers and shirt.”
10495𓀴
10496𓀴“Thank you. We have certainly been favoured with extraordinary luck
10497𓀴during this inquiry, and it will be entirely our own fault if we do not
10498𓀴succeed in clearing the matter up. With your permission, Mr. Holder, I
10499𓀴shall now continue my investigations outside.”
10500𓀴
10501𓀴He went alone, at his own request, for he explained that any
10502𓀴unnecessary footmarks might make his task more difficult. For an hour
10503𓀴or more he was at work, returning at last with his feet heavy with snow
10504𓀴and his features as inscrutable as ever.
10505𓀴
10506𓀴“I think that I have seen now all that there is to see, Mr. Holder,”
10507𓀴said he; “I can serve you best by returning to my rooms.”
10508𓀴
10509𓀴“But the gems, Mr. Holmes. Where are they?”
10510𓀴
10511𓀴“I cannot tell.”
10512𓀴
10513𓀴The banker wrung his hands. “I shall never see them again!” he cried.
10514𓀴“And my son? You give me hopes?”
10515𓀴
10516𓀴“My opinion is in no way altered.”
10517𓀴
10518𓀴“Then, for God’s sake, what was this dark business which was acted in
10519𓀴my house last night?”
10520𓀴
10521𓀴“If you can call upon me at my Baker Street rooms to-morrow morning
10522𓀴between nine and ten I shall be happy to do what I can to make it
10523𓀴clearer. I understand that you give me _carte blanche_ to act for you,
10524𓀴provided only that I get back the gems, and that you place no limit on
10525𓀴the sum I may draw.”
10526𓀴
10527𓀴“I would give my fortune to have them back.”
10528𓀴
10529𓀴“Very good. I shall look into the matter between this and then.
10530𓀴Good-bye; it is just possible that I may have to come over here again
10531𓀴before evening.”
10532𓀴
10533𓀴It was obvious to me that my companion’s mind was now made up about the
10534𓀴case, although what his conclusions were was more than I could even
10535𓀴dimly imagine. Several times during our homeward journey I endeavoured
10536𓀴to sound him upon the point, but he always glided away to some other
10537𓀴topic, until at last I gave it over in despair. It was not yet three
10538𓀴when we found ourselves in our rooms once more. He hurried to his
10539𓀴chamber and was down again in a few minutes dressed as a common loafer.
10540𓀴With his collar turned up, his shiny, seedy coat, his red cravat, and
10541𓀴his worn boots, he was a perfect sample of the class.
10542𓀴
10543𓀴“I think that this should do,” said he, glancing into the glass above
10544𓀴the fireplace. “I only wish that you could come with me, Watson, but I
10545𓀴fear that it won’t do. I may be on the trail in this matter, or I may
10546𓀴be following a will-o’-the-wisp, but I shall soon know which it is. I
10547𓀴hope that I may be back in a few hours.” He cut a slice of beef from
10548𓀴the joint upon the sideboard, sandwiched it between two rounds of
10549𓀴bread, and thrusting this rude meal into his pocket he started off upon
10550𓀴his expedition.
10551𓀴
10552𓀴I had just finished my tea when he returned, evidently in excellent
10553𓀴spirits, swinging an old elastic-sided boot in his hand. He chucked it
10554𓀴down into a corner and helped himself to a cup of tea.
10555𓀴
10556𓀴“I only looked in as I passed,” said he. “I am going right on.”
10557𓀴
10558𓀴“Where to?”
10559𓀴
10560𓀴“Oh, to the other side of the West End. It may be some time before I
10561𓀴get back. Don’t wait up for me in case I should be late.”
10562𓀴
10563𓀴“How are you getting on?”
10564𓀴
10565𓀴“Oh, so so. Nothing to complain of. I have been out to Streatham since
10566𓀴I saw you last, but I did not call at the house. It is a very sweet
10567𓀴little problem, and I would not have missed it for a good deal.
10568𓀴However, I must not sit gossiping here, but must get these disreputable
10569𓀴clothes off and return to my highly respectable self.”
10570𓀴
10571𓀴I could see by his manner that he had stronger reasons for satisfaction
10572𓀴than his words alone would imply. His eyes twinkled, and there was even
10573𓀴a touch of colour upon his sallow cheeks. He hastened upstairs, and a
10574𓀴few minutes later I heard the slam of the hall door, which told me that
10575𓀴he was off once more upon his congenial hunt.
10576𓀴
10577𓀴I waited until midnight, but there was no sign of his return, so I
10578𓀴retired to my room. It was no uncommon thing for him to be away for
10579𓀴days and nights on end when he was hot upon a scent, so that his
10580𓀴lateness caused me no surprise. I do not know at what hour he came in,
10581𓀴but when I came down to breakfast in the morning there he was with a
10582𓀴cup of coffee in one hand and the paper in the other, as fresh and trim
10583𓀴as possible.
10584𓀴
10585𓀴“You will excuse my beginning without you, Watson,” said he, “but you
10586𓀴remember that our client has rather an early appointment this morning.”
10587𓀴
10588𓀴“Why, it is after nine now,” I answered. “I should not be surprised if
10589𓀴that were he. I thought I heard a ring.”
10590𓀴
10591𓀴It was, indeed, our friend the financier. I was shocked by the change
10592𓀴which had come over him, for his face which was naturally of a broad
10593𓀴and massive mould, was now pinched and fallen in, while his hair seemed
10594𓀴to me at least a shade whiter. He entered with a weariness and lethargy
10595𓀴which was even more painful than his violence of the morning before,
10596𓀴and he dropped heavily into the armchair which I pushed forward for
10597𓀴him.
10598𓀴
10599𓀴“I do not know what I have done to be so severely tried,” said he.
10600𓀴“Only two days ago I was a happy and prosperous man, without a care in
10601𓀴the world. Now I am left to a lonely and dishonoured age. One sorrow
10602𓀴comes close upon the heels of another. My niece, Mary, has deserted
10603𓀴me.”
10604𓀴
10605𓀴“Deserted you?”
10606𓀴
10607𓀴“Yes. Her bed this morning had not been slept in, her room was empty,
10608𓀴and a note for me lay upon the hall table. I had said to her last
10609𓀴night, in sorrow and not in anger, that if she had married my boy all
10610𓀴might have been well with him. Perhaps it was thoughtless of me to say
10611𓀴so. It is to that remark that she refers in this note:
10612𓀴
10613𓀴    “‘MY DEAREST UNCLE,—I feel that I have brought trouble upon you,
10614𓀴    and that if I had acted differently this terrible misfortune might
10615𓀴    never have occurred. I cannot, with this thought in my mind, ever
10616𓀴    again be happy under your roof, and I feel that I must leave you
10617𓀴    forever. Do not worry about my future, for that is provided for;
10618𓀴    and, above all, do not search for me, for it will be fruitless
10619𓀴    labour and an ill-service to me. In life or in death, I am ever
10620𓀴    your loving,
10621𓀴
10622𓀴
10623𓀴    “‘MARY.’
10624𓀴
10625𓀴
10626𓀴“What could she mean by that note, Mr. Holmes? Do you think it points
10627𓀴to suicide?”
10628𓀴
10629𓀴“No, no, nothing of the kind. It is perhaps the best possible solution.
10630𓀴I trust, Mr. Holder, that you are nearing the end of your troubles.”
10631𓀴
10632𓀴“Ha! You say so! You have heard something, Mr. Holmes; you have learned
10633𓀴something! Where are the gems?”
10634𓀴
10635𓀴“You would not think £ 1000 apiece an excessive sum for them?”
10636𓀴
10637𓀴“I would pay ten.”
10638𓀴
10639𓀴“That would be unnecessary. Three thousand will cover the matter. And
10640𓀴there is a little reward, I fancy. Have you your cheque-book? Here is a
10641𓀴pen. Better make it out for £ 4000.”
10642𓀴
10643𓀴With a dazed face the banker made out the required check. Holmes walked
10644𓀴over to his desk, took out a little triangular piece of gold with three
10645𓀴gems in it, and threw it down upon the table.
10646𓀴
10647𓀴With a shriek of joy our client clutched it up.
10648𓀴
10649𓀴“You have it!” he gasped. “I am saved! I am saved!”
10650𓀴
10651𓀴The reaction of joy was as passionate as his grief had been, and he
10652𓀴hugged his recovered gems to his bosom.
10653𓀴
10654𓀴“There is one other thing you owe, Mr. Holder,” said Sherlock Holmes
10655𓀴rather sternly.
10656𓀴
10657𓀴“Owe!” He caught up a pen. “Name the sum, and I will pay it.”
10658𓀴
10659𓀴“No, the debt is not to me. You owe a very humble apology to that noble
10660𓀴lad, your son, who has carried himself in this matter as I should be
10661𓀴proud to see my own son do, should I ever chance to have one.”
10662𓀴
10663𓀴“Then it was not Arthur who took them?”
10664𓀴
10665𓀴“I told you yesterday, and I repeat to-day, that it was not.”
10666𓀴
10667𓀴“You are sure of it! Then let us hurry to him at once to let him know
10668𓀴that the truth is known.”
10669𓀴
10670𓀴“He knows it already. When I had cleared it all up I had an interview
10671𓀴with him, and finding that he would not tell me the story, I told it to
10672𓀴him, on which he had to confess that I was right and to add the very
10673𓀴few details which were not yet quite clear to me. Your news of this
10674𓀴morning, however, may open his lips.”
10675𓀴
10676𓀴“For Heaven’s sake, tell me, then, what is this extraordinary mystery!”
10677𓀴
10678𓀴“I will do so, and I will show you the steps by which I reached it. And
10679𓀴let me say to you, first, that which it is hardest for me to say and
10680𓀴for you to hear: there has been an understanding between Sir George
10681𓀴Burnwell and your niece Mary. They have now fled together.”
10682𓀴
10683𓀴“My Mary? Impossible!”
10684𓀴
10685𓀴“It is unfortunately more than possible; it is certain. Neither you nor
10686𓀴your son knew the true character of this man when you admitted him into
10687𓀴your family circle. He is one of the most dangerous men in England—a
10688𓀴ruined gambler, an absolutely desperate villain, a man without heart or
10689𓀴conscience. Your niece knew nothing of such men. When he breathed his
10690𓀴vows to her, as he had done to a hundred before her, she flattered
10691𓀴herself that she alone had touched his heart. The devil knows best what
10692𓀴he said, but at least she became his tool and was in the habit of
10693𓀴seeing him nearly every evening.”
10694𓀴
10695𓀴“I cannot, and I will not, believe it!” cried the banker with an ashen
10696𓀴face.
10697𓀴
10698𓀴“I will tell you, then, what occurred in your house last night. Your
10699𓀴niece, when you had, as she thought, gone to your room, slipped down
10700𓀴and talked to her lover through the window which leads into the stable
10701𓀴lane. His footmarks had pressed right through the snow, so long had he
10702𓀴stood there. She told him of the coronet. His wicked lust for gold
10703𓀴kindled at the news, and he bent her to his will. I have no doubt that
10704𓀴she loved you, but there are women in whom the love of a lover
10705𓀴extinguishes all other loves, and I think that she must have been one.
10706𓀴She had hardly listened to his instructions when she saw you coming
10707𓀴downstairs, on which she closed the window rapidly and told you about
10708𓀴one of the servants’ escapade with her wooden-legged lover, which was
10709𓀴all perfectly true.
10710𓀴
10711𓀴“Your boy, Arthur, went to bed after his interview with you but he
10712𓀴slept badly on account of his uneasiness about his club debts. In the
10713𓀴middle of the night he heard a soft tread pass his door, so he rose
10714𓀴and, looking out, was surprised to see his cousin walking very
10715𓀴stealthily along the passage until she disappeared into your
10716𓀴dressing-room. Petrified with astonishment, the lad slipped on some
10717𓀴clothes and waited there in the dark to see what would come of this
10718𓀴strange affair. Presently she emerged from the room again, and in the
10719𓀴light of the passage-lamp your son saw that she carried the precious
10720𓀴coronet in her hands. She passed down the stairs, and he, thrilling
10721𓀴with horror, ran along and slipped behind the curtain near your door,
10722𓀴whence he could see what passed in the hall beneath. He saw her
10723𓀴stealthily open the window, hand out the coronet to someone in the
10724𓀴gloom, and then closing it once more hurry back to her room, passing
10725𓀴quite close to where he stood hid behind the curtain.
10726𓀴
10727𓀴“As long as she was on the scene he could not take any action without a
10728𓀴horrible exposure of the woman whom he loved. But the instant that she
10729𓀴was gone he realised how crushing a misfortune this would be for you,
10730𓀴and how all-important it was to set it right. He rushed down, just as
10731𓀴he was, in his bare feet, opened the window, sprang out into the snow,
10732𓀴and ran down the lane, where he could see a dark figure in the
10733𓀴moonlight. Sir George Burnwell tried to get away, but Arthur caught
10734𓀴him, and there was a struggle between them, your lad tugging at one
10735𓀴side of the coronet, and his opponent at the other. In the scuffle,
10736𓀴your son struck Sir George and cut him over the eye. Then something
10737𓀴suddenly snapped, and your son, finding that he had the coronet in his
10738𓀴hands, rushed back, closed the window, ascended to your room, and had
10739𓀴just observed that the coronet had been twisted in the struggle and was
10740𓀴endeavouring to straighten it when you appeared upon the scene.”
10741𓀴
10742𓀴“Is it possible?” gasped the banker.
10743𓀴
10744𓀴“You then roused his anger by calling him names at a moment when he
10745𓀴felt that he had deserved your warmest thanks. He could not explain the
10746𓀴true state of affairs without betraying one who certainly deserved
10747𓀴little enough consideration at his hands. He took the more chivalrous
10748𓀴view, however, and preserved her secret.”
10749𓀴
10750𓀴“And that was why she shrieked and fainted when she saw the coronet,”
10751𓀴cried Mr. Holder. “Oh, my God! what a blind fool I have been! And his
10752𓀴asking to be allowed to go out for five minutes! The dear fellow wanted
10753𓀴to see if the missing piece were at the scene of the struggle. How
10754𓀴cruelly I have misjudged him!”
10755𓀴
10756𓀴“When I arrived at the house,” continued Holmes, “I at once went very
10757𓀴carefully round it to observe if there were any traces in the snow
10758𓀴which might help me. I knew that none had fallen since the evening
10759𓀴before, and also that there had been a strong frost to preserve
10760𓀴impressions. I passed along the tradesmen’s path, but found it all
10761𓀴trampled down and indistinguishable. Just beyond it, however, at the
10762𓀴far side of the kitchen door, a woman had stood and talked with a man,
10763𓀴whose round impressions on one side showed that he had a wooden leg. I
10764𓀴could even tell that they had been disturbed, for the woman had run
10765𓀴back swiftly to the door, as was shown by the deep toe and light heel
10766𓀴marks, while Wooden-leg had waited a little, and then had gone away. I
10767𓀴thought at the time that this might be the maid and her sweetheart, of
10768𓀴whom you had already spoken to me, and inquiry showed it was so. I
10769𓀴passed round the garden without seeing anything more than random
10770𓀴tracks, which I took to be the police; but when I got into the stable
10771𓀴lane a very long and complex story was written in the snow in front of
10772𓀴me.
10773𓀴
10774𓀴“There was a double line of tracks of a booted man, and a second double
10775𓀴line which I saw with delight belonged to a man with naked feet. I was
10776𓀴at once convinced from what you had told me that the latter was your
10777𓀴son. The first had walked both ways, but the other had run swiftly, and
10778𓀴as his tread was marked in places over the depression of the boot, it
10779𓀴was obvious that he had passed after the other. I followed them up and
10780𓀴found they led to the hall window, where Boots had worn all the snow
10781𓀴away while waiting. Then I walked to the other end, which was a hundred
10782𓀴yards or more down the lane. I saw where Boots had faced round, where
10783𓀴the snow was cut up as though there had been a struggle, and, finally,
10784𓀴where a few drops of blood had fallen, to show me that I was not
10785𓀴mistaken. Boots had then run down the lane, and another little smudge
10786𓀴of blood showed that it was he who had been hurt. When he came to the
10787𓀴high road at the other end, I found that the pavement had been cleared,
10788𓀴so there was an end to that clue.
10789𓀴
10790𓀴“On entering the house, however, I examined, as you remember, the sill
10791𓀴and framework of the hall window with my lens, and I could at once see
10792𓀴that someone had passed out. I could distinguish the outline of an
10793𓀴instep where the wet foot had been placed in coming in. I was then
10794𓀴beginning to be able to form an opinion as to what had occurred. A man
10795𓀴had waited outside the window; someone had brought the gems; the deed
10796𓀴had been overseen by your son; he had pursued the thief; had struggled
10797𓀴with him; they had each tugged at the coronet, their united strength
10798𓀴causing injuries which neither alone could have effected. He had
10799𓀴returned with the prize, but had left a fragment in the grasp of his
10800𓀴opponent. So far I was clear. The question now was, who was the man, and
10801𓀴who was it brought him the coronet?
10802𓀴
10803𓀴“It is an old maxim of mine that when you have excluded the impossible,
10804𓀴whatever remains, however improbable, must be the truth. Now, I knew
10805𓀴that it was not you who had brought it down, so there only remained
10806𓀴your niece and the maids. But if it were the maids, why should your son
10807𓀴allow himself to be accused in their place? There could be no possible
10808𓀴reason. As he loved his cousin, however, there was an excellent
10809𓀴explanation why he should retain her secret—the more so as the secret
10810𓀴was a disgraceful one. When I remembered that you had seen her at that
10811𓀴window, and how she had fainted on seeing the coronet again, my
10812𓀴conjecture became a certainty.
10813𓀴
10814𓀴“And who could it be who was her confederate? A lover evidently, for
10815𓀴who else could outweigh the love and gratitude which she must feel to
10816𓀴you? I knew that you went out little, and that your circle of friends
10817𓀴was a very limited one. But among them was Sir George Burnwell. I had
10818𓀴heard of him before as being a man of evil reputation among women. It
10819𓀴must have been he who wore those boots and retained the missing gems.
10820𓀴Even though he knew that Arthur had discovered him, he might still
10821𓀴flatter himself that he was safe, for the lad could not say a word
10822𓀴without compromising his own family.
10823𓀴
10824𓀴“Well, your own good sense will suggest what measures I took next. I
10825𓀴went in the shape of a loafer to Sir George’s house, managed to pick up
10826𓀴an acquaintance with his valet, learned that his master had cut his
10827𓀴head the night before, and, finally, at the expense of six shillings,
10828𓀴made all sure by buying a pair of his cast-off shoes. With these I
10829𓀴journeyed down to Streatham and saw that they exactly fitted the
10830𓀴tracks.”
10831𓀴
10832𓀴“I saw an ill-dressed vagabond in the lane yesterday evening,” said Mr.
10833𓀴Holder.
10834𓀴
10835𓀴“Precisely. It was I. I found that I had my man, so I came home and
10836𓀴changed my clothes. It was a delicate part which I had to play then,
10837𓀴for I saw that a prosecution must be avoided to avert scandal, and I
10838𓀴knew that so astute a villain would see that our hands were tied in the
10839𓀴matter. I went and saw him. At first, of course, he denied everything.
10840𓀴But when I gave him every particular that had occurred, he tried to
10841𓀴bluster and took down a life-preserver from the wall. I knew my man,
10842𓀴however, and I clapped a pistol to his head before he could strike.
10843𓀴Then he became a little more reasonable. I told him that we would give
10844𓀴him a price for the stones he held—£ 1000 apiece. That brought out the
10845𓀴first signs of grief that he had shown. ‘Why, dash it all!’ said he,
10846𓀴‘I’ve let them go at six hundred for the three!’ I soon managed to get
10847𓀴the address of the receiver who had them, on promising him that there
10848𓀴would be no prosecution. Off I set to him, and after much chaffering I
10849𓀴got our stones at £ 1000 apiece. Then I looked in upon your son, told
10850𓀴him that all was right, and eventually got to my bed about two o’clock,
10851𓀴after what I may call a really hard day’s work.”
10852𓀴
10853𓀴“A day which has saved England from a great public scandal,” said the
10854𓀴banker, rising. “Sir, I cannot find words to thank you, but you shall
10855𓀴not find me ungrateful for what you have done. Your skill has indeed
10856𓀴exceeded all that I have heard of it. And now I must fly to my dear boy
10857𓀴to apologise to him for the wrong which I have done him. As to what you
10858𓀴tell me of poor Mary, it goes to my very heart. Not even your skill can
10859𓀴inform me where she is now.”
10860𓀴
10861𓀴“I think that we may safely say,” returned Holmes, “that she is
10862𓀴wherever Sir George Burnwell is. It is equally certain, too, that
10863𓀴whatever her sins are, they will soon receive a more than sufficient
10864𓀴punishment.”
10865𓀴
10866𓀴
10867𓀴
10868𓀴
10869𓀴XII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE COPPER BEECHES
10870𓀴
10871𓀴
10872𓀴“To the man who loves art for its own sake,” remarked Sherlock Holmes,
10873𓀴tossing aside the advertisement sheet of _The Daily Telegraph_, “it is
10874𓀴frequently in its least important and lowliest manifestations that the
10875𓀴keenest pleasure is to be derived. It is pleasant to me to observe,
10876𓀴Watson, that you have so far grasped this truth that in these little
10877𓀴records of our cases which you have been good enough to draw up, and, I
10878𓀴am bound to say, occasionally to embellish, you have given prominence
10879𓀴not so much to the many _causes célèbres_ and sensational trials in
10880𓀴which I have figured but rather to those incidents which may have been
10881𓀴trivial in themselves, but which have given room for those faculties of
10882𓀴deduction and of logical synthesis which I have made my special
10883𓀴province.”
10884𓀴
10885𓀴“And yet,” said I, smiling, “I cannot quite hold myself absolved from
10886𓀴the charge of sensationalism which has been urged against my records.”
10887𓀴
10888𓀴“You have erred, perhaps,” he observed, taking up a glowing cinder with
10889𓀴the tongs and lighting with it the long cherry-wood pipe which was wont
10890𓀴to replace his clay when he was in a disputatious rather than a
10891𓀴meditative mood—“you have erred perhaps in attempting to put colour and
10892𓀴life into each of your statements instead of confining yourself to the
10893𓀴task of placing upon record that severe reasoning from cause to effect
10894𓀴which is really the only notable feature about the thing.”
10895𓀴
10896𓀴“It seems to me that I have done you full justice in the matter,” I
10897𓀴remarked with some coldness, for I was repelled by the egotism which I
10898𓀴had more than once observed to be a strong factor in my friend’s
10899𓀴singular character.
10900𓀴
10901𓀴“No, it is not selfishness or conceit,” said he, answering, as was his
10902𓀴wont, my thoughts rather than my words. “If I claim full justice for my
10903𓀴art, it is because it is an impersonal thing—a thing beyond myself.
10904𓀴Crime is common. Logic is rare. Therefore it is upon the logic rather
10905𓀴than upon the crime that you should dwell. You have degraded what
10906𓀴should have been a course of lectures into a series of tales.”
10907𓀴
10908𓀴It was a cold morning of the early spring, and we sat after breakfast
10909𓀴on either side of a cheery fire in the old room at Baker Street. A
10910𓀴thick fog rolled down between the lines of dun-coloured houses, and the
10911𓀴opposing windows loomed like dark, shapeless blurs through the heavy
10912𓀴yellow wreaths. Our gas was lit and shone on the white cloth and
10913𓀴glimmer of china and metal, for the table had not been cleared yet.
10914𓀴Sherlock Holmes had been silent all the morning, dipping continuously
10915𓀴into the advertisement columns of a succession of papers until at last,
10916𓀴having apparently given up his search, he had emerged in no very sweet
10917𓀴temper to lecture me upon my literary shortcomings.
10918𓀴
10919𓀴“At the same time,” he remarked after a pause, during which he had sat
10920𓀴puffing at his long pipe and gazing down into the fire, “you can hardly
10921𓀴be open to a charge of sensationalism, for out of these cases which you
10922𓀴have been so kind as to interest yourself in, a fair proportion do not
10923𓀴treat of crime, in its legal sense, at all. The small matter in which I
10924𓀴endeavoured to help the King of Bohemia, the singular experience of
10925𓀴Miss Mary Sutherland, the problem connected with the man with the
10926𓀴twisted lip, and the incident of the noble bachelor, were all matters
10927𓀴which are outside the pale of the law. But in avoiding the sensational,
10928𓀴I fear that you may have bordered on the trivial.”
10929𓀴
10930𓀴“The end may have been so,” I answered, “but the methods I hold to have
10931𓀴been novel and of interest.”
10932𓀴
10933𓀴“Pshaw, my dear fellow, what do the public, the great unobservant
10934𓀴public, who could hardly tell a weaver by his tooth or a compositor by
10935𓀴his left thumb, care about the finer shades of analysis and deduction!
10936𓀴But, indeed, if you are trivial, I cannot blame you, for the days of
10937𓀴the great cases are past. Man, or at least criminal man, has lost all
10938𓀴enterprise and originality. As to my own little practice, it seems to
10939𓀴be degenerating into an agency for recovering lost lead pencils and
10940𓀴giving advice to young ladies from boarding-schools. I think that I
10941𓀴have touched bottom at last, however. This note I had this morning
10942𓀴marks my zero-point, I fancy. Read it!” He tossed a crumpled letter
10943𓀴across to me.
10944𓀴
10945𓀴It was dated from Montague Place upon the preceding evening, and ran
10946𓀴thus:
10947𓀴
10948𓀴    “DEAR MR. HOLMES,—I am very anxious to consult you as to whether I
10949𓀴    should or should not accept a situation which has been offered to
10950𓀴    me as governess. I shall call at half-past ten to-morrow if I do
10951𓀴    not inconvenience you. Yours faithfully,
10952𓀴
10953𓀴
10954𓀴    “VIOLET HUNTER.”
10955𓀴
10956𓀴
10957𓀴“Do you know the young lady?” I asked.
10958𓀴
10959𓀴“Not I.”
10960𓀴
10961𓀴“It is half-past ten now.”
10962𓀴
10963𓀴“Yes, and I have no doubt that is her ring.”
10964𓀴
10965𓀴“It may turn out to be of more interest than you think. You remember
10966𓀴that the affair of the blue carbuncle, which appeared to be a mere whim
10967𓀴at first, developed into a serious investigation. It may be so in this
10968𓀴case, also.”
10969𓀴
10970𓀴“Well, let us hope so. But our doubts will very soon be solved, for
10971𓀴here, unless I am much mistaken, is the person in question.”
10972𓀴
10973𓀴As he spoke the door opened and a young lady entered the room. She was
10974𓀴plainly but neatly dressed, with a bright, quick face, freckled like a
10975𓀴plover’s egg, and with the brisk manner of a woman who has had her own
10976𓀴way to make in the world.
10977𓀴
10978𓀴“You will excuse my troubling you, I am sure,” said she, as my
10979𓀴companion rose to greet her, “but I have had a very strange experience,
10980𓀴and as I have no parents or relations of any sort from whom I could ask
10981𓀴advice, I thought that perhaps you would be kind enough to tell me what
10982𓀴I should do.”
10983𓀴
10984𓀴“Pray take a seat, Miss Hunter. I shall be happy to do anything that I
10985𓀴can to serve you.”
10986𓀴
10987𓀴I could see that Holmes was favourably impressed by the manner and
10988𓀴speech of his new client. He looked her over in his searching fashion,
10989𓀴and then composed himself, with his lids drooping and his finger-tips
10990𓀴together, to listen to her story.
10991𓀴
10992𓀴“I have been a governess for five years,” said she, “in the family of
10993𓀴Colonel Spence Munro, but two months ago the colonel received an
10994𓀴appointment at Halifax, in Nova Scotia, and took his children over to
10995𓀴America with him, so that I found myself without a situation. I
10996𓀴advertised, and I answered advertisements, but without success. At last
10997𓀴the little money which I had saved began to run short, and I was at my
10998𓀴wit’s end as to what I should do.
10999𓀴
11000𓀴“There is a well-known agency for governesses in the West End called
11001𓀴Westaway’s, and there I used to call about once a week in order to see
11002𓀴whether anything had turned up which might suit me. Westaway was the
11003𓀴name of the founder of the business, but it is really managed by Miss
11004𓀴Stoper. She sits in her own little office, and the ladies who are
11005𓀴seeking employment wait in an anteroom, and are then shown in one by
11006𓀴one, when she consults her ledgers and sees whether she has anything
11007𓀴which would suit them.
11008𓀴
11009𓀴“Well, when I called last week I was shown into the little office as
11010𓀴usual, but I found that Miss Stoper was not alone. A prodigiously stout
11011𓀴man with a very smiling face and a great heavy chin which rolled down
11012𓀴in fold upon fold over his throat sat at her elbow with a pair of
11013𓀴glasses on his nose, looking very earnestly at the ladies who entered.
11014𓀴As I came in he gave quite a jump in his chair and turned quickly to
11015𓀴Miss Stoper.
11016𓀴
11017𓀴“‘That will do,’ said he; ‘I could not ask for anything better.
11018𓀴Capital! capital!’ He seemed quite enthusiastic and rubbed his hands
11019𓀴together in the most genial fashion. He was such a comfortable-looking
11020𓀴man that it was quite a pleasure to look at him.
11021𓀴
11022𓀴“‘You are looking for a situation, miss?’ he asked.
11023𓀴
11024𓀴“‘Yes, sir.’
11025𓀴
11026𓀴“‘As governess?’
11027𓀴
11028𓀴“‘Yes, sir.’
11029𓀴
11030𓀴“‘And what salary do you ask?’
11031𓀴
11032𓀴“‘I had £ 4 a month in my last place with Colonel Spence Munro.’
11033𓀴
11034𓀴“‘Oh, tut, tut! sweating—rank sweating!’ he cried, throwing his fat
11035𓀴hands out into the air like a man who is in a boiling passion. ‘How
11036𓀴could anyone offer so pitiful a sum to a lady with such attractions and
11037𓀴accomplishments?’
11038𓀴
11039𓀴“‘My accomplishments, sir, may be less than you imagine,’ said I. ‘A
11040𓀴little French, a little German, music, and drawing—’
11041𓀴
11042𓀴“‘Tut, tut!’ he cried. ‘This is all quite beside the question. The
11043𓀴point is, have you or have you not the bearing and deportment of a
11044𓀴lady? There it is in a nutshell. If you have not, you are not fitted
11045𓀴for the rearing of a child who may some day play a considerable part in
11046𓀴the history of the country. But if you have, why, then, how could any
11047𓀴gentleman ask you to condescend to accept anything under the three
11048𓀴figures? Your salary with me, madam, would commence at £ 100 a year.’
11049𓀴
11050𓀴“You may imagine, Mr. Holmes, that to me, destitute as I was, such an
11051𓀴offer seemed almost too good to be true. The gentleman, however, seeing
11052𓀴perhaps the look of incredulity upon my face, opened a pocket-book and
11053𓀴took out a note.
11054𓀴
11055𓀴“‘It is also my custom,’ said he, smiling in the most pleasant fashion
11056𓀴until his eyes were just two little shining slits amid the white
11057𓀴creases of his face, ‘to advance to my young ladies half their salary
11058𓀴beforehand, so that they may meet any little expenses of their journey
11059𓀴and their wardrobe.’
11060𓀴
11061𓀴“It seemed to me that I had never met so fascinating and so thoughtful
11062𓀴a man. As I was already in debt to my tradesmen, the advance was a
11063𓀴great convenience, and yet there was something unnatural about the
11064𓀴whole transaction which made me wish to know a little more before I
11065𓀴quite committed myself.
11066𓀴
11067𓀴“‘May I ask where you live, sir?’ said I.
11068𓀴
11069𓀴“‘Hampshire. Charming rural place. The Copper Beeches, five miles on
11070𓀴the far side of Winchester. It is the most lovely country, my dear
11071𓀴young lady, and the dearest old country-house.’
11072𓀴
11073𓀴“‘And my duties, sir? I should be glad to know what they would be.’
11074𓀴
11075𓀴“‘One child—one dear little romper just six years old. Oh, if you could
11076𓀴see him killing cockroaches with a slipper! Smack! smack! smack! Three
11077𓀴gone before you could wink!’ He leaned back in his chair and laughed
11078𓀴his eyes into his head again.
11079𓀴
11080𓀴“I was a little startled at the nature of the child’s amusement, but
11081𓀴the father’s laughter made me think that perhaps he was joking.
11082𓀴
11083𓀴“‘My sole duties, then,’ I asked, ‘are to take charge of a single
11084𓀴child?’
11085𓀴
11086𓀴“‘No, no, not the sole, not the sole, my dear young lady,’ he cried.
11087𓀴‘Your duty would be, as I am sure your good sense would suggest, to
11088𓀴obey any little commands my wife might give, provided always that they
11089𓀴were such commands as a lady might with propriety obey. You see no
11090𓀴difficulty, heh?’
11091𓀴
11092𓀴“‘I should be happy to make myself useful.’
11093𓀴
11094𓀴“‘Quite so. In dress now, for example. We are faddy people, you
11095𓀴know—faddy but kind-hearted. If you were asked to wear any dress which
11096𓀴we might give you, you would not object to our little whim. Heh?’
11097𓀴
11098𓀴“‘No,’ said I, considerably astonished at his words.
11099𓀴
11100𓀴“‘Or to sit here, or sit there, that would not be offensive to you?’
11101𓀴
11102𓀴“‘Oh, no.’
11103𓀴
11104𓀴“‘Or to cut your hair quite short before you come to us?’
11105𓀴
11106𓀴“I could hardly believe my ears. As you may observe, Mr. Holmes, my
11107𓀴hair is somewhat luxuriant, and of a rather peculiar tint of chestnut.
11108𓀴It has been considered artistic. I could not dream of sacrificing it in
11109𓀴this offhand fashion.
11110𓀴
11111𓀴“‘I am afraid that that is quite impossible,’ said I. He had been
11112𓀴watching me eagerly out of his small eyes, and I could see a shadow
11113𓀴pass over his face as I spoke.
11114𓀴
11115𓀴“‘I am afraid that it is quite essential,’ said he. ‘It is a little
11116𓀴fancy of my wife’s, and ladies’ fancies, you know, madam, ladies’
11117𓀴fancies must be consulted. And so you won’t cut your hair?’
11118𓀴
11119𓀴“‘No, sir, I really could not,’ I answered firmly.
11120𓀴
11121𓀴“‘Ah, very well; then that quite settles the matter. It is a pity,
11122𓀴because in other respects you would really have done very nicely. In
11123𓀴that case, Miss Stoper, I had best inspect a few more of your young
11124𓀴ladies.’
11125𓀴
11126𓀴“The manageress had sat all this while busy with her papers without a
11127𓀴word to either of us, but she glanced at me now with so much annoyance
11128𓀴upon her face that I could not help suspecting that she had lost a
11129𓀴handsome commission through my refusal.
11130𓀴
11131𓀴“‘Do you desire your name to be kept upon the books?’ she asked.
11132𓀴
11133𓀴“‘If you please, Miss Stoper.’
11134𓀴
11135𓀴“‘Well, really, it seems rather useless, since you refuse the most
11136𓀴excellent offers in this fashion,’ said she sharply. ‘You can hardly
11137𓀴expect us to exert ourselves to find another such opening for you.
11138𓀴Good-day to you, Miss Hunter.’ She struck a gong upon the table, and I
11139𓀴was shown out by the page.
11140𓀴
11141𓀴“Well, Mr. Holmes, when I got back to my lodgings and found little
11142𓀴enough in the cupboard, and two or three bills upon the table, I began
11143𓀴to ask myself whether I had not done a very foolish thing. After all,
11144𓀴if these people had strange fads and expected obedience on the most
11145𓀴extraordinary matters, they were at least ready to pay for their
11146𓀴eccentricity. Very few governesses in England are getting £ 100 a year.
11147𓀴Besides, what use was my hair to me? Many people are improved by
11148𓀴wearing it short and perhaps I should be among the number. Next day I
11149𓀴was inclined to think that I had made a mistake, and by the day after I
11150𓀴was sure of it. I had almost overcome my pride so far as to go back to
11151𓀴the agency and inquire whether the place was still open when I received
11152𓀴this letter from the gentleman himself. I have it here and I will read
11153𓀴it to you:
11154𓀴
11155𓀴“‘The Copper Beeches, near Winchester.
11156𓀴
11157𓀴    “‘DEAR MISS HUNTER,—Miss Stoper has very kindly given me your
11158𓀴    address, and I write from here to ask you whether you have
11159𓀴    reconsidered your decision. My wife is very anxious that you should
11160𓀴    come, for she has been much attracted by my description of you. We
11161𓀴    are willing to give £ 30 a quarter, or £ 120 a year, so as to
11162𓀴    recompense you for any little inconvenience which our fads may
11163𓀴    cause you. They are not very exacting, after all. My wife is fond
11164𓀴    of a particular shade of electric blue and would like you to wear
11165𓀴    such a dress indoors in the morning. You need not, however, go to
11166𓀴    the expense of purchasing one, as we have one belonging to my dear
11167𓀴    daughter Alice (now in Philadelphia), which would, I should think,
11168𓀴    fit you very well. Then, as to sitting here or there, or amusing
11169𓀴    yourself in any manner indicated, that need cause you no
11170𓀴    inconvenience. As regards your hair, it is no doubt a pity,
11171𓀴    especially as I could not help remarking its beauty during our
11172𓀴    short interview, but I am afraid that I must remain firm upon this
11173𓀴    point, and I only hope that the increased salary may recompense you
11174𓀴    for the loss. Your duties, as far as the child is concerned, are
11175𓀴    very light. Now do try to come, and I shall meet you with the
11176𓀴    dog-cart at Winchester. Let me know your train. Yours faithfully,
11177𓀴
11178𓀴
11179𓀴    “‘JEPHRO RUCASTLE.’
11180𓀴
11181𓀴
11182𓀴“That is the letter which I have just received, Mr. Holmes, and my mind
11183𓀴is made up that I will accept it. I thought, however, that before
11184𓀴taking the final step I should like to submit the whole matter to your
11185𓀴consideration.”
11186𓀴
11187𓀴“Well, Miss Hunter, if your mind is made up, that settles the
11188𓀴question,” said Holmes, smiling.
11189𓀴
11190𓀴“But you would not advise me to refuse?”
11191𓀴
11192𓀴“I confess that it is not the situation which I should like to see a
11193𓀴sister of mine apply for.”
11194𓀴
11195𓀴“What is the meaning of it all, Mr. Holmes?”
11196𓀴
11197𓀴“Ah, I have no data. I cannot tell. Perhaps you have yourself formed
11198𓀴some opinion?”
11199𓀴
11200𓀴“Well, there seems to me to be only one possible solution. Mr. Rucastle
11201𓀴seemed to be a very kind, good-natured man. Is it not possible that his
11202𓀴wife is a lunatic, that he desires to keep the matter quiet for fear
11203𓀴she should be taken to an asylum, and that he humours her fancies in
11204𓀴every way in order to prevent an outbreak?”
11205𓀴
11206𓀴“That is a possible solution—in fact, as matters stand, it is the most
11207𓀴probable one. But in any case it does not seem to be a nice household
11208𓀴for a young lady.”
11209𓀴
11210𓀴“But the money, Mr. Holmes, the money!”
11211𓀴
11212𓀴“Well, yes, of course the pay is good—too good. That is what makes me
11213𓀴uneasy. Why should they give you £ 120 a year, when they could have
11214𓀴their pick for £ 40? There must be some strong reason behind.”
11215𓀴
11216𓀴“I thought that if I told you the circumstances you would understand
11217𓀴afterwards if I wanted your help. I should feel so much stronger if I
11218𓀴felt that you were at the back of me.”
11219𓀴
11220𓀴“Oh, you may carry that feeling away with you. I assure you that your
11221𓀴little problem promises to be the most interesting which has come my
11222𓀴way for some months. There is something distinctly novel about some of
11223𓀴the features. If you should find yourself in doubt or in danger—”
11224𓀴
11225𓀴“Danger! What danger do you foresee?”
11226𓀴
11227𓀴Holmes shook his head gravely. “It would cease to be a danger if we
11228𓀴could define it,” said he. “But at any time, day or night, a telegram
11229𓀴would bring me down to your help.”
11230𓀴
11231𓀴“That is enough.” She rose briskly from her chair with the anxiety all
11232𓀴swept from her face. “I shall go down to Hampshire quite easy in my
11233𓀴mind now. I shall write to Mr. Rucastle at once, sacrifice my poor hair
11234𓀴to-night, and start for Winchester to-morrow.” With a few grateful
11235𓀴words to Holmes she bade us both good-night and bustled off upon her
11236𓀴way.
11237𓀴
11238𓀴“At least,” said I as we heard her quick, firm steps descending the
11239𓀴stairs, “she seems to be a young lady who is very well able to take
11240𓀴care of herself.”
11241𓀴
11242𓀴“And she would need to be,” said Holmes gravely. “I am much mistaken if
11243𓀴we do not hear from her before many days are past.”
11244𓀴
11245𓀴It was not very long before my friend’s prediction was fulfilled. A
11246𓀴fortnight went by, during which I frequently found my thoughts turning
11247𓀴in her direction and wondering what strange side-alley of human
11248𓀴experience this lonely woman had strayed into. The unusual salary, the
11249𓀴curious conditions, the light duties, all pointed to something
11250𓀴abnormal, though whether a fad or a plot, or whether the man were a
11251𓀴philanthropist or a villain, it was quite beyond my powers to
11252𓀴determine. As to Holmes, I observed that he sat frequently for half an
11253𓀴hour on end, with knitted brows and an abstracted air, but he swept the
11254𓀴matter away with a wave of his hand when I mentioned it. “Data! data!
11255𓀴data!” he cried impatiently. “I can’t make bricks without clay.” And
11256𓀴yet he would always wind up by muttering that no sister of his should
11257𓀴ever have accepted such a situation.
11258𓀴
11259𓀴The telegram which we eventually received came late one night just as I
11260𓀴was thinking of turning in and Holmes was settling down to one of those
11261𓀴all-night chemical researches which he frequently indulged in, when I
11262𓀴would leave him stooping over a retort and a test-tube at night and
11263𓀴find him in the same position when I came down to breakfast in the
11264𓀴morning. He opened the yellow envelope, and then, glancing at the
11265𓀴message, threw it across to me.
11266𓀴
11267𓀴“Just look up the trains in Bradshaw,” said he, and turned back to his
11268𓀴chemical studies.
11269𓀴
11270𓀴The summons was a brief and urgent one.
11271𓀴
11272𓀴“Please be at the Black Swan Hotel at Winchester at midday to-morrow,”
11273𓀴it said. “Do come! I am at my wit’s end.
11274𓀴
11275𓀴
11276𓀴“HUNTER.”
11277𓀴
11278𓀴
11279𓀴“Will you come with me?” asked Holmes, glancing up.
11280𓀴
11281𓀴“I should wish to.”
11282𓀴
11283𓀴“Just look it up, then.”
11284𓀴
11285𓀴“There is a train at half-past nine,” said I, glancing over my
11286𓀴Bradshaw. “It is due at Winchester at 11:30.”
11287𓀴
11288𓀴“That will do very nicely. Then perhaps I had better postpone my
11289𓀴analysis of the acetones, as we may need to be at our best in the
11290𓀴morning.”
11291𓀴
11292𓀴By eleven o’clock the next day we were well upon our way to the old
11293𓀴English capital. Holmes had been buried in the morning papers all the
11294𓀴way down, but after we had passed the Hampshire border he threw them
11295𓀴down and began to admire the scenery. It was an ideal spring day, a
11296𓀴light blue sky, flecked with little fleecy white clouds drifting across
11297𓀴from west to east. The sun was shining very brightly, and yet there was
11298𓀴an exhilarating nip in the air, which set an edge to a man’s energy.
11299𓀴All over the countryside, away to the rolling hills around Aldershot,
11300𓀴the little red and grey roofs of the farm-steadings peeped out from
11301𓀴amid the light green of the new foliage.
11302𓀴
11303𓀴“Are they not fresh and beautiful?” I cried with all the enthusiasm of
11304𓀴a man fresh from the fogs of Baker Street.
11305𓀴
11306𓀴But Holmes shook his head gravely.
11307𓀴
11308𓀴“Do you know, Watson,” said he, “that it is one of the curses of a mind
11309𓀴with a turn like mine that I must look at everything with reference to
11310𓀴my own special subject. You look at these scattered houses, and you are
11311𓀴impressed by their beauty. I look at them, and the only thought which
11312𓀴comes to me is a feeling of their isolation and of the impunity with
11313𓀴which crime may be committed there.”
11314𓀴
11315𓀴“Good heavens!” I cried. “Who would associate crime with these dear old
11316𓀴homesteads?”
11317𓀴
11318𓀴“They always fill me with a certain horror. It is my belief, Watson,
11319𓀴founded upon my experience, that the lowest and vilest alleys in London
11320𓀴do not present a more dreadful record of sin than does the smiling and
11321𓀴beautiful countryside.”
11322𓀴
11323𓀴“You horrify me!”
11324𓀴
11325𓀴“But the reason is very obvious. The pressure of public opinion can do
11326𓀴in the town what the law cannot accomplish. There is no lane so vile
11327𓀴that the scream of a tortured child, or the thud of a drunkard’s blow,
11328𓀴does not beget sympathy and indignation among the neighbours, and then
11329𓀴the whole machinery of justice is ever so close that a word of
11330𓀴complaint can set it going, and there is but a step between the crime
11331𓀴and the dock. But look at these lonely houses, each in its own fields,
11332𓀴filled for the most part with poor ignorant folk who know little of the
11333𓀴law. Think of the deeds of hellish cruelty, the hidden wickedness which
11334𓀴may go on, year in, year out, in such places, and none the wiser. Had
11335𓀴this lady who appeals to us for help gone to live in Winchester, I
11336𓀴should never have had a fear for her. It is the five miles of country
11337𓀴which makes the danger. Still, it is clear that she is not personally
11338𓀴threatened.”
11339𓀴
11340𓀴“No. If she can come to Winchester to meet us she can get away.”
11341𓀴
11342𓀴“Quite so. She has her freedom.”
11343𓀴
11344𓀴“What _can_ be the matter, then? Can you suggest no explanation?”
11345𓀴
11346𓀴“I have devised seven separate explanations, each of which would cover
11347𓀴the facts as far as we know them. But which of these is correct can
11348𓀴only be determined by the fresh information which we shall no doubt
11349𓀴find waiting for us. Well, there is the tower of the cathedral, and we
11350𓀴shall soon learn all that Miss Hunter has to tell.”
11351𓀴
11352𓀴The Black Swan is an inn of repute in the High Street, at no distance
11353𓀴from the station, and there we found the young lady waiting for us. She
11354𓀴had engaged a sitting-room, and our lunch awaited us upon the table.
11355𓀴
11356𓀴“I am so delighted that you have come,” she said earnestly. “It is so
11357𓀴very kind of you both; but indeed I do not know what I should do. Your
11358𓀴advice will be altogether invaluable to me.”
11359𓀴
11360𓀴“Pray tell us what has happened to you.”
11361𓀴
11362𓀴“I will do so, and I must be quick, for I have promised Mr. Rucastle to
11363𓀴be back before three. I got his leave to come into town this morning,
11364𓀴though he little knew for what purpose.”
11365𓀴
11366𓀴“Let us have everything in its due order.” Holmes thrust his long thin
11367𓀴legs out towards the fire and composed himself to listen.
11368𓀴
11369𓀴“In the first place, I may say that I have met, on the whole, with no
11370𓀴actual ill-treatment from Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle. It is only fair to
11371𓀴them to say that. But I cannot understand them, and I am not easy in my
11372𓀴mind about them.”
11373𓀴
11374𓀴“What can you not understand?”
11375𓀴
11376𓀴“Their reasons for their conduct. But you shall have it all just as it
11377𓀴occurred. When I came down, Mr. Rucastle met me here and drove me in
11378𓀴his dog-cart to the Copper Beeches. It is, as he said, beautifully
11379𓀴situated, but it is not beautiful in itself, for it is a large square
11380𓀴block of a house, whitewashed, but all stained and streaked with damp
11381𓀴and bad weather. There are grounds round it, woods on three sides, and
11382𓀴on the fourth a field which slopes down to the Southampton high road,
11383𓀴which curves past about a hundred yards from the front door. This
11384𓀴ground in front belongs to the house, but the woods all round are part
11385𓀴of Lord Southerton’s preserves. A clump of copper beeches immediately
11386𓀴in front of the hall door has given its name to the place.
11387𓀴
11388𓀴“I was driven over by my employer, who was as amiable as ever, and was
11389𓀴introduced by him that evening to his wife and the child. There was no
11390𓀴truth, Mr. Holmes, in the conjecture which seemed to us to be probable
11391𓀴in your rooms at Baker Street. Mrs. Rucastle is not mad. I found her to
11392𓀴be a silent, pale-faced woman, much younger than her husband, not more
11393𓀴than thirty, I should think, while he can hardly be less than
11394𓀴forty-five. From their conversation I have gathered that they have been
11395𓀴married about seven years, that he was a widower, and that his only
11396𓀴child by the first wife was the daughter who has gone to Philadelphia.
11397𓀴Mr. Rucastle told me in private that the reason why she had left them
11398𓀴was that she had an unreasoning aversion to her stepmother. As the
11399𓀴daughter could not have been less than twenty, I can quite imagine that
11400𓀴her position must have been uncomfortable with her father’s young wife.
11401𓀴
11402𓀴“Mrs. Rucastle seemed to me to be colourless in mind as well as in
11403𓀴feature. She impressed me neither favourably nor the reverse. She was a
11404𓀴nonentity. It was easy to see that she was passionately devoted both to
11405𓀴her husband and to her little son. Her light grey eyes wandered
11406𓀴continually from one to the other, noting every little want and
11407𓀴forestalling it if possible. He was kind to her also in his bluff,
11408𓀴boisterous fashion, and on the whole they seemed to be a happy couple.
11409𓀴And yet she had some secret sorrow, this woman. She would often be lost
11410𓀴in deep thought, with the saddest look upon her face. More than once I
11411𓀴have surprised her in tears. I have thought sometimes that it was the
11412𓀴disposition of her child which weighed upon her mind, for I have never
11413𓀴met so utterly spoiled and so ill-natured a little creature. He is
11414𓀴small for his age, with a head which is quite disproportionately large.
11415𓀴His whole life appears to be spent in an alternation between savage
11416𓀴fits of passion and gloomy intervals of sulking. Giving pain to any
11417𓀴creature weaker than himself seems to be his one idea of amusement, and
11418𓀴he shows quite remarkable talent in planning the capture of mice,
11419𓀴little birds, and insects. But I would rather not talk about the
11420𓀴creature, Mr. Holmes, and, indeed, he has little to do with my story.”
11421𓀴
11422𓀴“I am glad of all details,” remarked my friend, “whether they seem to
11423𓀴you to be relevant or not.”
11424𓀴
11425𓀴“I shall try not to miss anything of importance. The one unpleasant
11426𓀴thing about the house, which struck me at once, was the appearance and
11427𓀴conduct of the servants. There are only two, a man and his wife.
11428𓀴Toller, for that is his name, is a rough, uncouth man, with grizzled
11429𓀴hair and whiskers, and a perpetual smell of drink. Twice since I have
11430𓀴been with them he has been quite drunk, and yet Mr. Rucastle seemed to
11431𓀴take no notice of it. His wife is a very tall and strong woman with a
11432𓀴sour face, as silent as Mrs. Rucastle and much less amiable. They are a
11433𓀴most unpleasant couple, but fortunately I spend most of my time in the
11434𓀴nursery and my own room, which are next to each other in one corner of
11435𓀴the building.
11436𓀴
11437𓀴“For two days after my arrival at the Copper Beeches my life was very
11438𓀴quiet; on the third, Mrs. Rucastle came down just after breakfast and
11439𓀴whispered something to her husband.
11440𓀴
11441𓀴“‘Oh, yes,’ said he, turning to me, ‘we are very much obliged to you,
11442𓀴Miss Hunter, for falling in with our whims so far as to cut your hair.
11443𓀴I assure you that it has not detracted in the tiniest iota from your
11444𓀴appearance. We shall now see how the electric-blue dress will become
11445𓀴you. You will find it laid out upon the bed in your room, and if you
11446𓀴would be so good as to put it on we should both be extremely obliged.’
11447𓀴
11448𓀴“The dress which I found waiting for me was of a peculiar shade of
11449𓀴blue. It was of excellent material, a sort of beige, but it bore
11450𓀴unmistakable signs of having been worn before. It could not have been a
11451𓀴better fit if I had been measured for it. Both Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle
11452𓀴expressed a delight at the look of it, which seemed quite exaggerated
11453𓀴in its vehemence. They were waiting for me in the drawing-room, which
11454𓀴is a very large room, stretching along the entire front of the house,
11455𓀴with three long windows reaching down to the floor. A chair had been
11456𓀴placed close to the central window, with its back turned towards it. In
11457𓀴this I was asked to sit, and then Mr. Rucastle, walking up and down on
11458𓀴the other side of the room, began to tell me a series of the funniest
11459𓀴stories that I have ever listened to. You cannot imagine how comical he
11460𓀴was, and I laughed until I was quite weary. Mrs. Rucastle, however, who
11461𓀴has evidently no sense of humour, never so much as smiled, but sat with
11462𓀴her hands in her lap, and a sad, anxious look upon her face. After an
11463𓀴hour or so, Mr. Rucastle suddenly remarked that it was time to commence
11464𓀴the duties of the day, and that I might change my dress and go to
11465𓀴little Edward in the nursery.
11466𓀴
11467𓀴“Two days later this same performance was gone through under exactly
11468𓀴similar circumstances. Again I changed my dress, again I sat in the
11469𓀴window, and again I laughed very heartily at the funny stories of which
11470𓀴my employer had an immense _répertoire_, and which he told inimitably.
11471𓀴Then he handed me a yellow-backed novel, and moving my chair a little
11472𓀴sideways, that my own shadow might not fall upon the page, he begged me
11473𓀴to read aloud to him. I read for about ten minutes, beginning in the
11474𓀴heart of a chapter, and then suddenly, in the middle of a sentence, he
11475𓀴ordered me to cease and to change my dress.
11476𓀴
11477𓀴“You can easily imagine, Mr. Holmes, how curious I became as to what
11478𓀴the meaning of this extraordinary performance could possibly be. They
11479𓀴were always very careful, I observed, to turn my face away from the
11480𓀴window, so that I became consumed with the desire to see what was going
11481𓀴on behind my back. At first it seemed to be impossible, but I soon
11482𓀴devised a means. My hand-mirror had been broken, so a happy thought
11483𓀴seized me, and I concealed a piece of the glass in my handkerchief. On
11484𓀴the next occasion, in the midst of my laughter, I put my handkerchief
11485𓀴up to my eyes, and was able with a little management to see all that
11486𓀴there was behind me. I confess that I was disappointed. There was
11487𓀴nothing. At least that was my first impression. At the second glance,
11488𓀴however, I perceived that there was a man standing in the Southampton
11489𓀴Road, a small bearded man in a grey suit, who seemed to be looking in
11490𓀴my direction. The road is an important highway, and there are usually
11491𓀴people there. This man, however, was leaning against the railings which
11492𓀴bordered our field and was looking earnestly up. I lowered my
11493𓀴handkerchief and glanced at Mrs. Rucastle to find her eyes fixed upon
11494𓀴me with a most searching gaze. She said nothing, but I am convinced
11495𓀴that she had divined that I had a mirror in my hand and had seen what
11496𓀴was behind me. She rose at once.
11497𓀴
11498𓀴“‘Jephro,’ said she, ‘there is an impertinent fellow upon the road
11499𓀴there who stares up at Miss Hunter.’
11500𓀴
11501𓀴“‘No friend of yours, Miss Hunter?’ he asked.
11502𓀴
11503𓀴“‘No, I know no one in these parts.’
11504𓀴
11505𓀴“‘Dear me! How very impertinent! Kindly turn round and motion to him to
11506𓀴go away.’
11507𓀴
11508𓀴“‘Surely it would be better to take no notice.’
11509𓀴
11510𓀴“‘No, no, we should have him loitering here always. Kindly turn round
11511𓀴and wave him away like that.’
11512𓀴
11513𓀴“I did as I was told, and at the same instant Mrs. Rucastle drew down
11514𓀴the blind. That was a week ago, and from that time I have not sat again
11515𓀴in the window, nor have I worn the blue dress, nor seen the man in the
11516𓀴road.”
11517𓀴
11518𓀴“Pray continue,” said Holmes. “Your narrative promises to be a most
11519𓀴interesting one.”
11520𓀴
11521𓀴“You will find it rather disconnected, I fear, and there may prove to
11522𓀴be little relation between the different incidents of which I speak. On
11523𓀴the very first day that I was at the Copper Beeches, Mr. Rucastle took
11524𓀴me to a small outhouse which stands near the kitchen door. As we
11525𓀴approached it I heard the sharp rattling of a chain, and the sound as
11526𓀴of a large animal moving about.
11527𓀴
11528𓀴“‘Look in here!’ said Mr. Rucastle, showing me a slit between two
11529𓀴planks. ‘Is he not a beauty?’
11530𓀴
11531𓀴“I looked through and was conscious of two glowing eyes, and of a vague
11532𓀴figure huddled up in the darkness.
11533𓀴
11534𓀴“‘Don’t be frightened,’ said my employer, laughing at the start which I
11535𓀴had given. ‘It’s only Carlo, my mastiff. I call him mine, but really
11536𓀴old Toller, my groom, is the only man who can do anything with him. We
11537𓀴feed him once a day, and not too much then, so that he is always as
11538𓀴keen as mustard. Toller lets him loose every night, and God help the
11539𓀴trespasser whom he lays his fangs upon. For goodness’ sake don’t you
11540𓀴ever on any pretext set your foot over the threshold at night, for it’s
11541𓀴as much as your life is worth.’
11542𓀴
11543𓀴“The warning was no idle one, for two nights later I happened to look
11544𓀴out of my bedroom window about two o’clock in the morning. It was a
11545𓀴beautiful moonlight night, and the lawn in front of the house was
11546𓀴silvered over and almost as bright as day. I was standing, rapt in the
11547𓀴peaceful beauty of the scene, when I was aware that something was
11548𓀴moving under the shadow of the copper beeches. As it emerged into the
11549𓀴moonshine I saw what it was. It was a giant dog, as large as a calf,
11550𓀴tawny tinted, with hanging jowl, black muzzle, and huge projecting
11551𓀴bones. It walked slowly across the lawn and vanished into the shadow
11552𓀴upon the other side. That dreadful sentinel sent a chill to my heart
11553𓀴which I do not think that any burglar could have done.
11554𓀴
11555𓀴“And now I have a very strange experience to tell you. I had, as you
11556𓀴know, cut off my hair in London, and I had placed it in a great coil at
11557𓀴the bottom of my trunk. One evening, after the child was in bed, I
11558𓀴began to amuse myself by examining the furniture of my room and by
11559𓀴rearranging my own little things. There was an old chest of drawers in
11560𓀴the room, the two upper ones empty and open, the lower one locked. I
11561𓀴had filled the first two with my linen, and as I had still much to pack
11562𓀴away I was naturally annoyed at not having the use of the third drawer.
11563𓀴It struck me that it might have been fastened by a mere oversight, so I
11564𓀴took out my bunch of keys and tried to open it. The very first key
11565𓀴fitted to perfection, and I drew the drawer open. There was only one
11566𓀴thing in it, but I am sure that you would never guess what it was. It
11567𓀴was my coil of hair.
11568𓀴
11569𓀴“I took it up and examined it. It was of the same peculiar tint, and
11570𓀴the same thickness. But then the impossibility of the thing obtruded
11571𓀴itself upon me. How could my hair have been locked in the drawer? With
11572𓀴trembling hands I undid my trunk, turned out the contents, and drew
11573𓀴from the bottom my own hair. I laid the two tresses together, and I
11574𓀴assure you that they were identical. Was it not extraordinary? Puzzle
11575𓀴as I would, I could make nothing at all of what it meant. I returned
11576𓀴the strange hair to the drawer, and I said nothing of the matter to the
11577𓀴Rucastles as I felt that I had put myself in the wrong by opening a
11578𓀴drawer which they had locked.
11579𓀴
11580𓀴“I am naturally observant, as you may have remarked, Mr. Holmes, and I
11581𓀴soon had a pretty good plan of the whole house in my head. There was
11582𓀴one wing, however, which appeared not to be inhabited at all. A door
11583𓀴which faced that which led into the quarters of the Tollers opened into
11584𓀴this suite, but it was invariably locked. One day, however, as I
11585𓀴ascended the stair, I met Mr. Rucastle coming out through this door,
11586𓀴his keys in his hand, and a look on his face which made him a very
11587𓀴different person to the round, jovial man to whom I was accustomed. His
11588𓀴cheeks were red, his brow was all crinkled with anger, and the veins
11589𓀴stood out at his temples with passion. He locked the door and hurried
11590𓀴past me without a word or a look.
11591𓀴
11592𓀴“This aroused my curiosity, so when I went out for a walk in the
11593𓀴grounds with my charge, I strolled round to the side from which I could
11594𓀴see the windows of this part of the house. There were four of them in a
11595𓀴row, three of which were simply dirty, while the fourth was shuttered
11596𓀴up. They were evidently all deserted. As I strolled up and down,
11597𓀴glancing at them occasionally, Mr. Rucastle came out to me, looking as
11598𓀴merry and jovial as ever.
11599𓀴
11600𓀴“‘Ah!’ said he, ‘you must not think me rude if I passed you without a
11601𓀴word, my dear young lady. I was preoccupied with business matters.’
11602𓀴
11603𓀴“I assured him that I was not offended. ‘By the way,’ said I, ‘you seem
11604𓀴to have quite a suite of spare rooms up there, and one of them has the
11605𓀴shutters up.’
11606𓀴
11607𓀴“He looked surprised and, as it seemed to me, a little startled at my
11608𓀴remark.
11609𓀴
11610𓀴“‘Photography is one of my hobbies,’ said he. ‘I have made my dark room
11611𓀴up there. But, dear me! what an observant young lady we have come upon.
11612𓀴Who would have believed it? Who would have ever believed it?’ He spoke
11613𓀴in a jesting tone, but there was no jest in his eyes as he looked at
11614𓀴me. I read suspicion there and annoyance, but no jest.
11615𓀴
11616𓀴“Well, Mr. Holmes, from the moment that I understood that there was
11617𓀴something about that suite of rooms which I was not to know, I was all
11618𓀴on fire to go over them. It was not mere curiosity, though I have my
11619𓀴share of that. It was more a feeling of duty—a feeling that some good
11620𓀴might come from my penetrating to this place. They talk of woman’s
11621𓀴instinct; perhaps it was woman’s instinct which gave me that feeling.
11622𓀴At any rate, it was there, and I was keenly on the lookout for any
11623𓀴chance to pass the forbidden door.
11624𓀴
11625𓀴“It was only yesterday that the chance came. I may tell you that,
11626𓀴besides Mr. Rucastle, both Toller and his wife find something to do in
11627𓀴these deserted rooms, and I once saw him carrying a large black linen
11628𓀴bag with him through the door. Recently he has been drinking hard, and
11629𓀴yesterday evening he was very drunk; and when I came upstairs there was
11630𓀴the key in the door. I have no doubt at all that he had left it there.
11631𓀴Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle were both downstairs, and the child was with
11632𓀴them, so that I had an admirable opportunity. I turned the key gently
11633𓀴in the lock, opened the door, and slipped through.
11634𓀴
11635𓀴“There was a little passage in front of me, unpapered and uncarpeted,
11636𓀴which turned at a right angle at the farther end. Round this corner
11637𓀴were three doors in a line, the first and third of which were open.
11638𓀴They each led into an empty room, dusty and cheerless, with two windows
11639𓀴in the one and one in the other, so thick with dirt that the evening
11640𓀴light glimmered dimly through them. The centre door was closed, and
11641𓀴across the outside of it had been fastened one of the broad bars of an
11642𓀴iron bed, padlocked at one end to a ring in the wall, and fastened at
11643𓀴the other with stout cord. The door itself was locked as well, and the
11644𓀴key was not there. This barricaded door corresponded clearly with the
11645𓀴shuttered window outside, and yet I could see by the glimmer from
11646𓀴beneath it that the room was not in darkness. Evidently there was a
11647𓀴skylight which let in light from above. As I stood in the passage
11648𓀴gazing at the sinister door and wondering what secret it might veil, I
11649𓀴suddenly heard the sound of steps within the room and saw a shadow pass
11650𓀴backward and forward against the little slit of dim light which shone
11651𓀴out from under the door. A mad, unreasoning terror rose up in me at the
11652𓀴sight, Mr. Holmes. My overstrung nerves failed me suddenly, and I
11653𓀴turned and ran—ran as though some dreadful hand were behind me
11654𓀴clutching at the skirt of my dress. I rushed down the passage, through
11655𓀴the door, and straight into the arms of Mr. Rucastle, who was waiting
11656𓀴outside.
11657𓀴
11658𓀴“‘So,’ said he, smiling, ‘it was you, then. I thought that it must be
11659𓀴when I saw the door open.’
11660𓀴
11661𓀴“‘Oh, I am so frightened!’ I panted.
11662𓀴
11663𓀴“‘My dear young lady! my dear young lady!’—you cannot think how
11664𓀴caressing and soothing his manner was—‘and what has frightened you, my
11665𓀴dear young lady?’
11666𓀴
11667𓀴“But his voice was just a little too coaxing. He overdid it. I was
11668𓀴keenly on my guard against him.
11669𓀴
11670𓀴“‘I was foolish enough to go into the empty wing,’ I answered. ‘But it
11671𓀴is so lonely and eerie in this dim light that I was frightened and ran
11672𓀴out again. Oh, it is so dreadfully still in there!’
11673𓀴
11674𓀴“‘Only that?’ said he, looking at me keenly.
11675𓀴
11676𓀴“‘Why, what did you think?’ I asked.
11677𓀴
11678𓀴“‘Why do you think that I lock this door?’
11679𓀴
11680𓀴“‘I am sure that I do not know.’
11681𓀴
11682𓀴“‘It is to keep people out who have no business there. Do you see?’ He
11683𓀴was still smiling in the most amiable manner.
11684𓀴
11685𓀴“‘I am sure if I had known—’
11686𓀴
11687𓀴“‘Well, then, you know now. And if you ever put your foot over that
11688𓀴threshold again’—here in an instant the smile hardened into a grin of
11689𓀴rage, and he glared down at me with the face of a demon—‘I’ll throw you
11690𓀴to the mastiff.’
11691𓀴
11692𓀴“I was so terrified that I do not know what I did. I suppose that I
11693𓀴must have rushed past him into my room. I remember nothing until I
11694𓀴found myself lying on my bed trembling all over. Then I thought of you,
11695𓀴Mr. Holmes. I could not live there longer without some advice. I was
11696𓀴frightened of the house, of the man, of the woman, of the servants,
11697𓀴even of the child. They were all horrible to me. If I could only bring
11698𓀴you down all would be well. Of course I might have fled from the house,
11699𓀴but my curiosity was almost as strong as my fears. My mind was soon
11700𓀴made up. I would send you a wire. I put on my hat and cloak, went down
11701𓀴to the office, which is about half a mile from the house, and then
11702𓀴returned, feeling very much easier. A horrible doubt came into my mind
11703𓀴as I approached the door lest the dog might be loose, but I remembered
11704𓀴that Toller had drunk himself into a state of insensibility that
11705𓀴evening, and I knew that he was the only one in the household who had
11706𓀴any influence with the savage creature, or who would venture to set him
11707𓀴free. I slipped in in safety and lay awake half the night in my joy at
11708𓀴the thought of seeing you. I had no difficulty in getting leave to come
11709𓀴into Winchester this morning, but I must be back before three o’clock,
11710𓀴for Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle are going on a visit, and will be away all
11711𓀴the evening, so that I must look after the child. Now I have told you
11712𓀴all my adventures, Mr. Holmes, and I should be very glad if you could
11713𓀴tell me what it all means, and, above all, what I should do.”
11714𓀴
11715𓀴Holmes and I had listened spellbound to this extraordinary story. My
11716𓀴friend rose now and paced up and down the room, his hands in his
11717𓀴pockets, and an expression of the most profound gravity upon his face.
11718𓀴
11719𓀴“Is Toller still drunk?” he asked.
11720𓀴
11721𓀴“Yes. I heard his wife tell Mrs. Rucastle that she could do nothing
11722𓀴with him.”
11723𓀴
11724𓀴“That is well. And the Rucastles go out to-night?”
11725𓀴
11726𓀴“Yes.”
11727𓀴
11728𓀴“Is there a cellar with a good strong lock?”
11729𓀴
11730𓀴“Yes, the wine-cellar.”
11731𓀴
11732𓀴“You seem to me to have acted all through this matter like a very brave
11733𓀴and sensible girl, Miss Hunter. Do you think that you could perform one
11734𓀴more feat? I should not ask it of you if I did not think you a quite
11735𓀴exceptional woman.”
11736𓀴
11737𓀴“I will try. What is it?”
11738𓀴
11739𓀴“We shall be at the Copper Beeches by seven o’clock, my friend and I.
