11740𓀴The Rucastles will be gone by that time, and Toller will, we hope, be
11741𓀴incapable. There only remains Mrs. Toller, who might give the alarm. If
11742𓀴you could send her into the cellar on some errand, and then turn the
11743𓀴key upon her, you would facilitate matters immensely.”
11744𓀴
11745𓀴“I will do it.”
11746𓀴
11747𓀴“Excellent! We shall then look thoroughly into the affair. Of course
11748𓀴there is only one feasible explanation. You have been brought there to
11749𓀴personate someone, and the real person is imprisoned in this chamber.
11750𓀴That is obvious. As to who this prisoner is, I have no doubt that it is
11751𓀴the daughter, Miss Alice Rucastle, if I remember right, who was said to
11752𓀴have gone to America. You were chosen, doubtless, as resembling her in
11753𓀴height, figure, and the colour of your hair. Hers had been cut off,
11754𓀴very possibly in some illness through which she has passed, and so, of
11755𓀴course, yours had to be sacrificed also. By a curious chance you came
11756𓀴upon her tresses. The man in the road was undoubtedly some friend of
11757𓀴hers—possibly her _fiancé_—and no doubt, as you wore the girl’s dress
11758𓀴and were so like her, he was convinced from your laughter, whenever he
11759𓀴saw you, and afterwards from your gesture, that Miss Rucastle was
11760𓀴perfectly happy, and that she no longer desired his attentions. The dog
11761𓀴is let loose at night to prevent him from endeavouring to communicate
11762𓀴with her. So much is fairly clear. The most serious point in the case
11763𓀴is the disposition of the child.”
11764𓀴
11765𓀴“What on earth has that to do with it?” I ejaculated.
11766𓀴
11767𓀴“My dear Watson, you as a medical man are continually gaining light as
11768𓀴to the tendencies of a child by the study of the parents. Don’t you see
11769𓀴that the converse is equally valid. I have frequently gained my first
11770𓀴real insight into the character of parents by studying their children.
11771𓀴This child’s disposition is abnormally cruel, merely for cruelty’s
11772𓀴sake, and whether he derives this from his smiling father, as I should
11773𓀴suspect, or from his mother, it bodes evil for the poor girl who is in
11774𓀴their power.”
11775𓀴
11776𓀴“I am sure that you are right, Mr. Holmes,” cried our client. “A
11777𓀴thousand things come back to me which make me certain that you have hit
11778𓀴it. Oh, let us lose not an instant in bringing help to this poor
11779𓀴creature.”
11780𓀴
11781𓀴“We must be circumspect, for we are dealing with a very cunning man. We
11782𓀴can do nothing until seven o’clock. At that hour we shall be with you,
11783𓀴and it will not be long before we solve the mystery.”
11784𓀴
11785𓀴We were as good as our word, for it was just seven when we reached the
11786𓀴Copper Beeches, having put up our trap at a wayside public-house. The
11787𓀴group of trees, with their dark leaves shining like burnished metal in
11788𓀴the light of the setting sun, were sufficient to mark the house even
11789𓀴had Miss Hunter not been standing smiling on the door-step.
11790𓀴
11791𓀴“Have you managed it?” asked Holmes.
11792𓀴
11793𓀴A loud thudding noise came from somewhere downstairs. “That is Mrs.
11794𓀴Toller in the cellar,” said she. “Her husband lies snoring on the
11795𓀴kitchen rug. Here are his keys, which are the duplicates of Mr.
11796𓀴Rucastle’s.”
11797𓀴
11798𓀴“You have done well indeed!” cried Holmes with enthusiasm. “Now lead
11799𓀴the way, and we shall soon see the end of this black business.”
11800𓀴
11801𓀴We passed up the stair, unlocked the door, followed on down a passage,
11802𓀴and found ourselves in front of the barricade which Miss Hunter had
11803𓀴described. Holmes cut the cord and removed the transverse bar. Then he
11804𓀴tried the various keys in the lock, but without success. No sound came
11805𓀴from within, and at the silence Holmes’ face clouded over.
11806𓀴
11807𓀴“I trust that we are not too late,” said he. “I think, Miss Hunter,
11808𓀴that we had better go in without you. Now, Watson, put your shoulder to
11809𓀴it, and we shall see whether we cannot make our way in.”
11810𓀴
11811𓀴It was an old rickety door and gave at once before our united strength.
11812𓀴Together we rushed into the room. It was empty. There was no furniture
11813𓀴save a little pallet bed, a small table, and a basketful of linen. The
11814𓀴skylight above was open, and the prisoner gone.
11815𓀴
11816𓀴“There has been some villainy here,” said Holmes; “this beauty has
11817𓀴guessed Miss Hunter’s intentions and has carried his victim off.”
11818𓀴
11819𓀴“But how?”
11820𓀴
11821𓀴“Through the skylight. We shall soon see how he managed it.” He swung
11822𓀴himself up onto the roof. “Ah, yes,” he cried, “here’s the end of a
11823𓀴long light ladder against the eaves. That is how he did it.”
11824𓀴
11825𓀴“But it is impossible,” said Miss Hunter; “the ladder was not there
11826𓀴when the Rucastles went away.”
11827𓀴
11828𓀴“He has come back and done it. I tell you that he is a clever and
11829𓀴dangerous man. I should not be very much surprised if this were he
11830𓀴whose step I hear now upon the stair. I think, Watson, that it would be
11831𓀴as well for you to have your pistol ready.”
11832𓀴
11833𓀴The words were hardly out of his mouth before a man appeared at the
11834𓀴door of the room, a very fat and burly man, with a heavy stick in his
11835𓀴hand. Miss Hunter screamed and shrunk against the wall at the sight of
11836𓀴him, but Sherlock Holmes sprang forward and confronted him.
11837𓀴
11838𓀴“You villain!” said he, “where’s your daughter?”
11839𓀴
11840𓀴The fat man cast his eyes round, and then up at the open skylight.
11841𓀴
11842𓀴“It is for me to ask you that,” he shrieked, “you thieves! Spies and
11843𓀴thieves! I have caught you, have I? You are in my power. I’ll serve
11844𓀴you!” He turned and clattered down the stairs as hard as he could go.
11845𓀴
11846𓀴“He’s gone for the dog!” cried Miss Hunter.
11847𓀴
11848𓀴“I have my revolver,” said I.
11849𓀴
11850𓀴“Better close the front door,” cried Holmes, and we all rushed down the
11851𓀴stairs together. We had hardly reached the hall when we heard the
11852𓀴baying of a hound, and then a scream of agony, with a horrible worrying
11853𓀴sound which it was dreadful to listen to. An elderly man with a red
11854𓀴face and shaking limbs came staggering out at a side door.
11855𓀴
11856𓀴“My God!” he cried. “Someone has loosed the dog. It’s not been fed for
11857𓀴two days. Quick, quick, or it’ll be too late!”
11858𓀴
11859𓀴Holmes and I rushed out and round the angle of the house, with Toller
11860𓀴hurrying behind us. There was the huge famished brute, its black muzzle
11861𓀴buried in Rucastle’s throat, while he writhed and screamed upon the
11862𓀴ground. Running up, I blew its brains out, and it fell over with its
11863𓀴keen white teeth still meeting in the great creases of his neck. With
11864𓀴much labour we separated them and carried him, living but horribly
11865𓀴mangled, into the house. We laid him upon the drawing-room sofa, and
11866𓀴having dispatched the sobered Toller to bear the news to his wife, I
11867𓀴did what I could to relieve his pain. We were all assembled round him
11868𓀴when the door opened, and a tall, gaunt woman entered the room.
11869𓀴
11870𓀴“Mrs. Toller!” cried Miss Hunter.
11871𓀴
11872𓀴“Yes, miss. Mr. Rucastle let me out when he came back before he went up
11873𓀴to you. Ah, miss, it is a pity you didn’t let me know what you were
11874𓀴planning, for I would have told you that your pains were wasted.”
11875𓀴
11876𓀴“Ha!” said Holmes, looking keenly at her. “It is clear that Mrs. Toller
11877𓀴knows more about this matter than anyone else.”
11878𓀴
11879𓀴“Yes, sir, I do, and I am ready enough to tell what I know.”
11880𓀴
11881𓀴“Then, pray, sit down, and let us hear it for there are several points
11882𓀴on which I must confess that I am still in the dark.”
11883𓀴
11884𓀴“I will soon make it clear to you,” said she; “and I’d have done so
11885𓀴before now if I could ha’ got out from the cellar. If there’s
11886𓀴police-court business over this, you’ll remember that I was the one
11887𓀴that stood your friend, and that I was Miss Alice’s friend too.
11888𓀴
11889𓀴“She was never happy at home, Miss Alice wasn’t, from the time that her
11890𓀴father married again. She was slighted like and had no say in anything,
11891𓀴but it never really became bad for her until after she met Mr. Fowler
11892𓀴at a friend’s house. As well as I could learn, Miss Alice had rights of
11893𓀴her own by will, but she was so quiet and patient, she was, that she
11894𓀴never said a word about them but just left everything in Mr. Rucastle’s
11895𓀴hands. He knew he was safe with her; but when there was a chance of a
11896𓀴husband coming forward, who would ask for all that the law would give
11897𓀴him, then her father thought it time to put a stop on it. He wanted her
11898𓀴to sign a paper, so that whether she married or not, he could use her
11899𓀴money. When she wouldn’t do it, he kept on worrying her until she got
11900𓀴brain-fever, and for six weeks was at death’s door. Then she got better
11901𓀴at last, all worn to a shadow, and with her beautiful hair cut off; but
11902𓀴that didn’t make no change in her young man, and he stuck to her as
11903𓀴true as man could be.”
11904𓀴
11905𓀴“Ah,” said Holmes, “I think that what you have been good enough to tell
11906𓀴us makes the matter fairly clear, and that I can deduce all that
11907𓀴remains. Mr. Rucastle then, I presume, took to this system of
11908𓀴imprisonment?”
11909𓀴
11910𓀴“Yes, sir.”
11911𓀴
11912𓀴“And brought Miss Hunter down from London in order to get rid of the
11913𓀴disagreeable persistence of Mr. Fowler.”
11914𓀴
11915𓀴“That was it, sir.”
11916𓀴
11917𓀴“But Mr. Fowler being a persevering man, as a good seaman should be,
11918𓀴blockaded the house, and having met you succeeded by certain arguments,
11919𓀴metallic or otherwise, in convincing you that your interests were the
11920𓀴same as his.”
11921𓀴
11922𓀴“Mr. Fowler was a very kind-spoken, free-handed gentleman,” said Mrs.
11923𓀴Toller serenely.
11924𓀴
11925𓀴“And in this way he managed that your good man should have no want of
11926𓀴drink, and that a ladder should be ready at the moment when your master
11927𓀴had gone out.”
11928𓀴
11929𓀴“You have it, sir, just as it happened.”
11930𓀴
11931𓀴“I am sure we owe you an apology, Mrs. Toller,” said Holmes, “for you
11932𓀴have certainly cleared up everything which puzzled us. And here comes
11933𓀴the country surgeon and Mrs. Rucastle, so I think, Watson, that we had
11934𓀴best escort Miss Hunter back to Winchester, as it seems to me that our
11935𓀴_locus standi_ now is rather a questionable one.”
11936𓀴
11937𓀴And thus was solved the mystery of the sinister house with the copper
11938𓀴beeches in front of the door. Mr. Rucastle survived, but was always a
11939𓀴broken man, kept alive solely through the care of his devoted wife.
11940𓀴They still live with their old servants, who probably know so much of
11941𓀴Rucastle’s past life that he finds it difficult to part from them. Mr.
11942𓀴Fowler and Miss Rucastle were married, by special license, in
11943𓀴Southampton the day after their flight, and he is now the holder of a
11944𓀴government appointment in the island of Mauritius. As to Miss Violet
11945𓀴Hunter, my friend Holmes, rather to my disappointment, manifested no
11946𓀴further interest in her when once she had ceased to be the centre of
11947𓀴one of his problems, and she is now the head of a private school at
11948𓀴Walsall, where I believe that she has met with considerable success.
11949𓀴
11950𓀴
11951𓀴
11952𓀴
11953𓀴
